Apple

70 varieties (back to top)

Akane

Akane is a Japanese apple developed at the Morioka research station from a cross of Jonathan and Worcester Pearmain. It is also known as Tokyo Rose, Tohoku No.3 and Prime Red, and was introduced to the United States in the late 1930s.

It is a bright-red, flattish to heart-shaped apple with crisp white flesh that does not brown quickly. The flavour is a lively sweet-sharp balance carrying the zesty character of Jonathan, often with strawberry and floral notes. It is one of the better early-season apples and keeps its shape when cooked.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Akane (apple)
  • Specialty Produce: Akane Apples

Anna

Anna is a low-chill apple bred by Abba Stein at the Ein Shemer kibbutz in Israel in 1959, descended from Golden Delicious and developed specifically for warm climates. Its very low chill requirement, far below what most apples need, makes it a popular choice for subtropical Australian gardens.

It is very early ripening and crops heavily, with crisp creamy flesh, a sweet balanced flavour, and green to greenish-yellow skin flushed with red. Anna needs cross-pollination and is commonly grown alongside Dorsett Golden or Ein Shemer.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Anna (apple)
  • Orange Pippin: Anna apple

Belle Cacheuse

Belle Cacheuse is the Australian rendering of the French name Belle Cauchoise, a traditional cider apple of Normandy whose name refers to the Pays de Caux district. The Australian spelling arose during importation, and the NSW Department of Primary Industries confirmed it as one of several French cider varieties that had been grown in Australia for years without being recognised as such.

It is a notably large, flattish apple with green skin striped red on the sunny side, unusual among dedicated cider apples in being useful for eating and cooking as well as juice. Because it is on the sweet side rather than strongly tannic, it is best added to a blend alongside sharp and bitter varieties. The tree is vigorous and tends to biennial cropping.

3 sources
  • NSW Department of Primary Industries: Additional cider varieties
  • Heritage & Rare Fruit Network: Cider Apples
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Belle Cacheuse Apple

Bolero Ballerina

Bolero is one of the Ballerina columnar apples, originally named Tuscan. It was bred at East Malling Research Station in Kent from a Wijcik McIntosh columnar mutation crossed with Greensleeves. The Ballerina habit is genetic, not the result of pruning: the tree forms a single upright stem with minimal branching, carrying its fruit on very short spurs close to the trunk. That makes it narrow and well suited to small gardens, courtyards and patio pots, needing little to no pruning.

It is a dessert apple, eaten fresh. The flesh is described as cream coloured, moderately crisp and juicy with a sweet-sharp flavour, similar to its Greensleeves parent.

3 sources
  • Pomiferous: Tuscan (Bolero)
  • RHS: Malus domestica Ballerina Bolero
  • Wikipedia: Flamenco (apple)

Bonza

Bonza is an Australian apple that arose as a chance seedling in the late 1950s, found by Ben Atkinson in his orchard in the Batlow area of the Snowy Mountains region of New South Wales. Unusually for an Australian apple, it is a relatively high-chill variety, reflecting its cool mountain origins.

The fruit is medium to large and flat-conic, ripening to cream with a crimson blush over much of the skin. The flesh is white, firm, crisp and juicy with a sweet flavour, and it keeps for several months in cold storage. It is grown mainly as a fresh-eating apple.

2 sources
  • Pomiferous: Bonza
  • NSW Department of Primary Industries: Bonza

Bramley s

Bramley's Seedling is widely regarded as the definitive English cooking apple. It traces to a single tree grown from pips planted by Mary Ann Brailsford in her garden at Southwell, Nottinghamshire, in 1809. The cottage and tree later passed to the butcher Matthew Bramley, and in the 1850s a local nurseryman, Henry Merryweather, took cuttings to propagate it on the condition the apples carried Bramley's name. Every Bramley today descends from that original tree.

Bramley's is a triploid, which means it cannot pollinate other apples and needs to be grown near two compatible pollinating varieties. The fruit is large and strongly acidic, so it is almost always eaten cooked rather than fresh. When baked it breaks down to a light, golden, fluffy puree, which is why it is prized for pies, crumbles and sauces.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia - Bramley apple
  • Orange Pippin - Bramley's Seedling
  • Apples and People - Bramley's Seedling

Brown Snout

Brown Snout is a 19th century bittersweet cider apple from Herefordshire, discovered around 1850 as a chance seedling on Mr Dent's farm at Yarkhill and later propagated widely by the H. P. Bulmer company of Hereford. Its name comes from the heavy patch of brown russet around the calyx end of the fruit, which resembles a snout.

It is a late-flowering variety with relatively high tannins and low malic acid, giving a mild bittersweet, dark cider with soft tannins. It can stand alone or be blended for added acidity.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Brown Snout
  • Pomiferous: Brown Snout

Bulmer s Norman

Bulmer's Norman is a French-origin bittersweet cider apple found growing in Normandy and brought to England by H. P. Bulmer & Co. of Hereford in the early 1890s. On its own it makes a mild bittersweet cider, with a golden, sweet, astringent juice that ferments quickly.

It is grown for its hard, bitter tannin, which adds body and texture in blends. The tree is large, vigorous and spreading, and a dependable but biennial cropper. It is also valued as a sturdy rootstock and interstem onto which other varieties are grafted.

2 sources
  • Pomiferous: Bulmer's Norman
  • CooksInfo: Bulmer's Norman Apples

Chataingier

Chataingier is the Australian rendering of the French cider apple Chataignier, whose name was slightly corrupted during importation. The NSW Department of Primary Industries lists it under the original French spelling among cider varieties confirmed to be growing in Australia. It is a very old variety, said to have originated in Normandy, France, perhaps as far back as the 1200s.

The fruit is medium to large and round-flattened, with thin glossy skin that is greenish-yellow washed and broken-striped with dull dark red. The flesh is white and breaking, fairly sweet and subacid with a tannic note, and it is used for cider.

3 sources
  • NSW Department of Primary Industries: Additional cider varieties
  • Pomiferous: Chataignier
  • Heritage & Rare Fruit Network: Cider Apples

Cornish Aromatic

Cornish Aromatic is a heritage dessert apple from Cornwall, England, first recorded in 1813 when Sir Christopher Hawkins brought it to notice by sending fruit to the London Horticultural Society, though it is thought to be far older. It became a popular apple of country estates and cottage gardens.

The fruit is handsome and bright red with patches of grey-brown russet and darker red striping. The firm, fine-textured flesh has a crisp, nutty, aromatic and lightly spicy flavour. It is a late-season apple, picked in mid-autumn and good for eating through the winter.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Cornish Aromatic
  • Orange Pippin: Cornish Aromatic

Cox Orange Pippin

Cox's Orange Pippin was first grown around 1825 to 1830 at Colnbrook in Buckinghamshire, England, by Richard Cox, a retired brewer turned horticulturist. It became England's leading dessert apple and by the early 2000s accounted for more than half the UK dessert apple acreage. DNA analysis points to Margil as a parent.

The medium-sized fruit is orange-red over a yellow background, mottled with carmine. The yellow-white flesh is fine-grained, crisp, very juicy and highly aromatic, with a sprightly subacid flavour carrying hints of cherry and anise that softens with age. It is celebrated as a richly flavoured eating apple.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Cox's Orange Pippin
  • Orange Pippin: Cox's Orange Pippin

Crimson Crisp

Crimson Crisp, originally trialled as Co-op 39, came from the cooperative apple breeding program run by the Indiana (Purdue), Illinois and New Jersey (Rutgers) experiment stations. The original seedling traces back to a 1971 cross at Cream Ridge, New Jersey, and carries scab resistance bred in from the crabapple Malus floribunda. The trade name highlights its red colour and crisp flesh.

It is a midseason dessert apple with an attractive crimson-red skin and yellow flesh. The texture is very crisp and breaking, and the flavour is rich and subacid. The fruit holds on the tree without dropping when mature, and keeps well in storage.

2 sources
  • Janick, Goffreda & Korban, 'Co-op 39 (CrimsonCrisp) Apple', HortScience 41(2), 2006 (Purdue)
  • Pomiferous: Co-op 39 (Crimson Crisp)

Cumulus

Cumulus is a columnar (pillar form) apple, one of a series bred at the Research and Breeding Institute of Pomology in Holovousy, Czech Republic. Like other columnar apples it descends from the Wijcik McIntosh mutation, so it grows as a narrow upright single stem with very short fruiting spurs and almost no side branches. That habit makes it suited to pots, courtyards and tight spaces rather than a spreading orchard tree, and it needs little structural pruning to keep its slim shape.

Cumulus carries genetic resistance to apple scab, though it can be susceptible to powdery mildew to varying degrees. In Australian supply it is sold as a late season red eating apple, and being self sterile it needs a compatible pollinator nearby such as the related columnar varieties Herald or Harmony.

2 sources
  • Horticultural Science (Czech Academy of Agricultural Sciences) - new columnar apple cultivars bred in Holovousy
  • Heritage Fruit Trees - Cumulus Apple (columnar)

Dabinett

Dabinett is a traditional English bittersweet cider apple from Somerset, found by William Dabinett growing as a wilding in a hedge at Middle Lambrook, South Petherton, around the early 1900s. One probable parent was the late bittersweet variety Chisel Jersey.

It is prized as a vintage cider variety, capable of making a soft, full-bodied, high quality single varietal cider. As a bittersweet it carries high tannin with low acidity, giving a sweet, astringent juice. The tree is a weak, spreading grower and fruit ripens late, usually November in Britain.

2 sources
  • National Fruit Collection: Dabinett (cider apple)
  • Wikipedia: Dabinett

Democrat

Democrat is a Tasmanian heritage apple found growing about 1900 in the orchard of J. Duffy near Hobart, and originally known as Duffy's Seedling. It was briefly renamed Tasma in 1913 to avoid confusion with an American apple, though that name did not endure. It is possibly a seedling of Hoover.

The fruit is medium to large and conic, maturing to a dull dark red over a greenish-yellow base, with very firm, crisp, juicy flesh that is sweet and mild. Democrat is best known for ripening very late and then keeping for many months in cold storage, which made it a mainstay of the Tasmanian export trade for decades.

2 sources
  • Pomiferous: Democrat
  • Salt Spring Apple Company: Democrat

Dorsett Golden

Dorsett Golden is a low-chill apple that originated in the Bahamas around 1953 as a seedling at the home of Mrs Irene Dorsett in Nassau. Its very low chill requirement makes it one of the best apples for warm, subtropical parts of Australia where standard apples will not fruit.

The fruit is yellow to yellow-green, often with a pink blush, with firm white flesh and a sweet, lightly aromatic flavour. It is largely self-fertile but crops better with a pollinator, and pairs well with the similarly low-chill Anna apple, which flowers at the same time.

3 sources
  • Specialty Produce: Golden Dorsett Apples
  • Orange Pippin: Dorsett Golden apple
  • Daleys Fruit: Apple Dorsett Golden

Ein Shemer

Ein Shemer is an Israeli apple raised by Abba Stein at the Ein Shemer kibbutz, a cross of Zabidani and Golden Delicious introduced in the 1960s. It is one of the standout low-chill apples for warm and subtropical districts, listed by University of California horticulturists among the varieties that fruit reliably where winter cold is minimal. That makes it a sensible pick for milder Australian gardens where most apples struggle to set fruit.

The fruit is medium to large and Golden Delicious in type, golden-yellow at maturity (sometimes with a light blush on the sunny side), with juicy, sweet-sharp pale flesh. It is partially self-fertile, so it can crop on its own but tends to do better with a pollinator nearby. It is an early, heavy bearer.

2 sources
  • Pomiferous
  • University of California Cooperative Extension, Low-Chill Apples (K. Carter, UC Riverside)

Empire

Empire is a McIntosh-type apple from Cornell University's New York State Agricultural Experiment Station at Geneva. It grew from a seed sown in 1945 by Lester C. Anderson and was released in 1966, named for the Empire State of New York. Its parents are McIntosh and Red Delicious.

The fruit combines the crisp, tangy McIntosh character with the sweetness and deep crimson colour of Red Delicious, over creamy white flesh. It is crisp, juicy and sweet-tart, slower to brown than most, and a good all-purpose apple. It is harvested in early autumn and stores well.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Empire (apple)
  • U.S. Apple Association: Empire

Fenouillet Gris

Fenouillet Gris is an old French dessert apple that originated in Anjou and was first described in 1608. Its defining feature is flavour. The name comes from fenouil (fennel), and the fruit carries a distinct, spicy aniseed taste that sets it apart from almost every other apple. It collected a long list of synonyms over the centuries, among them Pomme d'Anis and, in England, Caraway Russet.

The fruit is small to medium and heavily russeted, with a dull yellow base covered in netted reddish-brown and light russet. The flesh is fine, white, crisp and on the dry side, sweet with that signature anise note. Picked around mid October, it works as a fresh eating apple and is also used for cider and dried apple rings.

2 sources
  • UK National Fruit Collection: Fenouillet Gris
  • Pomiferous: Fenouillet Gris

Five Crown pippin

Five Crown Pippin is an old English apple, also known as London Pippin, with origins that are somewhat confused but documented from the 1500s. It was being sold in Norfolk in the 1700s as a genuine local apple. Its most recognisable feature is its shape: the fruit is prominently ribbed and crowned at the eye, giving the distinctive five-crowned outline that names it.

It is a medium green apple with no russet, becoming more yellow as it matures. The flesh is fairly acidic, sometimes with a nutty character, and the apple keeps its shape well when cooked, making it a useful culinary variety. It is also a long keeper, harvested in mid-autumn and usable through the winter months.

2 sources
  • Apples & Orchards Project - Five Crowned Pippin
  • Orange Pippin - Five Crown Pippin

Flamenco Ballerina

Flamenco is one of the Ballerina columnar apples, developed at East Malling Research Station in Kent. Its breeding traces back to a Wijcik McIntosh columnar mutation, and the columnar habit is a genetic trait rather than a product of pruning. The tree grows as a single upright stem with very little side branching, carrying its fruit on short spurs along the central trunk. This gives a tall, very narrow tree that is well suited to small gardens and patio containers and needs minimal pruning.

It is grown mainly as a fresh-eating dessert apple, with crisp white flesh, and is one of the later-maturing apples in the Ballerina range.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Flamenco (apple)
  • Frank P Matthews: Ballerina Flamenco

Forfar Pippin

Forfar Pippin is an old dual-purpose apple whose exact origin is uncertain. It is often linked to the Dutch Mignonne (also called Albury Park Nonesuch), thought to have come from the Netherlands and brought to Britain in 1771, though it is also recorded by Australian heritage nurseries as a Scottish apple. Whatever its roots, it earned its reputation in the kitchen as much as on the table.

It is best known as the traditional apple for Apple Charlotte, holding its shape well when cooked and making a flavourful, sweet-tart sauce. The fruit is medium to large and round-conic, often a little irregular and ribbed, with a yellow base, pale reddish streaks and patches of russet. The firm, crisp flesh is juicy, aromatic and sweet, so it doubles as a dessert apple as well as a cooker.

2 sources
  • Pomiferous: Forfar Pippin
  • Orange Pippin: Dutch Mignonne apple

Frequin Rouge

Frequin Rouge is a traditional French cider apple associated with Normandy, where it is one of the named varieties permitted under the Pays d'Auge appellation for cider and Calvados. In the French classification it falls among the bitter to bittersweet group, prized for the depth and tannin it brings to a blend. It is regarded as a vintage variety, and its sometimes-used Australian name is Frequin Rouge Amer, the word amer meaning bitter.

The fruit is small to medium, yellow or pale green with broken red stripes, and the flesh is crisp and juicy with very high tannin, far too astringent to eat fresh. Like many old cider varieties it tends to biennial bearing, cropping heavily in alternate years.

5 sources
  • NSW Department of Primary Industries: Additional cider varieties
  • Pomiferous: Frequin Rouge
  • Orange Pippin Trees: Cider apple varieties from France
  • Heritage & Rare Fruit Network: Cider Apples
  • Raintree Nursery: Frequin Rouge Cider Apple

Freyberg

Freyberg is a New Zealand dessert apple raised by J.H. Kidd at Greytown in the Wairarapa, from a cross of Golden Delicious and Cox's Orange Pippin. Bred in the 1930s and released in 1959, it was named after Lord Bernard Freyberg, the Governor-General of New Zealand. In appearance it strongly resembles a small Golden Delicious, with golden-green skin and creamy-white, crisp flesh.

Its appeal is in the flavour, which combines the sweetness of Golden Delicious with a Cox-like aromatic character, sometimes described as having a distinct pear-like note. Despite this quality it never became a major commercial apple, partly because of its close resemblance to Golden Delicious, so it remains a variety grown mostly by enthusiasts.

3 sources
  • Orange Pippin - Freyberg
  • Keepers Nursery - Freyberg
  • Salt Spring Apple Company - Freyburg

Fuji

Fuji was developed by growers at the Tohoku Research Station in Fujisaki, Aomori, Japan, from a cross of the American varieties Ralls Janet and Red Delicious. It was named and brought to market in 1962, with its name taken from the town of Fujisaki where it was bred.

The fruit is prized for dense flesh that is notably sweet, crisp and very juicy, with cream-coloured flesh and a flavour that can carry hints of honey and pear. It is a late-season apple that stores exceptionally well, staying fresh for many months in refrigerated storage, which is part of why it became a global supermarket staple.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Fuji (apple)
  • New York Apple Association: Fuji

Gala

Gala was raised by orchardist J.H. Kidd at Greytown, New Zealand in the 1930s, a cross of Kidd's Orange Red with Golden Delicious. It was selected in 1939 and introduced commercially around 1960, going on to become one of the most widely grown apples in the world.

The fruit is sweet and mild with a crisp but not hard texture and a fragrant, aromatic flavour. Its skin is non-uniform, usually striped or mottled with an overall orange to reddish colour. It is mainly an eating apple and ripens in late summer to autumn.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Gala (apple)
  • Specialty Produce: Gala Apples

Geeveston Fanny

Geeveston Fanny is a Tasmanian heritage apple thought to have originated at Geeveston in the Huon Valley, where the oldest known tree grew in James Evans's orchard in 1880. It was a popular commercial variety in Tasmania before modern supermarket apples pushed it aside.

The medium fruit has a pale lemon and light green base, with much of the skin flushed pale red and marked with darker stripes. The flesh is crisp with a subacid, aromatic flavour, and it is a late-season dessert apple.

2 sources
  • National Fruit Collection: Geeveston Fanny
  • Pomiferous: Geeveston Fanny

Golden Delicious

Golden Delicious arose as a chance seedling on the Mullins family farm in Clay County, West Virginia, and was discovered in the early twentieth century. Stark Brothers Nurseries bought the original tree and its propagation rights and began marketing it alongside Red Delicious in 1914. It is the official state fruit of West Virginia.

The fruit has thin yellowish-green skin and very sweet, crisp, juicy flesh, often with a honeyed, almost floral note. It is versatile for fresh eating, salads, sauce and baking, where it holds together well. The skin bruises and shrivels easily, so it needs gentle handling. It is also a parent of later cultivars including Gala.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Golden Delicious
  • e-WV: The West Virginia Encyclopedia, Golden Delicious

Golden Harvey

Golden Harvey, also known as the Brandy apple, is one of the older English varieties, believed to have originated in Herefordshire around the 1600s. It is a small dessert apple whose skin is almost entirely covered in rough golden russet. The name refers as much to its notably yellow flesh as to the russeted skin, and the flesh is crisp and firm.

Although it can be eaten fresh, Golden Harvey is best known as a cider apple. Its juice is intensely sweet and strong, producing a cider of unusual potency, which earned it the alternative name Brandy. Popular in Victorian times, it later fell out of favour and is now found mainly in specialist heritage orchards. It is also valued as a long keeper, holding its quality well into the following year.

3 sources
  • Slow Food Foundation - Ark of Taste - Golden Harvey
  • SkillCult - Golden Harvey
  • Pomiferous - Golden Harvey

Granny Smith

Granny Smith is an Australian apple discovered in 1868 by Maria Ann Smith at Eastwood, New South Wales. It grew as a chance seedling near a creek where she had discarded the remains of French crab-apples grown in Tasmania, and is thought to be a cross of the domestic apple with a wild apple species.

The fruit has bright green skin, hard firm flesh, and a tart, sour, acidic flavour. It is valued for both cooking (pies, crumbles) and fresh eating, and its excellent shelf life made it a major export variety worldwide.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Granny Smith
  • Australian Dictionary of Biography: Maria Ann Smith
  • City of Ryde: Granny Smith

Gravenstein

Gravenstein is an old triploid apple linked to Graasten Palace in southern Denmark, where it was recorded by 1669, though it likely originated earlier in Germany or Italy. Because it is triploid, it needs another apple to pollinate it and is itself a poor pollinator. In 2005 the Danish food minister declared it the national apple of Denmark.

The fruit has waxy yellow-green skin with crimson spots and reddish lines, and juicy, light yellow flesh with a lively, tart flavour. It is prized for cooking, especially applesauce and cider, but does not keep well, so it is mostly available only in season.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Gravenstein
  • Orange Pippin: Gravenstein

Herald

Herald is a columnar apple from the series bred at the Research and Breeding Institute of Pomology in Holovousy, Czech Republic. As with other columnar (pillar) apples it traces back to the Wijcik McIntosh mutation and grows as a slim, upright single stem that fruits on short spurs close to the trunk, so it stays narrow without training. That makes it a practical choice for tubs, balconies and small gardens where a normal apple tree would not fit.

A notable point of difference for Herald is its disease package: it possesses combined resistance to both apple scab and powdery mildew, which is stronger than several of its sibling cultivars. In Australia it is offered as a red skinned eating apple, and like all columnar apples it is not self fertile, so it needs another compatible apple flowering at the same time to set a crop.

2 sources
  • Horticultural Science (Czech Academy of Agricultural Sciences) - new columnar apple cultivars bred in Holovousy
  • Heritage Fruit Trees - Herald Apple (columnar)

Hubbardston Nonsuch

Hubbardston Nonsuch is an American heritage dessert apple that originated in the town of Hubbardston, Massachusetts, and was first recorded in 1832. The name Nonsuch (a form of nonpareil, meaning without equal) was attached to several prized apples of the period. It was a popular market apple in the New England and New York region during the nineteenth century, and Hubbardston still features apples on its town seal.

The fruit has firm, fine-textured white flesh with a sweet, subacid flavour. It carries a green-yellow ground colour, sometimes with brown streaking, on a conical fruit. Once a favourite around Boston, it is now a rare variety found mainly in specialist orchards and home gardens.

3 sources
  • National Fruit Collection - Hubbardston Nonsuch
  • Specialty Produce - Hubbardston Nonesuch
  • Salt Spring Apple Company - Hubbardston Nonsuch

Improved Foxwhelp

Improved Foxwhelp belongs to the Foxwhelp family of cider apples, a very old group from the west Midlands of England (Herefordshire or Gloucestershire) first recorded in the 17th century. Foxwhelps are classed as bittersharp, combining high malic acid with high tannin, which makes a powerful, very sharp cider traditionally used to add strength and kick to blends. The original Foxwhelp was so prone to producing branch sports that it spawned a string of selections, and the improved or rejuvenated Foxwhelps were 19th century attempts to revive the failing old variety by grafting its scions onto its own seedlings.

Improved Foxwhelp has skin deeply striped crimson and yellow, with tender yellow flesh tinged red and a rough, acid flavour. It is an early-ripening cider apple.

4 sources
  • Wikipedia: Foxwhelp
  • Heritage & Rare Fruit Network: Cider Apples
  • Adam's Apple Trees: Foxwhelp Cider Apple
  • Yalca Fruit Trees: Improved Foxwhelp cider apple

Isaac Newton s

The apple known as Isaac Newton's Tree is the variety Flower of Kent, an old green cooking apple. Its fame comes from its link to Sir Isaac Newton, who according to the well-known story watched an apple fall from a tree of this variety at his home, Woolsthorpe Manor, and was prompted to think about gravity. Most surviving Flower of Kent trees are said to descend from that historic tree.

Flower of Kent itself is a pear-shaped, mealy, sub-acid culinary apple, considered of generally poor eating quality by modern standards. It has largely vanished from commercial cultivation and survives mainly as a heritage curiosity, propagated for its scientific and historical associations rather than its flavour.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia - Flower of Kent
  • The Treeographer - Newton's Tree

Jerseymac

Jerseymac is an early McIntosh-style apple developed at the Rutgers Agricultural Experiment Station in New Jersey, released in 1971 from a cross of NJ-24 with July Red. Its appeal is timing: it ripens in mid to late summer, well ahead of true McIntosh, giving growers a McIntosh character early in the season.

The fruit is medium to large with a yellow-green base mostly washed in dark, bluish red, and greenish-white flesh that is soft, fine-grained and juicy. The flavour is a fragrant, well-balanced sweet-tart, a little sharper than McIntosh, which suits both fresh eating and cooking. Flavour peaks within about a week of picking and then fades, so it is best enjoyed soon after harvest.

2 sources
  • Pomiferous
  • Orange Pippin (McIntosh reference)

Jonagold

Jonagold was bred in 1943 at the New York State Agricultural Experiment Station in Geneva, New York, and released in 1968. It is a cross of Golden Delicious and Jonathan, and its name is a portmanteau of its two parents.

The fruit is large with yellow-green skin streaked or blushed with crimson, and creamy-yellow flesh with a breaking, crisp texture. The flavour combines honeyed sweetness with a tart edge, more complex than either parent. It is a triploid with sterile pollen, so it cannot pollinate other apples and needs a separate pollinator variety nearby. It suits fresh eating, sauces and baking.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Jonagold
  • New York Apple Association: Jonagold

Jonathan

Jonathan is an American heirloom that grew as a seedling of Esopus Spitzenburg on Philip Rick's farm near Woodstock in Ulster County, New York, and was named in 1826. It became one of the most important nineteenth century commercial apples and is now more of a specialty variety.

The fruit is medium-sized with tough, smooth, deep-red skin over a paler background, and white to cream flesh that is crisp and juicy. The flavour is a lively sweet-tart balance with a hint of spice, leaning sharper early in the season and mellowing later. It suits fresh eating and cooking, and is a parent of Jonagold and other cultivars. It is notably susceptible to apple scab and fire blight.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Jonathan (apple)
  • Home for the Harvest: Jonathan apple

King David

King David is an American heritage apple found in 1893 as a chance seedling along a fence row on Ben Frost's farm in Washington County, Arkansas. It was introduced commercially in 1902 by Stark Brothers Nursery of Missouri. Its parentage is uncertain, with Jonathan generally accepted as one parent and Winesap or Arkansas Black as the other.

The fruit is deep, dark red, often almost covering the skin, over firm yellow flesh. The flavour is spicy, slightly sweet and tart with a cidery, Winesap-like character that is far spicier than its Jonathan parent. It suits fresh eating, pies, sauce and cider, and turns a rich yellow when cooked.

2 sources
  • CooksInfo: King David Apples
  • Pomiferous: King David

Lady Williams

Lady Williams is a Western Australian heritage apple that began as a chance seedling at Boronia Farm in Donnybrook, thought to have come from a Granny Smith, with Rokewood as a likely pollen parent. It is named after Maud Williams, who recognised the tree's value and protected it, and it was introduced commercially in 1968.

The fruit matures very late in the season and is firm and crisp with good keeping qualities, which suited it to long storage and export. Lady Williams is best known as the pollen parent of Cripps Pink (sold as Pink Lady), as well as Cripps Red, after John Cripps crossed it with Golden Delicious in his WA breeding program.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Lady Williams (apple)
  • The Conversation: How 'Lady' Williams founded a great Australian apple

Leprechaun

Leprechaun is a naturally compact, dwarf form of Granny Smith. It arose as a branch sport on a Granny Smith tree, so it is a true dwarf in its own right (the tree itself is dwarfing) rather than a standard variety worked onto dwarfing rootstock. It is spur bearing and stays small with minimal pruning, which makes it well suited to pots and small gardens while still producing full sized, classic green Granny Smith apples.

The fruit keeps the Granny Smith character: bright green skin and crisp, tart, acidic flesh that is good both fresh and for cooking, and stores well. Granny Smith itself is an Australian apple, a chance seedling propagated by Maria Ann Smith near Sydney in the 1860s. Leprechaun is self sterile and needs a pollinator flowering at the same time; the dwarf Pink Lady, Pinkabelle, is a commonly recommended partner.

2 sources
  • PlantNet Australia - Leprechaun Dwarf Granny Smith Apple
  • Wikipedia - Granny Smith

Magnus Summer surprise

Magnus Summer Surprise is an Australian-bred apple from Bob Magnus, a Tasmanian heritage apple specialist, and is sold as a protected (PBR) variety. Its seed parent is the Huonville Crab, a tree Bob found growing on a roadside near Huonville, Tasmania; the pollen parent is unknown. It took roughly twenty years to develop.

The fruit is an early one, ripening in mid summer, which puts it among the earliest apples of the season. It is notable for deep pink flesh and a bright rose-red skin, with a sweet, slightly tart flavour carrying berry overtones. It eats well fresh and also makes a pink cider.

2 sources
  • ABC Organic Gardener, Bob's Summer Surprise
  • Hello Hello Plants (Magnus Summer Surprise)

McIntosh

McIntosh is a Canadian heirloom discovered as a chance seedling by John McIntosh on his Dundela farm in Upper Canada, now Ontario, in 1811. Its parentage is uncertain, but it may have descended from the Fameuse, or Snow apple. The family began grafting and selling it in the 1830s, and it became the most popular apple in eastern Canada and the northeastern United States.

The fruit has red and green skin that is tender and easy to peel, with white, juicy flesh and an aromatic, sweet-tart flavour reminiscent of cider. It softens readily during storage and cooking, so it is handled gently. It is a hugely important breeding parent, behind cultivars such as Cortland, Spartan and Macoun, and it lent its name to the Macintosh computer.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: McIntosh (apple)
  • The Canadian Encyclopedia: McIntosh Apple

Michelin

Michelin is a French bittersweet cider apple raised by nurseryman Legrand of Yvetot in Normandy, first fruiting in 1872 and named after the French cider pomologist Henri Michelin. It was brought to Herefordshire by the Woolhope Naturalists Club in the mid-1880s and went on to become one of the most widely planted cider varieties in England.

It is valued more for reliability than for its juice on its own. Michelin is a dependable, heavy cropper that largely avoids biennial bearing, but the cider it yields, with soft tannins and a mild aromatic character, is fairly undistinguished alone and is best used for blending.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Michelin (apple)
  • Pomiferous: Michelin

Monty's Surprise

Monty's Surprise is a New Zealand heritage apple, rediscovered in April 2000 when a large old seedling tree was found in a remote part of the central North Island. The fruit is big, notably disease resistant, crisp and well flavoured, and the cultivar has become a focus of phytochemical and cancer-prevention research by the Heritage Food Crops Research Trust.

In a published feeding study, phytochemicals from Monty's Surprise apple were absorbed in humans and significantly raised plasma antioxidant response. In laboratory (in vitro) testing, the apple's metabolites inhibited the proliferation of lung and breast cancer cells, which has made it one of the most studied heritage apples for potential health benefits.

2 sources
  • Heritage Food Crops Research Trust: Monty's Surprise Apple
  • Proceedings of the Nutrition Society (Cambridge): Phytochemicals from Monty's Surprise apple are absorbed in humans, increase plasma antioxidant response, and inhibit lung and breast cancer proliferation in vitro

Mutsu

Mutsu, also sold as Crispin, was introduced from Aomori Prefecture, Japan, in 1949. It is a cross of Golden Delicious and the Japanese cultivar Indo, and is a triploid, which makes it self-sterile and a poor pollinator for other trees.

It is a medium to large green to greenish-yellow apple with creamy, crisp, very juicy flesh and a spicy-sweet, slightly sharp flavour that has more depth than its Golden Delicious parent. It holds its shape well when cooked, so it works for both fresh eating and baking, and it keeps for several months in cool storage. It is prone to blister spot.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Mutsu (apple)
  • NC State Extension Gardener Plant Toolbox: Malus domestica 'Mutsu'

Orleans Reinette

Orleans Reinette is a French dessert apple first recorded around 1776 and named for the city of Orleans. Long valued across Europe as a high-quality variety, it received a Royal Horticultural Society Award of Merit in 1914 and again in 1921. DNA work points to Reinette de Hollande and Court Pendu Plat as parents.

The skin is golden yellow overlaid with bronze russet and a red-orange blush, giving a rough, sandy texture. The firm, fine-textured, creamy flesh has a rich flavour often described as orange and nuts, making it a connoisseur's eating apple at the end of the season.

2 sources
  • National Fruit Collection: Orleans Reinette
  • Specialty Produce: Orleans Reinette Apples

Peasgood s Nonsuch

Peasgood's Nonsuch was raised by Mrs Peasgood at Stamford, Lincolnshire, England, from seed sown about 1858. A basket of the fruit won first prize at the Burghley Park agricultural show, and the variety received a First Class Certificate from the Royal Horticultural Society in 1872, which helped launch it as a garden favourite.

It is a large, handsome culinary apple, pale yellow-green flushing to orange-yellow with red striping and some russet. The flesh is a little coarse and moderately juicy, cooking down well to a sweet, well-flavoured puree, though good specimens are pleasant enough to eat fresh.

3 sources
  • National Fruit Collection: Peasgood's Nonsuch
  • Wikipedia: Peasgood's Nonsuch
  • Orange Pippin: Peasgood's Nonsuch

Pink Lady

Pink Lady is the trademark name for fruit of the Cripps Pink cultivar, which was bred in Western Australia. John Cripps, working at the state Department of Agriculture, crossed the Australian apple Lady Williams with the American Golden Delicious in 1973, aiming to combine the long storage life of one parent with the sweetness of the other. It became one of the first apples sold under a trademarked brand, with only fruit that meets a set quality standard sold as Pink Lady.

The fruit has crisp, dense flesh and a high balance of sugar and acid that gives it a sweet-tart, almost effervescent flavour, with a pink blush over greenish-yellow skin. It is one of the last apples picked each season and needs a long, warm growing period, so it performs better in warmer districts than on cold sites. The signature pink colour develops best where warm autumn days are followed by cool nights.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Cripps Pink
  • Orange Pippin: Pink Lady apple variety

Pinkabelle

Pinkabelle is a compact, naturally dwarf form of Cripps Pink, the Western Australian apple marketed worldwide as Pink Lady. The point of difference is the tree, not the fruit: it is a true dwarf whose own growth is dwarfing rather than a standard variety grafted onto dwarfing rootstock, so it stays small (a couple of metres) while still bearing full sized apples. It is spur bearing and needs only minimal pruning, which makes it a popular pick for pots, courtyards and small gardens.

Eating quality follows its Pink Lady parentage: crisp, sweet, pinkish red fruit that colours up late in the season. Cripps Pink itself was bred by John Cripps in Western Australia from a cross of Lady Williams and Golden Delicious, and Pinkabelle is reported to have arisen from a Western Australian orchard. Being self sterile it fruits best with another apple flowering at the same time, and the dwarf Granny Smith Leprechaun is often paired with it.

2 sources
  • PlantNet Australia - Pinkabelle Apple
  • Wikipedia - Cripps Pink (Pink Lady)

Pixie Crunch

Pixie Crunch, trialled as Co-op 33, is a modern disease-resistant apple from the cooperative breeding program of the Indiana, Illinois and New Jersey research stations, with the cross made in 1971 and selected at West Lafayette, Indiana. Golden Delicious and Rome both appear in its pedigree. It is genetically resistant to apple scab.

It is a small, deep-red dessert apple known above all for its exceptionally crisp, breaking texture, comparable to Honeycrisp. The flavour is sweet with a mild, slightly spicy character. It ripens around mid-September, a week or two after Gala, and hangs on the tree in good condition for several weeks.

2 sources
  • Purdue University, Facts for Fancy Fruit: Pixy Crunch Apple
  • Pomiferous: Co-op 33 (Pixie Crunch)

Red Delicious

Red Delicious began as a chance seedling found by Iowa farmer Jesse Hiatt, who first fruited it in the 1870s and named it Hawkeye after his home state. Stark Nurseries bought the rights, renamed it Stark Delicious, and from 1914 it became known as Red Delicious.

The original fruit was prized for its sweetness, but decades of selecting strains for deep red colour, storage life, and cosmetic appeal rather than taste left the modern apple widely criticised for blander flavour. It is a sweet, mild eating apple with thick skin and crisp flesh.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Red Delicious
  • Notes on Iowa: Jesse Hiatt and Red Delicious Apples
  • Stark Bro's: A Delicious Discovery

Red Fuji

Red Fuji is a more deeply coloured bud sport of Fuji, the Japanese apple developed at the Tohoku Research Station from a cross of Red Delicious and Ralls Janet. Where standard Fuji often colours as a light blush or faint stripe, Red Fuji selections develop a bolder, richer red skin, while keeping the flavour and texture of the parent.

As with Fuji, the flesh is sweet, very crisp and dense, and the fruit is noted as an exceptionally good keeper that stores well. It is grown as a fresh-eating dessert apple.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Fuji (apple)
  • Dave Wilson Nursery: Red Fuji

Redlove

Redlove is not a single apple but a series of red-fleshed cultivars bred by Swiss breeder Markus Kobelt of the Lubera nursery, with selections such as Calypso, Circe, Era and Odysso. They are the result of crossing sour red-fleshed apples with sweeter, scab-resistant varieties, a process that took many years of natural cross-pollination to combine red flesh with good eating quality and disease resistance.

The standout trait is flesh that is red right through, not just red-skinned, typically with a white line through the middle. The red colour is a natural (non-GM) trait traced back to red-fleshed wild apples. Because Redlove is a trademarked brand covering several distinct selections, flavour and season vary by cultivar.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia, Redlove apples
  • Lubera / Fruit Grower (Markus Kobelt Redlove press feature)

Reine des Hâtives

Reine des Hâtives is a French cider apple from Normandy, grown by the nurseryman Dieppois of Yvetot in the Seine-Maritime and first fruiting in 1872. It is a sweet type rather than a sharp one, valued for the soft, sweet, amber juice it yields rather than for the table.

The fruit is medium to small, round-conic and often lopsided with distinct ribs, pale yellow flushed with an orange blush. The flesh is white, tender and medium-grained, moderately juicy, very sweet and aromatic with little acidity. It ripens early and the apples tend to drop as soon as they are ready, which suits its main job as a sweet cider variety, often used for blending.

2 sources
  • Pomiferous: Reine des Hatives
  • Heritage & Rare Fruit Network: Cider Apples

Rome Beauty

Rome Beauty traces to a chance seedling found in 1817 by Joel Gillet in a nursery shipment, planted on the banks of the Ohio River in Rome Township, Ohio. Originally called Gillett's Seedling, it was renamed Rome Beauty in 1832 in honour of the township and went on to become a staple of American commerce.

The fruit is rounded, glossy and deep red with a thick skin and firm flesh. It is mainly a baking apple, holding its shape well when cooked while its flavour develops with heat. Fresh, its taste is milder and less sweet or tart than many dessert varieties, so it is favoured more for the kitchen than the fruit bowl.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Rome apple
  • Specialty Produce: Rome Apples

Royal Gala

Royal Gala is a more highly coloured sport of Gala, the New Zealand apple bred from Kidd's Orange Red crossed with Golden Delicious. The redder mutation was discovered by Bill ten Hove at Matamata, New Zealand, who noticed improved red striping on a single branch of a Gala tree. Apart from the deeper, more striped red skin, it keeps the parent Gala's eating character.

Like Gala, it is a sweet, crisp dessert apple eaten fresh. The name commemorates Queen Elizabeth II, who reportedly enjoyed Gala apples on a visit to New Zealand, and the redder sport has largely displaced the original paler Gala in commercial markets.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Gala (apple)
  • Apples and People: A Royal Gala

Somerset Redstreak

Somerset Redstreak is a traditional English cider apple from Somerset, dating from before 1900. It is a late, mild bittersweet variety with deep red stripes over green skin, yielding a sweet, astringent juice of vintage quality.

It remains one of the main commercial cider cultivars. The vigorous trees crop well but often biennially, and the fruit needs to be pressed promptly after harvest rather than stored.

2 sources
  • RHS: Malus domestica Somerset Redstreak (Cider)
  • Pomiferous: Somerset Redstreak

Spartan

Spartan is a Canadian apple developed by R.C. Palmer at the federal research station in Summerland, British Columbia, and introduced in 1936. It is a McIntosh-type apple, long thought to be a McIntosh cross with Yellow Newtown Pippin, though later genetic analysis ruled out Newtown Pippin and left the second parent a mystery.

The fruit is deep red, sometimes almost purple-red, over a snowy-white flesh. It is crisp and juicy off the tree with a sweet, slightly wine-like (vinous) flavour, softening a little after picking. It is regarded as a good all-purpose apple for fresh eating and cooking.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Spartan (apple)
  • Dictionary of Canadianisms (DCHP-3), University of British Columbia: Spartan

Stoke Red

Stoke Red is a bittersharp English cider apple from Somerset, named after the village of Rodney Stoke where it rose to prominence in the 1920s after dairy farmer Herbert Sealey entered it in the Long Ashton Research Station competition. The small fruit is red striped over yellow and ripens very late, usually November.

It is regarded as a vintage quality variety, high in both tannin and acid, often compared to Kingston Black. That tannin and acid balance lets it make a good single varietal cider on its own, and it is highly resistant to scab, though the tree has an unruly growth habit.

2 sources
  • Pomiferous: Stoke's Red
  • Cidercraft Magazine: Stoke Red

Sundowner

Sundowner is the trademark name for fruit of the Cripps Red cultivar, bred by John Cripps at the Stoneville Research Station in Western Australia in 1973 from a cross of Golden Delicious and Lady Williams. It shares its parentage with Cripps Pink, sold as Pink Lady, and is also marketed as Joya in Europe.

It is a red-blushed apple with distinctive white lenticels, and crisp, dense, very juicy flesh that balances good acidity with sweetness, giving a tangy yet sweet flavour. It ripens very late in the season and is a warm-climate, low-chill apple, which is why it grows well in places like Western Australia. It is mainly eaten fresh.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Cripps Red
  • Good Fruit Guide: Cripps Red (Sundowner; Joya)

Sweet Alford

Sweet Alford is a traditional cider apple from Devon, England, dating to the 18th century. In the English four-category system for cider apples it sits at the sweet to mild bittersweet end, meaning it brings sweetness and only light tannin rather than the sharp acidity of sharps and bittersharps. It is regarded as a vintage variety, the term cidermakers use for apples that can make a good single-variety cider, though it is often blended with more tannic bittersweets to add balance.

The fruit is medium and conical with yellow, waxy skin flushed with orange, and the flesh is sweet and white with little astringency. As a pure sweet, its low acid and low tannin make it useful for softening over-sharp blends. It is a late-ripening variety.

3 sources
  • Pomiferous: Sweet Alford
  • Heritage & Rare Fruit Network: Cider Apples
  • Walcot Organic Nursery: Sweet Alford

Sweet Coppin

Sweet Coppin is an old cider apple from Devon, England, that was once common in the cider orchards around Exeter. It is classed as a sweet, sometimes very mildly bittersweet, cider variety in the English system, producing a full, sweet juice with low acidity and low tannin. Cidermakers value it for adding sweetness and softening over-sharp blends, and it is rated a vintage variety capable of a pleasant cider on its own.

The fruit is a pale yellow apple with a pink blush and fine russeting, ripening mid to late season. It is held in the UK National Fruit Collection, where its accession number is 1989-112.

2 sources
  • Orange Pippin: Sweet Coppin apple
  • Heritage & Rare Fruit Network: Cider Apples

Tremlett s Bitter

Tremlett's Bitter is a classic English bittersweet cider apple from Devon, dating to around 1880. The deep red, conical fruit is famous for its very robust, almost brutal tannins, earning it a reputation as a heavyweight bittersweet.

Because the bitterness is so pronounced, it is usually used in blends with sweeter or sharper varieties, where its structure and tannin add body. It tends to bear biennially, with heavy crops every second year.

2 sources
  • Pomiferous: Tremlett's Bitter
  • Cider Review: Spotlight on Tremlett's Bitter

Tropic Sweet

Tropic Sweet (selection Fla. 90-3) is a low-chill apple from the University of Florida breeding program at Gainesville, selected in 1990 and patented in 1995. It traces back to a cross involving Anna, and it is bred specifically for warm subtropical climates where high-chill apples will not set fruit.

The fruit is sweeter, firmer, and less red than Anna, with low acidity giving a very sweet taste and a crisp texture often compared to Gala. It carries a partial red blush over a yellow-green skin and ripens early in the season. The trees are not self-fruitful, so they need a pollinator such as Anna or Dorsett Golden.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS Extension: Low-Chill Apple Cultivars for North Florida
  • USPTO Plant Patent PP9708: Apple tree Fla. 90-3 (TropicSweet)

Tropical Beauty

Tropical Beauty is a warm-climate dessert apple raised around 1930 by Meredith B. Strapp in Maidstone, South Africa, from a seedling of unknown parentage. It was named and introduced in Australia in 1958 by Longbecker Nurseries, and it is valued as a low-chill variety that fruits in subtropical conditions where most apples will not.

The fruit is medium sized and round-conic with ribbed, angular sides, showing a dark red wash over a greenish-yellow base. The flesh is yellowish white, firm and rather coarse, with a dry, sweet flavour. It is only partly self-fertile and crops best with a compatible pollinator nearby.

2 sources
  • Pomiferous: Tropical Beauty
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Tropical Beauty Apple

Twenty Ounce

Twenty Ounce is an American heritage apple believed to come from Cayuga County, New York, discovered as a chance seedling in the early 19th century and first exhibited in 1843. Its parentage is unknown. The name reflects its remarkable size, with fruit that can grow very large and heavy.

It is a large apple with a yellow-green base overlaid by red blush, striations and striping, and firm, dense, pale flesh. The flavour is mildly tart, and it was prized for generations as a premier cooking apple, especially for pies and sauce. Today it is rare, kept going mainly by heirloom growers.

2 sources
  • Specialty Produce: Twenty Ounce Apples
  • Pomiferous: Twenty Ounce

Tydeman s Early Worcester

Tydeman's Early Worcester is an early-season dessert apple bred at the East Malling Research Station in Kent. It came from a cross of Worcester Pearmain and McIntosh made in 1929 by the apple breeder H.M. Tydeman, and was introduced in 1945. The variety takes after Worcester Pearmain but gains extra sweetness and juiciness from its McIntosh parent.

The fruit ripens early, with a pale yellow skin heavily flushed and striped in crimson red. The flavour is rich, sweet with a little acidity. It is a tip bearer, which makes it less suited to training as a cordon or espalier.

2 sources
  • Walcot Organic Nursery - Tydeman's Early Worcester
  • Specialty Produce - Tydeman's Early

Vista Bella

Vista Bella is one of the earliest apples of the season, raised at Rutgers University in New Jersey in the mid 1950s and introduced in 1956. It is widely regarded as among the first red apples to ripen, maturing in mid summer well before most other varieties.

The fruit is medium-sized with light yellow-green skin that flushes deep red where the sun reaches it, often deepening to a solid crimson when fully ripe. As an early apple it is best eaten soon after picking rather than stored. Its early timing makes it useful for gardeners wanting fresh fruit at the very start of the apple season.

2 sources
  • Orange Pippin
  • Pomiferous

Wandin Pride

Wandin Pride is an Australian apple from Victoria, also known as Wandin Glory, recorded as a Scoresby cross of Emperor Alexander and Jonathan. Its defining feature is a naturally weeping, pendulous growth habit, unusual among apples, which gives it ornamental value as well as fruit. Growers often train the drooping laterals along a trellis or pergola.

It bears well and is prized for flavour, producing small to medium red-blushed fruit that many describe as close to a Jonathan in taste, good both for fresh eating and in desserts. It is self-sterile, so it needs another apple flowering nearby to set a crop.

2 sources
  • Pomiferous
  • Peter Lachnewinsky, Heritage Apple Varieties at Gundungurra

Apricot

18 varieties (back to top)

Bentley

Bentley is a local apricot selection from the Northern Rivers of New South Wales, found just outside Kyogle. It is closely compared to Glengarry, another regional selection, and shares its hardy, early-fruiting habit. The variety suits warm-temperate and subtropical gardens, needing only a modest winter chill.

A hardy tree that comes into bearing early, Bentley produces good crops of good quality, medium-sized apricots. It is grouped with low to moderate chill apricots, which is why it is offered chiefly in the warmer growing districts of eastern Australia rather than the cold inland.

2 sources
  • Rare Fruit Club WA, Apricot Varieties
  • Daleys Fruit Tree Nursery, Apricot Bentley

Bulida

Bulida (Spanish Bulida or Bulida) is a traditional Spanish apricot associated with the south-east of Spain, the regions of Murcia, Alicante and Valencia, and dating to around 1940. It is prized in Spain as a hardy, heavy-cropping commercial variety that is frost and drought resistant and adapts to a wide range of soils.

The fruit is large with firm, yellow-orange skin (typically without a red blush) and yellow-orange flesh that is very sweet, juicy and melting. It is a freestone, with the flesh lifting cleanly from the stone. These qualities, plus its firmness and good keeping, make Bulida a favourite for fresh eating, canning, drying and the preserving and confectionery industries.

3 sources
  • Rare Fruit Club WA: Apricot Varieties
  • Jardineria On: Apricot Varieties (Bulida)
  • CSIC: Growth and phenological stages of Bulida apricot trees in south-east Spain

Castlebrite

Castlebrite is an early season Californian apricot that originated in Fresno through the work of J.H. Weinberger of the USDA Agricultural Research Service. It came from open pollinated seed of the selection B60-12, itself a cross of Perfection and Castleton, and was developed and recommended as an early shipping variety for San Joaquin Valley conditions.

The fruit is medium sized, firm, and bright orange with a slight red blush. Flavour is semi sweet but can be quite tart unless the fruit is fully ripe, so it is best left to sweeten on the tree. Castlebrite is self fruitful, meaning it does not need a separate pollinator, and it ripens roughly four weeks ahead of Blenheim, making it one of the earliest apricots of the season.

2 sources
  • UC Davis Foundation Plant Services: Castlebrite apricot
  • Dave Wilson Nursery: Castlebrite apricot

Cot N Candy interspecific

Cot N Candy is an interspecific apricot, marketed as a white aprium, bred by Gary, Grant, and Leith Zaiger of Zaiger Genetics and protected under US Plant Patent 17827. Rather than being a pure apricot, its parentage weaves together apricot, plum, and plumcot, described in the patent as apricot crossed with ((plum by plumcot) by plumcot) by apricot. That mixed lineage is the whole point of the variety.

Unusually for an apricot, the flesh is white rather than orange, set behind velvety orange yellow skin with a reddish pink blush. The patent describes a regular, productive tree bearing medium sized, white fleshed fruit of excellent eating quality, and growers note a sweet, juicy flavour with melon and plum notes alongside the apricot. It is self fruitful and ripens in the early to middle part of summer.

2 sources
  • US Plant Patent 17827: Interspecific tree named Cot-N-Candy
  • Dave Wilson Nursery: Cot-N-Candy aprium

Divinity

Divinity is a South Australian apricot that has been grown in Australia since the 1950s. It is valued as an early-season variety, ripening from late November into early December, well ahead of the classic Moorpark. The fruit is firm and slightly oval (oblong) with a light to deep orange flesh and an attractive red blush on the skin.

The flavour is sweet and juicy with a slightly acid skin, making it good for eating fresh as well as for jam, stewing, drying and preserving. A useful trait for the home grower is that Divinity is self-fertile, so it sets fruit without needing a second apricot nearby for pollination.

4 sources
  • Rare Fruit Club WA: Apricot Varieties
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Divinity Apricot
  • Fleming's Nurseries: Divinity Apricot
  • Lawn.com.au: Apricot Tree Varieties in Australia

Fireball

Fireball is one of the earliest apricots grown in Australia, with fruit ready from late November into December. It is a low-chill variety, which lets it crop in warm temperate and Mediterranean climates where many traditional apricots will not, and it is widely sold in a compact dwarf form for small gardens.

The fruit has vibrant orange to orange-red skin and sweet, juicy flesh that lifts cleanly from the stone, making it a freestone apricot. Fireball is only partially self-fertile, so while it can set some fruit alone, a second apricot nearby or hand pollination improves the crop.

3 sources
  • Rare Fruit Club WA: Apricot Varieties
  • Lawn.com.au: Apricot Tree Varieties in Australia
  • Daleys Fruit: Apricot Fireball

Glengarry

Glengarry stands out as a low-chill apricot, one of the few that will crop in subtropical and warm-temperate parts of Australia where most apricots fail to get enough winter cold. It is among the earliest to ripen, fruiting through spring into early summer, and it is self-fertile, so a single tree will set a crop.

The tree is large, vigorous and a heavy bearer. Its fruit is small to medium and pale golden with a slight blush, with light apricot-coloured flesh, good flavour and a freestone. That makes Glengarry well suited to fresh eating as well as jams and preserves, especially for growers in milder climates.

3 sources
  • Rare Fruit Club WA: Apricot Varieties
  • Daleys Fruit: Apricot Glengarry
  • Speciality Trees: Glengarry Apricot (Low-chill)

Goldrich

Goldrich is an apricot introduced by Washington State University, bred in the cooler apricot country of the Pacific Northwest and long used as a benchmark for large, firm, good flavoured fruit. Virginia Cooperative Extension trials recorded it as a mid season bloomer that ripens early, carrying large fruit of around two and a half ounces.

The fruit is distinctive for its flattened, oblong shape and smooth suture, with an orange red blush over an orange yellow background. The flesh is orange, moderately firm, juicy, aromatic, and mildly subacid, and the fruit was rated attractive and good for fresh use. Goldrich is not self fertile: it requires a pollinator, and notably it cannot be pollinated by Perfection, so a compatible second variety is needed for a crop.

2 sources
  • Virginia Cooperative Extension (Virginia Tech): 1988-1995 Apricot Variety Evaluations in Virginia, Publication 422-761
  • Van Well Nursery: Apricot varieties (Goldrich)

Hunter

Hunter is an Australian apricot, reported to have originated in South Australia around 1966. Its main claim to distinction is timing: it is one of the latest apricots to ripen, cropping from mid to late January and so extending the season after most other varieties have finished.

The fruit is large, firm, and plump, with deep orange flesh that is sweet, juicy, and full flavoured. It is well regarded both for fresh eating and especially for home drying, and it is self pollinating, so it does not need another variety nearby to set a crop.

2 sources
  • Rare Fruit Club WA: Apricot Varieties (Hunter)
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Hunter apricot

Katy

Katy is a low chill apricot bred by Zaiger Genetics and prized as a favourite for warm winter climates where many apricots fail to set. It needs only around 200 to 300 chill hours, and is self fruitful, so a single tree will crop without a separate pollinator. It ripens early, roughly three to four weeks ahead of Blenheim (Royal).

The fruit is large, firm, and freestone, so the flesh parts cleanly from the stone. Tree ripened Katy is subacid rather than tart, with a sweet, juicy, low acid flavour, and it is an all purpose apricot used fresh as well as for cooking, canning, drying, and preserves.

2 sources
  • Dave Wilson Nursery: Katy apricot
  • Ask The Green Genie: Low Chill Apricots (Katy)

Moorpark

Moorpark is an old English apricot, taking its name from the Moor Park estate in Hertfordshire and first sold under that name in the late 1700s. It has long been regarded as a benchmark for apricot flavour.

The fruit is large with deep orange skin, a heady aroma and rich, sweet flavour, and the flesh is freestone when fully ripe. It is a late-ripening variety. Moorpark is self-fertile, so a single tree will set fruit.

3 sources
  • Trees of Antiquity: Moorpark Apricot
  • Specialty Produce: Moorpark Apricots
  • Grow Great Fruit: Apricot tree varieties

Newcastle

Newcastle is a low-chill apricot that, despite its standing as an Australian heritage favourite, began as a California seedling. It originated in the orchard of O. M. Silva and Son at Newcastle, Placer County, and was first noticed in 1881. Its tolerance of mild winters has made it a long-running choice for warm-temperate and some subtropical gardens where higher-chill apricots struggle.

It is an early variety, cropping well before the old standard Oullins. The fruit is rounded and yellow-orange, a regular freestone with soft, juicy orange flesh, and old catalogue notes describe a rich golden yellow with a red cheek. A heavy bearer, it usually needs thinning, and the fruit suits fresh eating, drying, preserves and canning.

3 sources
  • Rare Fruit Club WA, Apricot Varieties
  • Heritage and Rare Fruit Network, Apricots
  • Speciality Trees, Prunus armeniaca 'Newcastle'

Patterson

Patterson is a California apricot from the breeding program of Fred Anderson at Le Grand, often called the father of the modern nectarine. It began as an F2 open pollinated seedling of Perfection, selected for testing in 1959 and released in 1968 to Burchell Nursery. Anderson and Irvin Burchell set out to develop a canning apricot that was a regular, reliable bearer and would resist pit burning, and it was named for Patterson, California, a town long associated with the crop.

Bred first for canning, Patterson is also well suited to drying, concentrate, and the fresh market, which made it a workhorse commercial variety. It produces medium to large, firm, uniform orange fruit that ships well, so it is one of the apricots most often found in supermarkets. Harvest falls in the mid to late part of the apricot season.

3 sources
  • Good Fruit Grower: Patterson, an all-round apricot
  • Dave Wilson Nursery: Patterson apricot
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Patterson apricot

Rival

Rival is an apricot often described as similar to Goldrich and is best known for producing a large tree carrying heavy crops of large, superior fruit. The fruit is large and oval, with light orange skin flushed or speckled red and deep orange flesh that is aromatic and slightly tart. It is typically a midseason to late variety.

A key point for growers is that Rival is not reliably self-fertile and needs cross-pollination from an early-blooming apricot to set a good crop. Sources differ on its exact origin (some describe it as Canadian), so the country of breeding is treated as uncertain here, but it is documented as a parent of later Washington-bred apricots such as Tomcot.

4 sources
  • Rare Fruit Club WA: Apricot Varieties
  • Dave Wilson Nursery: Rival Apricot
  • Harmony Orchards: Apricots (Rival)
  • Cultivar Guide: Apricot (Tomcot parentage)

Storeys

Storeys (also written Storey, and known as Early Moorpark or Moorpark Early) is an Australian apricot best understood as a faster-growing, earlier-fruiting version of the much-loved Moorpark. It ripens roughly three weeks before Moorpark, from about mid-December into January, which extends the apricot season at the early end.

The fruit is large with deep golden colouring and some red blush, and firm, deep-yellow flesh with good flavour. It is self-fertile, so it does not need a pollination partner, and it is a versatile all-rounder used for eating fresh, jam, bottling, canning, preserving and drying.

3 sources
  • Rare Fruit Club WA: Apricot Varieties
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Storeys Apricot (dwarf)
  • Lawn.com.au: Apricot Tree Varieties in Australia

Tardif de Bordaneil

Tardif de Bordaneil is a French apricot from a chance seedling found in the Eastern Pyrenees Orientales around 1970. Tardif means late, and that is its defining trait: it is among the latest known apricots, both in flowering and in ripening. The very late bloom helps it escape spring frosts, while harvest comes well into late summer, extending the apricot season at its tail end.

The fruit is large to very large and slightly oblong, with light orange skin overlaid by an intense red flush. The flesh is firm, juicy, sweet, and fragrant, making it a good quality eating apricot. It is self fertile, vigorous, and productive, so a single tree will crop on its own.

2 sources
  • Rare Fruit Club WA: Apricot Varieties (Tardif de Bordaneil)
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Tardif de Bordaneil apricot

Tilton

Tilton is a classic American apricot that began as a chance seedling discovered by J.E. Tilton in California in 1885, at a time when the state's dried-apricot trade was expanding. It quickly became a commercial standard, partly because the tree proved hardy and relatively tolerant of stormy weather and late frosts. It is also cold hardy and self-fertile.

The fruit is medium to large and heart-shaped, with glowing golden-yellow skin, a sunny blush and a distinct suture. The flesh is firm, fine-textured and aromatic, with a good balance of sweetness and acidity, and it is freestone at maturity. Tilton holds its colour and shape when preserved, which is why it is rated one of the best varieties for canning and drying as well as for fresh eating.

3 sources
  • Rare Fruit Club WA: Apricot Varieties
  • Trees of Antiquity: Tilton Apricot
  • Minneopa Orchards: The Tilton Apricot Tree

Trevatt

Trevatt is an Australian heirloom apricot dating to the early 1900s and long grown as a commercial variety. It is one of the classic old Australian apricots.

The fruit is large and rounded with light yellow skin, deeper orange flesh and a highly aromatic, flavourful taste. It ripens early to mid season, before Moorpark, and its firm flesh makes it popular for drying and preserving as well as fresh eating. Trevatt is self-fertile, though a second tree can improve cropping.

2 sources
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Trevatt Apricot
  • The Diggers Club: Apricot Trevatt

Avocado

20 varieties (back to top)

Bacon

Bacon is a cold-hardy avocado named after James Bacon of Buena Park, California, where the original seed was planted around 1928 and the variety was later registered with the California Avocado Society in 1948.

It is a Type B flowering variety, often planted as a pollinator for Type A trees such as Hass. The flesh is smooth and creamy but milder and less oily than Hass, and it is one of the more frost-tolerant avocados, cropping through the cooler months.

3 sources
  • Greg Alder's Yard Posts: The Bacon avocado tree
  • Specialty Produce: Bacon Avocados
  • Grow Organic: Avocado Type A and B flowers

Choquette

Choquette is a Florida avocado, grown from a seed planted in 1929 on the Miami property of Remi D. Choquette Sr. and likely a cross between Guatemalan and West Indian types. It carries A type flowers. The fruit is very large, oval, with glossy, smooth green skin that stays green when ripe rather than darkening like Hass.

The skin peels easily to reveal golden flesh that is silky and creamy, firmer than many varieties so it holds its shape. In Florida the fruit matures from about October to January, and Choquette remains one of the more important commercially grown Florida avocados.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Choquette (avocado)
  • Specialty Produce: Choquette Avocados

Edranol

Edranol is a Guatemalan-race hybrid that originated in Vista, California, from a seedling raised by E.R. Mullen and introduced in 1932. It carries a B-type flower, so it sets best alongside an A-type pollinator such as Hass. The pear-shaped fruit is medium to large with thin but rough, leathery, pebbly green skin that stays green (sometimes darkening) as it ripens.

The flesh is rich, fibreless and of high quality with a small seed, traits typical of good Guatemalan-race selections. Edranol matures in the spring window in California, making it a useful companion to fill out a longer harvest spread rather than a peak-summer variety.

3 sources
  • UC Riverside Avocado Variety Collection, variety list
  • Specialty Produce, Edranol Avocados
  • Greg Alder's Yard Posts, Avocado varieties for year-round harvest

Fuerte

Fuerte is a Mexican-Guatemalan hybrid that came from a tree found at Atlixco in Puebla, Mexico, and was introduced to California from cuttings sent in 1911. It earned the name Fuerte, Spanish for strong, after young trees survived a severe freeze in 1913.

It is a Type B flowering variety, making it a classic pollination partner for Type A trees such as Hass. The fruit is pear-shaped with relatively smooth green skin that peels easily, and the flesh has a rich, nutty, buttery flavour. It crops mainly through the cooler months.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Fuerte avocado
  • Grow Organic: Avocado Type A and B flowers
  • UC ANR: The AB's of Avocado

Gwen

Gwen was bred by Bob Bergh at the University of California, Riverside, and released in 1983, named after his wife. It is a descendant of Hass (a Guatemalan type line) selected as a green skinned, Hass like fruit, and it carries A type flowers. The skin is pebbled and stays green when ripe rather than blackening like Hass.

The fruit is small to medium and Hass like in flavour, on a notably compact, dwarf tree that suits small gardens and containers. Despite its quality Gwen was a commercial flop, because the market had settled on the black skinned Hass, but it remains valued by home growers and as breeding stock.

2 sources
  • University of California: UC Riverside releases a new avocado tree
  • Specialty Produce: Gwen Avocados

Hass

Hass is the world's most widely grown avocado, traced to a single seedling raised by postman Rudolph Hass at La Habra Heights, California in 1926 and patented in 1935. Every commercial Hass tree descends from grafts of that one mother tree.

It is a Type A flowering variety. The fruit has pebbly skin that turns from dark green to purplish-black as it ripens, with rich, nutty, high-oil flesh that yields to gentle pressure when ready.

4 sources
  • Wikipedia: Hass avocado
  • UC Riverside: A Short History of the Hass Avocado
  • Grow Organic: Avocado Type A and B flowers
  • UC ANR: The AB's of Avocado

Lamb Hass

Lamb Hass is a University of California selection (originally BL122) bred by Gray Martin and B.O. Bergh and patented in 1996. It arose as an open-pollinated seedling of Gwen, itself a descendant of Hass, and was named for the Bob Lamb ranch where it was grown. Like Hass, it carries an A-type flower and its green skin turns black as the fruit ripens, giving an easy ripeness cue.

The fruit runs larger than standard Hass (often sold as jumbo Hass) and matures later in the season, extending supply past the main Hass window. The upright, vigorous tree shows good heat tolerance, holding fruit through hot spells, which makes it a workhorse for warmer growing districts.

3 sources
  • US Plant Patent PP9753, Avocado tree called Lamb/Hass (Martin & Bergh, UC)
  • UC Riverside Avocado Variety Collection, variety list
  • Greg Alder's Yard Posts, The Lamb/Hass avocado tree profile

Linda

Linda is a Guatemalan-race avocado introduced to the continental United States around 1914 by E.E. Knight, from Guatemalan stock. It carries a B-type flower, so it benefits from an A-type partner for reliable fruit set. The fruit is notably large, round to oblong, with thick, rough-textured skin that ripens to a deep purple, distinguishing it from the green and black skins of most commercial varieties.

The flesh is light yellow (greener near the rind), buttery and mild-flavoured, over a small to medium seed. Linda is sometimes called the dieter's avocado for its lower oil content than many varieties while keeping good texture and flavour. It ripens in the late winter through spring window.

2 sources
  • Specialty Produce, Linda Avocados
  • Wikipedia, Avocado (list of cultivars)

Mexicola

Mexicola is a pure Mexican race avocado prized as one of the most cold hardy cultivars, with mature trees reported to recover quickly from hard frosts. Like other Mexican race avocados its leaves are anise scented when crushed. The fruit is small with a paper thin, shiny purplish black skin and a very large seed, traits typical of the Mexican race.

The flesh is regarded as high quality with rich flavour. Because the skin is so thin it is sometimes eaten with the fruit. Mexicola ripens in the autumn and, being a Mexican race selection, is suited to cooler avocado climates than the Guatemalan types.

3 sources
  • International Tropical Fruits Network: Avocado Common Varieties
  • Specialty Produce: Mexicola Grande Avocados
  • Encyclopaedia Britannica: Mexican avocado

Nabal

Nabal is a Guatemalan race avocado brought to the United States from Antigua, Guatemala, by plant explorer Wilson Popenoe in 1916. It is distinctive for being almost perfectly round, with thick, hard green skin that stays green even when ripe, sometimes developing speckling, over a large seed cavity.

The fruit is large, with dense, smooth, fibreless flesh and a rich, nutty flavour. Nabal is known for high quality fruit, though it tends to bear in alternate years.

2 sources
  • Specialty Produce: Nabal Avocados
  • International Tropical Fruits Network: Avocado Common Varieties

Pinkerton

Pinkerton is a Guatemalan-race hybrid that began as a chance seedling on the Pinkerton ranch in Saticoy, California, in the 1960s to early 1970s, from a Hass by Rincon cross. It carries an A-type flower, so it needs a B-type variety nearby for good fruit set. The fruit is distinctive for its long-necked, pear shape, with thin, leathery green skin that peels easily.

Its standout feature is a notably small seed paired with thick, smooth, creamy pale-green flesh of high oil content and good flavour, giving a high edible yield. Pinkerton ripens through winter into spring, filling an earlier window than Hass.

3 sources
  • UC Riverside Avocado Variety Collection, variety list
  • Wikipedia, Avocado (list of cultivars)
  • Laurie Meadows, Pinkerton Avocado

Reed

Reed is a Guatemalan-type avocado that began as a seedling germinated by James Reed at Carlsbad, California in 1948. It is a Type A flowering variety known for setting fruit reliably even when grown on its own.

The fruit is unusually large, round and heavy, and the thick, pebbled skin stays green rather than darkening as it ripens. The pale flesh is among the creamiest and smoothest of any avocado, and it is a summer-bearing variety that holds well on the tree.

3 sources
  • Greg Alder's Yard Posts: The Reed avocado tree
  • Specialty Produce: Reed Avocados
  • Grow Organic: Avocado Type A and B flowers

Rincon

Rincon is a hybrid avocado that originated in Carlsbad, California, in the 1940s. It carries an A-type flower, so it sets best with a B-type partner nearby. The tree is small and compact, which makes it handy where space is limited, and it bears small to medium, pear-shaped fruit with smooth green skin that resembles Fuerte.

The flesh is rich and buttery with good eating quality. Rincon crops early, through the winter into spring window, ahead of the main-season varieties. It is also notable as a parent of Pinkerton (Hass by Rincon), passing on its compact habit and early bearing.

3 sources
  • UC Riverside Avocado Variety Collection, variety list
  • Specialty Produce, Rincon Avocados
  • Wikipedia, Avocado (list of cultivars)

Russell

Russell is a pure West Indian avocado that originated in Islamorada in the Florida Keys, and it is the best known of the long neck avocados. The fruit is striking: pear shaped at the base with a long, gourd like neck, giving an excellent flesh to seed ratio from its small seed. The thin, smooth, glossy skin stays bright green when ripe and the flesh is yellow green, smooth and buttery.

Russell is an early season type, maturing roughly through July and August, and individual fruit are notably large. As a West Indian variety it has low cold tolerance and is very susceptible to frost damage, so it suits warm, near frost free climates.

2 sources
  • Specialty Produce: Long Neck (Russell) Avocados
  • Tropical Tree Guide: Russell Avocado Grow Guide

Ryan

Ryan is a Mexican by Guatemalan hybrid developed by nurseryman Albert Rideout in Whittier, California, in 1927, where it was first sold as Summer Fuerte before being renamed for Edward Ryan, who later bought the property. It is a B type flowering variety, so it pairs well with an A type such as Hass.

The skin is green, fairly thick and slightly rough, and stays green when ripe over firm flesh that holds its shape. Ryan is prized as a late season variety, often cropping after other types have finished, and it is regarded as cold resistant.

2 sources
  • Specialty Produce: Ryan Avocados
  • Good Fruit Guide: Ryan

Sharwill

Sharwill (correctly spelled Sharwil) is an Australian cultivar selected in Queensland in the early 1950s, its name combining the surnames of growers Frank Sharpe and James Wilson. It is predominantly Guatemalan race with some Mexican genes, and carries a B-type flower, so it pairs well with an A-type variety such as Hass for cross-pollination. The fruit is pear-shaped with smooth, green, slightly pebbly skin that stays green when ripe and peels well, over a small seed.

The flesh is fibreless with excellent eating quality and a flavour many describe as milder and richer than Hass. Sharwill matures mid-season, after Fuerte and ahead of Hass, and it is sensitive to cool temperatures during flowering. It went on to become the most important commercial avocado in Hawaii, where warm nights suit its B-type bloom.

3 sources
  • FAO, Avocado Production in Australia (A.W. Whiley)
  • Wikipedia, Avocado (list of cultivars)
  • Greg Alder's Yard Posts, The Sharwil avocado tree profile

Shepard
