Shepard is a green-skin avocado grown commercially almost exclusively in Australia, where it and Hass together define the local season. It is an early-maturing, semi-dwarf, heavy-cropping tree with a B-type flower, making it a useful pollination partner for A-type Hass. Unlike Hass, the smooth green skin stays green even when the fruit is fully ripe, so colour is not a ripeness guide; the tip should yield slightly instead.

Its standout trait is flesh that browns much more slowly than Hass once cut, which suits slices, garnishes and platters. The texture is firm, buttery and creamy, holding its shape when sliced, with a flavour often called milder and slightly sweeter than Hass. In Australia, Shepard typically fills the earlier part of the season (around February to April) before the main Hass crop.

3 sources
  • University of Wollongong, What's the difference between Hass and Shepard avocados?
  • FAO, Avocado Production in Australia (A.W. Whiley)
  • Specialty Produce, Shepard Avocados

Sir Prize

Sir Prize (officially SirPrize) came out of the University of California avocado breeding program led by B.O. Bergh, patented in 1997 with Bergh and Gray Martin as inventors. It is an open-pollinated seedling related to Hass and carries a B-type flower, which makes it a popular winter pollination partner for A-type Hass. Like Hass, and unusually for a green-bred Californian variety, its skin turns black as the fruit ripens to eating softness, and the pliable skin separates easily from the flesh.

The interior is buttery and yellow-green with a flavour and quality close to Hass, over a notably small seed. Sir Prize matures several weeks earlier than Hass (a winter variety), and its Mexican-influenced parentage gives it good cold tolerance, letting it crop in cooler sites than Hass.

3 sources
  • US Plant Patent PP9709, Avocado tree called Sir-Prize (Bergh & Martin, UC)
  • Specialty Produce, Sir Prize Avocados
  • Greg Alder's Yard Posts, Avocado varieties for year-round harvest

Wurtz

Wurtz, widely sold as Little Cado, is a Guatemalan-Mexican hybrid and one of the few genuinely dwarf avocados. Its compact size and weeping, spreading habit make it well suited to large containers and small gardens.

It is a Type A flowering variety. The flesh is creamy with a rich, nutty flavour often compared to Hass, and the tree typically crops over the warmer months.

3 sources
  • Tropical Tree Guide: Wurtz Avocado
  • Grow Organic: Avocado Type A and B flowers
  • Avocado Tree Growers: Little Cado (Wurtz)

Zutano

Zutano is a mainly Mexican race avocado that arose as a seedling in Fallbrook, California, in the 1920s. It is a B type flowering variety, which is why it is widely planted as a polleniser for the A type Hass. The fruit is green and pear shaped with thin, shiny skin that stays green when ripe.

Compared with oilier Guatemalan types Zutano has lower oil and a lighter, sometimes slightly fibrous flesh, so it is valued more as a hardy polleniser and early season fruit than as a premium eating avocado. It is cold tolerant and vigorous, and ripens early, opening the avocado season.

2 sources
  • Greg Alder's Yard Posts: Zutano avocado, a profile
  • Specialty Produce: Zutano Avocados

Banana

11 varieties (back to top)

Ambul Kesel

Ambul Kesel is a Sri Lankan banana famous for being slightly sour while still sweet, a tangy profile that sets it apart from ordinary dessert bananas. It belongs to the Mysore (AAB) group. The fruit is petite and slender with sharp, tapered ends and a thin pale yellow skin when ripe, revealing soft, creamy flesh. At maturity it gives off a distinctive pungent, fruity aroma.

Its acidity makes Ambul a favourite for both fresh eating and cooking, including use when unripe and in banana chips. In Sri Lankan tradition it is regarded as a digestive aid and a cooling fruit. The name translates roughly as sour banana, reflecting the tang that defines it.

2 sources
  • Biodiversity for Food and Nutrition - Ambul banana
  • Roar Media - Guide to Sri Lankan Bananas

Blue Java

Blue Java is a triploid ABB hybrid of Musa balbisiana and Musa acuminata, widely known as the Ice Cream banana and also called the Hawaiian banana, Vanilla banana, Ney Mannan, Krie, or Cenizo. Its standout feature is the unripe fruit, which shows a silvery blue green colour caused by a heavy coat of wax, fading to pale yellow as it ripens.

The cultivar is named for its fruit, which has a sweet, aromatic, ice cream like consistency and a flavour reminiscent of vanilla. It is also valued for being cold tolerant and wind resistant, thanks to strong pseudostems and root systems, which makes it a popular choice in cooler subtropical gardens.

2 sources
  • Blue Java banana - Wikipedia
  • Blue Java Ice Cream banana, nursery grounding

Cavendish

Cavendish is the subgroup of triploid AAA bananas that dominates world trade and Australian production. It rose to prominence from the 1950s after Panama disease (race 1) destroyed the previous export banana, Gros Michel. The name traces to William Cavendish, 6th Duke of Devonshire, whose gardener grew imported plants at Chatsworth in the 1830s. Named clones in the group include Dwarf Cavendish, Grand Nain and Williams.

Because Cavendish plants are grown as genetically identical clones with almost no genetic diversity, the whole crop shares the same weaknesses. It has no resistance to Fusarium wilt Tropical Race 4 (TR4), which is under active containment in North Queensland. In Australia over 95 percent of commercial plantation bananas are Cavendish, the bulk grown in North Queensland.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Cavendish banana
  • University of Queensland QAAFI: The world's favourite fruit needs a genetic boost
  • Wikipedia: List of banana cultivars

Ducasse

Ducasse is the Australian name for Pisang Awak, a triploid ABB banana also called the sugar banana and known as kluai nam wa in Thailand. It is one of the most widely grown bananas across Southeast Asia, valued for its sweet, fragrant flavour. The fruit is small and ripens to a bright yellow, and the plant suits backyard gardens at a moderate height.

The flesh is firm and resists browning, which makes Ducasse suitable for both fresh eating and cooking or processing. It tends to be a little more acid than Cavendish, and shows some tolerance to cooler growing areas, though frost should be avoided.

3 sources
  • Thai banana (Pisang Awak/Ducasse) - Wikipedia
  • Ducasse Bananas - Specialty Produce
  • Dwarf Banana Ducasse, Daleys, nursery grounding

Dwarf Cavendish

Dwarf Cavendish is a specific short cultivar within the Cavendish (AAA) subgroup. The word dwarf refers to the height of the pseudostem, not the fruit, which is normal Cavendish size. Its compact, stable, wind-resistant habit makes it easier to manage and popular both for plantations and for home and container growing. The cultivar is associated with the Canary Islands and is one of the most widely planted Cavendish types.

An easy identifying trait is that the male bracts and flowers are retained on the plant rather than being shed. Young plants often show maroon or purple blotches on the leaves that fade with maturity.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Dwarf Cavendish banana
  • Wikipedia: List of banana cultivars

Dwarf Duccase / Sugar Banana

Dwarf Ducasse is a compact form of the Ducasse, the Australian name for the Thai banana Pisang Awak (an ABB triploid). Widely called the sugar banana, it was introduced to northern Australia from Thailand in the 1880s and proved more adaptable than Cavendish. The fruit is small to medium with a thin, smooth, angular peel and rich, sweet flesh that is firm and starchy, turning soft and dense when fully ripe.

The dwarf form is a stout natural mutant of Pisang Awak (known overseas as Dwarf Namwah), making a shorter, more wind tolerant plant suited to home gardens. In Australia the Dwarf Ducasse is restricted to the south east Queensland banana growing region under quarantine rules.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia - Thai banana (Pisang Awak)
  • Specialty Produce - Ducasse Bananas
  • Fruitopia Nursery - Dwarf Duccase / Sugar Banana

Gold Finger

Gold Finger, formally FHIA-01, is a tetraploid AAAB hybrid dessert banana developed at the Honduran Foundation for Agricultural Research (FHIA) by a team led by Phillip Rowe and Franklin Rosales, with the subacid Brazilian clone Prata-ana in its parentage. It was bred specifically as a more disease resistant alternative to vulnerable varieties like Cavendish.

Its main appeal is resilience: it is resistant to black Sigatoka, Race 4 of Panama disease and burrowing nematode, and is more tolerant of cold and wind than most bananas. This makes it well suited to subtropical conditions. The ripe fruit is sweet with a slightly tart, apple like flavour, and the plant takes longer than many bananas to mature.

2 sources
  • Goldfinger banana - Wikipedia
  • Goldfinger (FHIA-01) Banana Grow Guide, nursery grounding

Horn Plantain

Horn Plantain belongs to the AAB plantain group, one of four plantain bunch types (French, French Horn, False Horn and Horn). It is a true cooking banana, not a dessert type, with the classic starchy plantain character that is eaten cooked rather than raw. The fruit is large and the male bud degenerates as the bunch matures, a trait shared with the related False Horn group.

Horn types are known for sparse bunches, typically carrying very few large fingers spread across only one to five hands (occasionally up to eight or ten). The starchy flesh is versatile in the kitchen, fried, boiled or roasted, and can be used green or ripe. Related Horn cultivars from Asia include Tanduk and Pisang Tandok.

2 sources
  • ProMusa - Plantain subgroup
  • Wikipedia - True plantains

Lady Finger

In Australia the name Lady Finger refers to a Pome subgroup banana (AAB group, a Musa acuminata by balbisiana hybrid), not the AA Sucrier sold under that name elsewhere. Pome bananas have a sub-acid, apple-like character, and Lady Finger is prized as sweeter and more flavoursome than Cavendish. It is the second most popular banana in Australia after Cavendish, grown mainly in NSW with more recent plantings on the Atherton Tablelands.

Its main drawback is disease. Lady Finger is highly susceptible to Panama disease (Fusarium wilt race 1), which Cavendish resists, and it is also affected by subtropical race 4.

4 sources
  • Wikipedia: Lady finger banana
  • ProMusa: Pome subgroup
  • Better Bananas (Australian banana industry): Panama disease research and development
  • Wikipedia: List of banana cultivars

Pisang Ceylon

Pisang Ceylon is a Mysore type banana, part of the AAB Mysore subgroup that originates from India and Pakistan. Mysore bananas are among the most widely grown cultivars in India, traditionally valued there as a fresh eating variety. The fruit is small and thin skinned, in the lady finger style.

The flesh is sweet, and the small fruits are borne in dense bunches on a tall, sturdy plant. Like other Mysore types it is a dessert banana eaten fresh rather than a cooking banana.

3 sources
  • List of banana cultivars (Mysore subgroup, AAB) - Wikipedia
  • Musa Mysore - Bananas.org wiki
  • Mysore Bananas - Specialty Produce

Red Dacca

Red Dacca is a red-skinned dessert banana, a triploid cultivar of Musa acuminata in the AAA group. Where most supermarket bananas are yellow Cavendish, Red Dacca ripens to a deep red or maroon rind, making it instantly recognisable. The fruit is often shorter and plumper than a Cavendish, with a slightly thicker skin. It is also known by names such as Cuban Red and Jamaican Red.

The creamy flesh is softer and sweeter than Cavendish, and the flavour is sometimes described as carrying a tangy raspberry note. It is eaten fresh and used in desserts and smoothies, prized more for its colour and dessert quality than for cooking.

2 sources
  • Red banana - Wikipedia
  • Red Dacca Banana, nursery grounding

Black Sapote

5 varieties (back to top)

Bernecker

Bernecker (also spelt Bernicker) is an Australian black sapote cultivar known as a prolific producer of nearly spherical, medium to large fruit with few seeds and of superior eating quality. It is widely grafted and is also grown in Florida. The reliable cropping and clean, low seed fruit make it a popular choice.

Black sapote (Diospyros nigra, also written D. digyna) is a persimmon relative whose ripe pulp has the colour, texture and flavour of chocolate pudding. The skin ripens from olive green to a muddy near black as the fruit softens, hence the common name chocolate pudding fruit.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Diospyros nigra
  • Bulleen Art Garden: Black Sapote
  • Growables: Black Sapote

Maher

Maher is an Australian black sapote selection prized for its compact habit. It is described as a small yet prolific tree (to around 4 metres), unusual among black sapotes, which suits it to home gardens. The fruit is very large and flattened, of good to very good eating quality, with few seeds.

Like all black sapote (Diospyros nigra, also written D. digyna), the ripe flesh has the colour, texture and flavour of chocolate pudding. The skin ripens from olive green to a muddy near black when soft and ready to eat, which is why it is called the chocolate pudding fruit.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Diospyros nigra
  • Bulleen Art Garden: Black Sapote
  • Growables: Black Sapote

Mossman

Mossman is an Australian selection of black sapote (Diospyros nigra, also written digyna), the chocolate pudding fruit whose ripe flesh turns dark brown with a soft, sweet, mild, custard-like character. The species is native to Mexico and Central America, and the green fruit ripens to olive or near-black.

The Mossman cultivar is known for very large, round fruit with few seeds and high pulp content, which suits both fresh eating and processing. Its flavour is generally rated as medium, and it is a vigorous, heavy-yielding tree.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Diospyros nigra (black sapote)
  • Daleys Fruit: Black Sapote Mossman

Ricks Late

Ricks Late (Rick's Late) is an Australian black sapote cultivar that originated in New South Wales. It is valued for producing heavy, late crops of excellent quality fruit, extending the harvest beyond the usual season. That late, reliable cropping is its defining trait.

Black sapote (Diospyros nigra, also written D. digyna) is a persimmon relative whose ripe pulp has the colour, texture and flavour of chocolate pudding. The skin ripens from olive green to a muddy near black as the fruit softens, hence the common name chocolate pudding fruit.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Diospyros nigra
  • Bulleen Art Garden: Black Sapote
  • Growables: Black Sapote

Superb

Superb is a black sapote selection from north Queensland, Australia. It bears large quantities of superb quality, small fruit that may be completely seedless if it is not cross pollinated. The combination of heavy bearing and small, often seedless fruit is what sets this cultivar apart.

Like all black sapote (Diospyros nigra, also written D. digyna), the ripe flesh has the colour, texture and flavour of chocolate pudding. The skin ripens from olive green to a muddy near black when the fruit is soft and ready to eat.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Diospyros nigra
  • Bulleen Art Garden: Black Sapote
  • Growables: Black Sapote

Blueberry

12 varieties (back to top)

Biloxi

Biloxi is a southern highbush blueberry released in 1998 from the USDA-ARS programme in Mississippi (with Mississippi State University). Like other southern highbush types it carries low-chill native ancestry (Vaccinium darrowii), which gives it a low chilling requirement and a semi-evergreen habit, letting it crop in regions where higher-chill northern types fail. The bushes are vigorous and upright.

It is one of the earliest-ripening blueberries: its fruit precedes the earliest rabbiteye cultivars by roughly two to three weeks. Berries are medium-sized, light blue and very firm, with a sweet, distinct flavour. Because it blooms early, Biloxi is best interplanted with other southern highbush varieties to aid fruit set, and early bloom can put it at risk in late spring frosts.

3 sources
  • Mississippi State University Extension - Blueberry Varieties
  • USDA ARS Poplarville - Blueberry Plant Releases
  • One Green World - Biloxi Southern Highbush Blueberry

Brightwell

Brightwell is a rabbiteye blueberry developed by the University of Georgia with the USDA-ARS and released in 1983. It was named for Dr W.T. Brightwell, a long-serving UGA rabbiteye breeder. It is an early to mid-season type, with berries that are medium to large, good colour and flavour, and small dry stem scars that suit fresh eating and handling.

A useful quirk is that Brightwell is partially self-fertile, so it can set some fruit on its own, though yields are better with a second early-blooming rabbiteye nearby. Its fruit can be sensitive to wet conditions and splitting. It carries a lower chilling requirement than later rabbiteyes, which broadens where it can be grown.

3 sources
  • Texas A&M AgriLife Extension, Rabbiteye Blueberries
  • University of Georgia / eXtension (Brightwell rabbiteye variety)
  • Minnetonka Orchards, The Brightwell Blueberry

Climax

Climax is a rabbiteye blueberry released jointly by the University of Georgia and the USDA-ARS in 1974 and long used as a standard early rabbiteye. Its signature trait is a concentrated ripening season: the crop comes off over a short window (handy for a single big pick), with small to medium, firm fruit.

Because Climax blooms early most years, it is more exposed to late frosts than later-blooming types. It is not self-fertile, so it needs another early-blooming rabbiteye nearby (Brightwell, Premier and Tifblue are commonly suggested) to set a good crop.

3 sources
  • Texas A&M AgriLife Extension, Rabbiteye Blueberries
  • University of Georgia / eXtension (Climax rabbiteye variety)
  • UF/IFAS Extension, Blueberry cultivars for Florida

Gulf Coast

Gulf Coast (Gulfcoast) is a southern highbush blueberry, a 1987 Mississippi release from the USDA breeding programme based at Poplarville. It was developed for the warm, humid conditions of the US Gulf Coast region, where mild winters suit its modest chill requirement. The bush is semi-evergreen in mild climates.

It ripens around mid-season (mid-May in south Mississippi) and produces medium-sized, light blue, firm berries with a small stem scar and good flavour. Extension sources note a recurring quirk, a tendency toward stemminess (fruit retaining bits of stem) where it has been grown commercially.

2 sources
  • Mississippi State University Extension - Blueberry Varieties
  • USDA ARS Poplarville - Blueberry Plant Releases

Legacy

Legacy is a highbush blueberry released in 1993 by the USDA and Rutgers (NJAES), with the parentage 'Elizabeth' x US75. Though grown as a northern highbush, it carries some southern (Vaccinium darrowii) ancestry, giving it broad adaptability; it is vigorous with an upright, open habit. Its chill requirement is on the high side, so it suits cooler regions rather than the mildest ones.

It ripens late in the season and is prized for fruit quality: light blue, firm berries with a small dry stem scar and a sweet, low-acid flavour often rated above most other cultivars. One drawback noted by sources is susceptibility to stem canker (Botryosphaeria), which growers manage with irrigation and pruning.

2 sources
  • NC Blueberry Journal - Blueberry cultivar 'Legacy'
  • Food Forest Nursery - Legacy Blueberry

Misty

Misty is a southern highbush blueberry released by the University of Florida, bred for low-latitude, mild-winter regions. The bush is upright and vigorous, carrying pink-to-white flowers and deep green, semi-evergreen foliage; leaves take on red-purple tones in autumn and stems can redden in winter, giving it ornamental appeal across the seasons.

It is an early-ripening, low-chill variety producing attractive light blue, firm berries with a sweet, slightly spicy flavour. Misty can set some fruit on its own but performs best cross-pollinated with another southern highbush such as Sharpblue or Biloxi, which improves yield and berry size.

3 sources
  • MyGardenLife - Southern Highbush Blueberry 'Misty'
  • Clemson University - Highbush Blueberry 'Misty'
  • UF/IFAS HS1245 - Southern Highbush Blueberry Cultivars from the University of Florida

Peach Sorbet

Peach Sorbet is a patented dwarf highbush blueberry in the Bushel and Berry range (bred by Fall Creek Nursery, US patent PP23325). Its main appeal is ornamental foliage: new leaves emerge in shades of peach, pink and orange before greening, then turn a rich red-purple, and in milder climates the plant is semi-evergreen. It stays very compact, around 0.6m tall and wide with a mounded habit, so it suits pots and small spaces.

Despite the showy leaves it still fruits, carrying mildly sweet, dark-blue berries about 1cm across in summer. It is self-pollinating, so a single plant will crop, though, as with most blueberries, a second variety improves yield. The dwarf, decorative form is the point of difference rather than heavy production.

2 sources
  • Missouri Botanical Garden Plant Finder, Bushel and Berry Peach Sorbet
  • Bushel and Berry, Peach Sorbet

Pink Icing

Pink Icing is a patented compact highbush blueberry in the Bushel and Berry range (a Toro x FLX-2 cross, US patent PP23336). Its standout feature is colourful foliage: leaves emerge with pink and red tones before maturing to dark green, then take on dark lavender-blue, almost iridescent tones in the cooler months, giving year-round ornamental value.

It is more substantial than the smallest Bushel and Berry types, reaching roughly 1 to 1.2m tall and wide with a mounded habit, and still produces plenty of large, mildly sweet, juicy berries in mid-summer. It is self-pollinating, so a single bush will fruit, making it a good dual-purpose plant for a pot or border.

2 sources
  • Missouri Botanical Garden Plant Finder, Pink Icing (Vaccinium 'ZF06-079')
  • Bushel and Berry, Pink Icing

Powder Blue

Powder Blue (Powderblue) is a rabbiteye blueberry (Vaccinium ashei/virgatum) released jointly by North Carolina State University and the USDA-ARS in 1978. It is a late-season type, ripening roughly late June into late July, well after the early rabbiteyes, which makes it useful for stretching out a planting's harvest. The berries are medium sized and a distinctive light, powdery blue, with good production and a relatively crack-resistant skin.

As a rabbiteye it has a higher chilling requirement (around 600 hours) than southern highbush types, so it suits warm-temperate areas with a real winter. It is not reliably self-fertile and crops best with a second compatible rabbiteye nearby (Tifblue and Brightwell are common pollenisers), so plant it in groups rather than singly.

3 sources
  • Texas A&M AgriLife Extension, Rabbiteye Blueberries
  • eXtension / NC State (Powderblue rabbiteye variety)
  • UF/IFAS Extension, Blueberry cultivars for Florida

Premier

Premier is a rabbiteye blueberry released jointly by North Carolina State University and the USDA-ARS in 1978. It is an early-ripening type valued for high yields of medium to large, light-blue fruit, and is a long-established home-garden favourite.

One practical quirk noted by extension growers is that the young limbs can be too limber to carry heavy crops, so the bush may need support or careful pruning. Like most rabbiteyes it crops best with a second compatible variety nearby (Climax, Tifblue and Powder Blue are common partners).

3 sources
  • Texas A&M AgriLife Extension, Rabbiteye Blueberries
  • NC State / eXtension (Premier rabbiteye variety)
  • UF/IFAS Extension, Blueberry cultivars for Florida

Sharpe Blue

Sharpe Blue (commonly spelled Sharpblue) is a landmark southern highbush blueberry. Released by Ralph Sharpe and Wayne Sherman at the University of Florida in 1976, it was among the first low-chill highbush blueberries bred for commercial production, created by crossing northern highbush types with low-chill native species such as Vaccinium darrowii. It effectively launched the Florida blueberry industry and remains a parent of many later southern highbush cultivars.

Because of its low chilling requirement, Sharpblue suits mild-winter regions and ripens early. It is largely self-fertile, so a single plant will crop, and it bears dark blue berries praised for good flavour and texture.

3 sources
  • American Pomological Society - The 'Sharpblue' Southern Highbush Blueberry
  • UF/IFAS HS1245 - Southern Highbush Blueberry Cultivars from the University of Florida
  • Edible Landscaping - Sharpblue Blueberry

Sunshine Blue

Sunshine Blue is a southern highbush blueberry valued as much for looks as for fruit. It forms a compact, semi-dwarf bush, roughly 1 to 1.2 metres tall and wide, with blue-green, semi-evergreen foliage that turns red and purple in cooler weather. In spring it carries showy hot-pink flowers that fade to white, making it a popular ornamental and container plant.

It is a low-chill variety suited to mild-winter regions, and it is self-fertile, so a single plant will set a crop (though a compatible partner can improve yield). Sources note it also tolerates slightly higher soil pH than many blueberries, which are otherwise fussy about acidic soil. The berries are medium-sized and sweet.

3 sources
  • Monrovia - Sunshine Blue Blueberry
  • Wayside Gardens - Vaccinium 'Sunshine Blue'
  • Plant Lust - Vaccinium 'Sunshine Blue'

Cacao

2 varieties (back to top)

Mocambo

Mocambo, sold alongside cacao, is Theobroma bicolor, a close relative of the chocolate tree (Theobroma cacao) rather than the same species. Native to Central and South America, including the Amazon basin of Brazil, Colombia, Ecuador and Peru, it goes by many names including jaguar tree, macambo and pataxte. Its seeds are edible, high in calories, and rich in protein and fibre, and like true cacao they can be processed into chocolate-like drinks.

Mocambo carries deep Mesoamerican history. It was cultivated by the Aztecs alongside cacao, and the Maya valued it highly: the beans make an unusually thick, stable foam, and the tree appears in the Popol Vuh, the Maya creation text, paired with cacao. Pataxte drinks are lower in caffeine than chocolate, and the beans are still prepared today for their prized foaming quality, with some artisan chocolate makers reviving its use.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Theobroma bicolor
  • Chocolate Class (Harvard): Pataxte, Cacao's Cast-out Cousin

Trinitario

Trinitario is not a single clone but a recognised hybrid group of cacao, arising from natural crosses between Criollo and Forastero types. It is named after Trinidad, where it emerged in the 18th century after disease devastated the island's prized Criollo trees and growers replanted with hardier Forastero stock. The surviving Criollo and the introduced Forastero crossed, producing a population that was more disease resistant than Criollo while tasting better than the workhorse Forastero.

Because it combines fine flavour with vigour and resistance, Trinitario is widely grown as a fine, or flavour, cacao and prized by chocolate makers. Its beans tend toward balanced, fruity and floral aromatics rather than the flat, plain profile of bulk Forastero cocoa. Any cross between Criollo and Forastero can fall under the Trinitario label, so trees and their beans vary considerably from one selection to the next.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Types of cocoa beans
  • Tropical Cacao: Understanding Cacao Varieties (Criollo, Forastero, Trinitario)
  • Chocolats du Monde: Trinitario cocoa, fusion of Criollo and Forastero

Cherry

25 varieties (back to top)

Bing

Bing is the classic large dark sweet cherry, created in 1875 in Milwaukie, Oregon, by horticulturist Seth Lewelling from an open pollination cross of Black Republican and Royal Ann (Napoleon). It was named for Lewelling's Chinese foreman, Ah Bing. The fruit is large and uniform with deep red to mahogany, near black skin, and firm, intensely sweet, juicy flesh with very little tartness.

Bing is not self fertile and needs a compatible pollinator of a different variety to set fruit. It cannot be reliably cross pollinated by Lambert or Royal Ann (Napoleon), as these are cross incompatible with it. It ripens in the mid part of the cherry season.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Bing cherry
  • Stark Bro's: Bing Sweet Cherry Tree

Burgsdorf

Burgsdorf, sometimes listed as Burgsdorf's Seedling, is an Australian heritage sweet cherry described as being of colonial origin, raised by a Mr Burgsdorf at Harcourt. It has been grown in Australia since early settlement and survives today mainly through heritage orchard and rare fruit collections.

It is prized as one of the earliest cherries of the season, ripening from about November in Australian conditions. The fruit is large and dark red with sweet flavour, making it a useful early fresh cherry and a living piece of Australian orchard history.

2 sources
  • Heritage & Rare Fruit Network: Cherries
  • Rare Fruit Society of South Australia: Burgsdorf

Capulin

Capulin is a different species from the common sweet cherry. It is Prunus salicifolia, the capulin or Mexican bird cherry, a true cherry native to the highlands of Mexico and Central America (and naturalised through the Andes) that has been cultivated there since pre-Columbian times. Unlike the shrubby orchard cherry, it is an evergreen tending tree with narrow, willow like leaves, which is what the name salicifolia refers to.

The round fruit is red to nearly black with thin, tender skin and pale green, juicy pulp, tasting sweet to sub acid with a slightly astringent aftertaste that varies by seedling. It is eaten fresh or stewed and made into preserves and jam, and in Mexico is used as a filling for special tamales. Because it suits cooler highland elevations, it sits apart from typical warm climate fruit.

2 sources
  • Capulin, Morton, Purdue University NewCROP
  • Prunus salicifolia, Plants For A Future

Early Burlat

Early Burlat, often just called Burlat, is the benchmark early season sweet cherry of Europe, originating in France with a grower named Burlat in the Lyon region in the early 20th century. It is prized as one of the very first cherries of the season, harvested from around late May into June. The fruit is large and glossy with dark red skin and juicy flesh.

Because it opens the cherry season, Burlat is grown widely across France and Europe. It is not self fertile and needs a pollinator such as Stella or other compatible varieties. As an early, softer cherry it can be susceptible to cracking and needs careful handling.

3 sources
  • Cherry Times: the sweet history of Burlat cherries
  • Van den Berk Nurseries: Prunus avium BURLAT (Bigarreau Burlat)
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Early Burlat Cherry

Lambert

Lambert is an old, large dark sweet cherry developed by Joseph Hamilton Lambert, who grafted a volunteer seedling found in 1848 under a Napoleon cherry tree in Oregon, introducing it commercially in 1876. It has been grown in the Pacific Northwest for over 150 years and ranks second only to Bing as a commercial cultivar. The fruit is large, firm, heart shaped, with dark red to near black skin and very dark, sweet flesh.

Lambert is a late season variety, ripening one to two weeks after Bing, which helps make it more resistant to late spring frost and less prone to cracking. It is not self fertile and needs a compatible pollinator, but note that Lambert and Bing are cross incompatible and cannot pollinate each other.

2 sources
  • UC Davis Foundation Plant Services: Lambert cherry
  • One Green World: Lambert Cherry

Lapins

Lapins is a sweet cherry bred at the Summerland Research Station in British Columbia, Canada, from a Van by Stella cross made in 1965. It was named for the Latvian agronomist Karlis Lapins, a pioneer of self fertile cherry breeding, and released in 1983 after his retirement. Like its Stella parent it is self fertile, so a single tree sets fruit without a pollinating partner, and it also pollinates other varieties.

The fruit is large, firm and deep red, regarded as very high quality and noted for good resistance to rain splitting. It turns dark red and tastes sweet before it is fully ready to pick. Lapins is a late season cherry, ripening roughly a fortnight after Bing.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Lapins cherry
  • Specialty Produce: Lapins Cherries

Merchant

Merchant is an English sweet cherry raised at the John Innes Institute in Norwich and introduced around 1970. It is valued as one of the most reliable early croppers, carrying heavy yields of large, dark, almost black fruit with a rich, sweet flavour in early summer.

It holds the Royal Horticultural Society Award of Garden Merit, given in 1995. Merchant is not self-fertile, so it needs a compatible pollinator, but it sits in pollination group 3 and works as a universal pollen donor for many other sweet cherries. The fruit is at its best eaten fresh.

3 sources
  • Royal Horticultural Society: Prunus avium 'Merchant'
  • Orange Pippin: Merchant cherry
  • Frank P Matthews: Merchant cherry

Minnie Royal

Minnie Royal is a low chill sweet cherry from Zaiger Genetics, granted U.S. Plant Patent 12942 in 2002. The patent records a breeding line tracing back to a cross between a Bing derived selection and an unnamed low chilling cherry. It is an early bloomer, opening roughly 10 days before Early Burlat with fruit maturing about 2 days after it.

Minnie Royal is not self fruitful and is the classic companion pollinator pair with Royal Lee, the two being planted together so each sets a crop. It bears medium sized fruit with attractive red to wine red skin and firm, crisp flesh.

2 sources
  • U.S. Plant Patent 12942 - Cherry tree named 'Minnie Royal' (Google Patents)
  • San Diego Horticultural Society, 'Trees, Please! Can You Bake A Cherry Pie?' by Robin Rivet

Morello

Morello is the classic sour or acid cherry, a cultivar of Prunus cerasus rather than the sweet Prunus avium, and the most widely planted sour cherry in central Europe. The fruit is roundish, with skin that ripens from red to a dark blackish red, and soft, very juicy, dark red flesh. Despite a high sugar content it tastes distinctly sour, a sharpness that intensifies with cooking.

Because it is too tart to enjoy fresh, Morello is grown as a cooking and processing cherry, used in pies, jams, preserves and liqueurs such as kirsch. It is usually self fertile, so it sets fruit without a partner and can pollinate other late flowering cherries. It ripens later than sweet cherries, around July in the northern hemisphere.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Morello cherry
  • Woodland Trust: Sour Cherry (Prunus cerasus)

Nanking

Nanking cherry is not the orchard sweet cherry but Prunus tomentosa, a hardy bush cherry (also called Manchu or downy cherry) native to northern and western China, Korea, and Mongolia. It grows as a dense, multi stemmed deciduous shrub rather than a tall tree, and is named tomentosa for the downy hairs that coat its leaves, stems, and fruit. It is prized as much for ornament as for fruit.

In early spring it carries profuse white to pink flowers, followed in early summer by small scarlet drupes that are sweet but slightly tart, used fresh and for juice, jam, and wine. It is exceptionally cold hardy, tolerating very harsh winters. A quirk worth noting is that despite the cherry name it is botanically closer to the plums than to true cherries.

2 sources
  • Prunus tomentosa, Wikipedia
  • Prunus tomentosa, NC State Extension Gardener Plant Toolbox

Napoleon

Napoleon is an old European blush cherry, also known as Royal Ann, Napoleon Bigarreau and Queen Anne, with references dating back centuries before it was named around 1820. It belongs to the Bigarreau group of firm fleshed sweet cherries. The large heart shaped fruit has pale yellow skin with a red blush where the sun strikes it, over firm, cream coloured flesh, similar in look and taste to Rainier.

Brought to Oregon in the 1840s, it was renamed Royal Ann there in 1850. Its sweet, mildly acidic flavour and firm texture make it a classic dual purpose cherry, eaten fresh but especially valued for canning, preserves and maraschino cherries.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Royal Ann cherry
  • Specialty Produce: Napoleon Cherries

Rainier

Rainier is a sweet cherry (Prunus avium) bred by Harold Fogle at Washington State University's research station in Prosser, crossing Bing with Van in 1952 and releasing it in 1960. It is named after Mount Rainier. The famous bicolour look came as a surprise: both red parents carried a recessive gene, so Fogle said he was as shocked as anyone when pale fruit appeared.

The fruit is a creamy yellow blushed with pink to bright red, with thin skin and firm, sweet, gently floral yellow flesh, and is treated as a premium cherry. That delicacy is also its quirk: the thin skinned fruit bruises easily and is sensitive to rain, wind, and heat, and birds favour it. Rainier is not self fertile and needs a pollinizer such as Bing, Van, or Lapins.

3 sources
  • Rainier cherry, Wikipedia
  • Rainier cherry developer dies at 88, WSU Insider
  • Rainier cherries, Washington State Magazine (WSU)

Ron s

Ron's, also recorded as Ron's Seedling, is a sweet cherry grown in Australia. The fruit has firm, slightly fibrous, juicy flesh with very good flavour and a small, nearly free stone, and the tree crops well, ripening in the early to middle part of the cherry season.

It stands out in research on Australian grown cherries. A published study found Ron's had the highest total flavonoid content of the cultivars tested and no detectable condensed tannins. It is not self-fertile, so it needs a compatible pollinator to set a crop.

2 sources
  • Orange Pippin: Ron's Seedling cherry
  • ACS Omega: Phenolic Compounds from Australia-Grown Sweet Cherries

Royal Crimson

Royal Crimson is a low chill sweet cherry released by Zaiger Genetics, tested under the selection code 6GM25. Its standout trait among the Zaiger low chill cherries is that it is self fruitful, so it can set a crop without a partner, unlike Royal Lee and Minnie Royal which both need a pollinator.

Beyond fruiting on its own, Royal Crimson also works as a pollinator for either Royal Lee or Minnie Royal, making it a useful single tree or anchor choice in warm, mild winter gardens. It carries a low chill requirement (around 200 to 300 hours), bears large crimson red fruit, and harvests early to mid May in its home climate.

2 sources
  • San Diego Horticultural Society, 'Trees, Please! Can You Bake A Cherry Pie?' by Robin Rivet
  • Dave Wilson Nursery - Royal Crimson cherry

Royal Lee

Royal Lee is a low chill sweet cherry from Zaiger Genetics (Modesto, California). It was patented in 2002 under the name Regal Lee (U.S. Plant Patent 12417), the name that later morphed into the commercial Royal Lee. The patent records its parentage as an open pollinated seedling tracing back to a cross involving a Bing seedling and a lower chilling seedling of unknown parentage.

Royal Lee is self sterile, so it needs a low chill pollinator. Minnie Royal is the standard companion, though the two do not always bloom together. It is an early, very productive variety bearing medium to large fruit with attractive red to garnet red skin and very firm flesh that holds well on the tree after maturity.

2 sources
  • U.S. Plant Patent 12417 - Cherry tree named 'Regal Lee' (Google Patents)
  • San Diego Horticultural Society, 'Trees, Please! Can You Bake A Cherry Pie?' by Robin Rivet

Royal Rainier

Royal Rainier is a yellow blush sweet cherry from Zaiger Genetics, covered by U.S. Plant Patent 10790 (issued 1999). It is distinct from the famous Rainier cherry: the patent records it as an open pollinated seedling of selection 32G153, itself descended from Stella, with yellowish white skin carrying an attractive red blush where the sun strikes it, plus firm flesh that holds on the tree several days after maturity.

Royal Rainier ripens early, about 3 to 5 days ahead of standard Rainier, and is noted for excellent flavour as a taste test winner. It is not self fertile and needs a pollinator; suitable partners include Bing, Black Tartarian and Lapins.

2 sources
  • U.S. Plant Patent 10790 - Cherry tree named 'Royal Rainier' (Google Patents)
  • Dave Wilson Nursery - Royal Rainier cherry

Saint Margaret

Saint Margaret, also written St Margaret, is an old, large, dark sweet cherry valued as a late season variety. The heart shaped fruit has dark red, almost black skin over firm, juicy, deeply coloured flesh, and the tree is a good cropper that also makes an attractive ornamental.

It ripens late, from about late December into early January in Australian gardens, well after the main early cherries. Beyond fresh eating it is traditionally used for jam and preserving. It is not self-fertile and sets best with pollinators such as Napoleon, Stella or Bing nearby.

2 sources
  • Orange Pippin: St Margaret cherry
  • Heritage & Rare Fruit Network: Cherries

Simone

Simone is a black sweet cherry that is one of the best performing and most widely grown varieties in the Australian cherry industry. The fruit is large, almost the size of a small plum, with dark, succulent, firm flesh and a sweet flavour. It is similar in appearance and timing to Lapins, and the trees are precocious, cropping young.

Its standout trait is that Simone is self fertile, setting a good crop planted on its own, so it does not require a separate pollinator. It also pollinates well with Ron's Seedling and Lapins. Simone is valued for being crack resistant, holding up when rained on near harvest, and it ripens around December to early January in Australia.

3 sources
  • Cherry Growers Australia: Cherry Varieties
  • Speciality Trees: Prunus avium Simone
  • Yalca Fruit Trees: Simone cherry

Sir don

Sir Don is an Australian sweet cherry from a Black Douglas by Stella cross, protected under Plant Breeder's Rights and assessed through national evaluation trials across Australia. It is self fertile, so a single tree will crop without a separate pollinator. It reaches full bloom about seven days after Stella and matures late in the season.

The fruit is large and kidney to heart shaped with mahogany to almost black skin, a long stem, and firm flesh with red juice and a rich, sweet flavour. A standout trait is its resistance to rain cracking. It suits fresh eating as well as jams and preserves.

2 sources
  • ANFIC: Sir Don cherry variety
  • Garden Express: Cherry Sir Don (PBR)

St Margaret

St Margaret, also written Saint Margaret, is an old, large, dark sweet cherry valued as a late season variety. The heart shaped fruit has dark red, almost black skin over firm, juicy, deeply coloured flesh, and the tree is a good cropper that also makes an attractive ornamental.

It ripens late, from about late December into early January in Australian gardens, well after the main early cherries. Beyond fresh eating it is traditionally used for jam and preserving. It is not self-fertile and sets best with pollinators such as Napoleon, Stella or Bing nearby.

2 sources
  • Orange Pippin: St Margaret cherry
  • Heritage & Rare Fruit Network: Cherries

Starkrimson

Starkrimson is the trade name under which the Lapins sweet cherry is sold by Stark Bro's of Missouri. It is a compact, productive tree well suited to smaller spaces, bearing large, firm, meaty dark red cherries with a bright, sweet taste that resemble Bing. The fruit resists cracking.

Its key advantage is that Starkrimson is self fertile, so a single tree will set a crop without a separate pollinator, and it also serves as a good pollinator for other cherry varieties that are not self fertile. It ripens in late June (early summer in the northern hemisphere).

2 sources
  • Missouri Botanical Garden Plant Finder: Prunus avium 'Lapins' STARKRIMSON SWEET
  • Stark Bro's: Starkrimson Sweet Cherry Tree

Stella

Stella is a sweet cherry bred at the Summerland Research Station in British Columbia, Canada, from a 1956 cross (Lambert with a John Innes seedling) and named in 1968. It is historically important as the first named self-fertile sweet cherry.

The fruit is large, heart-shaped and dark red with good quality, and it ripens about a week before Bing (mid to late season). Because Stella is self-fertile, a single tree will set fruit and it can also pollinate other sweet cherries.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Stella cherry
  • Specialty Produce: Stella Cherries

Sunburst

Sunburst is a sweet cherry from the Summerland Research Station in British Columbia, Canada, derived from a Van by Stella cross. Like its Stella parent it is self fertile, so a single tree crops without a pollinator, and it also pollinates other varieties. The large fruit is firm, juicy and very sweet, with skin so deep red it can look almost black.

It is a mid to late season cherry and is noted for resistance to rain splitting, a useful trait in wet ripening weather. The combination of large size, dark colour and self fertility makes it a popular choice for home and small orchard fresh eating.

2 sources
  • Paramount Plants: Prunus avium Sunburst
  • GardenFocused: Cherry variety Sunburst

Van

Van is a sweet cherry (Prunus avium) raised at the Summerland Research Station in British Columbia, Canada. It came from open pollination of an Empress Eugenie tree (crossed 1936, introduced 1944) and was named for horticulturist J. R. Van Haarlen. The fruit is medium sized with dark red, sweet flesh and near black skin, ripening in the mid season a few days ahead of Bing.

Van is best known as a workhorse pollinizer. It is self incompatible but flowers freely and slightly early, so it is widely planted to pollinate other sweet cherries such as Bing, Stella, and Lambert. It also passed its genes on directly, sitting in the parentage of both the Rainier and Lapins cherries.

2 sources
  • Van cherry, Wikipedia
  • Rainier cherries, Washington State Magazine (WSU)

Vista

Vista is an early season sweet cherry raised in Ontario, Canada, from a cross of Hedelfingen with a Victor seedling. It bears large, glossy, firm, dark red fruit, giving collectors the deep colour and bite of a main season cherry right at the start of the season.

Its defining trait is timing rather than durability. Vista ripens among the first dark cherries of the year, but the fruit is well known for being very prone to splitting after rain, which limits it commercially and suits it best to a home tree picked promptly in drier districts.

2 sources
  • Michigan State University Extension: Dark Sweet Cherry Varieties
  • Orange Pippin: Vista cherry

Custard Apple

9 varieties (back to top)

African Pride

African Pride is the main commercial atemoya (sugar apple x cherimoya hybrid) grown in Australia. It crops early and consistently on a smaller, productive tree, and it is the easiest cultivar to fruit because it is highly self compatible and does not generally require hand pollination. That reliability is why it became the workhorse variety, even though its fruit carries more seeds than the larger Pink's Mammoth type.

The heart shaped fruit is medium sized with a medium thick green skin and the sweet, custard like flesh of the type. Because it sets readily on its own, African Pride pollen is also used as the preferred pollen source when hand pollinating other, self incompatible cultivars. Its trade off is seediness: more seeds than premium lines, in exchange for early, dependable yields.

3 sources
  • Custard Apple Pollination, Archives RFC Australia (Growables)
  • Custard Apple Farming in Australia: Varieties
  • Custard Apple - Daleys Fruit

Geffner

Geffner is an atemoya, the hybrid of sugar apple (Annona squamosa) and cherimoya (Annona cherimola). It was selected in Israel and is valued as a productive cultivar that sets fruit reliably without hand pollination, a real advantage since many atemoyas struggle to self-set. The well-formed fruit is heart shaped with the bumpy green skin typical of the type.

The flesh is sweet and creamy, and the fruit is well formed and a good size. Its self-fruitful habit makes Geffner a popular, low-fuss choice for home growers who want a dependable crop from a single tree, rather than the hand pollination that premium Australian cultivars often demand.

3 sources
  • Atemoya - Wikipedia
  • Atemoya - Hortiforum
  • Custard Apple - Daleys Fruit

Hilary White

Hilary White (also spelt Hillary White) is an Australian atemoya and one of the main commercial custard apples grown here, alongside African Pride and Pink's Mammoth. The Australian custard apple is an atemoya, a hybrid of cherimoya (Annona cherimola) and sugar apple (Annona squamosa). Hilary White is a strain of Pink's Mammoth and carries that line's large, sweet, aromatic fruit with soft, creamy white flesh, but on a noticeably smoother skin than standard Pink's Mammoth.

Its main point of difference is reliability. Hilary White crops more consistently than Pink's Mammoth, with bearing performance sitting between Pink's Mammoth and the heavier-cropping African Pride. Like others in the Pink's Mammoth line it still benefits from hand pollination to set well shaped, good-quality fruit, the usual trade-off for the premium, large-fruited atemoyas.

3 sources
  • Atemoya - Wikipedia
  • Specialty Produce: Australian Custard Apples
  • Custard Apple Hilary White - Daleys Fruit

Hillary White

Hillary White (also spelt Hilary White) is an Australian atemoya and one of the main commercial custard apples grown here, alongside African Pride and Pink's Mammoth. The Australian custard apple is an atemoya, a hybrid of cherimoya (Annona cherimola) and sugar apple (Annona squamosa). Hillary White is a strain of Pink's Mammoth and carries that line's large, sweet, aromatic fruit with soft, creamy white flesh, but on a noticeably smoother skin than standard Pink's Mammoth.

Its main point of difference is reliability. Hillary White crops more consistently than Pink's Mammoth, with bearing performance sitting between Pink's Mammoth and the heavier-cropping African Pride. Like others in the Pink's Mammoth line it still benefits from hand pollination to set well shaped, good-quality fruit, the usual trade-off for the premium, large-fruited atemoyas.

3 sources
  • Atemoya - Wikipedia
  • Specialty Produce: Australian Custard Apples
  • Custard Apple Hilary White - Daleys Fruit

Maroochydore Gold

Maroochydore Gold (also sold as Maroochy Gold) is an Australian atemoya custard apple from Queensland's Maroochy Research Station, home to a long-running custard apple breeding program (around 25 years, and described as the world's largest for the crop). The name reflects its Maroochy (Sunshine Coast) research origin.

Unlike the older Pink's Mammoth and Hillary White, which need hand pollination, Maroochydore Gold is grown as a self-pollinating variety, making it easier for home gardeners. The fruit has sweet, flavoursome, pale flesh and is prized for its eating quality.

3 sources
  • FreshFruitPortal, Australia forges custard apples' future
  • Specialty Produce: Australian Custard Apples
  • Custard Apple Maroochydore Gold - Daleys Fruit

Paxton Prolific

Paxton Prolific (also sold as KJ Pinks) is an Australian atemoya, a seedling selection out of the Pink's Mammoth line. Its standout trait is that, unlike most atemoyas, it sets heavy crops naturally without hand pollination, the same self fruitful behaviour noted for Geffner. That makes it a productive, low effort choice that keeps Pink's Mammoth style fruit quality without the labour of hand pollination.

The fruit is a good size with relatively few seeds, and is effectively seedless when not pollinated. As a named selection it is protected by Plant Breeder Rights. For growers it combines large, sweet fruit with reliable natural fruit set, which is the main reason it is grown as a commercial and home garden cultivar.

3 sources
  • Atemoya - Hortiforum
  • Specialty Produce: Australian Custard Apples
  • Paxton Prolific Custard Apple - Daleys Fruit

Pink Mammoth

Pink Mammoth (also spelled Pink's Mammoth or Pinks Mammoth) was the first custard apple cultivar grown in Australia, named after Mr Pink, the man who introduced it. It is an atemoya, the sugar apple x cherimoya hybrid, and is prized as a premium variety: the fruit is among the largest of any custard apple, very large and virtually seedless with rich, melting flesh.

Quality comes at the cost of convenience. Pink Mammoth is highly self incompatible, so it produces few flowers when young and needs hand pollination to set a commercial crop and well shaped fruit. Yields run lower than African Pride, but it earns top marks for size and flavour, which is why it remains a benchmark for premium fruit despite the extra work.

3 sources
  • Custard Apple Pollination, Archives RFC Australia (Growables)
  • Custard Apple Farming in Australia: Varieties
  • Custard Apple - Daleys Fruit

Pinks Mammoth

Pinks Mammoth (also spelled Pink's Mammoth or Pink Mammoth) was the first custard apple cultivar grown in Australia, named after Mr Pink, the man who introduced it. It is an atemoya, the sugar apple x cherimoya hybrid, and is prized as a premium variety: the fruit is among the largest of any custard apple, very large and virtually seedless with rich, melting flesh.

Quality comes at the cost of convenience. Pinks Mammoth is highly self incompatible, so it produces few flowers when young and needs hand pollination to set a commercial crop and well shaped fruit. Yields run lower than African Pride, but it earns top marks for size and flavour, which is why it remains a benchmark for premium fruit despite the extra work.

3 sources
  • Custard Apple Pollination, Archives RFC Australia (Growables)
  • Custard Apple Farming in Australia: Varieties
  • Custard Apple - Daleys Fruit

Tropic Sun

Tropic Sun is a relatively recent Australian atemoya custard apple selection, released as a compact, home-garden option. It has a semi-dwarf habit (roughly two-thirds normal size and prunable to about 2 metres), which sets it apart from the very large traditional commercial trees like Pink's Mammoth.

Tropic Sun is grown as a self-pollinating variety on dwarfing rootstock. Its ripe fruit has sweet, creamy pulp and a moderate seed count, reported as a few more seeds than Pink's Mammoth but well below African Pride. It suits warm tropical to subtropical regions along Australia's eastern seaboard.

3 sources
  • Sub-Tropical Fruit Club of Qld, Custard Apple Tips
  • Specialty Produce: Australian Custard Apples
  • Dwarf Custard Apple Tropic Sun - Daleys Fruit

Dragon Fruit

9 varieties (back to top)

American Beauty

American Beauty is a Hylocereus guatemalensis type dragon fruit that was introduced from Guatemala to Florida and then to California. The fruit has reddish to pink skin with green fins and a thin rind, over soft, juicy pulp that bruises easily.

Its standout feature is the deep magenta to fuchsia flesh, coloured by betacyanin, the same pigment found in beetroot. The flavour is rated very good for the type, and it is self-fertile, so one plant can set fruit on its own.

3 sources
  • UC ANR San Diego County Small Farms, Hylocereus guatemalensis (American Beauty)
  • Gardener's Path, Best Dragon Fruit Varieties
  • Cal Poly Pomona Nursery, Dragon Fruit Variety Resource

Dark Star

Dark Star is a Hylocereus hybrid (H. guatemalensis crossed with H. undatus) that traces to the California breeding work of Paul Thomson. It carries deep purple flesh and dark pink toned skin with long, thin fins, which suit its name.

The flavour is well regarded, with mild grape-like undertones. Dark Star is self-sterile, meaning it needs pollen from a compatible variety to set fruit, so it is best grown alongside another dragon fruit.

2 sources
  • Gardener's Path, Best Dragon Fruit Varieties
  • Cal Poly Pomona Nursery, Dragon Fruit Variety Resource

Delight

Delight is a Hylocereus hybrid (H. undatus crossed with H. guatemalensis) associated with the California breeding work of Paul Thomson. The fruit is medium sized with light pink flesh, the feature that gives the variety its name.

It is a sweet, flavourful cultivar and is self-fertile, so a single plant can set fruit without another pollinator. That makes it an easier choice than the self-sterile purple fleshed hybrids.

2 sources
  • Gardener's Path, Best Dragon Fruit Varieties
  • Cal Poly Pomona Nursery, Dragon Fruit Variety Resource

Purple Haze

Purple Haze is a Hylocereus hybrid (H. guatemalensis crossed with H. undatus) developed by Paul Thomson in California, where it is also known as the 5-S seedling. The fruit is large with pink skin and green fins.

Its defining trait is the bright purple, sweet flesh, with a flavour carrying grape and kiwi notes. Purple Haze is self-sterile, so it needs pollen from another variety to set fruit reliably.

3 sources
  • Gardener's Path, Best Dragon Fruit Varieties
  • Cal Poly Pomona Nursery, Dragon Fruit Variety Resource
  • Exotic Plants in Southern California, Purple Haze

Red

Red dragon fruit refers to the red and magenta-fleshed types, mainly Hylocereus costaricensis (also classed as Selenicereus costaricensis), the red-fleshed pitaya. It has red skin and deeply coloured red to magenta flesh, sweeter and more intensely flavoured than the white-fleshed type, with tangy notes. The vivid pigment comes from betalains and readily stains.

A key difference from the common white type is pollination. Red-fleshed plants are generally self-sterile and rely on cross pollination with a genetically distinct plant, or hand pollination, to set a good crop. The species is native to Central America and northwestern South America.

3 sources
  • Pitaya - Wikipedia
  • Hylocereus costaricensis - Spicy Exotics
  • Pitaya: dragon fruit - NT Government

Sugar Dragon

Sugar Dragon, also coded S8, is a small magenta-fleshed dragon fruit prized as one of the sweetest selections. The skin is red with small dark fins, and the purplish-red flesh is semi-firm and succulent with berry or rosewater notes. It was bred by Paul Thomson in California from a cross of the Houghton and Rixford varieties.

Unlike many red and magenta-fleshed cactus fruit, Sugar Dragon is self-fertile and sets fruit without hand pollination. Its compact size and intense sweetness have made it a popular collector and small-scale commercial variety rather than a mass-market export type.

2 sources
  • S8 Sugar Dragon - Exotic Fruits and Vegetables
  • Sugar Dragon Fruit - Specialty Produce

Vietnamese White

Vietnamese White is a white-fleshed dragon fruit of the species Hylocereus undatus, the most commonly grown commercial type. It has vibrant pink to red skin with green-tipped scales and firm white flesh dotted with tiny edible black seeds. The flavour is mild and gently sweet, lighter and less intense than the red and magenta-fleshed types.

It is self-fertile, setting fruit without hand or cross pollination, which makes it a reliable, easy cropper. The variety traces to Vietnam, which is the lead exporter of dragon fruit, so this white-flesh, pink-skin type is the one most often seen in shops and export markets.

2 sources
  • Pitaya - Wikipedia
  • Dragon Fruit Varieties Selection Guide - Zenyr Garden

White

White dragon fruit is the species Hylocereus undatus (also classed as Selenicereus undatus), the white-fleshed pitaya and the most commonly seen dragon fruit. It has bright pink, crimson or red skin with fleshy green scales, and white flesh studded with many small black seeds. The flavour is mild and lightly sweet, gentler than the red and magenta-fleshed types.

This is the standard commercial and supermarket type, widely grown in Vietnam and other tropical regions. White-fleshed selections are typically self-fertile, setting fruit without hand pollination, which contributes to their reliability and broad cultivation.

3 sources
  • Pitaya - Wikipedia
  • Pitaya: dragon fruit - NT Government
  • Dragon Fruit Varieties Selection Guide - Zenyr Garden

Yellow

Yellow dragon fruit is usually Selenicereus megalanthus, a different species from the common red and pink skinned types (Hylocereus). It is native to northern South America and carries a bumpy yellow skin dotted with small spines, which sets it apart visually from the smooth finned fruit of other dragon fruit. The flesh is white with small black seeds.

Its main distinction is flavour. The white flesh is notably sweet, often described as the sweetest of the dragon fruit, with a mild, pleasant taste. Yellow pitayas are also self-fertile, so a single plant can set fruit without cross pollination, unlike many purple fleshed Hylocereus hybrids.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia, Selenicereus megalanthus
  • Gardener's Path, Best Dragon Fruit Varieties

Feijoa

9 varieties (back to top)

Apollo

Apollo is one of the small number of feijoa cultivars that is self fertile, so it can fruit on its own, though it sets bigger and heavier crops when planted with another variety. That self fertility, shared with Unique, sets it apart from most feijoas, which need a pollinator partner.

It is a mid to late season variety bearing large fruit, some uneven in shape, with smooth, thin, light green skin that bruises easily. The flesh is mild, sweet and juicy with good eating quality, making it a popular, productive choice.

3 sources
  • Auckland Botanic Gardens: Acca sellowiana Apollo
  • Feijoa Feijoa: Cultivars
  • NZ Feijoa Growers Association: Varieties

Kaiteri

Kaiteri is a New Zealand cultivar bred by Roy Hart. It is prized as an early cropper that carries large, smooth fruit on a vigorous tree, starting to ripen before the popular Unique but continuing to crop for longer. The flesh is sweet, and the skin is thin enough to eat. It is partially self-fertile, so it will set some fruit alone, but yields are noticeably better with a second variety planted nearby as a pollinator.

Among the larger feijoas in cultivation, Kaiteri is sold as a standout for size and flavour, which is why it is propagated under exclusive arrangements and plant variety protection in New Zealand.

4 sources
  • NZ Feijoa Growers Association, Varieties
  • Feijoa Feijoa, Cultivars
  • Waimea Nurseries, Feijoa Variety Brochure (commercial growers)
  • One Green World, Kaiteri Pineapple Guava

Mammoth

Mammoth is a New Zealand selection raised from seedlings of the Choiceana cultivar, and it is named for its relatively large fruit. The fruit is round to oval with a thick, somewhat wrinkled skin, and is smoother and more rounded than Triumph. It is self fertile but bears larger fruit when cross pollinated.

It matures early to mid season and tends to be softer than Triumph, so it needs careful handling. The flesh is moderately soft, juicy and grainy, with good flavour, though fruit can show a high incidence of empty segments inside.

5 sources
  • Wikipedia: Feijoa
  • Feijoa Feijoa: Cultivars
  • California Rare Fruit Growers: Feijoa Fruit Facts
  • NZ Feijoa Growers Association: Varieties
  • RFCA Archive: Feijoa (1983)

Nazematze

Nazematze is a vendor spelling of Nazemetz, a Californian cultivar raised in San Diego by Alexander Nazemetz. It is self-fertile, so a single tree will set a crop, but it bears most heavily when cross-pollinated, and it works well as a pollinator for other varieties. The fruit is large and pear-shaped with moderately thin side walls, and the flavour and quality are rated excellent. It ripens late in the season, from roughly late October into mid-December in California (autumn to early winter).

Its standout quirk is the flesh. Unlike many feijoas, the translucent, sweet pulp does not darken after the fruit is cut or as it ripens, instead holding its clear colour. The cultivar is also sold under the correct spelling Nazemetz and as Nazimet.

2 sources
  • California Rare Fruit Growers, Feijoa Fruit Facts
  • Feijoa Feijoa, Cultivars

Nazemetz

Nazemetz is a Californian cultivar raised in San Diego by Alexander Nazemetz. It is self-fertile, so a single tree will set a crop, but it bears most heavily when cross-pollinated, and it works well as a pollinator for other varieties. The fruit is large and pear-shaped with moderately thin side walls, and the flavour and quality are rated excellent. It ripens late in the season, from roughly late October into mid-December in California (autumn to early winter).

Its standout quirk is the flesh. Unlike many feijoas, the translucent, sweet pulp does not darken after the fruit is cut or as it ripens, instead holding its clear colour. The name is sometimes listed under variant spellings such as Nazimet or Nazematze, but these refer to the same cultivar.

2 sources
  • California Rare Fruit Growers, Feijoa Fruit Facts
  • Feijoa Feijoa, Cultivars

Nazimet

Nazimet is a vendor spelling of Nazemetz, a Californian cultivar raised in San Diego by Alexander Nazemetz. It is self-fertile, so a single tree will set a crop, but it bears most heavily when cross-pollinated, and it works well as a pollinator for other varieties. The fruit is large and pear-shaped with moderately thin side walls, and the flavour and quality are rated excellent. It ripens late in the season, from roughly late October into mid-December in California (autumn to early winter).

Its standout quirk is the flesh. Unlike many feijoas, the translucent, sweet pulp does not darken after the fruit is cut or as it ripens, instead holding its clear colour. The cultivar is also sold under the correct spelling Nazemetz and as Nazematze.

2 sources
  • California Rare Fruit Growers, Feijoa Fruit Facts
  • Feijoa Feijoa, Cultivars

Triumph

Triumph is a New Zealand selection raised from seedlings of the Choiceana cultivar, and it became the variety most favoured for commercial plantings there. The fruit is medium sized and oval, often with uneven halves, and carries an uneven but firm skin that handles and stores better than softer types.

It ripens late in the season and keeps well. The flesh is moderately soft, juicy and slightly gritty or grainy, with a sharp, tangy flavour that gives it a strong reputation for eating quality. Triumph is also a useful pollinator for other late cultivars such as Mammoth.

3 sources
  • Feijoa Feijoa: Cultivars
  • NZ Feijoa Growers Association: Varieties
  • RFCA Archive: Feijoa (1983)

Unique

Unique is a New Zealand selection notable as one of the very few feijoas that is reliably self fertile, so a single tree will set a crop without a pollinator partner. Most other feijoa cultivars need a second variety nearby for good fruit set, which makes Unique a practical choice for small gardens with room for only one plant.

It ripens very early in the feijoa season and stores well. The medium fruit has a distinctive split at the calyx base, with smooth, soft, juicy flesh and a mildly aromatic, sub acid flavour that many find less tart than older varieties.

4 sources
  • Wikipedia: Feijoa
  • Waimea Nurseries: Feijoa Unique
  • NZ Feijoa Growers Association: Varieties
  • Feijoa Feijoa: Cultivars

White Goose

White Goose is a New Zealand cultivar, bred by A. and J. Firmin and protected under plant variety rights. It is an early-season variety, cropping from around mid-autumn and carrying on for several weeks. The fruit is large, with semi-smooth, light green skin and smooth, soft flesh that is sweet, juicy and mildly aromatic.

It is partially self-fertile, setting some fruit on its own, but crops are much heavier when another variety is planted nearby as a pollinator. The variety is propagated under licence rather than from seed, which is why it appears in catalogues with a trademark and rights notice.

4 sources
  • ANFIC, White Goose Feijoa
  • Incredible Edibles, Feijoa White Goose
  • NZ Feijoa Growers Association, Varieties
  • Ross Creek Tropicals, Feijoa White Goose

Fig

12 varieties (back to top)

Black Genoa

Black Genoa is a large, pear-shaped fig with dark purple, almost black skin and deep red flesh. It has a very sweet, rich flavour and good keeping quality, which is why it is one of the leading commercial fresh-fig varieties grown in Australia.

Like most figs it can bear two crops a year, a small early breba crop on old wood and a heavier main crop on new growth, ripening through summer and autumn in Australia.

2 sources
  • Daleys Fruit: Fig Black Genoa
  • Ultimate Backyard: Fig Tree Varieties in Australia

Black Mission

Black Mission, also called Mission or Franciscan, is one of the most historic figs in cultivation. It came from Spain and was planted by Franciscan missionaries at San Diego in 1768, then spread up the California coast as new missions were built. The fruit has a dark purple to almost black skin and a strawberry to amber red interior. A telltale trait is that the skin often cracks or checks at full maturity.

It is a reliable two crop fig: a large, pyriform breba crop early, then a medium sized main crop later in the season. The flavour is rich and distinctly figgy, and the quality is rated excellent both fresh and dried, which made it a commercial mainstay in California. Its sweet, jammy flesh dries well, and it remains a benchmark dark fig for fresh eating.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Mission fig
  • Specialty Produce: Black Mission Figs
  • Fig Database: Black Mission

Bourjassotte Noire

Bourjassotte Noire is an old Mediterranean fig whose name traces to Burjassot near Valencia in Spain, though it has been grown around the western Mediterranean for centuries. In France it is the variety behind the Sollies figs of Provence, which carry a Protected Designation of Origin (PDO). The skin is a dark purple to near black with violet tones at the neck and a bluish bloom, and it is ribbed, often checking and cracking as it ripens.

Inside, the flesh is a deep red, dense and tender. The flavour is rich and sweet with a balancing tartness, giving it a floral, berry like character that fig growers prize. The skin cracking at full ripeness is a classic sign of a sweet, well ripened fig. It is grown mainly as a premium fresh eating fig, the same fruit marketed in France as the Sollies violette.

2 sources
  • Specialty Produce: Bourjasotte Noire Sollies Figs
  • Fig Database: Bourjassotte Noire

Brown Turkey

Brown Turkey is a popular, adaptable fig whose fruit ripens to a coppery purple-brown skin with pink to amber pulp and a mild, sweet flavour. It is a reliable, prolific cropper often grown both commercially and in home gardens, and is sometimes sold as Texas Everbearing.

It can produce two crops in a season, an early breba crop and a later main crop, and the fruit is good eaten fresh or used for cooking and preserving.

2 sources
  • NC State Extension: Ficus carica Brown Turkey
  • Daleys Fruit: Fig Brown Turkey

Cape White

Cape White is a very old white fig long associated with South Africa, where it is one of the oldest cultivars and the name by which it is best known. It is a small to medium, early ripening fig with green skin that turns only slightly yellow when fully ripe, and pale flesh (described as cream to light straw). In Australia it is grown across the eastern state fruit collections.

It is an early variety, ripening from about January in the southern hemisphere summer, well ahead of many other figs. The fruit has a sweet, delicate flavour and is especially popular for jam, while also being used fresh and for drying.

3 sources
  • Giving Trees (South Africa): Cape White
  • SouthAfrica.co.za: Fig Varieties
  • The Food Forest: Figs fact sheet

Celeste

Celeste, widely known as the Sugar fig, is a small to medium fig long grown across the south eastern United States. It is often listed under the synonyms Celestial, Malta and Sugar fig. The skin ripens to a purplish bronze over light brown, and the flesh is rose to pink. Its standout trait is a tightly closed eye that resists insects and souring, which suits humid climates and keeps the fruit from splitting.

Flavour is the other draw. Celeste is intensely sweet and honeyed, smooth and rich, which is exactly why it earned the Sugar fig name. It is a common fig and self pollinating, so it sets fruit without a wasp. The crop ripens early, from late summer, making it one of the first figs of the season. Good eaten fresh or made into preserves.

3 sources
  • NC State Extension: Ficus carica 'Celeste'
  • Specialty Produce: Celeste Figs
  • Fig Boss: The Celeste Fig

Panache

Panache, also sold as Tiger or Striped fig, is a striking variegated cultivar. It is a sport (a natural mutation) of the Spanish fig Bordissot Blanca, recorded in fig literature as far back as Risso in 1826. The unripe fruit shows alternating green and yellow lengthwise stripes, the feature that earns it the tiger name. As the fig ripens the stripes soften and the skin yellows, while inside the flesh is strawberry pink.

On flavour, Panache is prized for a rich, sweet, berry taste often likened to raspberry or strawberry jam. It is a unifera type, meaning it makes a single main crop and no early breba crop. The fruit ripens late in the season, so it does best where summers are long. The striped skin and jammy red flesh make it a sought after fresh eating fig rather than a drying variety.

2 sources
  • Fig Database: Panache
  • Fig Boss: The Panache Fig

Preston Prolific

Preston Prolific is an Australian fig thought to be a seedling of Black Genoa that originated in Victoria, and it has been sold in Australia for decades. It is a vigorous tree with large leaves bearing large fruit on a short stalk. The skin is green when immature and changes from straw to a purplish brown when fully ripe.

Inside, the flesh is thick and juicy, described as creamy white to amber and sometimes tinted red. It is a late maturing variety, ripening through summer into autumn, and is used fresh as well as for jam and drying.

3 sources
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Preston Prolific Fig
  • The Food Forest: Figs fact sheet
  • Fig Database: Genoa White

Violette de Bordeaux

Violette de Bordeaux is an old French fig, documented in France since the 1700s and also known as Negronne, Petite Aubique and Petite Negri, among other names. Genetic testing has shown that figs sold as Beer's Black and Vista are synonyms of it. The fruit is small to medium and pear shaped, with dark purple to black skin and deep red, jam like flesh.

It is one of the most highly rated figs for flavour, rich and sweet with a berry character that gives its name to the Bordeaux taste category. It produces an excellent early (breba) crop as well as a main crop, and despite a dwarf reputation the tree is actually vigorous and upright with deeply cut leaves.

2 sources
  • Fig Database: Violette de Bordeaux
  • Fig Boss: Violette de Bordeaux review

White Adriatic

White Adriatic is an old Italian fig long known in its homeland as Verdone (greenfinch) for its green skin, and also called Strawberry Fig and Fico di Fragola. The fruit keeps a light greenish to yellow skin even when ripe, but splits open to reveal striking strawberry-red flesh. The flavour is balanced and sweet with light berry notes, prized when fully ripened on the tree.

Adriatic has long been the principal drying fig of California, valued for its high sugar and concentrated flavour when dehydrated, and it also makes excellent jam. In Australia it is grown as an early variety suited to cooler areas, carrying a light early (breba) crop ahead of the main crop, with fruit ripening around February in southern districts.

3 sources
  • Fig Database: Adriatic
  • Specialty Produce: Adriatic Figs
  • The Food Forest: Figs fact sheet

White Genoa

White Genoa is an old, widely grown white fig described in literature as far back as the 18th century and imported into California in the 19th century. It carries greenish to yellow skin and amber flesh, often tinged strawberry, with the early (breba) crop showing lighter, more strawberry pulp than the main crop. In Australia it is valued as an adaptable variety that suits cooler and temperate areas.

It typically produces two crops where summers are warm enough, a smaller early crop followed by the main crop, in Australia often a light December crop with more fruit in February and March. The flavour is mild and sweet, and the fruit is good both fresh and for drying, though large soft fruit can be prone to spoilage.

2 sources
  • Fig Database: Genoa White
  • The Food Forest: Figs fact sheet

Yellow Excel

Excel is a yellow fig that came out of the University of California fig breeding program at Riverside, the work associated with Ira Condit and colleagues, and was introduced in 1975. It is sometimes listed as a Kadota hybrid. The fruit is medium sized with yellow to greenish yellow skin and amber pulp, and it carries a small, tight eye. That small eye plus a tough skin make it notably resistant to cracking and splitting, even in adverse weather.

Flavour is sweet and rich, and Excel is regarded as one of the better flavoured yellow figs and a more dependable cropper than the Kadota it resembles. The split resistance and dense flesh make it a genuine all purpose fig, good fresh and also suited to drying and canning.

2 sources
  • Fig Database: Excel
  • OC Fruit: Figs in Coastal Southern California

Finger Lime

5 varieties (back to top)

Alstonville

Alstonville is a selected, named cultivar of the finger lime (Citrus australasica), the thorny rainforest citrus native to the coastal border region of Queensland and New South Wales. It is one of the green-fleshed selections and was among the earliest finger limes available commercially, grown grafted for reliable cropping.

The cylindrical fruit has dark green to greenish-black skin and pale green flesh made up of caviar-like juice vesicles (pearls) that burst with a tangy, lime-like flavour. The pearls are mostly seedless and prized as a fresh garnish and in preserves.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Citrus australasica
  • Sustainable Gardening Australia: Finger Limes
  • Daleys Fruit: Finger Lime Alstonville

Byron Sunrise

Byron Sunrise is a named Australian finger lime cultivar held in the UC Riverside citrus collection, where it is recorded as one of a group of finger lime cultivars selected by Judy Viola (or colleagues) and used as a parent for further open-pollinated selections such as Rubirosa and Ruby My Dear.

It is grown chiefly for its colour: nursery sources consistently describe dark maroon skin and bright red to orange-red pearls, the sunrise tones that give the selection its name. The flesh is reported as comparatively mild and low in acidity, which suits it as an ornamental garnish for seafood, desserts and drinks.

3 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection, UC Riverside - Rubirosa finger lime (CRC4296)
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection, UC Riverside - finger lime (CRC4295)
  • Daleys Fruit - Grafted Finger Lime Byron Sunrise

Judy's Everbearing

Judy's Everbearing is a prolific finger lime selected by Judy Viola (or colleagues). Its name reflects an unusually long cropping habit: it carries a heavy crop from summer to autumn and produces fruit sporadically through the rest of the year, where most finger limes fruit in a single tighter window. The dark fruit holds clear, light green to pink pearls with few seeds.

It is one of the cultivars used in peer-reviewed chemotype work, where its peel oil was characterised by a distinctive limonene, citronellal and isomenthone profile, a combination not previously reported in other citrus. The aromatic lemon-lime pearls are used as for any finger lime, with seafood, in drinks, or in place of lemon and lime.

4 sources
