  • Gardening With Angus - Citrus australasica 'Judy's Everbearing'
  • Delort & Jaquier, Comparative analysis of three Australian finger lime cultivars (Phytochemistry, PubMed 25468539)
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection, UC Riverside - finger lime (CRC4295)
  • Daleys Fruit - Finger Lime Judy's Everbearing

Pink Ice

Pink Ice is one of the first commercialised finger lime varieties. It produces red to burgundy coloured fruit with clear to pink pearls of juice inside, giving a striking colour contrast when the fruit is opened.

Its distinctive draw is aroma: the pearls carry a scent likened to ruby grapefruit, which makes Pink Ice popular for cocktails, drinks and desserts as well as savoury dishes. Fruit is borne from summer to autumn and is ready when it comes away easily from the branch.

2 sources
  • Gardening With Angus - Citrus australasica 'Pink Ice'
  • Garden Express - Finger Lime Pink Ice

Rainforest Pearl

Rainforest Pearl is a pink-fleshed finger lime classified as Citrus australasica var. sanguinea, selected for its pink pulp, ease of propagation and ornamental garden value. The aromatic, finger-shaped fruit has skin that is green tinged with red, and inside sit small pearls of tangy juice with a pink cast.

Fruit is produced in autumn (late summer to autumn on the NSW north coast). Plants are usually grafted to make them hardier and more tolerant of frost and dry conditions, forming an upright tree. The tangy pearls suit seafood, Asian cooking and cocktails.

2 sources
  • Gardening With Angus - Citrus australasica var. sanguinea 'Rainforest Pearl'
  • Australian Native Plants Society - Citrus australasica

Grape

11 varieties (back to top)

Autumn Royal

Autumn Royal is a black seedless table grape bred by David Ramming and Ron Tarailo at the USDA-ARS lab in California from the cross Autumn Black by C74-1, and released in 1996. It was developed to extend the table grape season into late autumn.

The berries are large and ovaloid with purple to black skin, among the bigger seedless table grapes. It is a late maturing variety with firm, crisp flesh and a sweet, mild flavour.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Autumn Royal
  • Plantgrape (IFV / INRAE): Autumn Royal table grape variety

Black Muscat

Black Muscat is another name for Muscat Hamburg (Muscat of Hamburg), a black skinned Vitis vinifera grape. It arose from a cross of Schiava Grossa (also known as Frankenthal) and Muscat of Alexandria, raised by head gardener Seward Snow at Wrest Park, Bedfordshire, England, around 1850. The dark, ellipsoid berries grow in medium to large bunches with a moderately thick skin.

Its defining trait is a pronounced muscat flavoured pulp, aromatic and sweet. The variety is dual purpose, grown as a table grape and used for wine, including richly coloured, aromatic dessert wines, plus sparkling wine and grape juice. Authoritative ampelography notes it has relatively low sugar and colour potential for red winemaking.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Black Muscat
  • Plantgrape (IFV-INRAE): Muscat de Hambourg
  • Wine-Searcher: Schiava grape variety

Chambourcin

Chambourcin is a French American interspecific hybrid red wine grape, produced by Joannes Seyve in France and made available in 1963. It carries several North American Vitis species in its background, which gives it good tolerance to fungal disease, useful in humid climates. This is a relatively modern variety compared with the classic European grapes.

It is a teinturier grape, meaning the juice is pink to red rather than the clear juice of most black grapes, so it yields a deeply coloured, aromatic red wine. In Australia it is grown in New South Wales, including the Hunter Valley, where it is often blended with Shiraz to add colour and depth.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Chambourcin
  • Winetraveler: Chambourcin grape profile

Crimson Seedless

Crimson Seedless is a red seedless table grape bred by the USDA-ARS grape program at Fresno, California, from an Emperor cross, and released to industry in 1989. It is one of the most widely planted table grapes in California and is also a major variety in Australia.

The berries are firm and crisp with a sweet, fairly neutral flavour and a bright to brick red skin, sometimes showing greenish streaks. It is one of the latest ripening seedless varieties, maturing in mid to late autumn, and is valued for its long shelf life and good shipping.

2 sources
  • USDA ARS: 100th Anniversary of Grapevine Breeding Research Program
  • Australian Table Grape Association: Crimson Seedless

Flame Seedless

Flame Seedless is a red seedless table grape developed by the USDA-ARS grape breeding program in California and grown by ARS scientists from 1973. Red seedless grapes were essentially unknown to US consumers before the Flame release, making it a market pioneer.

It produces large clusters of medium to large round red berries with firm, crisp flesh and a sweet flavour. It is a complex Vitis vinifera hybrid (its parentage includes Thompson Seedless and Cardinal) and is one of the most popular red seedless table grapes grown.

2 sources
  • USDA ARS: 100th Anniversary of Grapevine Breeding Research Program
  • Wikipedia: Flame Seedless

Gordo Blanco

Gordo Blanco is the Australian name for Muscat of Alexandria, one of the oldest grape varieties still grown. It is a white (yellow-green) seeded grape carrying a strong, sweet muscat aroma, and is prized as an aromatic all-rounder.

It is a true multipurpose variety, eaten fresh as a table grape, dried (its dried form is sold in Australia as the Lexia raisin), and used for wine. In Australia it is grown for commercial winemaking, often used in Moscato style and to lift the muscat character of blends.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Muscat of Alexandria
  • Wine Grape Variety Selector: Muscat Gordo Blanco

Isabella Black

Isabella is an old American grape derived from Vitis labrusca (the fox grape), a natural hybrid with Vitis vinifera. It was popularised in the early 1800s and is named after Isabella Gibbs of South Carolina, where it was recorded around 1816. DNA analysis published in 2023 identified its labrusca parent as the cultivar Ives.

When ripe the skin is dark purple, almost black, over tender green-yellow flesh, and it is a slip-skin grape (the skin separates easily from the pulp). It carries the distinctive musky, grapey foxy aroma of labrusca grapes and is used for table fruit, juice and wine.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Isabella (grape)
  • Wikipedia: Vitis labrusca

Red Globe

Red Globe is a large, seeded red table grape bred in California by H.P. Olmo and A.T. Koyama at the University of California, and released in 1981. It is one of the most widely planted table grapes in the world.

The berries are round and among the largest of any table grape, with firm, crisp flesh, a thick skin carrying a waxy bloom, and a mild, sweet, neutral flavour. The large berry size and good shipping and storage tolerance make it popular for fresh eating.

3 sources
  • Plantgrape (IFV / INRAE): Red Globe table grape variety
  • Wikipedia: Red Globe
  • Specialty Produce: Red Globe Grapes

Thompson Seedless

Thompson Seedless is the same grape as Sultanina and the Sultana, an ancient white (pale green) variety that traces to Asia Minor, in the region now covered by Turkey and Iran. The Thompson Seedless name honours William Thompson, an early Californian grower credited with introducing the variety, and US federal regulations treat the two names as synonymous. The berries are oval and genuinely seedless.

Often called a three way grape, it is used as a table grape, dried into raisins, and used in winemaking. It dominates Californian production, accounting for the large majority of raisin output, and as a wine grape it is known for a sweet, mild, low acid character rather than strong flavour.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Sultana (grape)
  • Encyclopaedia Britannica: Thompson Seedless grape

Waltham Cross

Waltham Cross is the Australian name for the white grape known internationally as Dattier de Beyrouth (also Afus Ali and Rosaki). The variety originates from the Middle East, possibly Lebanon, and its roughly 220 synonyms reflect its great age and wide distribution. It is a seeded grape, not a seedless one.

It is a white multi purpose grape, used as a fresh table grape and also for drying and winemaking. In Australia it is a leading white multi purpose variety: together with Sunglo it makes up about 85 percent of the area planted to white multi purpose grapes in South Australia.

3 sources
  • wein.plus Lexicon: Waltham Cross
  • wein.plus Lexicon: Afus Ali
  • Vinehealth Australia: Multi-purpose and table grape insights

Zante Currant

Zante currant is the dried fruit of Black Corinth, a tiny seedless Vitis vinifera grape native to Greece. The berries are very small, in the wild smaller than a pea, and they are easy to sun dry into the dark, intensely flavoured dried currants of commerce. Despite the name, the grape is unrelated to the Ribes shrubs that bear blackcurrants and redcurrants.

The name comes from the Anglo French raisins de Corinthe (grapes of Corinth) and the Ionian island of Zakynthos (Zante), once the major exporter. The grape has a long recorded history, described by Pliny the Elder in 75 AD, and Greece still produces about 80 percent of the world's Zante currants.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Zante currant
  • Specialty Produce: Champagne (Black Corinth) Grapes

Grapefruit

8 varieties (back to top)

Flame

Flame is a red grapefruit that arose as a sport of Ruby Red in the Houston, Texas grove of C. Henderson, with the final selection made by C. J. Hearn and the variety released in 1987. It has a smooth yellow rind, usually with a pink blush, and tender, juicy flesh whose internal colour is almost as dark as Star Ruby, making it one of the deepest-coloured grapefruits available.

The red pigmentation comes from lycopene, the carotenoid responsible for the colour of red grapefruit flesh. Flame is a mid to late-season variety (ripening around February to June at Riverside) that holds well on the tree, though the internal colour fades somewhat when held past maturity. It is reported to be more cold-tolerant than Star Ruby.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Flame grapefruit
  • Pigmentation and color comparison of Ruby Red and Star Ruby grapefruit juice, Journal of Agricultural and Food Chemistry

Marsh

Marsh originated as a chance seedling planted around 1860 near Lakeland, Florida, and was named after C. M. Marsh, who acquired the Lakeland Nursery and propagated it. It was the first seedless grapefruit to be promoted commercially and went on to become the leading variety planted in Florida and around the world.

The fruit is round with a smooth, pale to light yellow rind and tender, very juicy, buff-coloured (white) flesh with few or no seeds. Its flavour is good though milder than the older seedy grapefruits. At Riverside it ripens roughly January to May.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Frost Marsh grapefruit
  • Specialty Produce: Marsh Ruby Grapefruit

Oro Blanco

Oro Blanco (Oroblanco) is not a true grapefruit but a triploid hybrid bred at UC Riverside by James W. Cameron and Robert K. Soost. The original cross, made in 1958, paired an acidless pummelo with a seedy white grapefruit, and the selection was released in 1980. Its triploid genetics make it essentially seedless, and the flesh is very pale yellow.

Its defining trait is flavour: Oro Blanco is mild and sweet with low acid and lacks the bitterness usually associated with grapefruit, which led to its Sweetie brand name in some markets. A notable quirk is that the rind stays greenish-yellow well after the fruit is mature, so a green peel does not mean it is unripe.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Oroblanco grapefruit hybrid
  • Wikipedia: Oroblanco

Rio Red

Rio Red is a deep red-fleshed grapefruit developed in Texas through mutation breeding, by treating budwood (from the Ruby Red line) with thermal neutrons. It was registered in 1984 and is marketed under names such as Rio Star and Ruby-Sweet. Its sister variety Star Ruby, the darkest red, came from irradiated Hudson grapefruit.

The deep red colour of the flesh comes from lycopene, the same pigment that makes tomatoes red, and the variety holds its intense colour better than the older red grapefruits. It is a sweeter, less bitter grapefruit than the classic white types.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Grapefruit
  • Specialty Produce: Rio Red Grapefruit

Ruby Red

Ruby Red (also known as Redblush) originated as a limb sport of Thompson grapefruit found in McAllen, Texas, by A. E. Henninger in 1929 and patented in 1934. It was the first citrus variety ever to receive a plant patent. The flesh is more deeply pigmented than Thompson, ranging from light to medium pink, and the fruit is relatively few-seeded.

Thanks to its attractive rind and flesh colour, Ruby Red rapidly superseded Thompson and became the leading pigmented grapefruit and one of the major commercial grapefruit varieties. Its pink-to-red colour comes from lycopene, the carotenoid that pigments red grapefruit flesh. The variety is so identified with Texas that it was named the official state fruit in 1993.

3 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Redblush grapefruit
  • Wikipedia: Grapefruit
  • Pigmentation and color comparison of Ruby Red and Star Ruby grapefruit juice, Journal of Agricultural and Food Chemistry

Star Ruby

Star Ruby is a product of mutation breeding: it was developed from irradiated Hudson grapefruit seed in the programme of R. A. Hensz at Texas A&I University, Weslaco, Texas. UC Riverside records it as the darkest of the pigmented grapefruits, with very darkly pigmented, juicy flesh that is low-seeded or seedless. The intense red colour comes from lycopene, the carotenoid that gives red grapefruit its flesh colour.

Despite its prized appearance, Star Ruby is harder to grow than other grapefruits, being more sensitive to cold, phytophthora, nutrient deficiencies and herbicides. It has found only limited commercial success for that reason, but when grown well it produces strikingly coloured, nearly seedless fruit.

3 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Star Ruby grapefruit
  • Wikipedia: Grapefruit
  • Pigmentation and color comparison of Ruby Red and Star Ruby grapefruit juice, Journal of Agricultural and Food Chemistry

Thompson's Pink

Thompson (also called Pink Marsh) is a pink-fleshed bud sport of Marsh, discovered in 1913 by S. A. Collins in the W. B. Thompson orchard at Oneco, Florida, and marketed from 1924 by Royal Palms Nurseries. It is historically important as the first seedless pigmented grapefruit, and it gave rise by further bud mutation to the more deeply coloured Redblush (Ruby Red) and Burgundy.

Like its Marsh parent it is seedless or nearly so, with tender, juicy flesh and a flavour similar to Marsh. The flesh is light pink under favourable conditions (though the colour does not carry into the juice) and tends to fade as the fruit holds on the tree or in storage. Redblush, its own deeper-coloured descendant, eventually superseded it commercially.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Marsh Pink (Thompson) grapefruit
  • Wikipedia: Grapefruit

Wheeny

Wheeny is an Australian grapefruit that originated as a chance seedling at Wheeny Creek near Kurrajong, New South Wales, and was named by R. J. Benton, a government citrus specialist. Although grapefruit-like in most respects, the monoembryonic nature of its seeds and other characters suggest it is probably a pummelo hybrid. The flesh is straw-coloured and very juicy, with a distinct, somewhat lemon-like flavour, and the fruit is moderately seedy.

Its standout quirk is cool-climate adaptability. Wheeny has a lower total heat requirement than true grapefruits, ripening satisfactorily in places like New Zealand and coastal southern California that are too cool for ordinary grapefruit. That cold tolerance makes it well suited to subtropical and warm-temperate parts of Australia, and even sheltered cool-temperate gardens.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Wheeny grapefruit
  • Urban Food Garden: Wheeny Grapefruit

Grumichama

2 varieties (back to top)

Black

The black grumichama is the common, typical form of Eugenia brasiliensis, the Brazilian cherry, a Myrtaceae tree native to coastal southern Brazil. As it ripens the fruit turns from green to bright red and finally to dark purple, almost black, looking much like a dark cherry and carrying a sweet, subacid flavour likened to a Bing cherry with a touch of aromatic resin.

The small globular fruit has soft, melting pulp over one to a few hard seeds, with much of the flavour residing in the thin skin, and persistent green sepals on top. It is mostly eaten fresh but also used for jams, jellies and pies, and trees can fruit more than once a year with the first crop the largest.

3 sources
  • Morton, Fruits of Warm Climates (Purdue NewCROP): Grumichama
  • Useful Tropical Plants: Eugenia brasiliensis
  • University of Florida IFAS Extension (Collier County): Brazilian Cherry or Grumichama

Yellow

The yellow grumichama is a documented colour form of Eugenia brasiliensis, recognised botanically as var. leucocarpa. Where the common form ripens dark purple to black, this variant bears yellow fruit with pale, white fleshed pulp, and it is less common than the usual red to dark fleshed type.

Apart from the fruit colour it is much like the standard grumichama, an evergreen Myrtaceae tree from southern Brazil with glossy leaves, wine red new growth and a sweet, subacid, cherry like flavour. The yellow form is grown as a novelty and collector tree rather than a major commercial type.

3 sources
  • Morton, Fruits of Warm Climates (Purdue NewCROP): Grumichama
  • Useful Tropical Plants: Eugenia brasiliensis
  • Monaco Nature Encyclopedia: Eugenia brasiliensis

Guava

9 varieties (back to top)

China Pear

China Pear is a common guava, Psidium guajava, of the pear-shaped (pyriferum) type. The fruit is large and pear-shaped with light yellow skin, distinguishing it from the rounder apple-guava forms. The pear-shaped guavas are sometimes grouped under the name pear guava.

The flesh is white, thick, creamy and finely textured with small seeds and a smooth, very sweet guava flavour. It is mainly eaten fresh. The tree is a small subtropical to tropical guava that can be pruned to keep it compact.

2 sources
  • RHS: Psidium guajava pyriferum (pear-shaped guava)
  • Tradewinds Fruit: Pear Guava (Psidium guajava ssp pyriferum)

Egyptian Long Neck

Egyptian Long Neck is a common guava, Psidium guajava, named for its distinctive shape. The fruit is oval to pyriform with a bulbous base that tapers to a slender, sometimes curved neck, giving it the long-necked look. The thin, tender skin ripens from green to yellow, occasionally with a faint blush where sun-exposed.

Inside, the flesh is white, thick and juicy, becoming soft and succulent when ripe, with a sweet, mildly tart and floral flavour and a musky aroma. It is enjoyed fresh and also used in juices and preserves. Egyptian guavas are grown commercially in Egypt and valued there for their fragrance and heavy cropping.

2 sources
  • Specialty Produce: Egyptian Guavas
  • Tradewinds Fruit: Egyptian Guava

Hawaiian

Hawaiian guava is a pink fleshed selection of common guava (Psidium guajava), the type behind Hawaii's commercial guava industry. The fruit is large and round with yellow skin and soft, juicy, pink to deep pink flesh. Its aroma is strongly tropical, often compared to passionfruit, with a sweet flavour carrying a mild tang. It is a vigorous, heavy cropper, which is part of why pink Hawaiian lines became the backbone of island plantings.

The pink flesh holds its colour well, so Hawaiian guava is prized as much for processing as for eating fresh. In Hawaii these guavas are widely turned into juice, nectar, jam and jelly, and the rich pink pulp is valued for the colour it lends finished products. Eaten out of hand the fruit is aromatic and soft when fully ripe.

2 sources
  • Lacademie: Types of Guava
  • Daleys Fruit Tree Nursery: Guava Hawaiian

Hawaiian Pink

Hawaiian Pink is a pink-fleshed common guava, Psidium guajava. The medium-sized fruit has smooth skin that ripens to yellow, enclosing juicy, aromatic pink flesh dotted with small seeds. The flavour is sweet and fragrant with a hint of citrus, making it popular for eating fresh as well as for juices, smoothies and desserts.

Like other common guavas it is a small to medium evergreen tree that crops prolifically and can be kept compact for pots. Pink-fleshed guavas such as this are valued for their rich colour and tropical aroma.

2 sources
  • Tradewinds Fruit: Hawaiian Pink Guava
  • Permaculture Haven Australia: Hawaiian Pink Guava

Mexican Cream

Mexican Cream is a white fleshed cultivar of common guava (Psidium guajava), named for its pale yellow skin and soft, creamy interior. The fruit is small, roughly bite sized and round, ripening to a yellowish green exterior with thick, juicy, cream coloured flesh. The texture is soft and succulent, sometimes likened to a ripe pear.

It is noted for being unusually sweet for a guava, with an aromatic flavour that carries notes of strawberry, pineapple, passionfruit and papaya. That sweetness makes it a favourite for eating fresh and for blended drinks, juices and desserts, where its mild creamy pulp needs little added to it.

2 sources
  • Tasting Table: What Makes Mexican Cream Guava Unique
  • Specialty Produce: Mexican Cream Guavas

Red Cherry

Red Cherry guava is the red-fruited form of Psidium cattleianum, also sold as red strawberry guava, purple guava or red cattley guava. This is a different species from the common guava (Psidium guajava). The fruit is small and round, roughly the size of a small plum, ripening through green and mottled shades to a deep wine red or purple. The skin is thin, soft and edible.

Inside, the translucent whitish flesh is juicy and sweet-tart, often described as having one of the finest flavours among guavas, and is studded with hard pale seeds. The fruit is eaten fresh or made into jam and juice. In parts of Australia the species is regarded as a weed where it naturalises.

4 sources
  • Wikipedia: Psidium cattleyanum
  • NSW DPI WeedWise: Cherry guava
  • Green Harvest: Cherry / Strawberry Guava Growing Information
  • Specialty Produce: Strawberry Guavas

Strawberry

Strawberry guava is a different species from common guava: it is Psidium cattleianum, also called cattley guava or cherry guava, a small evergreen tree native to Brazil. It bears small round to pear shaped fruit, around three to four centimetres across, that ripen from green to a dark red. Inside, the whitish flesh is juicy and not mealy, with a sweet tart flavour and a distinct strawberry like aroma. A yellow fruited form, sometimes sold as lemon guava, also exists.

Beyond the fruit, strawberry guava is notorious as an aggressive weed. It has escaped cultivation in many tropical regions and is regarded as one of the most invasive plants in Hawaii, forming dense thickets that crowd out native vegetation. The small fruits are eaten fresh or made into jams and jellies.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS: Psidium cattleianum, Strawberry Guava (ENH-687/ST529)
  • Wikipedia: Psidium cattleyanum

Thai White

Thai White is an apple guava type of common guava (Psidium guajava), widely grown in Thailand and known for its crisp texture. The fruits are large and round with pale green skin and dense, white flesh. Unlike the soft, juicy tropical guavas, this variety stays firm and crunchy even when ripe, closer in bite to an apple, with relatively few seeds clustered toward the centre.

The flavour is mild, sweet and lightly tart with a fresh floral note rather than the heavy musky aroma of softer guavas. In Thailand and across Southeast Asia it is typically eaten green and crunchy, sliced and dipped in a mix of chilli, salt or sugar, and sold as a street snack. It also suits fresh eating and fruit salads.

2 sources
  • Exotic Fruits and Vegetables: Thai Guava
  • Specialty Produce: Thai Guavas / Bangkok Giant White Guavas

Yellow Cherry

Yellow Cherry guava is the yellow-fruited form of Psidium cattleianum, the same species as red cherry guava but distinct from common guava (Psidium guajava). It goes by several names, including yellow strawberry guava, yellow cattley guava and lemon guava, and in Hawaii it is called waiawi. The small, marble-sized fruit ripens to a golden yellow with thin, soft skin.

The flesh is juicy and fragrant with a sweet, slightly acidic flavour that some compare to a mix of lemon and passionfruit, and it carries small seeds. The yellow form tends to bear heavily and generally produces somewhat larger fruit than the red form. It is eaten fresh or used for juices, jams and desserts.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Psidium cattleyanum
  • Specialty Produce: Lemon Guavas

Jaboticaba

7 varieties (back to top)

Costada

Costada is a selection of Plinia phitrantha, a jaboticaba species native to the south-eastern Brazilian states of Sao Paulo and Minas Gerais. The name comes from the Portuguese for ribbed, a nod to the fruit's distinctive lengthwise ribs or grooves that set it apart from the smooth, round common jaboticaba. The skin ripens a deep purple-red, and the small fruit holds the sweet, grape-like jaboticaba flavour collectors prize.

It is a compact, bushy grower often sold as a dwarf, which makes it well suited to pots and small gardens. Unlike the famously slow common jaboticaba, this species is reported to be faster growing and earlier to bear, fruiting in around two years in tropical conditions. It remains uncommon in cultivation, so it is mostly a collector's plant rather than a mainstream nursery line.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Plinia phitrantha
  • Trade Winds Fruit: Plinia phitrantha Costada Jaboticaba
  • Daleys Fruit: Jaboticaba Costada (Plinia phitrantha)

Giant yellow

Giant yellow is a large-fruited form of the yellow jaboticaba, Myrciaria glazioviana (also called cabeluda), a Myrtaceae species native to south-eastern Brazil around Rio de Janeiro and Minas Gerais. Unlike the familiar dark purple-black jaboticaba, its round fruit ripens golden-yellow and carries a fine fuzzy, pubescent skin, so the berries look more like large gooseberries. The flavour is sweet and aromatic and is often likened to apricot, with one or two seeds inside.

The 'Giant' selection is valued for fruit larger than the standard yellow form. The plant grows as a large shrub or small tree and is notably precocious for the genus, reported to begin bearing in only a few years while still small, rather than the long wait common jaboticabas are known for.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Myrciaria glazioviana
  • Growables: Cabelluda / Yellow Jaboticaba (Myrciaria glazioviana)
  • Ross Creek Tropicals: Jaboticaba Giant yellow

Grimal

Grimal is a selected large-fruited jaboticaba named after Adolph Grimal, the eccentric fruit collector who established a grove on Big Pine Key in the Florida Keys and propagated it there. It produces purple-black fruit that is noticeably larger than Sabara, with thick pulp and a sweet flavour carrying Concord-grape notes, which is why it is often rated among the best eating jaboticabas.

Compared with Sabara it grows more upright and tends to fruit later in the season, making the two complementary if you want a longer harvest. Like other jaboticabas the fruit is borne directly on the trunk and main branches.

3 sources
  • Florida Fruit Geek: Jaboticaba has some tasty cousins
  • Savour Soil Permaculture: Jaboticaba Plant Profile
  • Edible South Florida: Digging up the Past, Grimal Grove

Phitrantha Costada

Phitrantha Costada is the same ribbed jaboticaba as Costada, listed here under its full botanical name Plinia phitrantha 'Costada'. The species is native to the south-eastern Brazilian states of Sao Paulo and Minas Gerais. 'Costada' is Portuguese for ribbed, describing the fruit's distinctive lengthwise ribs or grooves, a clear point of difference from the smooth, round common jaboticaba. The skin ripens a deep purple-red over the usual sweet, grape-like jaboticaba flesh.

It tends to form a compact, bushy plant, often offered as a dwarf, so it suits pots and smaller gardens. Reports suggest it grows faster and bears earlier than the notoriously slow common jaboticaba, with fruiting in around two years under tropical conditions. It stays an uncommon collector's variety rather than a widely stocked nursery line.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Plinia phitrantha
  • Trade Winds Fruit: Plinia phitrantha Costada Jaboticaba
  • Daleys Fruit: Jaboticaba Costada (Plinia phitrantha)

Red Hybrid

Red Hybrid is a jaboticaba bred from Plinia cauliflora crossed with the red-fruited Plinia aureana. Its standout trait is speed: where ordinary jaboticabas can take six to eight years (or much longer from seed) to bear, the red hybrid often fruits in about three to five years, earning it the nickname Precocious.

The fruit is red rather than the usual purple-black, smaller than purple jaboticaba, with a sweet-tart, grape-like flavour that suits palates used to temperate fruit. Once established it bears repeatedly, with multiple crops through the year, and new growth and honey-scented trunk flowers add ornamental appeal.

3 sources
  • Florida Fruit Geek: Jaboticaba has some tasty cousins
  • Live Better Garden: Red Jaboticaba (Plinia cauliflora x aureana)
  • Daleys Fruit: Jaboticaba Red Hybrid (Plinia cauliflora x aureana)

Sabará

Sabara (Plinia cauliflora) is the classic Brazilian jaboticaba and the most prized, most often planted selection for commercial production. It bears small, thin-skinned, intensely sweet purple-black fruit directly on the trunk and branches, the cauliflorous habit jaboticabas are known for. Sabara flavour is widely treated as the benchmark for the fruit.

It is valued above all for productivity and speed. The tree is precocious and very productive, with reports of up to four flowering and fruiting cycles a year, well ahead of the single-crop, larger-fruited types. That fast, repeat-bearing habit on a compact tree is what makes it the standard choice for both growers and home gardens.

4 sources
  • Growables: Jaboticaba Varieties
  • Trade Winds Fruit: Sabara Jaboticaba
  • Savour Soil Permaculture: Jaboticaba Plant Profile
  • Wikipedia: Jabuticaba

Yellow

The yellow jaboticaba is a different species, Myrciaria glazioviana (known in Brazil as cabeludinha or cabeluda), native to south-eastern Brazil around Rio de Janeiro and Minas Gerais. Unlike the smooth purple jaboticabas, its round fruit ripens yellow to yellow-orange with a distinctive fuzzy, pubescent skin, so it looks more like a hairy gooseberry than a grape.

Flavour is its main draw, a sweet, aromatic, apricot to peach character (with hints of tangerine) wrapped around translucent juicy pulp. The trade-off is small fruit with relatively little flesh, one or two largish seeds, and a slightly tough, resinous skin. It grows as a slow, multi-trunked shrub or small tree.

4 sources
  • Wikipedia: Myrciaria glazioviana
  • Trade Winds Fruit: Yellow Jaboticaba (Myrciaria glazioviana)
  • Growables: Yellow Jaboticaba (Cabelluda)
  • Florida Fruit Geek: Jaboticaba has some tasty cousins

Jackfruit

2 varieties (back to top)

Black Gold

Black Gold is an Australian selection from Queensland, valued for soft, deep orange flesh with a strong, sweet flavour and aroma. The flesh separates from the fruit more easily than in many other cultivars, and the variety carries low latex. It is a late-season type, ripening roughly September to October in the Northern Hemisphere references that document it.

One quirk sets it apart at harvest. The skin spines do not flatten or open as the fruit matures, so judging the right picking time is harder than with cultivars whose spines spread when ripe. The tree is naturally vigorous but responds well to pruning, and is commonly kept to a compact two to two and a half metres, making it a manageable choice for smaller gardens.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS Extension, Jackfruit Growing in the Florida Home Landscape (HS-882/MG370), Crane et al.
  • Fairchild Tropical Botanic Garden Virtual Herbarium, Jackfruit Cultivars

Honey Gold

Honey Gold is an Australian cultivar grown for its firm flesh, which sets it apart from the softer, melting jackfruit types. The flesh is dark yellow to orange with a sweet, rich flavour and aroma, and the bulbs are thick walled with a texture rated as excellent. It is a mid-season variety, ripening around July to August in the Northern Hemisphere references that document it.

It is a relatively compact, small-fruited type. UF/IFAS records the fruit as small to medium-small, in the order of 10 to 12 pounds, which is modest for a species famous for the largest tree-borne fruit in the world. That smaller fruit size, combined with firm flesh and a tree that prunes back readily, makes it a practical pick for home growers who want crisp jackfruit without wrestling a giant.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS Extension, Jackfruit Growing in the Florida Home Landscape (HS-882/MG370), Crane et al.
  • Growables, Jackfruit Varieties (reproducing the UF/IFAS HS-882 selected cultivars table)

Jujube

14 varieties (back to top)

Admiral Wilkes

Admiral Wilkes traces back to a US South Seas expedition of the late 1830s and 1840s, and is one of the very last jujubes to ripen. The elongated, tiger tooth shaped fruit has a good sweet flavour but is best dried or used for processing rather than eaten fresh.

The tree is very vigorous, growing tall and productive with a slightly weeping habit. Because it crops so late (in Australia around March to May), it is useful for extending the jujube harvest into late autumn.

3 sources
  • Trees and Shrubs Online: Ziziphus jujuba
  • Cultivar Guide: Jujube Varieties
  • Daleys Fruit: Jujube Admiral Wilkes

Chico

Chico (also known as GI 7-62) was developed at the USDA plant introduction station at Chico, California, as a seedling related to old Chinese clones. The fruit is medium to large and oblate (round but flattened on the bottom, like a little pumpkin or small apple), often with a rounded or even absent seed.

Chico has a tart sweet, balanced flavour and fine, crisp texture. It is a mid season cultivar with a long harvest period, holding its texture well, and is enjoyed fresh and can also be dried. It is popular in Australia.

3 sources
  • New Mexico State University Jujube Project: Varieties for Fresh Eating
  • Trees and Shrubs Online: Ziziphus jujuba
  • Cultivar Guide: Jujube Varieties

GA866

GA866 is a US selection from the Chico Plant Introduction Station in California, made during the most extensive jujube trials run in the country. The fruit is midsize and elongated, sometimes slightly curved, on an almost thornless tree, and it ripens late in the season.

It is best known for being outstandingly sweet, one of the highest sugar jujubes recorded by New Mexico State University at 36 to 39.5 degrees Brix. The dense, crisp flesh is excellent fresh, and the fruit also dries well into chewy, date like pieces.

3 sources
  • New Mexico State University Jujube Project: Varieties for Fresh Eating
  • Trees and Shrubs Online: Ziziphus jujuba
  • Cultivar Guide: Jujube Varieties

Honey Jar

Honey Jar is a small Chinese cultivar imported to the US in 1995 (its name translates as something close to honey pot). The fruit is short and obovate with thin skin and fine, very juicy flesh, and it is widely regarded as one of the sweetest and juiciest jujubes grown. It is among the earliest to ripen, which makes it useful in regions with short seasons.

The small fruit size limits commercial appeal but suits home growers well. Honey Jar is excellent eaten fresh for its crisp, sweet, juicy bite and can also be dried.

3 sources
  • New Mexico State University Jujube Project: Varieties for Fresh Eating
  • Cultivar Guide: Jujube Varieties
  • Jujube Trees Australia: Cultivars

Lang

Lang is a classic old Chinese cultivar, imported to the US in 1908 from Shanxi Province by plant explorer F.N. Meyer. The fruit is medium to large and pear shaped (pyriform) with dark red brown skin, on an upright, almost thornless tree. It is self fertile, so it can set a crop without a separate pollinator.

Its flavour is mild rather than intense, and Lang is grown above all for drying. Fruit is best harvested at the full red stage and left to dry, which gives chewy, date like results. It remains one of the major commercial drying jujubes.

2 sources
  • New Mexico State University Jujube Project: Varieties for Drying
  • NT Department of Agriculture and Fisheries: Jujube

Li

Li is a Chinese jujube (Ziziphus jujuba) cultivar imported to the United States from China by plant explorer Frank Meyer. It is one of the largest jujube varieties, bearing big, rounded fruit that is among the best for fresh eating.

Eaten fresh while the skin is still reddish-brown, the fruit is crisp and sweet with a texture often compared to an apple. Left to dry, jujubes wrinkle and become chewy and date-like.

3 sources
  • New Mexico State University Extension: Jujube, Chinese Date in New Mexico
  • Specialty Produce: Li Jujube
  • Wikipedia: Jujube

Porterville

Porterville is a Californian seedling selection, originally found at Porterville, California. Its standout trait is the fruit shape: large, unusually lumpy and pumpkin like, with lobes, which makes it one of the more visually distinctive jujubes. It is broadly similar to Chico in shape and taste but larger.

The fruit has very good apple and date flavour and is crisp when green. Porterville ripens early to mid season and suits both fresh eating and drying. The tree is vigorous with moderate spines.

3 sources
  • Trees and Shrubs Online: Ziziphus jujuba
  • Jujube Trees Australia: Cultivars
  • Daleys Fruit: Jujube Porterville

Redlands

Redlands is a Californian selection, found at an old homestead in Redlands, California, and belongs to the Li family of jujubes. It produces large, sweet, round fruit, reportedly even larger than Li, with crisp texture and rich flavour. It is similar to Li and Shanxi Li in shape and taste, though some note a slightly better texture and a hint of acidity that Li lacks.

Redlands ripens mid season and is productive. It is best eaten fresh when the fruit is around half brown, and it also dries well.

3 sources
  • Trees and Shrubs Online: Ziziphus jujuba
  • Daleys Fruit: Jujube Redlands
  • Jujube Trees Australia: Cultivars

Shanxi Li

Shanxi Li originates from Shanxi Province in China and is one of the Li family of jujubes. It is among the largest fruited jujubes, producing big, round to ovoid fruit that is crisp and sweet. It is valued as a fresh eating cultivar and was once a dominant variety in the United States.

Shanxi Li ripens mid season (described as early in some climates, which helps in areas with shorter seasons). The crisp fruit is excellent eaten fresh when about half brown, and it can also be dried.

3 sources
  • New Mexico State University Jujube Project: Varieties for Fresh Eating
  • Trees and Shrubs Online: Ziziphus jujuba
  • Jujube Trees Australia: Cultivars

Si Hong

Si Hong (also spelled Sihong) comes from Sihong County in Jiangsu Province, China. The fruit is medium to large and ovoid to slightly lopsided, with attractive brown red skin, ripening mid season. The flesh is thick, crisp and airy, with an apple like snap when fresh.

It is very sweet with a subtle tang, and is rated among the best jujubes for both fresh eating and drying. New Mexico State University records its sugar at 34 to 36.8 degrees Brix. When dried, the fruit develops fine, attractive wrinkles.

3 sources
  • New Mexico State University Jujube Project: Varieties for Multipurpose Use
  • Specialty Produce: Sihong Jujube
  • Cultivar Guide: Jujube Varieties

Silverhill

Silverhill is a late ripening jujube selection from the United States (Georgia or Alabama), often listed as a Tigertooth type. The fruit is elongated and tooth like, sometimes likened to a molar, and turns reddish brown as it matures. It carries very few thorns or suckers compared with many jujubes.

What sets Silverhill apart is its very sweet, crisp flesh, which makes it one of the better varieties for fresh eating rather than processing. It can also be dried for storage. In Australian gardens it crops in autumn (around March to May), at the tail end of the jujube season.

4 sources
  • Trees and Shrubs Online: Ziziphus jujuba
  • Cultivar Guide: Jujube Varieties
  • American Gardener: Types of Jujube
  • Daleys Fruit: Jujube Silverhill

Sugar Cane

Sugar Cane is one of the earliest jujubes to fruit, ripening from early to mid season. The fruit is midsize and ovoid with reddish brown skin. It is prized for being very sweet and crunchy, with a crisp, apple like snap when eaten fresh.

Both its fresh eating and drying quality are rated very good, so a single tree gives you crisp fruit early in the season and chewy dried fruit later. New Mexico State University records its sugar level at roughly 25 to 30 degrees Brix.

3 sources
  • New Mexico State University Jujube Project: Varieties for Multipurpose Use
  • Trees and Shrubs Online: Ziziphus jujuba
  • Cultivar Guide: Jujube Varieties

Ta Jan

Ta Jan (also written Ta-Jan) is a Chinese cultivar whose name points to a large fruit. It produces large, oval to pear shaped fruit, broadly similar to Lang, with sweet, mild flesh. It ripens through the mid to late part of the season.

The fruit is fairly good eaten fresh once it has coloured fully to red brown, but it is at its best dried or used for processing into soups and candied preparations, where its size and sweetness suit long keeping products.

2 sources
  • Cultivar Guide: Jujube Varieties
  • American Gardener: Types of Jujube

Tiger Tooth Early

Tiger Tooth jujubes are known for their distinctive very elongated fruit, long and slender like an animal's canine tooth, which sets them apart from the round and oval jujube types. The Tiger Tooth Early selection is grown in Australia for its earlier ripening, with a long wide oval shape that is more incisor like, and a sweetness compared to Honey Jar.

The fruit is crisp and sweet when fresh and develops a chewy, date like character when dried, making it suitable for both fresh eating and drying.

3 sources
  • Trees and Shrubs Online: Ziziphus jujuba
  • Cultivar Guide: Jujube Varieties
  • Jujube Trees Australia: Cultivars

Lemon

10 varieties (back to top)

Bush

The Bush lemon is the naturalised rough lemon (Citrus jambhiri), a vigorous citrus hybrid of mandarin and citron that escaped cultivation and grows wild across subtropical Australia. Hardy, drought resistant and cold tolerant, it adapted to tough conditions and now grows feral, which is how it earned the bush lemon name. It forms a large, often thorny tree.

Its fruit has a thick, rough, knobbly rind and is seedy, making it mostly rind and less suited to eating than selected lemons like Eureka or Lisbon. Its main historical value is as a rootstock, valued for vigour and drought tolerance, onto which finer lemon and citrus cultivars are budded. The fruit is still used at home for juice and zest.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Rough lemon
  • Daleys Fruit: Bush Lemon (Citrus jambhiri)

Eureka

Eureka originated in Los Angeles, California, selected from seedlings grown from Italian seed and introduced around 1877 as Garey's Eureka. Along with Lisbon it became one of the world's principal lemon varieties.

The tree is largely thornless and strongly everbearing, producing fruit at the ends of long branches, which makes it a popular choice for home gardens. The acidic fruit has a fairly rough, prominently ridged rind and only a small nipple.

2 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection (UC Riverside): Frost nucellar Eureka lemon
  • Specialty Produce: Eureka Lemons

Fino

Fino is a leading Spanish commercial lemon, the most widely grown cultivar in Spain, probably arising as a seedling of a common Murcia-region lemon. It is an early to mid-season (mainly winter) lemon. The tree is exceptionally vigorous, large, very thorny and highly productive, with dense foliage.

Compared with the other major Spanish lemon, Berna (Verna), Fino fruit is paler, with a smoother and thinner rind, and is higher in juice and acid content, though more seedy. The fruit is spherical to oval with a small, sharp-pointed nipple. Its high juice yield and acidity make it a favourite of processors.

2 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection at UCR: Limonero Fino 49 lemon
  • Frutas y Hortalizas: Lemon types and varieties

Lemonade

Lemonade is not a true lemon but a sweet, low-acid hybrid widely believed to be a cross of lemon and mandarin, discovered as a chance seedling in New Zealand in the early 1980s and now grown across Australia. Its exact parentage remains uncertain. The low acidity lets the natural sugars come through, giving a mild, sweet, faintly tangy flavour quite unlike a sour lemon.

Unlike a cooking lemon, ripe Lemonade fruit can be peeled and eaten fresh straight off the tree, segmenting easily thanks to its mandarin heritage. The fruit has a glossy yellow rind and juicy yellow flesh, sweetens the longer it hangs on the tree, and is also juiced for a naturally sweet drink. The tree is heavy bearing and often thorny.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Lemonade fruit
  • Victorian Citrus Farms: Lemonade lemons
  • Good Harvest: Lemonade Fruit

Lisbon

Lisbon is a lemon of Portuguese origin, believed to be a Gallego seedling selection, and was introduced to Australia early in the 1800s. It became one of the leading commercial lemons in both Australia and the United States.

The tree is vigorous, large, thorny and densely foliated, and is notably resistant to frost, heat, wind and neglect, more so than Eureka. Because much of the fruit is borne within the canopy it is well protected, and the oblong, very acidic fruit carries a prominent nipple.

2 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection (UC Riverside): Frost nucellar Lisbon lemon
  • Specialty Produce: Lisbon Lemons

Meyer

The Meyer lemon is native to China and was introduced to the United States in 1908 by USDA plant explorer Frank Meyer. It is not a true lemon but a hybrid, generally described as a cross of citron with a mandarin/pomelo type.

Its fruit is sweeter and less acidic than standard Eureka or Lisbon lemons, with thin, deep-yellow to faintly orange skin and a subtle floral, almost orange-like character. A virus-free selection was released by the University of California in 1975 as the Improved Meyer lemon.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Meyer lemon
  • Specialty Produce: Meyer Lemons

Seedless Eureka

Seedless Eureka is a selection of the classic Eureka lemon that arose as a limb sport (bud mutation) and is distinguished from ordinary Eureka by its consistently seedless fruit. In other respects it closely resembles its parent, carrying the same bright, tart, high-acid lemon flavour collectors expect from a Eureka.

The fruit is medium to large with smooth, thin yellow skin that zests and slices cleanly, and like the Eureka the tree tends to be near everbearing, cropping over much of the year rather than in one short flush. The seedlessness makes it convenient for juicing and cooking without picking out pips.

2 sources
  • St Clements Citrus: Discovering the Lemon Seedless Eureka
  • Victorian Citrus Farms: Eureka Seedless lemons

Variegated Eureka

The Variegated Eureka, also called the variegated pink lemon or pink-fleshed Eureka, is an ornamental sport that appeared on an ordinary Eureka lemon tree in Burbank, California, in 1931. It is prized as much for its looks as its fruit, with green-and-cream variegated foliage and immature fruit that is striped green and yellow before ripening to yellow.

Inside, the flesh is a soft pink (similar in look to a pink grapefruit), though the juice runs clear rather than pink. The flavour is a true acidic lemon, useful in the kitchen wherever a standard Eureka would be, so the variety offers the novelty of pink flesh and striped, variegated growth without losing classic lemon tartness.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Variegated pink lemon
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection at UCR: Variegated Pink Fleshed Eureka lemon
  • Gardenia.net: Citrus x limon Eureka Pink Variegated

Verna

Verna (also spelt Berna) is a major Spanish lemon of unknown origin, historically the dominant lemon of the Spanish Levant and still the second most grown Spanish cultivar after Fino. It is a distinctly late-season variety, ripening from late winter into summer and holding well on the tree, which extends the supply beyond earlier lemons.

The fruit is medium sized, oval to broad-elliptical, with a short neck, a well developed nipple and bright yellow rind at maturity. The medium-thick rind is somewhat pebbled and rough, and seed content is variable but usually few to none. It is grown primarily as a fresh-market table lemon.

3 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection at UCR: Berna lemon
  • Todoli Citrus Fundacio: Berna - Verna
  • Frutas y Hortalizas: Lemon types and varieties

Yuzu

Yuzu (Citrus junos) is a distinct cold-hardy citrus, not a true lemon. It is an F1 hybrid of a mandarin and the ichang papeda, originating in central China and long cultivated in Japan and Korea. Its papeda ancestry makes it unusually frost tolerant, growing where more sensitive citrus would fail (winters down to around minus 7 degrees Celsius). The fruit resembles a small, bumpy yellow to green mandarin with uneven skin.

Yuzu is prized for its intensely aromatic rind and tart, seedy juice rather than for fresh eating, which it is rarely used for. The zest and juice carry a complex fragrance blending lemon, mandarin and grapefruit with herbal notes, and feature heavily in Japanese and Korean cooking, including the citrus sauce ponzu and the chilli paste yuzu kosho.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Yuzu
  • Gardenia.net: Citrus x junos (Yuzu)

Lilly Pilly

5 varieties (back to top)

Brush Cherry

Brush Cherry is the common name for Syzygium australe, a rainforest tree native to the east coast of Australia, growing in New South Wales and into Queensland, often near streams. It is one of the most widely planted lilly pillies and the parent of many named hedging cultivars. The reddish to pink new growth is attractive for much of the year, maturing to glossy green foliage.

Cream to white flowers are produced in large numbers, about 15 mm across with long stamens, creating a fluffy flowering effect. These are followed by red-pink, somewhat pear-shaped edible berries that can be used to make jam. Brush Cherry is valued as a dense hedge and screen and is attractive to birds.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Syzygium australe
  • Australian Plants Society: Syzygium australe

Bush Christmas

Bush Christmas is a compact, dense cultivar of Syzygium australe grown for its tidy form and colourful new growth. It is well suited to clipping, hedging, screening, topiary and pots, and pruning encourages a fresh flush of bright new foliage.

The new growth emerges in orange to red tones against glossy dark green mature leaves. White powder-puff flowers, which can appear around Christmas, are followed by pinkish edible fruit that is pleasantly tart. It prefers a warm, well-drained position and is frost sensitive, so it is best protected from heavy frost.

2 sources
  • Gardening With Angus: Syzygium australe Bush Christmas
  • Australian Plants Online: Syzygium Bush Christmas

Cherry Surprise

Cherry Surprise is a lilly pilly cultivar of Acmena smithii (the genus, not Syzygium), bred from the naturally compact minor form. It is a compact, dense small shrub valued for vivid new growth, growing well as a clipped hedge, screen or topiary specimen and responding readily to pruning.

The standout feature is its bright red to burgundy new foliage, which matures to deep green. White flowers appear in spring, and the plant is noted as psyllid resistant and rarely troubled by pests, which is a key advantage over older lilly pillies. It tolerates a wide range of soils in a sunny to semi-shaded position.

2 sources
  • Gardening With Angus: Acmena smithii Cherry Surprise
  • Plants for Spaces: Lilly Pilly Cherry Surprise

Resilience

Resilience is a cultivar of Syzygium australe selected for strong psyllid resistance, the leaf-distorting pest that disfigures many older lilly pillies. It is a fast-growing, compact selection used mainly for hedging, screening, topiary and bonsai, with dense evergreen foliage that holds clean leaves without chemical treatment.

The new growth emerges in reddish bronze tones against glossy green mature leaves. Cream to white flowers appear in spring and are followed by edible, decorative fruit that attracts bees and nectar-feeding birds. Its dense habit and pest tolerance make it a reliable choice for low-maintenance screens.

2 sources
  • Gardening With Angus: Syzygium australe Resilience
  • The Australian Plant Shop: Syzygium australe Resilience

Riberry

Riberry is the bush-food lilly pilly, botanically Syzygium luehmannii, a coastal rainforest tree native to eastern Australia from New South Wales into Queensland. It is grown as much for its fruit as its looks. The young foliage flushes pink to red before maturing to glossy green, and clusters of small white to cream flowers appear in late spring or early summer.

The pear-shaped berries ripen red over summer and have a tart, cranberry-like flavour with a hint of cloves. Riberry has been a popular gourmet bush food since the early 1980s, used in jams, sauces, syrups and confectionery, and the tree also serves well as an ornamental and street tree.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Syzygium luehmannii
  • Naturally Native: What is Riberry

Lime

8 varieties (back to top)

Kaffir

The Kaffir lime (Citrus hystrix), increasingly called makrut lime (a more neutral name, as kaffir derives from an ethnic slur), is native to tropical Southeast Asia. It is instantly recognisable by its distinctive double, hourglass-shaped leaves, which are actually a leaf blade plus a winged, leaf-like petiole.

The small fruit has a bumpy, warty rind and yields little juice, so the plant is grown chiefly for its intensely aromatic leaves and zest, which are central to Thai and other Southeast Asian curries, pastes and soups.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Kaffir lime
  • Gardenia.net: Kaffir Lime (Citrus hystrix)

Rangpur

The Rangpur lime (Citrus x limonia) is a lime in name only. Genomic work shows it is an F1 hybrid of a female citron (Citrus medica) and a male mandarin (Citrus reticulata), so it is really a mandarin-lime. Named for Rangpur in Bangladesh, it is also called the mandarin-lime in the United States and limao-cravo (cravo lemon) in Brazil. The fruit is unusual for a lime: it has an orange, yellowish to reddish-orange rind and orange flesh.

Despite the orange colour, the tender, juicy flesh is strongly acidic with a sour, lime-like flavour, which makes it useful in cooking and drinks. Its biggest role, however, is horticultural: it is a vigorous, hardy citrus rootstock tolerant of the tristeza virus and soil-borne disease, used heavily in Brazil. In cooler areas it is also grown as an ornamental.

2 sources
  • Rangpur lime, Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection at UC Riverside
  • Rangpur (fruit), Wikipedia

Red Centre

The Red Centre Lime is a CSIRO-bred native citrus hybrid of the Australian finger lime (Citrus australasica) and an acid mandarin (described in some accounts as the Rangpur lime, Citrus x limonia). It is closely associated with, and often sold alongside, the Australian Blood Lime. The striking fruit has a deep red, glossy, slightly dimpled skin in a small oval to teardrop shape, with flesh edged or tinged in pink and red.

Like its finger lime parent it carries juice vesicles (caviar-like pearls), but the juice is also spread through the fruit, so it is easier to juice than a pure finger lime. The flavour combines the spicy zing of the finger lime with the aromatic acidity of the mandarin parent for a sweet-sour profile. It suits sauces, preserves, condiments, beverages, and fresh garnish, and was granted Plant Breeder's Rights in 2004.

3 sources
  • A taste of the outback, CSIRO lime varieties, CSIROpedia
  • Australian lime, Wikipedia
  • Native lime, Citrus hybrid Red Centre Lime, Gardening With Angus

Sudachi

Sudachi (Citrus sudachi) is a small Japanese acid citrus, the specialty and symbol of Tokushima Prefecture, which grows about 98 per cent of Japan's crop. Genetic analysis confirms it is a hybrid rather than a true species, with one parent being yuzu and the other an unidentified relative of Japanese citrus such as koji and tachibana. The fruit is round and small (roughly 20 to 40 grams) and turns yellow-orange when mature.

It is almost always harvested and used while still green, when its sharp, clean acidity and intense aroma are most pronounced (often likened to lime with herbal, peppery notes). The tart juice and zest replace lemon or lime in sauces, marinades, dressings, and drinks, and halved sudachi is a traditional garnish for grilled fish, soba, and udon. It is not eaten as a table fruit.

2 sources
  • Sudachi, Wikipedia
  • Sudachi ichandrin (papeda hybrid), Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection at UC Riverside

Sunrise

The Sunrise Lime (often Australian Sunrise Lime) is a CSIRO-bred native citrus hybrid, a cross between the Australian finger lime (Citrus australasica) and a calamondin (itself a mandarin and cumquat cross). It was selected from a faustrimedin, with the parent tree chosen in 1990 at CSIRO. The result is a distinctive small fruit shaped like a pear in golden orange hues, appearing in winter.

Unlike its finger lime parent, the Sunrise can be eaten whole because the rind and pith are sweet, much like a kumquat, while the juice inside is tangy and fresh with a delicate floral note. It works fresh as a garnish or in sauces, marmalade, beverages, and preserves. It is one of three CSIRO native lime varieties (with Australian Blood and Australian Outback) granted Plant Breeder's Rights in 2004.

2 sources
  • A taste of the outback, CSIRO lime varieties, CSIROpedia
  • Citrus Sunrise Lime, Gardening With Angus

Sweet

The sweet lime, best known as the Palestine sweet lime (Citrus limettioides, sometimes placed under Citrus limetta), is an oddity among limes: a low-acid citrus that lacks the sharp bite of ordinary limes and lemons. The small, round to slightly oblong fruits have a thin, smooth rind with prominent oil glands that ripens from pale green to orange-yellow, and a pale yellow, very juicy, tender flesh.

Its defining trait is the near absence of acidity. Reference descriptions call the flavour insipid or bland because of the lack of acid, sometimes with a slightly bitter aftertaste, which makes it appealing to people who want citrus flavour without tartness. Long grown across India, the Near East, Egypt, and the Mediterranean as a fresh juice fruit, it is also used as a citrus rootstock.

2 sources
  • Palestine sweet lime, Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection at UC Riverside
  • Palestinian sweet lime, Wikipedia

Tahitian

The Tahitian lime is the Persian lime (Citrus latifolia), also sold as Bearss or seedless lime. It is a triploid hybrid, generally regarded as a cross of Key lime with lemon; the Bearss selection arose around 1895 from a seedling of Tahitian origin at Porterville, California.

Being triploid, the tree is essentially sterile, so the fruit is reliably seedless. It is larger and thicker-skinned than the Key lime, with juicy, acidic flesh that lacks the Key lime's bitterness, and it is the most widely grown lime worldwide.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Persian lime
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection (UC Riverside): Bearss lime

West Indian

The West Indian lime (Citrus aurantiifolia), also called the Key, Mexican, or true lime, is the small, intensely aromatic lime behind Key lime pie. Wikipedia describes it as a natural hybrid of Citrus micrantha (a wild papeda) and citron (Citrus medica), and it is widely treated as its own species. Fruits are small (roughly 3 to 6 cm across), almost round, and ripen from green to yellow with an exceptionally thin, fragrant rind.

Compared with the larger seedless Persian (Tahitian) lime, it is smaller, seedier, more acidic, and more aromatic. The greenish-yellow flesh is very juicy, highly acidic, and prized for its complex sour fragrance, which is why it is favoured for drinks, dressings, marinades, and ceviche. The trees are cold sensitive and the seeds are strongly polyembryonic, so they often grow true to type.

2 sources
  • Mexican lime, Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection at UC Riverside
  • Key lime, Wikipedia

Longan

4 varieties (back to top)

Biew Kiew

Biew Kiew (also spelled Biu Kiew or Biew Khiew) is one of Thailand's leading longan cultivars. The fruit is round and large with a brownish green skin and good aril recovery. The flesh is crisp, sweet and pleasantly scented, rated as excellent quality, with high sugar measured at around 21 to 22 percent total soluble solids.

It is a late-maturing selection in Thailand, flowering in late summer and harvesting toward the end of the season. Like several top Thai cultivars it bears irregularly, with strong crops in some years and lighter ones in others.

2 sources
  • FAO, Cultivars of Main Longan Centres in Asia
  • Rare Fruit Council Australia Archives, Performance of Longan

Chompoo

Chompoo (also written Choompoo or Sri Chompoo) is a Thai cultivar whose name means pink, a reference to the faint pink tinge of its flesh. The fruit is medium sized and oval with a small seed, which yields a high proportion of edible aril. It is very sweet, recorded at around 21 to 22 percent total soluble solids, with a pleasant aroma.

It is a mid-season selection, flowering in early winter and ripening from about midsummer. The cultivar is well regarded in Thailand but tends toward irregular cropping as a result of poor or inconsistent flowering.

2 sources
  • FAO, Cultivars of Main Longan Centres in Asia
  • Rare Fruit Council Australia Archives, Performance of Longan

Haew

Haew is a Thai longan cultivar valued for fruit that runs medium to large with a relatively small seed, which gives a good proportion of edible aril. The flesh is firm and crisp with a sweet, good flavour, and the rind is rather rough and thick, a trait that helps the fruit hold up in storage and transit.

It is a late-maturing selection in Thailand, flowering in late summer and harvesting toward the end of the season. Its main weakness is an alternate (irregular) bearing habit, so heavy crops tend to be followed by lighter ones.

2 sources
  • FAO, Cultivars of Main Longan Centres in Asia
  • Rare Fruit Council Australia Archives, Performance of Longan

Kohala

Kohala is a longan cultivar selected in Hawaii and introduced to Florida in 1954, where it became the dominant variety. It produces medium to large fruit of good to very good quality with sweet, translucent flesh and a single seed, in the manner of its relative the lychee. It is an early to mid-season longan.

Despite being the leading cultivar, Kohala does not crop reliably every year. University of Florida horticulturists describe its bearing as erratic, reflecting that inconsistent bearing is the major production problem for longan worldwide.

2 sources
  • University of Florida IFAS: Longan Growing in the Florida Home Landscape (MG049)
  • Wikipedia: Longan

Loquat

3 varieties (back to top)

Champagne

Champagne is an old white fleshed loquat introduced into cultivation in California around 1908 by the grower C.P. Taft, who selected it from seedlings and considered it among his best introductions. It sits in the white fleshed group of loquats (named cultivars carry either orange or white flesh), which are generally better suited to cooler coastal districts than the orange fleshed types.

The fruit is medium to large with pale golden to deep yellow skin and white, translucent, melting and very juicy flesh. The flavour is mildly subacid, bright and pleasant, of very good quality. Champagne is self infertile, so it crops best with another loquat nearby, but it is noted as prolific and ripens late in the season.

2 sources
  • California Rare Fruit Growers: Loquat Fruit Facts
  • Pacific Horticulture: Orchard Trees of Rancho Los Cerritos, Loquats

Herds Mammoth

Herds Mammoth (Herd's Mammoth) is a large fruited loquat in the white fleshed group. The fruit is large with yellow orange skin and white to cream coloured flesh of good quality, distinguishing it from the deep orange fleshed Japanese types. Loquat flesh can be white, yellow or orange depending on the cultivar, and white fleshed selections like this one are generally better suited to cooler coastal districts.

It is an early ripening variety, maturing earlier than Victory, which extends the picking window at the start of the loquat season. The tree is hardy and cold tolerant, which suits it to cooler parts of southern Australia, and it is widely grown there for its large fruit.

2 sources
  • California Rare Fruit Growers: Loquat Fruit Facts
  • Blerick Tree Farm: Loquat Herds Mammoth

Nagasakiwase

Nagasakiwase (Nagasaki Wase) is a Japanese loquat cultivar, regarded as the earliest maturing variety in Japan, ripening even earlier than the well known Mogi. It carries deep orange flesh and a very sweet flavour, with a high flesh to seed ratio, putting it among the orange or yellow fleshed group of loquats rather than the white fleshed types.

Loquats are largely self fertile and do not need a pollination partner, though cropping is improved by cross pollination. In warmer subtropical districts Nagasakiwase can carry two crops a year, a first picking around autumn and a second later in the season, which makes it a productive choice for early fruit.

2 sources
  • RFCA Archives: The Loquat (Rare Fruit Council of Australia)
  • Daleys Fruit: Loquat Nagasakiwase

Lychee

3 varieties (back to top)

Kwai Mai Pink

Kwai Mai Pink (also sold in Australia as Bosworth 3) is a Chinese lychee cultivar believed to have been brought from southern China to Australia in the 1930s. The fruit is small to medium, heart-shaped with blotchy red and yellow skin. The leaflets carry a characteristic twist towards the tip.

Its main appeal is eating quality and a small seed. The seeds are nearly all small (chicken tongue) in most seasons, which lifts the proportion of edible flesh, and the fruit quality is rated excellent.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Kwai Mai (lychee)
  • RFCA Archives: Lychee cultivar names

Kwai May Pink

Kwai May Pink (also sold in Australia as Bosworth 3) is a Chinese lychee cultivar believed to have been brought from southern China to Australia in the 1930s. The fruit is small to medium, heart-shaped with blotchy red and yellow skin. The leaflets carry a characteristic twist towards the tip.

Its main appeal is eating quality and a small seed. The seeds are nearly all small (chicken tongue) in most seasons, which lifts the proportion of edible flesh, and the fruit quality is rated excellent.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Kwai Mai (lychee)
  • RFCA Archives: Lychee cultivar names

Tai So

Tai So is a vigorous Chinese lychee cultivar and the most commonly grown variety in Australia. It is widely known as Mauritius in the western trade, with other names including Hong Huey and Da Zao (big crop). The fruit is medium to large, ripening pink to red, with sweet, aromatic, juicy flesh.

It is valued as a consistent, regular bearer. The main drawbacks are that the tree is prone to limb breakage and the fruit quality is rated only fair compared with the best eating cultivars.

3 sources
  • Canarius: Litchi chinensis Tai So
  • RFCA Archives: Lychee cultivar names
  • Daleys Fruit: Lychee Tai So

Mandarin

25 varieties (back to top)

Afourer

Afourer, also marketed as Nadorcott, is a late mandarin discovered in Morocco (a chance seedling thought to derive from a Murcott tree) and imported to California in 1985. It is classed as a tangor of uncertain parentage.

The flattened fruit has a thin, smooth, easy-to-peel rind and rich, sweet, juicy flesh with a refreshing acidity. It is low-seeded when grown in isolation but becomes seedy if cross-pollinated, and it holds well on the tree late into the season.

3 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection (UC Riverside): W. Murcott Afourer
  • Specialty Produce: Afourer Mandarins
  • Good Fruit Guide: Nadorcott (Afourer)

Amigo

Amigo is an Australian mandarin, a probable natural hybrid of Imperial mandarin and Ellendale tangor. It arose as a seedling growing beneath an Ellendale tree on the property of Bill Foreman. It is an early to mid season variety that overlaps the Imperial season, maturing about one to two weeks after Imperial and extending into late August in the Sunraysia and Riverland districts.

The fruit is larger and more uniform in size than Imperial, with soft, juicy flesh, low to moderate rag and segments that separate easily. The skin is easy to remove and lacks the off aromas that can affect some Ellendale hybrids. Flavour is sweet, and seed counts vary from zero to more than ten per fruit, with most fruit tending to the low end.

2 sources
  • Citrus Australia: Amigo mandarin variety fact sheet
  • Victorian Citrus Farms: Mandarins

Avana

Avana is the Italian name for the common Mediterranean or Willowleaf mandarin group (Citrus deliciosa), the traditional small mandarins long grown in Sicily. The best known selection, Tardivo di Ciaculli, arose as a spontaneous late-ripening mutation near Palermo and matures up to two months later than other Avana mandarins.

The fruit is somewhat flat with a pale orange to yellow rind that peels easily. The flesh is sweet and low in seeds, with reduced central columns (bores). Maturity is very late, from January to March in the northern hemisphere, which sets the Ciaculli selection apart from the rest of the group.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Avana tardivo di Ciaculli mandarin
  • Wikipedia: Mandarin orange varieties

Clauselliana

Clausellina (also written Clauselliana) is an early satsuma mandarin from Spain, a bud mutation of Owari satsuma found in 1962 at Almazora in Castellon. Its defining trait is early maturity, ripening roughly two to three weeks ahead of Owari, which made it commercially important in Spain as an early season satsuma.

Like other satsumas it is seedless and easy to peel, with sweet flesh carried on a compact tree. At full maturity it is slightly less sweet than Owari but very similar in other respects, and like many early satsumas the rind can puff if the fruit hangs too long.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Clausellina Satsuma mandarin
  • Good Fruit Guide: Clausellina

Clementine

The Clementine (Citrus clementina) is an easy-peel mandarin hybrid of a willowleaf mandarin and a sweet orange that appeared in late 19th century Algeria, at Misserghin, and was named after the French missionary Clement Rodier. It is one of the earliest mandarins of the season and a mainstay of winter citrus markets.

Its thin rind slips off cleanly and the flesh is sweet and juicy. The tree is self-incompatible, so fruit is largely seedless when grown in isolation, but it loses that seedlessness and turns seedy when cross-pollinated by other citrus nearby.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Clementine
  • Arab America: The Surprising Algerian History of the Clementine
  • Good Fruit Guide: Clemenules (Nules)

Clementine Marisol

Marisol is an early clementine from Spain, a bud mutation of the Oroval clementine found at Bechi in Castellon in 1970. It is valued for ripening early, about two weeks ahead of Oroval, reaching colour break around late September to early October in Spain. Because it colours before it is fully ripe, it is often harvested soon after colour break and de greened.

The fruit is juicy and tender with bright colour, though it can be a little acidic when picked early. The pale skin is relatively easy to peel but tends to break. Seediness is variable. A notable quirk is that the fruit puffs and granulates if left hanging too long, so timely picking matters.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Marisol clementine
  • Good Fruit Guide: Marisol

Daisy

Daisy is a US mandarin hybrid from a cross of Fortune and Fremont mandarins, named by Dowlin Young of Young's Nursery, Thermal, California, after his wife. It blends Fremont's sweetness with Fortune's acidity to give a balanced, early to mid-season fruit.

The fruit is medium to large with an attractive dark orange rind and deep-coloured flesh. It is moderately seedy, carrying roughly one to three seeds per segment, and the tree is strongly alternate bearing, which a later improved selection (DaisySL) was bred to address.

2 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection (UC Riverside): Daisy mandarin
  • Specialty Produce: Daisy Tangerines

Ellendale

Ellendale is an Australian tangor (a natural mandarin and sweet orange hybrid) found as a chance seedling on the Ellendale property near the Burrum River, Queensland, around 1878. It became a prized commercial variety in Australia, valued for its large size and quality.

It is a late mid-season fruit with attractive dark orange flesh that is especially juicy, sweet and rich. Seediness is variable: trees grown in solid blocks set few seeds, while those near other citrus become very seedy, and the monoembryonic seeds make it useful in breeding.

2 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection (UC Riverside): Ellendale tangor
  • Citrus Australia: Ellendale fact sheet

Emperor

Emperor is an old Australian mandarin thought to have originated as a seedling from fruit imported from the Orient, and it was once a leading commercial mandarin in Australia. It has served as a parent or source for other varieties, including the South African Empress.

It is a mid-season mandarin with large, oblate fruit and a puffy, loosely adherent rind that is extremely easy to peel. The light-orange flesh is tender, juicy and pleasantly sweet, though the fruit is moderately seedy and loses quality if left on the tree past maturity.

3 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection (UC Riverside): Scarlet Emperor mandarin
  • Citrus Australia: Emperor mandarin variety fact sheet
  • Daleys Fruit: Mandarin Emperor

Encore

Encore is a mandarin hybrid of King and Willowleaf (Mediterranean) mandarin, bred by H. B. Frost at the UC Citrus Research Center in Riverside and introduced by Cameron and Soost in 1965. It is one of the latest-ripening mandarins, often in season after most other mandarins have finished.

The medium, strongly oblate fruit has a thin, yellowish-orange rind and deep orange flesh that is firm yet tender, juicy and rich in flavour. It holds well on the tree late into the season, but is quite seedy, which limited its commercial use despite excellent eating quality.

2 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection (UC Riverside): Encore mandarin
  • Specialty Produce: Encore Mandarins

Fallglo

Fallglo is a US mandarin hybrid from a 1962 cross of Bower and Temple made by P. C. Reese, released by C. J. Hearn in 1987. Its parentage works out to about five eighths tangerine, one quarter orange and one eighth grapefruit, so it grows and markets as a tangerine. It is one of the earliest mandarins, maturing from late September into November, with best eating around late November.

The fruit is fairly large, usually about 2.75 to 3.25 inches across, with a deep reddish orange rind that peels with moderate ease. The flesh is sweet and juicy. The main drawback is heavy seediness, typically 20 to 40 seeds per fruit.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS EDIS: Fallglo Tangerine (HS173/CH075)
  • Oranges Online: Buyers Guide to Fallglo Tangerines

Fremont

Fremont is a Clementine and Ponkan mandarin hybrid bred by P. C. Reece of the USDA at Orlando, Florida, then selected and released by J. R. Furr at Brawley, California, in 1964. It ripens early, between Clementine and Dancy, and holds its quality well past maturity.

The fruit has a bright reddish-orange rind of moderate adherence that peels reasonably well, and deep orange, tender, juicy flesh with a rich, lively flavour. It is moderately seedy, about half of the seeds being monoembryonic.

2 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection (UC Riverside): Fremont mandarin
  • Four Winds Growers: Fremont Mandarin

Hickson

Hickson is an Australian mandarin that arose near Roma, Queensland, as a sporting limb on an Ellendale tangor, and it became a popular Australian commercial mandarin. It is similar to its Ellendale parent but matures mid-season, about two weeks earlier (from late June in Australia), and is reckoned to hang on the tree better.

The fruit has a smooth, glossy, yellowish-orange rind that peels more easily than Ellendale, and juicy, well-flavoured orange flesh. It is moderately seedy, typically carrying around twelve to fifteen seeds.

2 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection (UC Riverside): Hickson mandarin
  • Citrus Australia: Hickson fact sheet

Honey Murcott

Honey Murcott is a selection of the Murcott, a tangor (mandarin crossed with sweet orange) that traces to a USDA hybrid given to growers in Florida and named after Charles Murcott Smith, who propagated it in the 1920s. It is also known as the Honey tangerine.

It is a late-season mandarin prized for its very sweet, rich, honey-like flavour and high juice content, often considered one of the sweetest mandarins. The trade-off is that it is seeded and not as easy to peel as earlier varieties such as Imperial.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Murcott (fruit)
  • Good Fruit Guide: Murcott (Honey Tangerine)
  • Westridge Fruit & Vegetables: Australian Mandarin Varieties

Imperial

Imperial is an Australian mandarin that arose around 1890 at Emu Plains, west of Sydney, believed to be a chance hybrid (possibly of a Willowleaf-type mandarin with another mandarin such as Emperor). It became one of Australia's most popular commercial mandarins.

It is very early maturing, typically the first mandarin of the Australian season (around April onwards), with a thin, smooth, glossy skin that peels easily and segments that separate cleanly. The flesh is sweet and pleasantly subacid with few seeds (usually four or fewer). The tree tends toward alternate (biennial) bearing.

4 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection (UC Riverside): Imperial mandarin
  • Specialty Produce: Imperial Mandarins
  • Hill Street Grocer: All you need to know about Imperial Mandarins
  • Westridge Fruit & Vegetables: Australian Mandarin Varieties

Lemonade

Lemonade is a sweet, low-acid citrus that arose as a chance seedling in New Zealand in the 1980s and was quickly taken up in Australia. Its exact parentage is uncertain and often described as a lemon-mandarin type, so it is grouped here with mandarins but is not a true mandarin.

Although it looks like a rounded lemon, the yellow rind peels by hand and the flesh is sweet with only mild acidity, so the fruit is eaten fresh out of hand like a mandarin or orange rather than juiced like a lemon. It carries few to no seeds.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Lemonade fruit
  • Specialty Produce: Lemonade Fruit

Miho Wase Satsuma
