Miho Wase is an early satsuma mandarin (Citrus unshiu) raised in Japan as a sister nucellar seedling to Okitsu Wase and registered in 1963. Wase means early, and the fruit matures early in the season, ahead of standard Miyagawa satsumas.

Compared with Miyagawa, the somewhat oblate fruit runs higher in sugar and usually lower in acid, giving sweet, juicy flesh. Like other satsumas it is easy to peel and typically seedless. It is also valued by home growers for the cold tolerance characteristic of satsumas.

3 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection (UC Riverside): Miho Wase Satsuma mandarin
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection (UC Riverside): Okitsu Wase Satsuma mandarin
  • Restoring Eden: Miho Wase Satsuma Mandarin

Nules Clementine

Nules Clementine, also called Clemenules, is the leading Spanish Clementine selection, a mutation of the Fina Clementine found at Nules, in Castellon, around 1953. Larger and slightly earlier than Fina, it became the most widely grown Clementine in Spain and a benchmark easy-peel mandarin across Europe.

The fruit has a smooth, deeply coloured rind that lifts off easily and sweet, soft, juicy segments. It is essentially seedless when grown away from other citrus, ripens early in the season, and tends to puff if left on the tree too long after maturity.

2 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection (UC Riverside): Nules clementine
  • Good Fruit Guide: Clemenules (Nules)

Okitsu wase Satsuma

Okitsu Wase is an early satsuma mandarin (Citrus unshiu) that arose as a nucellar seedling of Miyagawa, raised by M. Kajiura and T. Iwasaki at the Horticultural Research Station at Okitsu, Japan, in 1940 and registered in 1963. Wase means early, and it ripens about a week ahead of Miyagawa.

The fruit is somewhat more oblate than Miyagawa but higher in sugar, with sweet, juicy, aromatic flesh. The thin skin peels very easily and the fruit carries few seeds. It became one of the most widely planted satsuma cultivars in Japan.

2 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection (UC Riverside): Okitsu Wase Satsuma mandarin
  • Comparison of four early-maturing Satsuma mandarins (NZ Journal of Crop and Horticultural Science)

Ortanique

Ortanique is a tangor (a natural mandarin crossed with sweet orange) found in Jamaica in the early 1900s, where it became an important export fruit. Its name combines orange, tangerine and unique.

It is usually late in maturity and holds very well on the tree. The medium, obovate fruit has a pebbled dark orange rind, and the orange flesh is tender, exceptionally juicy and rich and sweet. Like many tangors it is seedy when cross-pollinated, and the firmly attached rind makes it harder to peel than a true mandarin.

2 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection (UC Riverside): Ortanique tangor
  • Wikipedia: Tangor (Ortanique)

Page

Page is a USDA hybrid from a cross of Minneola tangelo and Clementine mandarin made by Gardner and Bellows in 1942 and released in Florida in the 1960s. Because its parentage is roughly three-quarters mandarin, it is widely sold and eaten as a mandarin even though it is technically a tangelo.

The fruit is small to medium and deep orange, with a thin rind that peels easily. The flesh is notably juicy and sweet with high sugar, and it ripens early in the season. Seediness varies from few to a moderate number of seeds depending on growing conditions.

2 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection (UC Riverside): Page tangelo
  • Specialty Produce: Page Tangerines

Pixie

Pixie is a seedless mandarin from UC Riverside, raised by H. B. Frost from an open pollination of Kincy (a King and Dancy cross) and released in 1965. It is prized as a very late variety that holds exceptionally well on the tree, sometimes into summer in mild districts.

The fruit has a yellow-orange, slightly pebbled rind that peels easily, and mild, sweet, juicy flesh. It is virtually seedless, which along with its late season has made it a popular home-garden and specialty mandarin.

2 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection (UC Riverside): Pixie mandarin
  • Wikipedia: Pixie mandarin

Shiranui Dekupon

Shiranui is a Japanese mandarin hybrid of Kiyomi tangor and Ponkan, developed in Japan in 1972. It is most famous under its market name Dekopon, and is sold as Sumo Citrus in the United States and as Hallabong in South Korea. The fruit is easily recognised by a large protruding bump or knob at the stem end, the feature that gives Dekopon its name (deko meaning convex, plus pon from Ponkan).

The fruit is large and very sweet, with quality standards typically requiring a sugar level above 13 Brix and citric acid below 1.0 percent. After picking it is usually rested for 20 to 40 days so acidity falls and sugar rises before sale. It is widely sold as seedless when trees are grown away from pollen sources, although some plantings remain seedy. The rind peels readily and the season runs through winter, roughly December to February.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Shiranui mandarin
  • Wikipedia: Dekopon

Silverhill - Satsuma

Silverhill is an early Satsuma mandarin (Citrus reticulata), a nucellar seedling selection of Owari raised by W. T. Swingle of the USDA around 1908 and introduced about 1931. It was field tested at Silverhill, Alabama, where it proved notably cold resistant, surviving freezes that damaged standard Satsumas.

The fruit is medium sized and slightly oblate with a thin, smooth rind, and is seedless or nearly so. The juicy flesh is high in sugars and low in acid, giving a very sweet, mild flavour. It ripens early, around November, and the tree is more upright when young than the more drooping Owari.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Silverhill Satsuma mandarin
  • Wikipedia: Mandarin orange varieties

Sunburst

Sunburst is a Florida mandarin hybrid from a cross of Robinson and Osceola, made in 1961 and released for commercial use in 1979 by C. J. Hearn of the USDA in Orlando. It became one of the most widely grown early Florida tangerines.

It is prized for a beautiful dark orange rind that is thin, smooth and fairly easy to remove. The catch is seeds: the tree is self-incompatible, so it sets poorly in solid blocks and produces seedy fruit when cross-pollinated by other citrus, needing polleniser trees nearby to crop well.

2 sources
  • Givaudan Citrus Variety Collection (UC Riverside): Sunburst mandarin
  • UF/IFAS Extension: Sunburst Tangerine (HS168/CH079)

Mango

78 varieties (back to top)

Ah Ping

Ah Ping is a Hawaiian cultivar that originated as a seedling planted by Mrs Chun Ah Ping of Mapulehu, Molokai, and is one of the varieties recommended for growing in Hawaii.

The fruit is medium-large with attractive skin colour similar to Haden, good appearance and good eating quality, and generally ripens in June and July. It is monoembryonic, so it does not come true from seed and is propagated by grafting.

2 sources
  • University of Hawaii CTAHR: Origin and Classification of Mango Varieties in Hawaii (Hamilton)
  • Wikipedia: List of mango cultivars

Alphonso

Alphonso, known in India as Hapus, is grown mainly in the Konkan region of western India, especially the Ratnagiri, Devgad and Sindhudurg districts of Maharashtra. The cultivar is named after Afonso de Albuquerque, a Portuguese viceroy of India, and Alphonso fruit from the Konkan region received Geographical Indication protection in 2018.

It is among the most prized and most expensive Indian mangoes, widely called the king of mangoes. The saffron coloured pulp is rich, creamy and non-fibrous, prized for export, and the fruit is harvested roughly from the end of March through the end of June.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Alphonso (mango)
  • Deshpande et al., Front. Plant Sci. (PMC5562913): Transcriptional transitions in Alphonso mango during fruit development and ripening

Anderson

Anderson grew from a Sandersha seed sent from Jamaica in 1926 and planted on L. F. Anderson's property in Miami, Florida. The tree fruited in 1931 and was named in 1948; a 2005 analysis pointed to a Sandersha by Haden cross. It carries a monoembryonic seed.

Like its Sandersha parent the fruit is large and elongated with a small lateral beak, with green to yellow skin and some crimson blush. The yellow flesh is fibreless with a mild, slightly acidic flavour. A tendency to split on the tree kept it out of commercial production, and it ripens July to August in Florida.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Anderson (mango)
  • Wikipedia: List of mango cultivars

Ataulfo

Ataulfo is a Mexican cultivar from Soconusco in Chiapas, named for grower Ataulfo Morales Gordillo. It descends from the Philippine (Manila type) mango brought to Mexico via the Manila-Acapulco galleon trade before 1779, then crossed with other varieties. In the United States it is marketed under the trade names champagne mango, honey mango, and Manila mango.

Being a Philippine-type mango, it is polyembryonic, unlike Indian-type cultivars which are monoembryonic. The fruit is small and golden yellow, weighing roughly 170 to 280 grams, with a somewhat S-shaped (sigmoid) form, a thin pit, and flesh that is not fibrous. Since 2003 it has held a Mexican Designation of Origin for Chiapas.

2 sources
  • Ataulfo (mango), Wikipedia
  • Ataulfo Mangoes, Specialty Produce

Banganapalli

Banganapalli (also spelt Banaganapalle) is a major South Indian cultivar whose primary centre of origin is the Kurnool district of Andhra Pradesh. It is widely known by the synonyms Beneshan, Baneshan, Benishan, Safeda and Chappatai. Banaganapalle mangoes received a Geographical Indication tag, with Andhra Pradesh recognised as the source.

The fruit has skin with very light spots, an oblong stone with a thin seed, and only sparse, soft fibre, giving a high flesh ratio. It keeps well, reportedly retaining quality in cold storage for up to three months, which suits long-distance and export markets.

2 sources
  • S.S. Rana & Co.: Banaganapalle Mangoes Get GI Tag
  • Getfarms: Banganapalle Mango GI-Tagged

Beverley

Beverley (usually spelled Beverly) is a Florida cultivar, selected by the Zill family of Boynton Beach. Long thought to be a Haden seedling, a 2005 pedigree analysis estimated Cushman as the parent. It never gained wide commercial acceptance because the fruit stays dull green to yellow at maturity rather than colouring up.

It is prized as a late-season, almost fibreless dooryard mango, ripening in Florida around July to August (after most other varieties have finished). The flesh is firm, rich and aromatic, and the round to oval fruit can be large.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Beverly (mango)
  • Pine Island Nursery: Beverly mango

Bowen

Bowen is not a separate cultivar but another name for Kensington Pride, Australia's most widely grown mango. The variety was identified in the 1880s at Bowen in north Queensland, which is why it is also sold as Bowen, Bowen Special, KP Bowen and Pride of Bowen.

As Kensington Pride, the fruit has yellow to orange-yellow flesh, little to moderate fibre and a sweet, aromatic, slightly spicy flavour, ripening through spring and summer. So a Bowen mango and a Kensington Pride are the same fruit under different trade names.

2 sources
  • Red Rich Fruits: Are Bowen mangoes the same as Kensington Pride?
  • Wikipedia: Kensington Pride

Brooks

Brooks is a historic Florida cultivar that grew from a seed of the Indian 'Totapuri' (also called 'Sandersha'), planted in Miami in 1910. The tree first fruited in 1916, making it one of the earliest named Florida selections after Haden. It went on to become an important parent, lying behind Kent, Sensation, Hatcher and probably Keitt.

Brooks ripens late, from August into early October in Florida, which sets it apart from the mid season crowd. The fruit is oblong and lacks a beak, with skin that turns green-yellow when ripe and yellow flesh carrying a medium amount of fibre. The flavour is mild and sweet rather than rich or spicy. The seed is monoembryonic, so seedlings do not come true to type.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Brooks (mango)
  • Kiddle Encyclopedia: Brooks (mango)

Brooks Late

Brooks Late, usually just called Brooks, is a late-season Florida mango. It grew from a seed of the Indian Sandersha (Totapuri) mango planted in Miami in 1910, first fruited in 1916, and after Haden was the second Florida cultivar to be named. The seed is monoembryonic. Brooks went on to parent several Florida mangoes, including Kent.

The oblong fruit lacks a beak and ripens to a green-yellow skin over yellow flesh that carries medium fibre and a mild, sweet flavour. In Florida it typically ripens from August into early October, well after the main-season cultivars, which is the source of the Late name.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia, Brooks (mango)
  • International Tropical Fruits Network, common mango varieties

Carabao

Carabao is the leading mango of the Philippines, also known internationally as the Philippine or Manila mango. It is named after the carabao, the country's national animal. The famous Guimaras strain became the first Philippine product granted a Geographical Indication, reputed for exceptional sweetness.

Like other South East Asian mangoes it carries a polyembryonic seed, in contrast to Indian type mangoes. The flesh is very sweet and aromatic with a tender, melting consistency. It ripens from green to a yellow or yellow orange skin and is a popular ripe eating and export mango.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Carabao (mango)
  • IPOPHL: Registered Geographical Indications
  • Philstar: Guimaras mango first Philippine GI

Carrie

Carrie is a Florida dooryard mango raised by Laurence H. Zill in Boynton Beach, where it first fruited in 1940. Originally reported as a seedling of Sophie Fry, a 2005 pedigree analysis named Julie as its most likely parent. It was named after Zill's mother, Carrie Zill, and carries a monoembryonic seed.

The flesh is orange, not fibrous, and rich in flavour with a strong aroma, which has made it a favourite for home growers. Unusually, the small fruit does not develop the red blush seen on many Florida mangoes, ripening green to yellow instead. Carrie also has a reputation for good disease resistance, with fruit described as highly resistant to fungus.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia, Carrie (mango)
  • Tropical Tree Guide, Carrie mango grow guide

Cat Chu

Cat Chu (Xoai Cat Chu) is a premium Vietnamese dessert mango from the Cao Lanh area of Dong Thap province in the Mekong Delta, where it is a regional point of pride. It is smaller than its cousin Cat Hoa Loc, with an elongated shape and thin skin that ripens from green to golden yellow.

The variety is prized for a small seed, thick flesh, and very low fibre. The ripe flesh is soft and fragrant, with a rich sweetness balanced by a gentle tang. Both ripe and green Cat Chu are exported, including to Australia, Japan and the United States.

3 sources
  • Vietnam News: Cao Lanh's Cat Chu mango
  • VietnamNet: Cao Lanh's Cat Chu mango
  • TH Vietnam: Distinguishing Vietnam mango varieties

Cat Hoa Loc

Cat Hoa Loc is widely regarded as Vietnam's premium mango, originating from Hoa Loc commune in the Mekong Delta and centred on the Cai Be district of Tien Giang province. The fruit is elongated and oblong with thin skin that turns light yellow when ripe. The flesh is firm, smooth, fibreless and aromatic, with a rich, naturally sweet flavour.

It is treated as a luxury and gift fruit in Vietnam and carries Geographical Indication protection tied to its origin. Because the fruit is delicate with a short shelf life, it is usually exported in limited volumes to premium markets. Harvest peaks roughly from April to July.

2 sources
  • Mekong Delta Tours: Hoa Loc Mango, Cai Be Specialty
  • Navinuts: Cat Hoa Loc Mango, Vietnam's Premium Mango Variety

Chok Anan

Chok Anan (also spelled Choc Anan or Chokanon) is a sweet mango from Thailand, often nicknamed the Miracle Mango. Its claim to fame is off season cropping: it can flower and fruit outside the normal season and sometimes carries two crops in one year, in both summer and winter. This ever bearing habit is unusual among mangoes and is the variety's signature trait.

The medium sized fruit ripens to a golden yellow skin. The light yellow flesh is firm and fibreless, and the flavour is intensely sweet, frequently described as honey like with a hint of coconut. It is eaten ripe as a dessert mango.

4 sources
  • Wikipedia: Chok Anan
  • Wikipedia: List of mango cultivars
  • Mangopedia: Chok Anan
  • Top Tropicals: Mango Varieties Guide

Coconut Cream

Coconut Cream is a named Florida cultivar developed by the Zill family at Boynton Beach, Florida. Its defining trait, noted by UF/IFAS, is the fruit's rich, sweet, pronounced coconut flavour, set in light yellow, completely fiberless pulp of excellent quality.

The fruit are roundish, medium to large, with a light yellow green peel and a pinkish blush, and the seed is polyembryonic. UF/IFAS classes it as an early to mid season cultivar with moderate to good disease resistance. The unusual coconut note is what sets it apart from other mangoes.

3 sources
  • UF/IFAS Mango Science, Coconut Cream
  • Wikipedia, Coconut Cream (mango)
  • Crane, mango cultivars developed by Gary Zill (FSHS / mango.org)

Cotton Candy

Cotton Candy is a modern Florida cultivar from the breeding program of Gary Zill at Boynton Beach, Florida. It was selected as a seedling of the well known late season mango Keitt and was released to growers in 2016, partly as a late season alternative to its parent.

Despite the name it does not taste of spun sugar. It is prized for an intensely sweet, classic flavour (some find it almost too sweet), with completely fiberless, creamy yellow flesh and a polyembryonic seed. The fruit are medium to large and ripen late in the season, around July and August in Florida.

2 sources
  • Mangopedia
  • Crane, mango cultivars developed by Gary Zill (FSHS / mango.org)

Cushman

Cushman originated in Miami, Florida, from a seed planted in 1936 on the property of E. L. Cushman. A 2005 pedigree analysis suggested it is likely a cross of Haden and Amini. It carries a monoembryonic seed and ripens midseason, from July to August in Florida.

The fruit is round and yellow when ripe, resembling a grapefruit more than a typical mango, and shows a distinctive orange halo near the skin when cut. The flesh is completely fibreless with a sweet, rich flavour and aroma. The trees are poor bearers, so the variety stayed a dooryard tree rather than a commercial one.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Cushman (mango)
  • Tropical Acres Farms: Cushman

Dasheri

Dasheri (also spelt Dussehri) is a classic North Indian cultivar that originated in a village near Malihabad in the Lucknow district of Uttar Pradesh. The original Mother Dasehri tree, in the orchard of the Nawab of Kakori, is about 185 years old and reportedly still bears fruit. Malihabad in Uttar Pradesh is the largest producer.

Dasheri is prized as a sweet, fragrant mango. The flesh is tender and almost fibreless, and the fruit is elongated with a slightly curved, pointed tip. It is one of India's most important commercial cultivars.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Dasheri
  • American Gardener: Dasheri Mangoes

Duncan

Duncan was grown by David Sturrock of West Palm Beach, Florida, from a seed planted in 1956; it first fruited in 1960 and was named after Ralph V. Duncan of Boynton Beach. Sturrock reported it as an Edward by Pico cross, while a 2005 analysis suggested Nam Doc Mai as the likely parent. The variety carries a monoembryonic seed.

The fruit is oblong, turns yellow when ripe, and has orange, fibreless flesh with a sweet, mild flavour. It ripens in July in Florida, and both the trees and fruit are noted for very good disease resistance.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Duncan (mango)
  • UF/IFAS Mango Science: Duncan

Elephant Tusk

Elephant Tusk is the Vietnamese green-eating mango Xoai Tuong (also written Xoai Tong, Tuong or Green Elephant), thought to have arisen in the Phu Cat district of Binh Dinh Province on the central Vietnamese coast. It is one of the largest mangoes grown in Vietnam, with a long, slightly curved, elongated shape that gives it the elephant-tusk name. The skin is green when unripe and turns yellow as it matures.

Unlike dessert mangoes prized at full ripeness, Elephant Tusk is grown mainly to eat green. Picked unripe it has a firm, crunchy, succulent texture and a refreshing sweet-sour, tangy flavour that becomes milder and sweeter with age. In Vietnam it is traditionally sliced and dipped in salty, sweet and spicy condiments, commonly a sugar-and-fish-sauce dip, and used in green-mango salads rather than eaten soft and ripe.

2 sources
  • Specialty Produce, Green Elephant Mangoes
  • VN Xanh, Vietnamese mango

Falan

Falan (sometimes spelled Fralan or Falang) is a Thai mango grown chiefly for eating green and unripe. Unlike most mangoes it stays predominantly green at maturity, with small to medium, oblong fruit that lack the usual yellow or red blush.

The pale, firm flesh is fibreless and eaten while still crisp, with a sweet, mildly tangy flavour rather like a crunchy apple. In Thailand it is sliced fresh or used in green mango salads. The tree has a slow, spreading, dwarf like habit.

2 sources
  • Mangopedia: Falan
  • Top Tropicals: Mango Varieties Guide

Florigon

Florigon is a Florida cultivar that grew from a seed planted in 1932 on the property of John G. Kaiser of Fort Lauderdale, first fruiting in 1936. It was long believed to be a seedling of the Saigon mango (the name combines Florida and Saigon), though a 2005 pedigree analysis suggested Haden as the likely parent. Unusually for a named Florida mango, Florigon has a polyembryonic seed.

It is an early-season mango, maturing from roughly late May to July in Florida. The fruit is small and ovate, averaging less than a pound, ripening to an almost entirely yellow skin sometimes with a light blush. The yellow flesh is sweet, mild and fibreless. Florigon is valued for its good anthracnose tolerance and adaptation to humid conditions, which made it a reliable home grower.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Florigon
  • Mangopedia: Florigon

Fruit Punch

Fruit Punch is one of the boutique Florida cultivars from Gary Zill's breeding program at Boynton Beach. It is a recent selection, distinctive for its colourful skin that ripens in a blend of pink, red, purple and yellow tones, with numerous lenticels, on a medium to large ovoid fruit.

The pulp is aromatic, light orange and fiberless but firm, carrying a moderately intense Indian style flavour of excellent quality, with measured sugar levels (Brix) running roughly 13 to 17. True to its name, tasters describe a medley of tropical fruit notes. It is an early to mid season mango.

2 sources
  • Crane, mango cultivars developed by Gary Zill (FSHS / mango.org)
  • Mangopedia

Gedong

Gedong, usually sold as Gedong Gincu, is an Indonesian cultivar from West Java, mostly grown around Cirebon and Majalengka. Its standout feature is the skin, which ripens to an orange base with a natural red blush, paired with a strongly fragrant aroma. Fruit are on the smaller side with a slightly flat shape, and the flesh is yellow.

The variety is grown as a premium and export commodity in Indonesia, valued for its scent and colour. The red blush develops more strongly under full sun, which is part of why it commands a premium in the Indonesian market.

4 sources
  • Indoindians: 7 Popular Indonesian Mangoes
  • Alamy editorial caption: Mangga Gedong Gincu
  • Specialty Produce: Gedong Gincu Mangoes
  • ISHS: Marketing and value chain of Gedong Gincu mango

Glenn

Glenn is a Florida cultivar, reportedly a seedling planted in Miami around 1940. Though originally publicised as a Haden seedling, a 1995 analysis disputed the Haden parentage while a 2005 analysis found Haden to be the most likely parent. The fruit has a monoembryonic seed. UF/IFAS lists Glenn among the cultivars recommended for fruit quality in Florida home landscapes.

It is an early to midseason cultivar, ripening 2 to 3 weeks before Haden. The pulp is deep yellow and fibreless with a pleasant aroma and a rich, spicy flavour of excellent quality. The peel is yellow with an orange-red blush dotted with numerous small yellow and white dots, and fruit weighs about 373 to 620 grams. The tree is low to moderately vigorous with a rounded canopy.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS Mango Science: Glenn
  • Wikipedia: Glenn (mango)

Golek

Golek is a traditional Indonesian cultivar, instantly recognisable for its slender, elongated shape. The skin is greenish yellow, often flecked with light brown dots, and the flesh is deep yellow to orange. It is sweet with little fibre, and the thick flesh is soft rather than watery.

Golek is widely grown across Indonesia and also appears in Malaysia. The young, less ripe fruit is used in rujak (an Indonesian fruit salad), while ripe fruit is eaten fresh for its sweet, aromatic flavour.

2 sources
  • Indoindians: 7 Popular Indonesian Mangoes
  • Wikipedia: List of mango cultivars

Graham

Graham originated in Trinidad as a seedling of the Julie mango and was introduced to the United States by the USDA through Florida in 1932. Unlike its compact Julie parent, Graham is a vigorous grower forming a round, dense canopy, and it carries a monoembryonic seed.

The fruit has yellow, bumpy skin and deep orange, fibreless flesh with a sweet, rich, aromatic flavour. It ripens from June to August in Florida and has become a popular dooryard tree thanks to its fine flavour and good disease resistance.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Graham (mango)
  • UF/IFAS Mango Science: Graham

Gullivers Triumph

Gulliver's Triumph is a historic Australian mango bred by Thomas Allen Gulliver, who experimented with tropical crops including mangoes at his Roseneath property near Townsville, north Queensland. The Gulliver family ran the Acacia Vale Gardens and Nursery from the 1880s, and this is recorded as one of his most successful bred varieties.

In cultivation today it is offered as a dwarf to semi-dwarf, pot-friendly tree. The fruit is large with golden-yellow skin and a rich, sweet flavour carrying a light citrus note, making it a tree valued for fresh eating in home gardens.

2 sources
  • Encyclopedia of Australian Science and Innovation: Thomas Allen Gulliver
  • Fruit Tree Cottage: Gullivers Triumph mango

Haden

Haden is the foundational Florida mango. Captain John J. Haden of Coconut Grove planted seeds from a Mulgoba mango in 1902, and the standout tree first fruited in 1910. It is thought to be a cross between the Indian Mulgoba and a local Turpentine mango. The seed is monoembryonic, so it does not breed true and trees are grafted.

Rich and aromatic in flavour, the fruit has firm flesh that carries some fibre, and the skin ripens to a bright red over green and yellow. Haden dominated the Florida mango industry for roughly its first 25 years and became the parent of many later Florida cultivars, including the line that leads to Kent and Zill.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia, Haden (mango)
  • International Tropical Fruits Network, common mango varieties

Harumanis

Harumanis (also spelt Arumanis or Harum Manis, meaning fragrant and sweet) is an Indonesian cultivar that first appeared in the late 1950s, reached Malaysia about a decade later, and became famous in the state of Perlis from the early 1980s. Since 2011 the Harumanis name is reserved for fruit grown in Perlis, whose lime-rich soil and climate suit it.

Its standout trait is that the skin stays green even when fully ripe, so ripeness is judged by aroma and a slight softening rather than colour. The elongated fruit has thick, juicy, almost fibreless golden flesh that is richly sweet with an intense, distinctive perfume that people tend to either love or dislike.

2 sources
  • Arumanis Mangoes, Specialty Produce
  • 6 things you might not know about Harumanis mangoes, Tatler Asia

Harvest Moon

Harvest Moon is a Florida selection bred by Gary Zill at Boynton Beach (originally designated 4-20). It is a seedling of Edward, with Carrie, Val-Carrie or Gary suspected as the pollen parent.

It produces large, round, Kent-shaped fruit, ripening greenish-yellow with a light orange-red blush. The flesh is yellow, fibreless, juicy, creamy and somewhat firm. The flavour is a rich classic mango, sweet with a balancing acidity that gives a slight citrus edge and, depending on ripeness, notes of stone fruit such as plum and pear. It ripens mid to late season (July to August) and, being a vigorous upright tree, is not considered a condo mango.

2 sources
  • Tropical Tree Guide: Harvest Moon Mango Grow Guide
  • Tropical Acres Farms: Harvest Moon

Honey Gold

Honey Gold is an Australian mango that arose by chance in Queensland's Rockhampton region in 1991, from a Kensington Pride off-type pollinated by an unknown variety. Pinata Farms acquired the plant breeders' rights in 2002 and sent the first commercial fruit to market in 2009, and the company owns and markets the variety under its own brand.

The fruit has glossy yellow-orange skin and smooth, orange, fibreless flesh with an intense, distinctive flavour, and it holds its shape when cut. The Australian season runs roughly October to March, starting in the north in spring and finishing in the southern states in autumn.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Honey Gold
  • Pinata Farms: Honey Gold mangoes

Hong Sa

Hong Sa (also written Hong-sa) is one of the old Thai mango cultivars, named in lists of heritage varieties grown in Chachoengsao province, a major mango-growing region east of Bangkok.

It is a regional heirloom rather than a modern commercial variety, and features each year among the ancient cultivars showcased at Chachoengsao's long-running mango festival alongside names like Thong Dam and Nang Klang Wan.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: List of mango cultivars
  • Bangkok Post: In praise of mangoes
  • Holidify: Mangoes in Thailand

Irwin

Irwin is a Florida cultivar, a seedling of Lippens open-pollinated with Haden, planted on the property of F.D. Irwin in Miami in 1939. The tree first fruited in 1945 and was named and described in 1949. The fruit contains a monoembryonic seed. It is ovate with a rounded base and pointed apex, lacking a beak, and develops an eye-catching dark red blush at maturity, often borne in clusters.

It is an early-season cultivar, maturing from roughly June to July in Florida. The flesh is yellow and fibreless with a mild but sweet flavour and a pleasant aroma. While only modestly grown in Florida, Irwin became a major commercial variety in Asia, prized in markets such as Taiwan, Japan and South Korea (notably Jeju Island), as well as Australia.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Irwin (mango)
  • Specialty Produce: Irwin Mangoes

Julie

Julie (also called Saint Julian) is a West Indian cultivar of likely Indian ancestry that became a Caribbean staple, particularly popular in Trinidad, Jamaica and across the West Indies. The U.S. Virgin Islands legislature even named it the territory's fruit of choice. Its monoembryonic seed points to Indian mango lineage.

It is famous as a dwarf, slow-growing tree well suited to small gardens, often holding around three metres in South Florida. The fruit is small, oval with a flattened side and green skin blushed crimson. The deep orange flesh is juicy and fibreless with a rich, sweet flavour. It is prone to fungal disease, which limits commercial growing.

2 sources
  • Julie (mango), Wikipedia
  • List of mango cultivars, Wikipedia (Julie entry)

Jumbo Kesar

Jumbo Kesar is the Kesar cultivar sold as a large-fruited grade, so it carries the same story as Gujarat's famous Kesar. The GI-tagged Gir Kesar grows in the Girnar foothills near Junagadh. It was grown in 1931 by Junagadh Wazir Sale Bhai, and in 1934 the Nawab of Junagadh named it Kesar (saffron) for the colour of its pulp. Gir Kesar received GI status in 2011.

Like all Kesar, it is prized for bright saffron-orange pulp, a rich perfumed aroma, and smooth, low-fibre flesh, often called the queen of mangoes in Gujarat. Crops typically reach market around April and May.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Gir Kesar
  • American Gardener: Kesar Mangoes

Kaew

Kaew (also spelt Keaw or Kaeo) is a Thai cultivar grown widely across the country and is one of the most important mangoes for Thai processing, used heavily for pickling and preserved products.

It is typically eaten while still green and unripe, when the small to medium green fruit has a crunchy, firm texture and a refreshing sourness, often served raw with chilli and salt rather than ripened for sweetness.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: List of mango cultivars
  • Chaichada: Mango (Kaew)
  • Fruit quality and antioxidant capacity of six Thai mango cultivars (ScienceDirect)

Kalapadi

Kalapadi (also spelt Kalapad or Kalapady) is a traditional South Indian cultivar from the orchards around Salem in Tamil Nadu, and is closely tied to the Dharmapuri, Krishnagiri and Salem mango belts. Less famous than cultivars such as Banganapalli, it has long been a favoured home garden and regional market mango across southern India.

The fruit is small to medium and oval to oblong, easy to eat out of hand. Beneath the thick skin the flesh is dark orange to yellow-orange, dense and aqueous, with a tender, succulent, soft texture and very little fibre. It is prized as one of the sweeter, juicier southern Indian mangoes, eaten fresh rather than processed.

2 sources
  • Specialty Produce: Kalapadi Mangoes
  • Salem Mango: Kalapadi Mango

Kasturi

Kasturi is not a selection of the common mango but a distinct species, Mangifera casturi, native to the Kalimantan region of Borneo, where it is a mascot of South Kalimantan province. The IUCN lists it as extinct in the wild, surviving only in cultivation, after deforestation linked to mining and palm oil destroyed its native habitat.

The fruit is small, usually under 80 grams, ovoid, with thin green skin speckled and flushed purple-black to maroon, and young leaves that emerge dark purple. The dark orange flesh is intensely fragrant and somewhat fibrous, with a flavour that is less sweet but stronger and more aromatic than the common mango. Its seeds are polyembryonic.

2 sources
  • Mangifera casturi, Wikipedia
  • Casturi Mango, Slow Food Foundation Ark of Taste

Keitt

Keitt is a Florida cultivar that arose as a seedling on the property of Mrs J. N. Keitt at Homestead, Florida, around 1939. Unlike many Asian mangoes it has a monoembryonic seed.

It is one of the most valued late-season mangoes, often the last picked, with large oval green fruit that may carry a light blush. The flesh is firm, juicy and notably low in fibre with a sweet, mildly tangy flavour.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Keitt (mango)
  • Specialty Produce: Keitt Mangoes (variety database)

Kensington Pride

Kensington Pride, also called Bowen mango or Pride of Bowen, originated from a tree in Bowen, Queensland in the late 1880s and is Australia's most widely grown commercial mango. Its polyembryonic seed (multiple seedlings per stone) points to a South-East Asian background.

The fruit has a yellow to orange-yellow flesh with little to moderate fibre, and a sweet, aromatic, slightly spicy flavour that made it the benchmark Australian mango. It ripens through the spring and summer season.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Kensington Pride
  • University of Florida IFAS Mango: Kensington Pride

Kent

Kent is a late-season Florida mango planted on 1 January 1933 by Leith D. Kent in Coconut Grove. It came from a seedling of the Brooks cultivar and is generally regarded as a Brooks by Haden cross, a parentage supported by a 2005 pedigree analysis. The tree first fruited in 1938 and the cultivar was named in 1945. The seed is monoembryonic.

Kent is known for sweet, rich flavour and smooth, nearly fibreless flesh. The oval fruit ripens to a greenish-yellow with some red blush. The tree is susceptible to anthracnose and the fruit has a relatively short shelf life, yet Kent became one of the most widely planted commercial mangoes in the Western Hemisphere, especially across Latin America.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia, Kent (mango)
  • UF/IFAS Mango Science, Kent phenology

Keow Savoey

Keow Savoey (also spelled Khiew Sawoey, meaning roughly green eat) is regarded as one of the premier green eating mangoes of Thailand. Rather than waiting for it to soften, the fruit is picked mature but still green and eaten crisp, when it is often compared to a juicy granny smith apple.

It keeps a bright green skin even when mature, with thin, easily peeled skin and firm yellow flesh that has little fibre. The flavour is crisp and sweet with a fresh, slightly citrus note. It is commonly shredded into Thai green mango salads such as som tum.

3 sources
  • RFCA Archives: The Green Thai Mangoes
  • Top Tropicals: Mango Varieties Guide
  • SAG Thai Fruit Exporter: Keow Savoey

Kesar

Kesar is the famous Gujarat cultivar, with the GI-tagged Gir Kesar grown in the foothills of Girnar near Junagadh. It was grown in 1931 by Junagadh Wazir Sale Bhai, and the name dates to 1934 when the Nawab of Junagadh, on seeing the orange pulp, declared it Kesar (saffron in Gujarati and Hindi). Gir Kesar earned GI status in 2011.

Kesar is known for its bright, saffron-orange pulp and an intense, perfumed aroma. The flesh is smooth, creamy and low in fibre, and the fruit is often called the queen of mangoes in Gujarat. Crops typically come to market around April and May.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Gir Kesar
  • American Gardener: Kesar Mangoes

King Thai

King Thai is the Australian trade name for Mahachanok (Maha Chanok), a Thai mango bred in the 20th century and named in honour of the Mahajanaka story associated with the late King Bhumibol Adulyadej. It is a hybrid of the Sunset and Nang Klang Wan varieties.

The fruit is long, slender and curved, with a yellowish skin carrying a distinctive pinkish red blush when ripe. The seed is monoembryonic. The orange yellow flesh is fibreless and sweet with a pleasant floral aroma, and it is eaten ripe rather than green.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS Mango: Mahachanok
  • Wikipedia: Mahachanok (mango)

Lady Grace

Lady Grace is an Australian cultivar. It was selected by K. Rayner and P. Lu from open-pollinated Irwin seedlings, chosen for its small tree size, precocious bearing and good fruit quality, and described in an International Society for Horticultural Science paper alongside Lady Jane.

The tree is semi-dwarf and very symmetrical, which suits smaller gardens. The fruit has red to purple skin that turns deep orange-red when ripe, with rich apricot-coloured flesh that is firm, fine-textured, juicy and free of fibre. It carries an Irwin and Kent type flavour.

2 sources
  • ISHS: Two new mango cultivars from Australia, Lady Jane and Lady Grace (Rayner & Lu, Acta Horticulturae 1183)
  • Acta Horticulturae 1183.13: Two new mango cultivars from Australia, Lady Jane and Lady Grace

Langra

Langra, also called Banarasi Langra, is a North Indian cultivar first grown around Banaras (Varanasi) in Uttar Pradesh some 250 to 300 years ago. Langra is Hindi for lame, a name traditionally linked to a lame man in whose orchard the original tree is said to have grown. The Banarasi Langra holds a Geographical Indication tag.

Its hallmark is the skin, which keeps a greenish tinge even when fully ripe, unlike most mangoes that turn yellow or orange. The pulp is lemon-yellow, juicy and sweet with a sub-acid tang. It is harvested in the second half of the season, roughly mid-June into July.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Langra
  • Mango Dose: Langra Mango Origin and Characteristics

Lemon Meringue

Lemon Meringue (also called Po Pyu Kalay) is a Myanmar cultivar, introduced to the United States in 1995 by Maurice Kong of the Miami Rare Fruit Council. Gary Zill gave it the Lemon Meringue nickname and used it as a parent in his citrus-mango breeding line (it is the seed parent of Lemon Zest and Orange Sherbet).

The small, elongated fruit ripens to a golden yellow with pale, fibreless, juicy flesh. It is prized for a mild, sugary-sweet flavour carrying a clear lemon-citrus note, one of the early citrus-flavoured mangoes in the US. It is an early-season variety (May to June) and is not regarded as a container or condo mango.

2 sources
  • Tropical Tree Guide: Lemon Meringue (Po Pyu Kalay) Mango Grow Guide
  • Tropical Acres Farms: Lemon Meringue / Po Pyu Kalay

Lemon Zest

Lemon Zest is a Florida cultivar from the breeding program of Gary Zill in Boynton Beach, selected from the Po Pyu Kalay lineage (also called Lemon Meringue). It is one of the best-known of the citrus-flavoured mangoes, with intense sweetness carrying candied orange and tangelo citrus notes. The fruit has a polyembryonic seed.

The peel is bright yellow with irregular splotches of light green, and the deep yellow to orange-yellow pulp carries some non-objectionable fibre with a pleasant aroma and rich flavour of excellent quality. Fruit typically weighs around 361 to 577 grams. It is an early to midseason cultivar on a vigorous, upright tree.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS Mango Science: Lemon Zest
  • Mangopedia: Lemon Zest

Little Gem

Little Gem is a named semi-dwarf cultivar from Gary Zill's Florida breeding program, originally numbered 29-26 and propagated at Boynton Beach. It is a seedling of Julie, with Gary the suspected pollen parent. The trees are low to moderately vigorous and compact, with a very dense canopy that can be held to a small size, which makes Little Gem a popular condo or container mango.

Little Gem is highly precocious and a heavy bearer of small, rounded fruit that turn orange near maturity, sometimes with a pinkish blush. The skin is thick and the flesh firm yet essentially fibre free, carrying a medium bodied Indian and West Indian type flavour described as a rich, gently spicy sweetness. It is a late season variety, ripening from late July into August.

2 sources
  • Tropical Tree Guide: Little Gem Mango Grow Guide
  • Crane et al, Selected mango cultivars developed by Gary Zill (FSHS), National Mango Board

Mallika

Mallika is an Indian hybrid mango, a cross of Neelum and Dasheri introduced by Dr R. N. (Ramnath) Singh. It combines Neelum's late ripening with Dasheri's sweetness and typically harvests around June to July.

The fruit is fibreless with deep, rich orange flesh and an exceptionally sweet flavour carrying citrus, melon and honey notes. It is valued both for fresh eating and for processing into juices, nectar and canned slices.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Mallika (mango)
  • J Food Sci Technol (PMC): processing of Sindura, Mallika and Totapuri cultivars

Manjeera

Manjeera is an Indian hybrid mango bred by crossing the cultivars Rumani and Neelum, released from the Fruit Research Station at Sangareddy in the Telangana region of South India. The fruit is described as attractive and large, resembling its Rumani parent, and the tree is noted as a dwarf, regular and prolific bearer that suits high density planting.

Its flesh is reported as firm, very sweet and free of fibre, traits inherited from its parent cultivars. Because the tree stays compact and crops reliably, it is favoured for closer spacing than many traditional Indian mangoes.

2 sources
  • Kisaan Helpline: Manjeera (Rumani x Neelum) mango variety
  • SKLTSHU: Fruit Research Station, Sangareddy

Manzanillo

Manzanillo (the cultivar Manzanillo-Nunez) originated as a chance seedling, of possible Indian-race ancestry, in Manzanillo, Colima, Mexico, where the tree was first observed in 1972 and later described as a new Mexican cultivar in 1984.

The fruit is large and showy, averaging about 660 g with roughly 75 percent red blush over the skin. Its flesh is almost fibreless, bright yellow and juicy, with a pleasant aroma and around 15 degrees Brix. Trees show little alternate bearing, flowering January to April and ripening June to July.

2 sources
  • EurekaMag: Manzanillo-Nunez, a new Mexican mango cultivar
  • Semantic Scholar: Manzanillo-Nunez, A New Mexican Mango Cultivar (Nunez-Elisea, Proc. FSHS)

Nam Doc Mai

Nam Doc Mai is a Thai cultivar and one of the most popular mangoes in Thailand. The fruit is elongated with a prominent beak and a yellow skin, often retaining a little green even when ripe. It has a polyembryonic seed.

It is celebrated as one of the sweetest mangoes, with very little fibre and a strong, honeyed, floral aroma. The flesh is fine-grained and silky, and the fruit is eaten both green and fully ripe.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Nam Dok Mai
  • Specialty Produce: Nam Doc Mai Mangoes

Neelam

Neelam (also spelt Neelum) is a South Indian cultivar grown commercially on a large scale, notably across Tamil Nadu and Andhra Pradesh. It is a true late-season mango, ripening well after most varieties have finished, and is a reliable, regular bearer. The seed is monoembryonic, so a seedling will not come true to the parent.

The fruit is small and oval, smooth-skinned and bright yellow when ripe, with deep yellow to orange flesh that is firm and fibreless. The flavour is rich and aromatic with a sub-acid edge and a distinct resinous, spicy note.

2 sources
  • Tropical Tree Guide: Neelum Mango Grow Guide
  • Pure Aam: Neelam Mango Complete Guide

Okrong

Okrong (also written Ok Rong or Okrung) is one of the most popular Thai dessert cultivars, a small to medium fruit with smooth green to green-yellow skin that takes on a yellow-orange hue when ripe.

It belongs to the Indo-Chinese group with a sweet, fragrant, slightly fibrous flesh, and is the variety traditionally paired with sticky rice in Thailand's classic mango sticky rice dessert. A prized regional strain, Ok Rong Damnoen from Ratchaburi, is noted for being especially sweet and fragrant.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: List of mango cultivars
  • Hua Hin Thai Culinary School: The Thai Mango
  • Holidify: Mangoes in Thailand

Orange Sherbet

Orange Sherbet is a Florida selection bred by Gary Zill at Boynton Beach (originally designated G-32). It is a seedling of Lemon Meringue and a sibling of Lemon Zest, part of Zill's citrus-flavoured mango line.

The medium oval fruit ripens yellow over orange with smooth flesh. It is celebrated for an intensely rich, sweet flavour with complex orange-citrus candy notes (often likened to an orange push pop or creamsicle) and a near-fibreless, melt-in-the-mouth texture. It ripens mid-season (June to July). Despite the small fruit, it forms a large tree and is not considered a condo mango.

2 sources
  • Tropical Tree Guide: Orange Sherbet Mango Grow Guide
  • Tropical Acres Farms: Orange Sherbet

Osteen

Osteen is a Florida mango that grew from a seed planted in 1935 on the Merritt Island property of S. A. Osteen, the first County Commissioner of Brevard County. The tree first fruited in 1940. It was reportedly a seedling of Haden, a link later supported by pedigree analysis, and it carries a monoembryonic seed.

The flesh is nearly fibreless with a mild, sweet flavour, and the oblong fruit has yellow skin that usually turns dark purple as it ripens. Though only a minor commercial mango in Florida, Osteen became the dominant variety in Spain: trade figures put it at around 85 per cent of Spanish shipments, grown mainly on the subtropical coast of Andalucia.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia, Osteen (mango)
  • Fruitrop, Spanish mango country profile

Pairi

Pairi (also spelt Payri or Paheri) is an early-season Indian cultivar associated with the Konkan coast of Maharashtra, where it ripens ahead of most other varieties. It is closely related to, and often confused with, the Raspuri mango of southern India, though many growers treat the two as distinct selections.

The fruit is prized less for sweetness than for its juicy, aromatic, somewhat fibrous pulp with a tangy edge. In western India it is a traditional base for aam ras (mango pulp), frequently blended with Alphonso. The skin turns yellow with a saffron-toned interior, and the season is short.

3 sources
  • List of mango cultivars, Wikipedia
  • Raspuri Mangoes, Specialty Produce
  • Raspuri, Wikipedia

Palmer

Palmer is a Florida mango cultivar that grew from a seed planted around 1925 on the Miami property of Victor Mell. It was first propagated in 1945 and named in 1949, and a 2005 pedigree analysis found it was most likely a seedling of Haden. The seed is monoembryonic.

It is a late season mango, ripening from about July to early September. The skin can colour up to a purple or red blush well before the flesh is ripe, which can lead to fruit being picked early. The flesh is orange-yellow and nearly fibreless with a mild, aromatic flavour. It is grown commercially in Florida, Spain, Africa and Australia.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Palmer (mango)
  • University of Florida IFAS Mango: Palmer

Parvin

Parvin is an older Florida cultivar, a chance seedling of Haden discovered in the 1940s on the property of Clint Fisk Parvin at Bradenton, Florida. It saw only limited commercial planting in Florida, but is now grown commercially in Puerto Rico and parts of Latin America.

The fruit is oval to oblong with a rounded apex and no beak, wrapped in thick, waxy skin that is yellow with a red to crimson blush over much of its surface at maturity. The deep orange flesh is sweet and rich, with little to no fibre. Parvin ripens in the mid to late summer, typically July to August in Florida.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Parvin (mango)
  • Good Fruit Guide: Parvin

Pickering

Pickering is a Florida mango prized as a condo or dwarf variety. UF/IFAS describes it as a low vigour tree, which lets it be kept compact and grown in containers, making it a favourite for small gardens. The seed is monoembryonic, so seedlings will not come true to type and trees are grafted.

Its flesh is yellow with low fibre, and the flavour is distinctive: UF/IFAS records a mild coconut-like flavour of good quality, the trait Pickering is best known for. The skin ripens to light green or light yellow with a pinkish-red splotchy blush.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS Mango Science, Pickering phenology
  • Fairchild Tropical Botanic Garden, Pickering dwarf condo mango

Pico

Pico (also spelt piko, and known as padero) is a Philippine cultivar that, alongside the Carabao, is one of the most widely grown mangoes in the Philippines, generally ranked second to it commercially. Unlike the monoembryonic Indian types, Pico is polyembryonic, so a single seed can raise several seedlings that come true to the parent.

The fruit is elongated and noticeably flattened compared with the Carabao, with a distinct beak at the apex. The flesh is light orange-yellow and fibrous, with a classic sweet mango taste. It ripens through the middle of the year, roughly May to July. The fibrous flesh and beaked shape are its clearest points of difference from the smoother, plumper Carabao.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Pico (mango)
  • Mango ISP, PCAARRD (DOST, Philippine Government)

R2E2

R2E2 is a Queensland Department of Primary Industries selection raised from seedlings of the Florida cultivar Kent at the Bowen research station (planted 1978). It keeps the code name R2E2 that the original seedling was given in the research program.

It is prized for large to very large, well-coloured fruit with a yellow-green skin and orange-red blush, firm flesh, and a sweet flavour. It is a mid-to-late season mango, cropping a couple of weeks after Kensington Pride, and stores and travels well.

2 sources
  • Rare Fruit Council Australia archive: R2E2, a new mango cultivar
  • Business Queensland (DAF): R2E2 mango variety

Red Mulgoba

Mulgoba (also spelt Mulgoa or Malgova) is a classic mango from South India, grown mainly in Tamil Nadu, Kerala and Karnataka. Twelve grafted trees were sent from India to the United States in 1889, where the cultivar became prized for its eating quality and colour. The Florida grown fruit is yellow with a crimson blush, and the Red Mulgoba name reflects this reddish skin colour.

The flesh is yellow, soft and fibreless with a rich, spicy and sweet flavour, and it ripens late in the season. Mulgoba is historically important as a parent of the Haden mango (a Mulgoba seedling), which in turn founded much of the Florida mango lineage.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Mulgoba
  • Wikipedia: List of mango cultivars

Sabre

Sabre is a South African mango best known not as dessert fruit but as a rootstock. Its seed is polyembryonic, meaning seedlings come up largely true to type, which makes it valuable for raising uniform nursery trees. It is the most widely used rootstock in South Africa.

The fruit itself is a traditional fibre mango: small to medium, oblong, with attractive pink to red blushed skin over yellow, and notably fibrous flesh of modest eating quality. As a rootstock it tends toward low vigour, producing smaller canopies, with a dwarfing effect reported in trials in Israel and South Africa.

2 sources
  • Mango Rootstocks Literature Review (mango.org / National Mango Board)
  • South Africa Mango Cultivars

Saigon

Saigon originated in Vietnam, and its seeds were shipped to the USDA plant introduction station in Miami, Florida, in 1902, making it one of the early founding mangoes of Florida horticulture. It later became a parent of other cultivars, including Florigon, whose name blends Florida and Saigon.

The seed is polyembryonic, a South East Asian trait. The skin is greenish yellow to bright yellow, rarely with a pink blush, and the pale yellow flesh is completely fibreless. The flavour is mild and pleasantly sweet, and it ripens early to mid season.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS Mango: Saigon
  • Wikipedia: Saigon (mango)

Spirit of '76

Spirit of '76 originated in south Florida on the property of Laurence Zill at Boynton Beach, where the original tree first fruited during the US bicentennial, giving it its name. A 2005 pedigree analysis estimated it to be a Haden by Zill cross.

The oblong fruit averages about a pound, with yellow flesh and a bright red blush that develops well before harvest. It is completely fibreless, soft, smooth and very sweet, with a rich aromatic mango flavour balanced by acidity and notes of peach and nectarine. It ripens June to July in Florida. Its soft flesh kept it from commercial acceptance, so it remains a dooryard variety.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Spirit of '76 (mango)
  • Tropical Tree Guide: Spirit of 76 Mango Grow Guide

Sweet Tart

Sweet Tart is a boutique Florida cultivar from Gary Zill's breeding program at Boynton Beach. The fruit is small to medium, oblong ovoid with a prominent beak, and is grown for its flavour rather than its size.

Its signature is a rich, complex flavour that swings from tart to very sweet, an intense Southeast Asian style profile that, as UF/IFAS notes, intensifies the longer the fruit hangs on the tree. The acidity gives it a candy like, almost cola syrup character when fully ripe. It is a mid season mango.

2 sources
  • Crane, mango cultivars developed by Gary Zill (FSHS / mango.org)
  • Specialty Produce, Sweet Tart Mangoes

Taiwan Gold

Taiwan Gold is better known as Golden Queen or Jin Huang, a Taiwanese cultivar reported to be a hybrid of the Ivory and Keitt mangoes. It also circulates in Southeast Asia under names such as Kiew Yai, and is now grown commercially in Thailand and the Philippines as well as Taiwan.

The fruit is notably large and oblong, with skin that turns radiant golden yellow at maturity, sometimes with a pinkish blush near the stem. The flesh is deep yellow, creamy and fibreless. The flavour sits in the Thai class, sweet with floral and honey notes, and the variety is valued for vigorous, productive growth.

2 sources
  • Golden Queen, Mangopedia
  • Jinhuang Mangoes, Specialty Produce

Titan

Titan is an Australian mango bred by hobbyist breeder Gordon Tait (also spelled Tate) on his property in the Bundaberg region of southern Queensland. It is grown by collectors as much for novelty as for fruit, and a community planting describes the variety as huge in every part: the leaves, the branches and the fruit.

The fruit is notably large, commonly over a kilogram and reported up to two or three kilograms, with juicy, low-fibre flesh. Nursery sources describe its flavour as sitting between Kensington Pride and R2E2. Mature trees carry very long leaves.

3 sources
  • Lennox Community Gardens: Mango Kasturi & Titan
  • Ross Creek Tropicals: Titan Mango
  • Ladybird Nursery: Giant Titan Mango

Tommy Atkins

Tommy Atkins is the dominant commercial mango of the Americas. The original tree reportedly grew from a Haden seed planted around 1922 on the property of Thomas H. Atkins in Broward County, Florida. The Florida Mango Forum's variety committee rejected it during the 1950s for unremarkable eating quality and considerable fibre.

It succeeded commercially anyway on the strength of its appearance and durability. The large oval fruit has dark red skin with green and yellow accents, firm fibrous flesh, and a mild, sweet flavour. Its long shelf life and tolerance of handling and transport made it the most widely planted commercial mango, a position it still holds.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia, Tommy Atkins (mango)
  • South Africa Mango Cultivars

Tong Dum

Tong Dum (also written Thong Dam) is a Thai cultivar whose name translates as 'black gold', a reference to its unusually dark green skin that stays green even when the fruit is fully mature.

Despite the green exterior, the flesh ripens to a yellow-orange and is eaten ripe as a sweet dessert mango. It belongs to the Indo-Chinese group and is one of the traditional varieties served at peak ripeness, including at the Chachoengsao mango festival.

4 sources
  • Wikipedia: List of mango cultivars
  • Hua Hin Thai Culinary School: The Thai Mango
  • Bangkok Post: In praise of mangoes
  • Holidify: Mangoes in Thailand

Totapuri

Totapuri is a South Indian cultivar known for its oblong fruit with a prominent, pointed beak at the non-stem end. The name translates as parrot face (gini meaning parrot), and it carries synonyms including Bangalora, Sandersha, Ginimoothi and Kili Mooku. It was imported to Florida as Sandersha in 1901 and again as Totapuri in the 1960s.

It is the main processing variety, valued for high pulp recovery, a stable bright yellow colour and balanced sugar and acidity that give a sweet-tart taste. Most Totapuri is processed into pulp, juice and canned slices rather than eaten fresh. Notably, its skin lacks the usual bitterness, so it is often eaten with the flesh.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Totapuri
  • J Food Sci Technol (PMC): processing of Sindura, Mallika and Totapuri cultivars

Valencia pride

Valencia Pride is a Florida cultivar grown from a Haden mango seed planted in 1937 on the property of Charles Brown in Miami, with the tree first fruiting in 1941. A 2005 pedigree analysis confirmed Haden as the parent. The fruit contains a monoembryonic seed in an elongated husk. The mango has a distinctive long, slender sigmoid shape with a rounded base and apex and a large apical beak.

It is a late-season cultivar, ripening from roughly July to August in Florida. The skin is yellow, much of it usually covered in a brilliant crimson blush, and the flesh is yellow, nearly fibreless, firm and juicy with a sweet flavour and aroma. The tree is a very vigorous, fast grower with a large, open, spreading canopy, with a reputation as one of the fastest-growing Florida mangoes.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Valencia Pride
  • Specialty Produce: Valencia Pride Mangoes

Van Dyke

Van Dyke arose from a seed planted around the 1930s on the property of Madeline Van Dyke in Miami, Florida, fruiting in the 1940s. A 2005 pedigree analysis estimated Haden as its likely parent, and the variety carries a monoembryonic seed.

The fruit is oval with smooth yellow skin and a bright red blush, with yellow, near fibreless flesh and a rich, sweet flavour and aroma. It ripens from June to July in Florida. Better suited to export than the home Florida market, it found commercial success in Brazil and eastern Africa.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Van Dyke (mango)
  • Top Tropicals: Mangifera indica Van Dyke

Willard

Willard is a native Sri Lankan mango recommended by the country's Department of Agriculture for cultivation in the dry zone. It is a round fruit with a soft skin that carries a red blush, the peel being yellow with red colour towards the stem end, a look prized in markets that favour red toned mangoes.

The flesh is low in fibre and the variety is widely appreciated in Sri Lanka for its distinctive taste and aroma. It is one of the island's better known dessert mangoes, valued for fruit quality alongside cultivars such as Karthacolomban and Velleicolomban.

2 sources
  • Department of Agriculture Sri Lanka: Fruit Crops, Mango
  • ISHS: Fruit Quality of Karthacolomban, Ampalavi and Willard Mangoes in Jaffna, Sri Lanka

Zill

Zill is a Florida mango that grew from a Haden seed planted in 1922 by Carl King of Lake Worth. The tree first fruited in 1930, and Laurence Zill began propagating it in 1940, with the cultivar named and described in 1945. A 2005 pedigree study suggests it may be a cross of Haden and Bombay. The seed is monoembryonic.

The flesh is yellow and fibreless, with a sweet flavour and strong aroma sometimes compared to pineapple. The thin skin is yellow with a dark red blush covering much of the fruit, which averages just under a pound. In Florida it typically ripens from May to July.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia, Zill (mango)
  • Mangopedia, Zill

Mulberry

18 varieties (back to top)

Black

The black mulberry (Morus nigra) is native to south-western Asia and has been cultivated so long its exact wild range is unknown. Its fruit ripens to a dark purple, almost black, and is richly flavoured, with an intense sweetness balanced by a pleasant tartness that is generally regarded as the best tasting of the mulberries.

The berries are relatively large and juicy and stain readily, so they are usually eaten fresh or made into jams, pies and cordials rather than shipped.

2 sources

  • Daleys Fruit: Black Mulberry

Black Classic

The black mulberry (Morus nigra) is native to south-western Asia and has been cultivated so long its exact wild range is unknown. Its fruit ripens to a dark purple, almost black, and is richly flavoured, with an intense sweetness balanced by a pleasant tartness that is generally regarded as the best tasting of the mulberries.

The berries are relatively large and juicy and stain readily, so they are usually eaten fresh or made into jams, pies and cordials rather than shipped.

Black English

Black English is a common name for the true black mulberry (Morus nigra), native to south-west Asia (the highlands of Mesopotamia and Persia). It is one of the main black mulberry varieties grown in Australia, alongside Hicks Fancy.

Black mulberry produces the best-flavoured fruit of the mulberries, with large, juicy, dark purple to almost black berries that balance rich sweetness and tartness. It is prized for fresh eating and cooking, though the dark juice stains readily.

2 sources

  • SBA's Kitchen: Mulberry Hicks Fancy (Morus nigra)

Hick s Fancy

Hicks Fancy is a black mulberry (Morus nigra) and, along with Black English, is one of the main M. nigra varieties grown in Australia. It is a smaller tree than the standard black mulberry, which makes it more manageable in suburban gardens.

It is valued for very good, consistent crops of slightly smaller but still large, luscious berries. When fully ripe the fruit is very sweet and juicy with a slight tartness, picked fresh over an extended season.

Hicks Fancy

King White Shahtoot

King White is a name for Morus macroura, the shahtoot or king mulberry, native to Tibet and the Himalayas. It bears very long, pale fruit, several times the length of a common mulberry, that softens and becomes honey sweet when ripe.

The fruit is white to pale and described as honey sweet with low acidity and fruity notes. The long berries hang clear of the foliage, making them quick and easy to pick fresh.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Morus macroura
  • Specialty Produce: King White Mulberries
  • Growing Fruit forum: Morus macroura King White / Shahtoot

Pakistan

Pakistan (often sold as Pakistan Black) is a long-fruited Morus macroura, selected in Islamabad and sometimes mislabelled as Morus alba. Its berries are exceptionally long, far longer than common mulberries, and ripen from red to a dark purple, almost black.

The flavour is sweet and complex, good in the red stage as well as when fully dark. The fruit is firmer than most mulberries and reported to stain less than other dark cultivars, helped by a slightly thicker skin, making it cleaner to eat fresh.

3 sources
  • Cricket Hill Garden: Morus macroura 'Pakistan' mulberry
  • Specialty Produce: Pakistan Mulberries
  • Cultivar Guide: Mulberry ('Pakistan' Morus macroura)

Pakistan Black

Pakistan (often sold as Pakistan Black) is a long-fruited Morus macroura, selected in Islamabad and sometimes mislabelled as Morus alba. Its berries are exceptionally long, far longer than common mulberries, and ripen from red to a dark purple, almost black.

Red Shahtoot

Red Shahtoot is a long-fruited mulberry of the Morus macroura group (the shahtoot or king mulberries), native to Tibet and the Himalayas. Its berries are notably elongated, far longer than common mulberries, and ripen to a deep red.

Unlike the white shahtoot, the red shahtoot has red fruit that will stain. Shahtoot mulberries are described as honey sweet, with the flavour rated close to that of the black mulberry, and the long berries make for easy fresh picking.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Morus macroura
  • Gallifrey Forest Farm: Plant profile, mulberry
  • Daleys Fruit: Mulberry Dwarf Red Shahtoot (Morus macroura)

Tree Red Shahtoot

Tree White King Shahtoot

Weeping

Weeping mulberry is Morus alba 'Pendula', a dwarf, weeping cultivar of the Chinese white mulberry. It is typically top-grafted onto a standard trunk, with gnarled branches that cascade to the ground, and is grown chiefly as an ornamental feature tree.

It stays small, around two to three metres, suiting courtyards and small gardens. Female trees still set sweet, blackberry-like fruit, though the weeping form is valued mainly for its sculptural, cascading habit rather than a heavy crop.

2 sources
  • Missouri Botanical Garden Plant Finder: Morus alba 'Pendula'
  • Wikipedia: Morus alba

White

White mulberry (Morus alba) is native to China and is the species long cultivated to feed silkworms in commercial silk production. Its fruit ranges from white to pink in cultivated plants (deep purple in the wild), unlike the dark fruit of black mulberry.

The flavour is sweet but often described as bland, with less intensity than the black or red mulberries. It is grown as much for its foliage and ornamental forms as for the fruit, which is mild and easy to eat off the tree.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Morus alba
  • Wikipedia: Morus nigra
  • Missouri Botanical Garden Plant Finder: Morus alba 'Pendula'

White Shahtoot

White Shahtoot is a long-fruited Morus macroura, native to Tibet and the Himalayas. Its berries are genuinely white or pale pink and several times longer than a common mulberry, ripening soft and honey sweet.

Because the fruit is white or pale it will not stain, which sets it apart from the red shahtoot. The flavour is honey sweet with low acidity, best eaten fresh straight from the tree.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Morus macroura
  • Gallifrey Forest Farm: Plant profile, mulberry
  • Specialty Produce: King White Mulberries

White Shahtoot / King White

The fruit is white to pale and described as honey sweet with low acidity and fruity notes. The long berries hang clear of the foliage, making them quick and easy to pick fresh.

Nectarine

22 varieties (back to top)

Arctic Rose

Arctic Rose is a white-fleshed freestone nectarine bred by Zaiger Genetics at their orchard near Modesto, California (US Plant Patent 7889). The skin is a mottled red over a chalk-white to pale-yellow ground, and the white flesh shows pale red streaks near the pit. Its flavour is sub-acid, mild and sweet.

Unusually for a white nectarine, the fruit is firm enough to be handled like a yellow-fleshed variety, and it holds firm on the tree for several days after ripening. It is self-fruitful and ripens in early to mid summer.

2 sources
  • US Plant Patent PP7889 (Arctic Rose nectarine), Zaiger et al.
  • Dave Wilson Nursery: Arctic Rose White Nectarine

Crimson Baby

Crimson Baby is a nectarine bred by David W. Ramming (with retired colleague Owen L. Tanner) at the USDA stone fruit breeding program in Fresno, California. It is an early-season variety, popular in Australia where it is widely sold in a dwarf form for pots and small gardens.

The fruit is large and round with clear yellow flesh. A bright red overcolour can tint nearly 90 percent of the skin, sometimes lightly dusted with pale speckles. It is a clingstone, so the flesh holds to the stone rather than parting freely.

2 sources
  • USDA AgResearch Magazine: Mouth-Watering New Fruit (Aug 1997)
  • Blerick Tree Farm: Nectarine Crimson Baby Dwarf

Early Rivers

Early Rivers is an old British nectarine bred in the nineteenth century by the noted nurseryman Thomas Rivers of Sawbridgeworth, Hertfordshire. It is valued as one of the best early-ripening nectarines, cropping in early to mid summer (early to mid July in the UK).

The fruit is roundish and usually large, with greenish-yellow skin almost wholly covered by a scarlet flush. The pale yellow flesh is very soft and juicy with excellent flavour. The tree is compact, bushy and self-fertile, so it fruits without a pollination partner.

2 sources
  • RHS: Prunus persica var. nectarina 'Early Rivers'
  • Pomona Fruits: Grow Your Own Peaches and Nectarines

Early Rivers High Chill

Early Rivers is an old British nectarine bred in the nineteenth century by the noted nurseryman Thomas Rivers of Sawbridgeworth, Hertfordshire. It is valued as one of the best early-ripening nectarines, cropping in early to mid summer (early to mid July in the UK).

2 sources
  • RHS: Prunus persica var. nectarina 'Early Rivers'
  • Pomona Fruits: Grow Your Own Peaches and Nectarines

Fairlane

Fairlane is a late-season, yellow-fleshed nectarine from California. The fruit is large and firm with red over yellow skin, and it is a clingstone, meaning the flesh holds tightly to the stone rather than separating cleanly.

Its very late ripening extends the nectarine season well past mid-season varieties, making it useful for spreading out a home harvest. The tree is vigorous and productive.

2 sources
  • Harvest to Table: Nectarine varieties
  • Battistini Vivai: Fairlane nectarine

Fantasia

Fantasia is a popular yellow-fleshed nectarine introduced from California breeding work. It is widely grown both commercially and in home gardens.

The fruit is large with a red-blushed yellow skin and firm, freestone yellow flesh. The flavour is sweet-tart and aromatic, becoming sweeter and juicier the longer it hangs on the tree. It ripens mid to late season and is self-fertile, so one tree will crop on its own.

3 sources
  • Stark Bro's: Fantasia Nectarine
  • One Green World: Fantasia Nectarine
  • IFA Country Stores: Nectarine Tree Varieties

Firebrite

Firebrite (also written Fire Brite) is a yellow fleshed nectarine of Californian origin, grown widely as an early to midseason commercial variety. The flesh is firm, smooth and juicy, and it is usually described as semi cling (semi freestone), so the flesh parts from the stone only fairly readily.

The skin carries a bright red blush over a golden yellow background, giving the fruit a vivid appearance. It ripens around the same window as the Redhaven peach, making it one of the earlier nectarines of its season.

2 sources
  • Harvest to Table: Nectarine Varieties
  • Grandpa's Orchard: Firebrite Nectarine

Flavortop

Flavortop is a yellow-fleshed freestone nectarine developed by the USDA in Fresno, California, and introduced in 1969. The fruit has red over yellow skin and very flavourful flesh, and it consistently rates among the top-scoring nectarines in taste comparisons. It ripens in mid to late summer.

The tree carries showy pink spring blossoms and is self-fertile, so a single tree will set fruit without a separate pollinator. Suited to fresh eating, baking and canning.

3 sources
  • NC State Extension Gardener Plant Toolbox: Prunus persica 'Flavortop'
  • Frutas-Hortalizas: Nectarine types and varieties
  • Harvest to Table: Nectarine varieties

Flavourtop

Flavortop is a yellow-fleshed freestone nectarine developed by the USDA in Fresno, California, and introduced in 1969. The fruit has red over yellow skin and very flavourful flesh, and it consistently rates among the top-scoring nectarines in taste comparisons. It ripens in mid to late summer.

Goldmine

Goldmine is a heritage white nectarine that began as a chance seedling found in an Auckland garden in New Zealand in the 1890s. It went on to become a leading commercial nectarine in both New Zealand and Australia, and is still grown today as a prized heirloom.

The fruit has red skin over a yellow-green base and aromatic, sweet, juicy white flesh. It is a freestone, so the stone lifts cleanly from the flesh, and the tree is self-fertile, meaning a single tree will set fruit on its own.

3 sources
  • Te Ara: The Encyclopedia of New Zealand, Goldmine nectarines
  • The Diggers Club: Nectarine Goldmine
  • Trees of Antiquity: Goldmine Nectarine

May grand

Maygrand (May Grand) is an early-season nectarine cultivar covered by US Plant Patent 2,794. The patent credits Frederic W. Anderson, working in an experimental orchard near Le Grand, Merced County, California, and records the parents as Red Grand crossed with Early Sun Grand nectarines.

The patent describes a productive tree bearing large, yellow-fleshed fruit of globose to oblong shape, with a yellow ground colour heavily overspread by red. It ripens very early, around ten days before the Grand River and Sunrise nectarines. May Grand later served as a parent of other early low-chill nectarines such as May Glo.

2 sources
  • US Plant Patent PP2794: Nectarine tree (Red Grand x Early Sun Grand)
  • US Plant Patent PP5245: Nectarine tree 'May Glo' (cites May Grand, PP2,794)

Maygrand

Maygrand (May Grand) is an early-season nectarine cultivar covered by US Plant Patent 2,794. The patent credits Frederic W. Anderson, working in an experimental orchard near Le Grand, Merced County, California, and records the parents as Red Grand crossed with Early Sun Grand nectarines.

Okee Dokee Flat Style

OkeeDokee is the trademark name for the cultivar Mesembrine, a flat (doughnut or saturn shaped) nectarine. It has firm, juicy yellow flesh and a sweet flavour, with red skin over a yellow background. The stone is semi freestone, so the flesh comes away from the pit fairly easily.

It is a self pollinating, midseason variety, ripening around February in Australia, and is often marketed as a neat lunchbox fruit because of its squat flat shape.

3 sources
  • Shoot Gardening: Prunus persica var. nectarina 'Mesembrine'
  • Fleming's Nurseries: OkeeDokee cv. Mesembrine Flat-style Nectarine
  • Daleys Fruit: Nectarine OkeeDokee cv. Mesembrine

OkeeDokee

OkeeDokee Mesembrine

Spicezee

Spicezee (Spice Zee NectaPlum) is a white-fleshed interspecific cross of nectarine and plum, the first NectaPlum from the Zaiger Hybrids breeding program in Modesto, California. The US plant patent (PP13503) lists Gary Zaiger, Leith Gardner and Grant Zaiger as breeders, with parents tracing to nectarine, peach and plum (including Red Beaut plum).

The fruit is a freestone with unusual maroon-red skin and white flesh, and its flavour shows traits of both parents. The tree is self-fertile and notably ornamental, opening pink blossom in spring followed by reddish new foliage that matures to green over summer. It ripens in early to mid summer.

2 sources
  • US Plant Patent PP13503: Interspecific tree 'Spicezee'
  • Dave Wilson Nursery: Spice Zee NectaPlum

Spicezee interspecific

Spicezee (Spice Zee NectaPlum) is a white-fleshed interspecific cross of nectarine and plum, the first NectaPlum from the Zaiger Hybrids breeding program in Modesto, California. The US plant patent (PP13503) lists Gary Zaiger, Leith Gardner and Grant Zaiger as breeders, with parents tracing to nectarine, peach and plum (including Red Beaut plum).

  • Dave Wilson Nursery: Spice Zee NectaPlum

Sugar and Spice interspecific

Sugar and Spice (sold as Sugar N Spice in Fleming's F2 Fruit range) is an interspecific nectarine, a cross between a nectarine and a plum, the type of hybrid often called a nectaplum. These crosses were pioneered by the Californian breeder Floyd Zaiger and combine nectarine smoothness with plum sweetness.

The fruit has crisp white flesh and a rustic red over yellow skin, with a sweet flavour balanced by mild acidity. It is a clingstone, self fertile, and ripens midsummer (around January to March in Australia).

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Nectaplum
  • Fleming's Nurseries: F2 Fruit Sugar N Spice Nectarine x Plum
  • Daleys Fruit: Sugar N Spice Nectarine x Plum

Sunlite

Sunlite is a low-chill, yellow-fleshed nectarine of US origin, registered in the South African variety system maintained by SAPO Trust. SAPO lists it as a low-chill variety with strong, semi-upright vigour, dark red skin marked with many lenticels, and fine, melting, semi-acid flesh.

It produces medium fruit (around 115 grams on SAPO's record) of long shape, and Australian growers describe it as a self-fertile, freestone variety that ripens mid-season. The low chill requirement suits coastal and warm-temperate gardens.

2 sources
  • SAPO Trust: Sunlite nectarine variety record
  • Great Southern Nurseries: Sunlite Nectarine

Sunraycer

Sunraycer is a low-chill, yellow-fleshed nectarine released in 1993 and documented by the University of Florida (UF/IFAS), which lists it as a non-patented melting-flesh cultivar with a chill requirement of about 275 chill units. That low requirement makes it suited to warm-winter and subtropical districts where standard nectarines struggle.

UF/IFAS records describe large, semi-clingstone fruit with an extensive bright red blush over a yellow ground colour and good resistance to bacterial spot. It is an early-ripening variety, and Australian growers report a self-fertile tree fruiting in the low-chill season from spring into early summer.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS MG374: Florida Peach and Nectarine Varieties
  • Daleys Fruit: Nectarine Sunraycer

Trixzie Nactazee

Trixzie Nectazee is the Australian (Flemings) brand name for Necta Zee, a genetic-dwarf yellow nectarine from Floyd Zaiger's breeding program in Modesto, California (US plant patent PP6283). It was selected from a cross involving Early Sun Grand nectarine and a dwarf nectarine seedling, and its compact size is genetic rather than rootstock-driven.

The tree stays naturally small, around 1.5 to 2 metres, which suits pots and small gardens, yet it carries full-size fruit. The nectarines are yellow-fleshed and freestone with red skin. It is self-fertile, so a single tree will set fruit, and it ripens in summer.

4 sources
  • US Plant Patent PP6283: Nectarine tree 'Necta Zee'
  • Dave Wilson Nursery: Necta Zee Miniature Nectarine
  • Flemings Nurseries: Trixzie Nectazee Miniature Nectarine
  • Daleys Fruit: Dwarf Nectarine Trixzie Nectazee

Trixzie Nectazee
