Olive

15 varieties (back to top)

Arbequina

Arbequina is a small-fruited Spanish oil olive from Catalonia, named after the village of Arbeca and historically linked to Aragon and Andalusia. It is grown as a primary oil cultivar worldwide and has become a mainstay of intensive, super high density plantings thanks to its weak vigour and early entry into production. The IOC catalogue lists it as self-compatible, so a single tree can set fruit without a pollinating partner.

Its oil is prized for being soft and approachable: buttery, fruity, and very mild, low in bitterness and pungency. The trade-off is low oxidative stability and a shorter shelf life than more robust varieties, a consequence of low polyphenol levels. Although occasionally sold as a table olive, Arbequina is grown mostly for its high oil yield.

2 sources
  • IOC World Catalogue of Olive Varieties: Arbequina
  • Wikipedia: Arbequina

Arbosana

Arbosana is a Spanish oil olive native to the Penedes area of Catalonia, where it was once so scarce that only a couple of hundred trees remained in the 1990s. Like its Catalan relative Arbequina, it has a compact, low-vigour habit that suits it perfectly to hedgerow and super high density orchards with mechanical harvesting, and it carries higher planting densities than most other varieties while staying highly and consistently productive.

It is grown for oil and comes into bearing very early. The oil is well regarded for its organoleptic qualities, with a green, fresh-cut profile when picked early. Its reduced vigour has also made it a valued low-vigour parent in international olive breeding programmes.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Arbosana
  • Livita+ / Buccelletti: Arbosana olive tree profile

Ascolana Tenera

Ascolana Tenera is the celebrated large green table olive of Ascoli Piceno in Italy's Marche region, known since Roman times. The fruit is very large with tender, meaty, mild flesh, and it is picked green before full ripening.

It is the variety behind olive all'ascolana, the regional speciality where the olives are pitted, stuffed with seasoned minced meat, breaded, and deep fried. Both the table olive and the stuffed dish carry protected geographic status, underlining the variety's strong link to its home district.

2 sources
  • CooksInfo: Ascolane Olives
  • Wikipedia: Olive all'ascolana

Azapa

Azapa is a Chilean table olive grown in the Azapa Valley of northern Chile, descended from the Spanish Sevillana variety brought to the region in the 16th century. It is harvested as a violet to black olive and ranges from medium to large.

The fruit has a thin skin over very fleshy meat, giving a high flesh to pit ratio that suits it well for table use. It is traditionally prepared in a simple water and salt brine to draw out bitterness before being conserved for table consumption.

2 sources
  • Slow Food Foundation, Ark of Taste: Azapa Olive
  • RecipeTips: Azapa Olive

Barouni

Barouni is a Tunisian heirloom variety famous for producing exceptionally large fruit, counted among the largest table olives in the world. It is one of the handful of cultivars long grown commercially for table olives in California.

It is valued chiefly as a table olive, with the big, meaty fruit picked and cured rather than pressed for oil. Its size and firm flesh make it a popular pickling olive, well suited to hand harvesting.

2 sources
  • LizPlants growing guide: Barouni Olive
  • Wikipedia: Mission olive (lists Barouni among California table cultivars)

coratina

Coratina is the great oil olive of Puglia in southern Italy, taking its name from the town of Corato near Bari. It is one of the region's most important cultivars, with deep roots in the local olive-growing tradition. The tree is of weak to medium vigour and cold tolerant, though the IOC catalogue notes it has low self-fertility, so cross-pollination helps fruit set.

Its signature is an oil exceptionally rich in polyphenols, among the highest of any variety, which gives an intensely fruity, bitter, and pungent taste with a peppery throat catch. Fruit ripening and oil accumulation are late and staggered. The oil also carries a high content of oleic acid, and it is grown for oil rather than the table.

2 sources
  • IOC World Catalogue of Olive Varieties: Coratina
  • Wikipedia: Coratina

Correggiola

Correggiola is a Tuscan oil olive long considered the same as Frantoio, and the two are extremely close, though it carries a slightly different genetic fingerprint and ripens later. It is also known as Raggiolo and is grown across Tuscany, Umbria, and the Marche.

It produces small to medium fruit with a high oil content, yielding a spicy, fruity oil. In Australia it is one of the leading oil varieties chosen for commercial groves, valued for heavy cropping and quality oil.

2 sources
  • CooksInfo: Correggiolo Olives
  • Fruit Tree Lane: Olive variety selection for commercial groves

frantoio

Frantoio is a classic Tuscan oil olive, one of the principal raw materials behind central Italian oils and now extensively grown across Tuscany, Umbria, Liguria, and Puglia. The tree is strong-growing with a spreading habit. The IOC catalogue records a high rate of self-fertility, and Frantoio is widely valued as a reliable pollinator for other cultivars, so it often anchors mixed plantings.

The oil is its calling card: fresh and green-fruity with a fuller, more bitter and peppery finish than milder varieties, carried by a high content of oleic acid and total phenols. Fruit ripening is medium-late and staggered, which helps spread the harvest. It is an oil cultivar rather than a table olive.

2 sources
  • IOC World Catalogue of Olive Varieties: Frantoio
  • Wikipedia: Frantoio

Kalamata

Kalamata is a Greek table olive cultivar from the Messenia region of the southern Peloponnese, around the city of Kalamata. The fruit is large, almond-shaped and a deep purple-black, harvested fully ripe in late autumn rather than green.

Because the raw fruit is bitter, Kalamata olives are cured in brine and typically preserved in wine vinegar or olive oil, giving them their meaty texture and rich, savoury flavour. In the EU the name carries Protected Designation of Origin (PDO) status.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Kalamata olive
  • MANI Bio: Kalamata Olives origin and characteristics

Koroneiki

Koroneiki is Greece's most widespread oil olive, the backbone of Greek production, with most plantings in Crete and the Peloponnese. Its fruit is notably small (low weight, moderately elongated), but it is highly and consistently productive and prized for high oil yield. It is grown exclusively for oil, not as a table olive, and ripens early to intermediate in the season.

The oil is celebrated for a very high content of oleic acid and a strong stability index, so it keeps well. Flavour tends green and herbaceous with a peppery, pleasantly bitter edge driven by its high polyphenol content. Early-harvest Koroneiki oils push those phenolic and aromatic qualities further.

2 sources
  • IOC World Catalogue of Olive Varieties: Koroneiki
  • Wikipedia: Koroneiki

Leccino

Leccino is one of Italy's most widely planted oil olives, native to Tuscany and closely related to the Frantoio cultivar. It ripens early, with fruit colour and oil accumulation developing ahead of many other Italian varieties.

Its oil is prized for being mild, delicate, and low in bitterness, which is exactly why growers often blend it with stronger cultivars such as Frantoio, Coratina, Moraiolo, and Pendolino to build flavour. The same gentleness makes single-variety Leccino oil approachable and smooth.

2 sources
  • IOC World Catalogue of Olive Varieties: Leccino
  • Wikipedia: Leccino

Manzanillo

Manzanillo (Manzanilla) is a Spanish olive cultivar originating around Seville. It is a dual-purpose variety, valued both as a table olive and for oil, with medium to large, rounded fruit.

It is one of the most widely grown table olives and is a leading commercial table-olive variety in California, prized for its plump flesh and high flesh-to-pit ratio.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Manzanilla olive
  • Novavine: Olive Varieties

mission

Mission is the heritage olive developed in California by Spanish missions along El Camino Real in the late 18th century, and it is the only American cultivar listed in the International Olive Council's world catalogue. Recent DNA work suggests it may be related to the Moroccan variety Picholine Marocaine.

It is a dual-purpose olive, used both as a ripe black table olive and for oil. In Western Australia the Mission name is linked to New Norcia, the Benedictine monastery town where monks have grown olives from Spanish cuttings since the 1850s and still press an award-winning oil.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Mission olive
  • Northern Valleys News: Hand-picked heritage (New Norcia olives)

Picholine

Picholine is the classic French table olive, originally from the Gard region in southern France and now common across Provence. The fruit is medium, ovoid, and slightly asymmetrical, picked while still bright green.

It is best known as a cocktail olive: lye cured then fermented in brine, giving a crisp, firm bite and a lightly salty, lemony character. Picholine is dual purpose, also pressed for a fruity oil with a hint of bitterness, though it is celebrated far more as a table olive.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Picholine
  • Moulin du Calanquet: Picholine

picual

Picual is the dominant oil olive of Andalusia in southern Spain, centred on the province of Jaen, and the most widely planted olive variety in Spain. Easy to cultivate and heavy bearing, it is grown for oil rather than as a table fruit.

Its oil is prized for stability: a high oleic acid content and strong resistance to oxidation give it a long shelf life. The flavour is robust and slightly bitter, with green, grassy, and woody notes, though quality drops noticeably if the fruit is left to over-ripen before pressing.

2 sources
  • IOC World Catalogue of Olive Varieties: Picual
  • Wikipedia: Picual

Orange

43 varieties (back to top)

Arnold Blood

Arnold blood orange is an Australian blood orange named after Mike Arnold, a citrus grower at Waikerie in the Riverland region of South Australia. Arnold had a particular interest in niche varieties, and the blood orange that carries his family name became one of his successes. He was inducted into the Citrus Australia Hall of Fame in 2023.

Like other blood oranges it has red, anthocyanin-pigmented flesh that develops its colour in suitable cool-night, Mediterranean-style climates. It is reported to be a sport of the Moro blood orange and is one of the better-known blood oranges in Australian markets.

2 sources
  • Tree Crop: Visionary Mike Arnold in Citrus Hall of Fame
  • Specialty Produce: Arnold Blood Oranges

Bergamot

Bergamot (Citrus bergamia) is not a true sweet orange but a probable hybrid of lemon and bitter orange. It is grown mainly along the Ionian coast of Reggio di Calabria in Italy, where most of the world's bergamot is produced, and its aromatic rind oil flavours Earl Grey and Lady Grey teas.

The fruit is grown for its fragrant oil rather than for eating. The juice tastes less sour than lemon but more bitter than grapefruit, and the fruit is not suitable for raw consumption. Bergamot oil is also one of the most widely used ingredients in perfumery.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Bergamot orange
  • Wikipedia: Earl Grey tea

Blood

Blood oranges owe their crimson flesh to anthocyanins, a family of pigments common in flowers and many fruits but unusual in citrus. The colour develops when fruit ripens under low night temperatures, so the deepest colour comes from regions with cool nights. The pigment can keep accumulating in cold storage after harvest.

Flavour is distinctive, with a raspberry-like note alongside the usual citrus character. Flesh ranges from orange streaked with red to deep crimson, and sometimes the rind shows dark colouring too. The main cultivars are Moro, Tarocco and Sanguinello, long associated with Sicily and the Mediterranean.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Blood orange
  • Specialty Produce: Sicilian Blood Oranges

Blood Arnold

Arnold blood orange is an Australian blood orange named after Mike Arnold, a citrus grower at Waikerie in the Riverland region of South Australia. Arnold had a particular interest in niche varieties, and the blood orange that carries his family name became one of his successes. He was inducted into the Citrus Australia Hall of Fame in 2023.

Like other blood oranges it has red, anthocyanin-pigmented flesh that develops its colour in suitable cool-night, Mediterranean-style climates. It is reported to be a sport of the Moro blood orange and is one of the better-known blood oranges in Australian markets.

2 sources
  • Tree Crop: Visionary Mike Arnold in Citrus Hall of Fame
  • Specialty Produce: Arnold Blood Oranges

Blood Maltese

The Maltese blood orange (Maltaise Sanguine, also Maltaise demi sanguine) is a Mediterranean blood orange most associated with Tunisia, where it is prized as a top eating orange despite the name pointing to Malta. It is a half-blood (demi-sanguine) type: it develops red blood colour mainly on the side of the fruit exposed to the sun, so the flesh ranges from lightly to moderately pigmented rather than deep crimson throughout.

The flesh is tender, juicy and virtually seedless, with a sweetness balanced by gentle acidity that has earned it the nickname Queen of Oranges. The skin is deep orange, thick but easy to peel, and the fruit is relatively small. It is a late-season orange, typically picked around March and April, with most production exported to France.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Maltaise demi sanguine blood orange
  • Good Fruit Guide: Maltaise Sanguine

Blood Tarocco Ippolito

Tarocco is a famous Italian blood orange, widely regarded as one of the sweetest and most flavourful blood oranges and a leading table orange in Italy. It is often called a 'half-blood' because its flesh shows less red pigmentation than Moro or Sanguinello, and at Riverside the rind develops very little colour. It is essentially seedless.

The red colour comes from anthocyanins (the same pigments in pomegranates) and develops properly only with chilly winter nights, so colour varies by climate. At Riverside the fruit ripens around December to February, similar to Moro. The tree is vigorous but only moderately productive.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Tarocco blood orange
  • Wikipedia: Blood orange

Blood Tarocco Rosso

Tarocco is a famous Italian blood orange, widely regarded as one of the sweetest and most flavourful blood oranges and a leading table orange in Italy. It is often called a 'half-blood' because its flesh shows less red pigmentation than Moro or Sanguinello, and at Riverside the rind develops very little colour. It is essentially seedless.

The red colour comes from anthocyanins (the same pigments in pomegranates) and develops properly only with chilly winter nights, so colour varies by climate. At Riverside the fruit ripens around December to February, similar to Moro. The tree is vigorous but only moderately productive.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Tarocco blood orange
  • Wikipedia: Blood orange

Cara Cara

Cara Cara is a pink-fleshed navel orange that arose as a bud mutation on a Washington navel tree, discovered in 1976 at Hacienda Cara Cara in Venezuela. Most tree and fruit traits reflect its Washington navel ancestry, but the flesh is deep pink. Unlike blood oranges, which get their colour from anthocyanins, the pink comes from the carotenoid lycopene.

The fruit is seedless, sweet and notably low in acid, with the rich navel flavour plus berry-like notes often described as cranberry or rose. It is a mid-to-late season orange, ripe at Riverside roughly from November to January.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Cara Cara navel orange
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Cara Cara navel orange

Cara Cara Blood Navel

Cara Cara is a pink-fleshed navel orange that arose as a bud mutation on a Washington navel tree, discovered in 1976 at Hacienda Cara Cara in Venezuela. Most tree and fruit traits reflect its Washington navel ancestry, but the flesh is deep pink. The fruit is seedless, sweet and notably low in acid, with the rich navel flavour plus berry-like notes often described as cranberry or rose. It is a mid-to-late season orange, ripe at Riverside roughly from November to January.

  • Wikipedia: Cara Cara navel orange
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Cara Cara navel orange

Cara Cara Navel

Cara Cara is a pink-fleshed navel orange that arose as a bud mutation on a Washington navel tree, discovered in 1976 at Hacienda Cara Cara in Venezuela. Most tree and fruit traits reflect its Washington navel ancestry, but the flesh is deep pink. Unlike blood oranges, which get their colour from anthocyanins, the pink comes from the carotenoid lycopene.

The fruit is seedless, sweet and notably low in acid, with the rich navel flavour plus berry-like notes often described as cranberry or rose. It is a mid-to-late season orange, ripe at Riverside roughly from November to January.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Cara Cara navel orange
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Cara Cara navel orange

Chinotto

Chinotto is the myrtle-leaved orange (Citrus myrtifolia), a sour citrus whose small leaves resemble common myrtle. It is a compact, thornless tree growing to about three metres and is found in Malta, Libya, southern France and Italy (especially Liguria around Savona, and also Tuscany, Sicily and Calabria).

The small, bitter fruit is not eaten fresh but is prized as a flavouring. It is an essential ingredient of most Italian amari and of Campari, lends its name to chinotto soft drinks, and is also made into jams, candied fruit and syrups.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Citrus myrtifolia
  • Wikipedia: Chinotto (drink)

Hamlin

Hamlin is an early-season sweet orange that began as a chance seedling in an orchard near Glenwood, Florida (planted in 1879) and was named for the owner, A. G. Hamlin. Its cold tolerance helped it rise to prominence after Florida's freeze of 1894 to 1895, and it became one of Florida's leading juice oranges.

It is one of the earliest oranges to mature, with well-coloured fruit that has very few seeds or none. The juicy flesh is sweet and low in acid, which suits it to juicing, though in humid climates the fresh-eating quality can be underwhelming.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Hamlin sweet orange
  • Specialty Produce: Hamlin Oranges

Jaffa

Jaffa is the Shamouti orange, a sweet orange that arose around the 1840s as a limb sport (bud mutation) on a local Baladi tree in an orchard near Jaffa, in what was then Ottoman Palestine. It became known as Jaffa in Europe, where the name stuck as a brand for the export trade.

The fruit is oval, medium to large, and practically seedless, with a thick, tough, leathery rind that peels easily and ships well. The flesh is sweet, fragrant and firm. It yields relatively little juice and tends toward delayed bitterness, so it suits fresh eating and storage rather than juicing. It is a midseason variety and notably cold tolerant.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Shamouti sweet orange
  • Wikipedia: Jaffa orange

Lane

Lane's Late is an Australian-bred navel, discovered in 1950 as a bud mutation of the Washington navel and named for the tree's owner. It was the first of several late-maturing Australian navel bud sports later imported into California, and is now the predominant late-season navel grown in Australia.

It matures roughly four to six weeks later than Washington navel and holds well, storing on the tree for several months after reaching maturity before quality declines. Compared with Washington navel it has a smoother peel and a slightly smaller navel, with very few or no seeds.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Lane Late navel orange
  • NSW DPI: Orange scions and varieties

  • NSW DPI: Orange scions and varieties

Navel Newhall

Newhall is an early ripening navel orange that arose as a limb sport of the Washington navel in the Duarte area of California, selected by Paul Hackney of the Newhall Land and Water Company. Like other navels it is essentially seedless. Its main appeal is timing: it ripens earlier than the standard Washington navel, reaching markets ahead of it.

Compared with Washington navel, Newhall fruit tends to be slightly smaller, more oblong, and deeper orange in both rind and flesh colour. The trees are somewhat less vigorous, with slightly darker leaves. At Riverside the fruit ripens roughly November to January.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Newhall navel orange
  • Specialty Produce: Newhall Navel Oranges

Navel Washington

The Washington Navel arose from a single bud mutation on an orange tree in Bahia, Brazil in the 1810s or 1820s. The USDA imported trees from Brazil in the 1870s, and cuttings sent to Eliza Tibbets in Riverside, California, in 1873 founded the Californian industry. Because the mutation made the fruit seedless, every tree is propagated by grafting and is effectively a clone of the original.

It is a large, seedless, easy-peeling winter orange named for the small secondary fruit (the navel) at its blossom end. The flesh is rich-flavoured and best eaten fresh, as it contains limonin that turns bitter in standing juice.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Navel orange
  • Specialty Produce: Navel Oranges

Navelina

Navelina is an early-season navel with an interesting trans-Atlantic history. It began as a budsport called Smith's Early, selected at Riverside, California around 1910, was exported to Spain in 1933, then reintroduced to California from Spain in 1990 under the Spanish name Navelina. In Portugal it is grown as Dalmau.

The fruit is slightly pear-shaped with a small navel and a very smooth, reddish orange rind. It matures very early (around November to January in California), and the juicy flesh is sweet, though less sprightly than Washington navel. Trees are not very vigorous and tend to be semi-dwarf.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Navelina navel orange
  • Specialty Produce: Navelina Oranges

Navelina 7.5 Spain

Pineapple

Pineapple is a Florida sweet orange that originated as a seedling near Citra, Florida, around 1860 on J. B. Owens' property. It was first propagated about 1873 under the name Hickory and later renamed Pineapple for its delicate fragrance. The flesh is light orange, tender, and juicy, with a rich yet sweet flavour, and it is well coloured.

It is a midseason variety and is moderately seedy, though virtually seedless limb sports have been found. After the freeze of 1894 to 1895 forced extensive replanting it became Florida's principal midseason orange. The tree is highly productive, more frost-sensitive than most, and the fruit is excellent for processing.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Pineapple sweet orange
  • US Citrus: Pineapple Orange Tree

Pineapple Sweet

Pineapple is a Florida sweet orange that originated as a seedling near Citra, Florida, around 1860 on J. B. Owens' property. 2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Pineapple sweet orange
  • US Citrus: Pineapple Orange Tree

Salustiana

Salustiana is a Spanish sweet orange that originated around 1950 as a limb sport on a Comuna (common sweet orange) tree in Enova, Valencia, named after Don Salustiano Pallas who propagated it. It is prized chiefly as a juice orange: the flesh is melting and very juicy, with a rich, sweet flavour, and the fruit is virtually seedless.

It is the earliest of the Spanish sweet orange varieties, ripening from about December, and the fruit holds well on the tree without much loss of quality. The combination of early maturity, low seed count and juice quality led to extensive planting in Spain and parts of North Africa.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Salustiana sweet orange
  • Good Fruit Guide: Salustiana

Seedless Valencia

Seedless Valencia is a Valencia selection in the UC Riverside collection (budwood taken in 1942 from a tree at Riverside) and is also sold in Australia as a bud sport of Valencia chosen for low seed count. Standard Valencias carry seeds (zero to nine per fruit), so a near-seedless Valencia is the point of difference, though the name overstates it: collection notes record variable seediness across seasons rather than a reliably seedless fruit.

Like other Valencias it is a late, summer-maturing orange grown mainly for juice, and it is a heavy cropper favoured for juicing. Compared with standard clones the fruit tends to be slightly larger, coarser and more elongate, with a pebbly rind, and it clusters on the tree.

3 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Seedless Valencia orange
  • Wikipedia: Valencia orange
  • Daleys Fruit: Orange varieties

Seville

Seville is the classic bitter (sour) orange, Citrus aurantium, not a sweet orange. Its fruit is seedy, bitter and acidic, which makes it unsuitable for eating fresh but ideal for cooking. It is the orange traditionally used for British orange marmalade, being higher in pectin than the sweet orange, so it gives a better set and a higher yield.

Beyond marmalade, the fruit is valued for its oil and juice, and the fragrant flowers are distilled to yield neroli oil and orange flower water for perfumery. At Riverside the fruit matures over winter, roughly January to March. The tree is vigorous and cold tolerant, with medium to large, round fruit and a pebbled dark orange rind.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Seville sour orange
  • Wikipedia: Bitter orange

Seville Smooth

  • Wikipedia: Bitter orange

Tarocco Blood

Tarocco is a famous Italian blood orange, widely regarded as one of the sweetest and most flavourful blood oranges and a leading table orange in Italy. It is often called a 'half-blood' because its flesh shows less red pigmentation than Moro or Sanguinello, and at Riverside the rind develops very little colour. It is essentially seedless.

The red colour comes from anthocyanins (the same pigments in pomegranates) and develops properly only with chilly winter nights, so colour varies by climate. At Riverside the fruit ripens around December to February, similar to Moro. The tree is vigorous but only moderately productive.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Tarocco blood orange
  • Wikipedia: Blood orange

Valencia

The Valencia orange was developed in mid-19th century California by agronomist William Wolfskill on his farm in the Santa Ana area, and named after Valencia, Spain, for that city's reputation for sweet oranges. It went on to become the most popular juice orange in the United States.

It is a late-season variety, prized worldwide as the orange in season through summer (the Florida harvest runs roughly March to June). The fruit is thin-skinned, very juicy and sweet, the mainstay orange for juicing, and can carry anywhere from zero to about nine seeds.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Valencia orange
  • Specialty Produce: Valencia Oranges

Valencia Delta

Delta (Delta Seedless) is a Valencia orange selection that arose in South Africa, found in 1952 as a dooryard seedling of presumed Valencia parentage near Rustenburg. It is grown as a juicing and all-round orange and is valued for being completely seedless, unlike most standard Valencias.

The fruit has an attractive smooth rind and can be slightly elongated with a shoulder on vigorous growth. It matures a little earlier than standard Valencia, allowing harvest roughly two to three weeks ahead, because its acid drops early. Soluble solids run slightly lower than other Valencias, so eating and juice quality depend on the rootstock.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Delta Valencia orange
  • Good Fruit Guide: Delta Seedless

Valencia Espalier

Valencia Late

Valencia Midnight Seedless

Midknight (often sold as Midnight or Valencia Midnight Seedless) is a South African Valencia selection. It traces to budded trees planted in 1927 at A.P. Knight's property at Addo in the Eastern Cape, where it was noticed growing among Valencia trees and later named after Knight. Its main attraction over standard Valencia is that it is virtually seedless.

The fruit is medium to large and somewhat oblong, with a very smooth rind and excellent internal quality, high juice content and good sugar levels. The rag is slightly tougher and it can be a little harder to peel. In the Southern Hemisphere it has a short late season (around August to October) that brings the citrus year to a close.

2 sources
  • UC Riverside Citrus Variety Collection: Midknight Valencia orange
  • Wikipedia: Midknight Valencia Orange

Valencia Seedless

Passionfruit

11 varieties (back to top)

Banana

Banana passionfruit is a distinct species, Passiflora tarminiana (long confused with Passiflora mollissima and P. mixta), not the round purple Passiflora edulis. The fruit is elongated, yellow to light orange and shaped like a small, straight banana, with edible orange pulp inside. It is native to the high uplands of tropical South America, cultivated in the Andes, and is sometimes used as a cold tolerant rootstock for grafting other passionfruit.

Its real notoriety is as a weed. In Hawaii the vines, known there as banana poka, smother large areas of native forest, and in New Zealand it is so invasive that selling, cultivating or distributing the plant is illegal. Seeds are spread by birds, feral pigs and people, which has helped it naturalise widely, including in Australia.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia, Passiflora tarminiana
  • New Zealand Plant Conservation Network, Passiflora tarminiana

Black

Black passionfruit is the common purple passionfruit (Passiflora edulis), the classic Australian backyard type and the most popular style grown in the country. The fruit is medium sized with deep purple to purple-black skin that wrinkles as it ripens, surrounding rich orange-yellow pulp and small black seeds.

Unlike the yellow Panama types, the purple form is self-compatible, so a single vine can set fruit on its own. It suits subtropical and temperate gardens, preferring warm, frost-free, sheltered positions, and tends to be less acidic with a richer aroma than the yellow-fruited form.

3 sources
  • Passionfruit Australia Inc - Varieties
  • Wikipedia - Passiflora edulis
  • BeeAware - Passionfruit Pollination

Giant Granadilla

Giant granadilla is Passiflora quadrangularis, a separate species from the common passionfruit and the one that produces the largest fruit of the entire Passiflora genus. The large, oblong fruit reaches up to about 30 cm long and is greenish yellow, holding numerous seeds in a subacid, mild, melon-like pulp. It is also known as barbadine, grenadine, giant tumbo and badea.

It is a fast growing perennial climber native to the Neotropics that thrives in warm, humid tropical conditions. As well as being juiced when ripe, the immature fruit is treated as a vegetable, cooked in curries or soups, which sets it apart from passionfruit grown only for their sweet pulp.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia, Passiflora quadrangularis
  • Plants For A Future, Passiflora quadrangularis

Misty Gem

Misty Gem is one of the two main purple passionfruit varieties grown commercially in Australia, alongside Sweetheart. It is a Passiflora edulis type with glossy, dark purple fruit and pulp that ranges from bright yellow to a deeper pumpkin colour, with a tangy, guava like flavour.

It is a commercial selection managed within the Australian passionfruit industry rather than a freely propagated seed line. As a purple type it benefits from the species self-compatibility, distinguishing it from the yellow Panama varieties that generally need cross-pollination.

3 sources
  • BeeAware - Passionfruit Pollination
  • Passionfruit Australia Inc - Varieties
  • Wikipedia - Passiflora edulis

Nellie Kelly

Nellie Kelly is a well known Australian grafted passionfruit brand rather than a single fruit type. The vines are grafted onto blue passionflower (Passiflora caerulea) rootstock, which makes the plant more resistant to disease, pests and frost and helps it withstand cooler temperatures, making it a popular choice for the southern states.

The brand traces back to Victoria, where Clarence Walter Kelly began grafting passionfruit in 1921. Because the rootstock is a vigorous species, suckers can shoot from below the graft and should be removed promptly, as the blue passionflower is a known spreading weed if left unchecked.

3 sources
  • Nellie Kelly - Passionfruit
  • Better Homes and Gardens - Grafted vs Non-grafted Passionfruit
  • New Idea - Tips for Growing Passionfruit

Panama Gold

Panama Gold is a large fruited, yellow skinned Panama type passionfruit, much bigger than the ordinary purple varieties. It produces golden, soft skinned fruit with juicy, aromatic pulp that is generally sweeter and less acidic than the black passionfruit.

It performs in subtropical and tropical conditions and is noted as cold hardy enough to grow as far south as Tasmania, bringing tropical growth to cooler southern gardens. As a yellow Panama type it is largely self-incompatible, so cross-pollination with another vine improves fruit set.

3 sources
  • New Idea - Tips for Growing Passionfruit
  • Wikipedia - Passiflora edulis
  • BeeAware - Passionfruit Pollination

Panama Red

Panama Red is one of the large fruited Panama style passionfruit, the warm climate group whose skin ranges from light yellow through to a rich red. It produces large, soft skinned fruit with a red to burgundy skin, and the flavour is milder and less tart than the purple black passionfruit.

Suited to backyards across southern Australia as well as subtropical and temperate regions, Panama Red is valued for its size and sweetness. As a Panama type it is largely self-incompatible, so planting more than one vine or another variety nearby helps with cross-pollination and fruit set.

4 sources
  • BeeAware - Passionfruit Pollination
  • New Idea - Tips for Growing Passionfruit
  • Passionfruit Australia Inc - Varieties
  • Wikipedia - Passiflora edulis

Pandora

Pandora is one of the Panama style passionfruit grown in Australia, classed with the yellow-skinned Passiflora flavicarpa group rather than the small black purple type. Panama style vines carry large, juicy fruit with skin ranging from light yellow through to a rich red, and Pandora itself bears red-skinned fruit. The flavour is sweeter and more tropical tasting than the common black passionfruit, which is why some growers prefer it.

Pandora is a newer selection often described as a reselection of Panama Red, and it tends to sell better than the older line. It is promoted as a cold climate passionfruit, though its skin colour can vary depending on where it is grown. Like other red types it shows good resistance to woodiness virus and fruit fly compared with yellow varieties.

3 sources
  • Passionfruit Australia Inc, Varieties
  • Sub-Tropical Fruit Club of Qld, Don Ellison talk on growing passionfruit
  • Daleys Fruit, Passionfruit Panama Red Pandora

Sweet Calabash

Sweet calabash is Passiflora maliformis, a separate species from the common passionfruit, also known as conch apple, sweet cup and wild purple passionfruit. The fruit is small and round, roughly apple sized, with skin that can be purple, yellow or green. Its standout feature is the rind, which is unusually hard and tough, harder than most passionfruit, so a hammer or similar is often needed to crack it open.

Inside is a fragrant, greyish yellow to orange pulp with small black seeds, sweet and aromatic with a flavour likened to grape. It is native to the Caribbean, Central America and northern South America, and the pulp is eaten fresh or used to flavour drinks. In Jamaica it is scooped from the shell and served with wine and sugar.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia, Passiflora maliformis
  • Top Tropicals, Passiflora maliformis (Sweet Calabash)

Sweet Granadilla

Sweet granadilla is a distinct species, Passiflora ligularis, not the common purple passionfruit (Passiflora edulis). The fruit is round to oval and ripens from green to a yellowish orange flecked with small white dots, wrapped in a hard, slippery shell with soft padding inside. The aril surrounding the black seeds is soft, jelly-like and very aromatic, with a mild, floral, sweet flavour that is gentler than the tart edulis types.

It is native to the mountains of Central and northwestern South America, growing in the cool, high altitude Andes. It has since spread to tropical highlands elsewhere, including parts of Africa, Asia, New Zealand and Australia. In Papua New Guinea it is sold in local markets as sugar fruit, and it also goes by the names granadia and sugarfruit.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia, Passiflora ligularis
  • Gardenia.net, Passiflora ligularis (Sweet Granadilla)

Sweetheart

Sweetheart is one of the two main purple passionfruit varieties grown commercially in Australia, alongside Misty Gem. It is a Passiflora edulis type with deep purple, burgundy toned skin, rich orange-yellow pulp and black seeds, known for a high pulp content and a sweet, intense flavour.

As a purple type it benefits from the self-compatibility of the species, and it is a commercial selection managed within the Australian passionfruit industry rather than a freely propagated seed line. It produces fruit through most of the year apart from the cooler months.

3 sources
  • BeeAware - Passionfruit Pollination
  • Passionfruit Australia Inc - Varieties
  • Wikipedia - Passiflora edulis

Peach

28 varieties (back to top)

Albatross

Albatross is an Australian heritage clingstone peach known for its very large, almost round fruit. The light yellow skin is marked with crimson tinges and darker crimson speckles, and the flesh is white with red colouring next to the stone.

It is an early ripening peach with juicy, melt in the mouth flesh and a distinctive sharp tang, which sets it apart from sweeter early peaches. It survives today mainly through Australian heritage fruit collections.

2 sources
  • Werribee Park Heritage Orchard: Albatross
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Albatross Peach

Anzac

Anzac is an Australian heritage peach released in 1915 and named in honour of the Anzacs. It is a classic old white-fleshed home-garden peach now more than a century old.

The fruit has pale white to greenish flesh, a freestone stone, and a juicy, sweet to slightly tart flavour that is best when fully soft and tree-ripened. It is an early-season peach, cropping heavily from late December into January in Australia, and is self-fertile.

2 sources
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Anzac Peach
  • The Diggers Club: Peach Anzac

Aztec Gold

Aztec Gold is a yellow fleshed freestone peach suited to warm, low chill districts and the subtropics. It ripens in the mid to late part of the season, with golden, melting, sweet flesh and very good eating quality.

It is valued in warm climates for its good resistance to bacterial spot, a common problem for stone fruit in humid areas. Aztec Gold has also served as a parent in low chill peach breeding, including at the University of Florida, where it stands behind the variety Gulfprince.

3 sources
  • SAPO Trust: Aztec Gold peach
  • Auburn University ACES: Release of New Peach Varieties (1998)
  • Daleys Fruit Trees: Aztec Gold peach

Bendigo Beauty

Bendigo Beauty is an Australian heritage peach named after the old goldfields city of Bendigo in Victoria. It is a medium, round, white fleshed peach with cream skin flushed red, juicy and full flavoured, ripening around the first week of January.

The tree is precocious and a good cropper, and it ripens its fruit gradually over several weeks rather than all at once. It is self pollinating, and the fruit is used fresh as well as for drying and stewing.

2 sources
  • Heritage & Rare Fruit Network: Peaches & Nectarines
  • Werribee Park Heritage Orchard: Bendigo Beauty

Blackboy

Blackboy is a heritage red fleshed (blood) peach, the name used in New Zealand and Australia for a group of dark, wine coloured peaches. The Heritage Food Crops Research Trust notes Blackboy is a collective name for around six red fleshed varieties imported predominantly from France in the early 20th century, related to the European blood peach types.

It is a late maturing freestone with pale purple to dark skin and deep wine red, melting flesh, prized for its rich flavour and striking colour for fresh eating, cooking, jam and bottling. It is self fertile, and is often sold as a dwarf in Australia.

2 sources
  • Heritage Food Crops Research Trust (Plums and Peaches)
  • Heritage Fruit Trees (Blackboy Peach)

Briggs Red May

Briggs Red May arose about 1870 as a chance seedling on the farm of John G. Briggs near Yuba City, California, and it has long since become an Australian heritage favourite. It is one of the earliest peaches of the season, prized for ripening when few other peaches are ready.

The medium to large, round fruit has white skin flushed with a rich red cheek and white, melting, juicy flesh with a slight green tinge near the skin. It is a freestone with a notably rich flavour, regarded as one of the best of the very early peaches.

2 sources
  • Heritage & Rare Fruit Network: Peaches & Nectarines
  • Werribee Park Heritage Orchard: Briggs Red May

China Flat

China Flat is a flat (peento or pan tao type) peach, oblate and saucer shaped like a small UFO or doughnut rather than the usual round form. Flat peaches originated in China, where they were known by names meaning coiled or flat peach. The skin is yellow flushed with red and tends to be less fuzzy than standard peaches.

The flesh is white and juicy, and flat peaches are often noted as sweeter and more complex than round types, sometimes with almond undertones. The China Flat sold by Australian nurseries is a white fleshed, low chill, self fertile variety suited to subtropical and warm temperate gardens.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Flat peach
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: China Flat Peach
  • Daleys Fruit Trees: Peach China Flat

Coronet

Coronet is an older peach grown as an early to mid season variety. It bears red skinned fruit with sweet, juicy flesh and a semi-freestone stone, meaning the flesh parts from the pit more readily than a clingstone but not as cleanly as a true freestone.

It ripens ahead of many main season peaches and is grown in Australia as a heritage variety. The mix of early timing, good colour and sweet flavour makes it a useful fresh eating peach for the start of the stone fruit season.

2 sources
  • PickYourOwn.org: Peach Varieties Guide
  • Heritage & Rare Fruit Network: Peaches & Nectarines

Crimson rocket

Crimson Rocket is a columnar or pillar peach developed by USDA Agricultural Research Service horticulturist Ralph Scorza and colleagues at the Appalachian Fruit Research Station in Kearneysville, West Virginia, and patented and released in 2001. Its narrow, upright habit comes from two copies of a naturally occurring gene that sends the branches skyward.

Because the tree stays slim, with a spread of only about five feet, it suits small gardens and high density orchard planting. The fruit is yellow fleshed, firm, sweet and aromatic, a dessert type that stores well and softens when fully ripe.

2 sources
  • USDA ARS: Crimson Rocket Peach Making Its Way to Market
  • Good Fruit Grower: Upright peach plantings are productive

Double Jewel

Double Jewel, cultivar name Zaiwel, is an ornamental and fruiting peach introduced by the Californian breeder Zaiger Genetics under US Plant Patent 6410. It is grown as much for its display as its crop, carrying profuse, double, pink blossoms in spring.

Unusually for such a heavily flowering peach, it also sets a worthwhile crop: large, richly flavoured, yellow fleshed freestone fruit with red skin in mid season. The tree is self fruitful, so it does not need a second peach nearby to set fruit.

2 sources
  • US Plant Patent PP6410 (Double Jewel)
  • Dave Wilson Nursery: Double Jewel peach

Elberta

Elberta is a famous American heirloom raised by Samuel H. Rumph at Marshallville, Georgia, from seed planted in 1870, and named after his wife Clara Elberta Moore. It is a large, golden skinned freestone with juicy, firm, sweet yellow flesh.

Valued for its good handling and shipping qualities, Elberta became the most widely planted peach in the United States and helped establish the Southern peach industry. It suits fresh eating, canning and preserving.

2 sources
  • University of Georgia, Peaches history
  • Specialty Produce, Fay Elberta Peaches

Flavourcrest

Flavourcrest (Flavorcrest) is a yellow fleshed Californian peach that ripens early, a handful of days before Redhaven. It produces medium to large fruit, mostly covered in dark crimson red over a yellow ground, with very firm flesh and very good flavour, and it has been a mainstay commercial variety for its consistency and shelf life.

It is generally classed as a semi freestone (the stone parts fairly cleanly from the ripe flesh). A notable drawback is its high susceptibility to bacterial spot, which matters in humid climates. The fruit suits fresh eating and handles shipping well.

2 sources
  • Rutgers NJAES FS1201 Yellow-Fleshed Peach Varieties
  • Dave Wilson Nursery Flavorcrest

Flordaprince

Flordaprince is a very early, low chill peach released by the University of Florida in 1982. UF/IFAS records a chilling requirement of about 150 units, making it one of the lowest chill peaches and well suited to warm, mild winter regions where standard peaches fail. The fruit ripens very early in the season and develops roughly 80 percent red blush with dark red stripes over a yellow ground colour.

Inside, Flordaprince has yellow flesh and a semi clingstone pit, with medium to large, firm fruit. It is widely grown as a dependable early cropper, including in subtropical and coastal Australian gardens where it is offered as a popular low chill choice.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS - Florida Peach and Nectarine Varieties (MG374)
  • Daleys Fruit Tree Nursery - Peach Flordaprince

Golden Queen

Golden Queen is a New Zealand heirloom that arose around 1908 as a chance seedling in Edwin Reeve's garden at Greerton, Tauranga, and was propagated and introduced commercially by the Auckland nursery D. Hay and Son. It is a late ripening clingstone with firm, deep yellow flesh and yellow orange skin.

Prized as a bottling and canning peach because the firm clingstone flesh holds its shape and the flavour intensifies when preserved, it is also eaten fresh. It remains a long standing favourite in Australian and New Zealand home orchards.

3 sources
  • Te Ara, Encyclopedia of New Zealand
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Golden Queen Peach
  • Woodbridge Fruit Trees: Golden Queen Peach

Indian

The Indian peach, also called Indian Blood or Blood Cling, is a red fleshed heirloom whose flesh and skin are stained deep crimson. The Spanish brought red fleshed peaches to the Americas in the sixteenth century, after which Native American peoples, including Cherokee and Muskogee growers, cultivated it widely across the South.

Unusually for stone fruit, it grows readily true enough from seed rather than only by grafting, which helped it spread through trade. The large clingstone fruit is sweet when fully ripe and was long valued for pickling and canning, where the red flesh looks striking in the jar.

3 sources
  • Specialty Produce, Indian Blood Peaches
  • Slow Food Foundation, Ark of Taste, Indian Blood Peach
  • Monticello, Indian Blood Cling Peach

J.H. Hale Million Dollar

J.H. Hale, often sold as the Million Dollar Peach, is a famous American heirloom that appeared as a single chance tree on J.H. Hale's farm at South Glastonbury, Connecticut, around 1912. It became one of the most celebrated yellow fleshed freestone peaches of its era.

The fruit is exceptionally large, round and uniform, with smooth, almost fuzzless skin and firm, fine grained, stringless flesh of rich flavour. A well known quirk is that its pollen is poor, so unlike most peaches it needs a different peach variety nearby to pollinate it and set a crop.

2 sources
  • Chatham Apples: 1937 Peaches (historical pomology)
  • Trees of Antiquity: J.H. Hale Peach

Late Italian Red

Late Italian Red, also recorded as Late Red Italian, is an Australian heritage clingstone peach documented in catalogues from around 1914. As its name suggests, it ripens late in the season, well after the early summer peaches.

The large, almost round fruit has greenish yellow skin with a deep red cheek over firm, juicy, sweet yellow flesh. A strong growing, heavy bearing tree, it is valued for cooking and preserving as well as fresh eating.

3 sources
  • Werribee Park Heritage Orchard: Late Red Italian
  • Heritage & Rare Fruit Network: Peaches & Nectarines
  • PickYourOwn.org: Peach Varieties Guide

Loring

Loring is a large to very large yellow fleshed freestone peach that ripens in mid season, roughly a couple of weeks after Redhaven. It is valued for firm flesh and very good flavour, and it is a long standing favourite for fresh eating, pies, canning and preserves.

The tree is described as very vigorous but only moderately productive, with medium susceptibility to bacterial spot. Loring is self fruitful, so it does not require a separate pollinator, though it can crop more heavily when other peaches are nearby.

2 sources
  • Rutgers NJAES FS1201 Yellow-Fleshed Peach Varieties
  • Grandpa's Orchard Loring Peach

Maycrest

Maycrest is an early season peach from California, long used in the trade as a benchmark for very early ripening, with fruit coming in around the middle of May in California's San Joaquin Valley. Later varieties are often described by how their timing compares to Maycrest.

It is a vigorous tree bearing firm fleshed fruit with good eating quality, valued for arriving at the very start of the peach season. Its early ripening and firmness made it a long standing commercial early peach.

2 sources
  • Google Patents: USPP6229 (Early Maycrest, describing the Maycrest peach)
  • PickYourOwn.org: Peach Varieties in order of Ripening

O Henry

O'Henry is a late season freestone peach developed by the private Californian breeder Grant Merrill, who patented it in the early 1970s. The golden yellow flesh is streaked with red around a freestone pit, and the skin carries a dark red blush over an orange yellow base.

It balances sweetness with a slightly tangy aftertaste and is firm enough to ship well, which made it a major commercial variety from the mid 1980s. It is enjoyed fresh and for cooking.

3 sources
  • Specialty Produce, O'Henry Peaches
  • Good Fruit Grower
  • US Plant Patent PP4399 (Merrill)

Okinawa

Okinawa is a low chill peach (about 150 chill units) better known as a seedling rootstock than as a fresh fruit variety. Introduced as seed from Japan, it was resistant to the historically important root knot nematodes Meloidogyne incognita and M. javanica and was widely used to graft peaches in warm climates such as Florida.

Its low chilling also caused early blooming. After a new nematode species (M. floridensis) was found on Okinawa rootstock in Gainesville in 1966, it is no longer recommended in Florida. Okinawa was a parent of the UF Flordaguard rootstock, which combined its nematode resistance with other peach material.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS Rootstocks for Florida Stone Fruit (HS366)
  • UF/IFAS EDIS Peach Root-knot Nematode (HS1320)

Pullar s Cling

Pullar's Cling is an Australian heritage canning peach raised by a Mr P Pullar of Ardmona in Victoria, and listed in nursery catalogues as early as 1914. It was the clingstone preferred above all others by the old canning factories.

It is one of the last peaches to ripen, bearing large, round fruit with a pointed tip, a red blush over yellow skin, and firm, golden, clingstone flesh stained red around the stone. A heavy bearer, it is valued for bottling and preserving as well as fresh eating.

3 sources
  • Werribee Park Heritage Orchard: Pullar Cling
  • Heritage & Rare Fruit Network: Peaches & Nectarines
  • The Diggers Club: Pullar Cling peach

Red Haven

Redhaven was bred by Stanley Johnston at Michigan State University's South Haven station and introduced in 1940, part of the famous Haven series selected from tens of thousands of seedlings. It became the most widely planted freestone peach in the world and is still treated as the standard for its early to mid season slot, against which other varieties are dated.

The fruit is medium to large with firm, melting yellow flesh, near fuzzless red blushed skin and good flavour. It is a yellow fleshed, semi freestone peach (the stone separates more cleanly as it fully ripens), and it suits fresh eating, canning and freezing. The tree is noted as vigorous, productive and relatively low in susceptibility to bacterial spot.

4 sources
  • Historical Marker Database (Haven Peaches marker)
  • Good Fruit Grower
  • Rutgers NJAES FS1201 Yellow-Fleshed Peach Varieties
  • Stark Bro's Redhaven Peach

Stark Earliglo

Stark EarliGlo is an early season freestone peach introduced by the long established American nursery Stark Bro's. It is closely related to the popular Redhaven peach and shares its bright red skin and firm, good flavoured flesh, but ripens earlier in the season.

Its main appeal is hardiness and timing: it is notably winter hardy, which suits it to colder districts, and it is self fertile, so a single tree can set a crop without another peach nearby.

2 sources
  • Stark Bro's: Stark EarliGlo Peach
  • DigTheDirt: Prunus persica 'Stark EarliGlo'

Tatura 204

Tatura 204 is an Australian bred clingstone peach developed at the Tatura research station in Victoria, from a cross of Babygold 7 and Oom Sarel. It became an industry standard canning peach, valued for consistent heavy yields and reliable fruit quality.

The fruit is medium to large with firm, sweet, yellow flesh and golden yellow skin. Because it holds its shape so well and is naturally sweet, it is a favourite for bottling and canning, and it ripens around midsummer.

4 sources
  • Hort Innovation: Canning Peach Breeding and Evaluation Program (CF10003)
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Tatura 204 Peach
  • Yalca Fruit Trees: Tatura 204 Peach
  • Werribee Park Heritage Orchard: Tatura 204 Peach (Scion)

Trixzie Pixzee

Trixzie Pixzee is the Pix Zee peach (bred by Zaiger Genetics, US Plant Patent 6364) marketed in Australia within the Trixzie dwarf fruit range. It is a genetic dwarf, staying naturally compact at roughly two metres, which makes it well suited to pots, patios and small gardens without needing heavy pruning.

Despite its small size it carries full sized fruit with yellow, freestone flesh and red blushed skin. The tree is self fertile, so a single plant will set a crop without a pollinating partner.

4 sources
  • Gardening Know How: Pix Zee Peach Tree Care
  • Bay Laurel Nursery: Pix Zee Miniature Peach
  • Daleys Fruit Trees: Dwarf Peach Trixzie Pixzee
  • Newmans Nursery: Dwarf Peach Trixzie Pixzee

Tropic Beauty

Tropic Beauty (UFBeauty) is a low chill peach released jointly by the University of Florida and Texas A&M in 1989. It needs only about 150 chill units, which makes it one of the standout choices for mild winter and subtropical gardens where most peaches will not set fruit.

It carries medium sized fruit with yellow, melting flesh and a semi freestone pit. The skin develops a heavy red blush over a yellow ground. In Florida it ripens early, roughly 89 days after bloom, giving an early season harvest.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS: Florida Peach and Nectarine Varieties (MG374)
  • Chestnut Hill Nursery: Tropic Beauty Peach

Tropic Snow

Tropic Snow (TropicSnow) is a low chill, white fleshed peach released jointly by the University of Florida and Texas A&M in 1989. It needs only about 225 chill units, so it fruits reliably in mild winter regions where higher chill peaches fail.

The melting white flesh is very low in acid with excellent sweetness, sitting behind a 40 to 50 percent red blush over a creamy white background. The pit is semi freestone. It is an early season peach with a fruit development period of about 90 to 97 days.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS: Florida Peach and Nectarine Varieties (MG374)
  • Dave Wilson Nursery Variety Finder: Tropic Snow White Peach

Pear

34 varieties (back to top)

Beurre Bosc

Beurre Bosc is a European pear that emerged in the early 1800s, with sources differing on whether it began in Belgium or France. It is named after the French horticulturist Louis Bosc, and Beurre means buttery in French, a nod to its rich texture.

It is easily recognised by its long tapering neck and warm cinnamon-brown russeted skin. The flesh is dense, crisp and sweet with a woodsy, spicy note, and because it holds its shape well when heated it is a favourite for baking, poaching and broiling as well as fresh eating. The season runs from autumn into spring.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Bosc pear
  • Specialty Produce: Bosc Pears
  • USA Pears: Bosc

Beurre Diel

Beurre Diel is a historic Belgian dessert pear from the Van Mons era. The chance seedling was found around 1800 at the Chateau of Perck near Vilvoorde by Meuris, head gardener to Dr Jean-Baptiste Van Mons, who named it after the German pomologist Augustin Diel. It is a large fruit with a yellowish green to orange skin marked by patches of russet and sometimes a faint flush.

The flesh is creamy white, melting and juicy, with a rich, sweet, aromatic flavour, though it can be a little gritty near the core and needs a warm season to ripen well. It is a mid season pear, harvested in October and used through to about December as a dessert fruit. The tree is a triploid and is self sterile, so it needs a compatible pollinator.

2 sources
  • Hortus Camdenensis: Pyrus communis 'Beurre Diel'
  • Keepers Nursery: Beurre Diel

Beurre Easter

Beurre Easter, known in French as Doyenne d'Hiver and often called Easter Beurre, is an old pear of Belgian origin, traced to an aged tree at the Capucin monastery at Louvain that was recorded around 1823 as the Pastorale de Louvain. English and American authorities later settled on the name Easter Beurre, reflecting its very late season.

Its defining quality is keeping: it is one of the finest of the long storing pears, ripening in storage long after autumn pears are finished and lasting into April or May. The large fruit has melting, juicy flesh with a bright, vinous flavour, and unusually it will ripen in a cool cellar without special care.

2 sources
  • ChestOfBooks (horticultural journal): The Easter Beurre Pear
  • Promesse de Fleurs: Doyenne d'Hiver Pear

Beurre Hardy

Beurre Hardy is a classic French dessert pear (Pyrus communis) raised around 1820 by M. Bonnet at Boulogne in northern France, and named after M. Hardy, the director of arboriculture at the Luxembourg Gardens in Paris. It reached England in the 1840s and has long been valued as one of the great traditional French butter pears.

Its standout feature is flavour: the buttery, melting, juicy flesh carries a distinctive rosewater or floral aroma that sets it apart from other pears. The medium to large, conical fruit has greenish-yellow skin heavily overlaid with a fine bronze russet. It ripens in mid-season. Beurre Hardy is generally grown with a pollinator rather than relied upon as self-fertile.

2 sources
  • Specialty Produce: French Butter (Beurre Hardy) Pears
  • Trees of Antiquity: Beurre Hardy Pear

Beurre Superfin

Beurre Superfin is a French dessert pear raised by M. Goubault at Mille Pieds near Angers in 1837, first fruiting around 1844. It is a medium to large fruit with a slightly rough, russet patched skin that ripens from mid green to yellow.

It is celebrated for flavour: the pale yellow flesh is melting, juicy and sweet with a delicate, scented, slightly tart character, ranking among the most outstanding flavoured pears. An autumn variety, it is picked in late September and keeps into October.

2 sources
  • Orange Pippin: Beurre Superfin
  • Frank P Matthews: Beurre Superfin

Chojuro Nashi

Chojuro is a classic Japanese nashi (Asian pear) that arose as a chance seedling in Japan around the 1890s. It is one of the russet nashi, with yellow to brownish, russeted skin rather than the smooth green or yellow of many other Japanese pears. The flesh is crisp and juicy but a little coarse in texture, and like all nashi it is eaten firm and crunchy, more like a crisp apple than a soft European pear.

Its standout quality is flavour: Chojuro is widely noted for a rich, sweet taste with distinctive butterscotch and caramel notes, a result of high sugar and low acidity. It ripens in late summer (around late August in the northern hemisphere) and was historically a very productive, disease-tolerant orchard variety, though its market share in Japan declined as smoother-skinned cultivars rose in popularity.

3 sources
  • Advances in Japanese pear breeding in Japan (Saito, PMC/NIH)
  • NC State Extension Gardener Plant Toolbox: Pyrus pyrifolia 'Chojuro'
  • Gardener's Path: 9 of the Best Asian Pear Cultivars

Clapp

Clapp's Favourite is an old American dessert pear raised by the Clapp family of Dorchester, Massachusetts in the mid 19th century and introduced around 1860. It is generally regarded as a cross of Bartlett and Flemish Beauty, and it resembles Bartlett in shape but ripens noticeably earlier, roughly a week to two weeks ahead of it.

The white flesh is fine grained, very juicy and sweet with a refreshing hint of acidity, making it a prized fresh eating pear. Its main quirk is poor keeping: the fruit should be picked while still firm and ripened off the tree, because left on the branch the core can break down, and ripe fruit lasts only a few days.

3 sources
  • Dorchester Atheneum: Clapp's Favorite Pear
  • Specialty Produce: Clapp's Favorite Pears
  • Orange Pippin: Clapp's Favourite

Conference

Conference is an English dessert pear (Pyrus communis) raised by Thomas Francis Rivers at Rivers Nursery in Sawbridgeworth, Hertfordshire. Its name comes from the National British Pear Conference held in London in 1885, where it won first prize. It is by far the most widely grown pear in the United Kingdom, valued for reliable cropping and good scab resistance.

The fruit has a distinctive long, narrow, bottle shape with thick greenish-brown skin that is often heavily russeted, turning pale yellow when ripe. The flesh is fine, soft and sweet. Conference is unusually self-fertile for a pear and can set a crop on its own, though yields improve with a nearby pollinator such as Williams. It can also set seedless fruit without pollination.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Conference pear
  • Orange Pippin: Conference pear

Corella

Corella is a small Australian dessert pear (Pyrus communis) of the blush, or Forelle, type, sometimes called Australia's prettiest pear after the native parrot. It is grown widely in Australia, with early plantings associated with German settlers in South Australia's Barossa Valley.

Its appeal is its looks and eating quality at a small size: smooth green-yellow skin develops a striking rosy-red blush on the sunny side as it ripens. The flesh is creamy, fragrant, sweet and juicy with a slightly firm bite, making it popular eaten fresh, sliced into salads, or served with cheese.

2 sources
  • Australian Pears: About Australian Pears
  • Specialty Produce: Corella Pears

Doyenne Du Comice

Doyenne du Comice is a French dessert pear (Pyrus communis) raised in 1849 to 1850 at the Comice Horticole garden in Angers by the gardener Dhomme and Millet de la Turtaudiere. Widely regarded as one of the finest eating pears ever bred, it was famously praised in 19th century England, with the London Horticultural Journal calling it the best pear in the world in 1894. It has twice received the Royal Horticultural Society's Award of Garden Merit.

The large fruit is greenish-yellow, often with a red blush and some russet, ripening late in the season. Its flesh is pale, exceptionally sweet, melting, buttery and very juicy, which is the source of its reputation. The skin is delicate and bruises easily, so the fruit needs careful handling. It is not reliably self-fertile and is best grown with a compatible pollinator.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Doyenne du Comice
  • Specialty Produce: Doyenne du Comice Pears

Duchess D Anglouleme European

Duchesse d'Angouleme arose as a chance seedling in a garden near Angers, in the Maine-et-Loire region of France. The nurseryman M. Audusson began propagating it, and in 1820 he sent fruit to the Duchesse d'Angouleme seeking permission to name the pear in her honour.

It is one of the largest of the dessert pears, often attaining great size, with an irregular, bumpy surface. The thin skin ripens to a warm yellow netted with russet. When well grown the white flesh becomes buttery and melting, with a rich, sweet flavour.

2 sources
  • Slow Food Foundation Ark of Taste - Duchesse d'Angouleme Pear
  • Specialty Produce - Duchesse d'Angouleme Pears

Durondeau

Durondeau is a Belgian dessert pear raised in 1811 by M. Durondeau at Tongre-Notre-Dame, near Tournai. It is a handsome fruit with a golden to yellow green skin overlaid by red to brown russet and attractive lenticels, often with a flush on the sunny side.

The flesh is sweet and juicy with a pronounced, aromatic flavour carried by a little underlying acidity. It is an autumn pear, picked around late September and at its peak through October, with a short season. The tree has an upright, compact habit suited to small gardens and turns a fiery red in autumn.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Durondeau pear
  • Frank P Matthews: Durondeau

Flemish Beauty

Flemish Beauty is an old Belgian dessert pear (Pyrus communis) believed to descend from a chance seedling found in the woods of East Flanders around 1800, originally called Bosc Peer, or pear of the woods. The prolific Belgian breeder Jean-Baptiste Van Mons took up its propagation and renamed it Fondante des bois, loosely melt of the woods, under which name it became widely known across Europe. It later became one of the most common commercial pears in the United States.

The medium-sized fruit has yellow skin marked with russet, often with an attractive blush, and soft, buttery, juicy yellow flesh. The flavour is rich, sweet and notably musky, with a balance of sugar and acidity. For a European pear it is unusually self-fertile, though it crops more heavily with a pollinator. It is, however, quite susceptible to pear scab.

2 sources
  • Orange Pippin: Flemish Beauty pear
  • Silver Creek Nursery: Flemish Beauty European Pear

Flordahome

Flordahome is a low-chill pear developed by the University of Florida for warm, subtropical climates where most pears fail to get enough winter cold. It is a hybrid pear (a cross involving European Pyrus communis and Asian Pyrus pyrifolia) and needs only about 150 chill hours, making it one of the few pears that fruit reliably in mild-winter regions.

The fruit is soft-fleshed, sweet and juicy with smooth green to yellow skin, ripening early in summer (around July to August in Florida). It is good for fresh eating and canning, carries useful fire blight and leaf spot resistance, and is only partially self-fertile, so it crops better with a compatible pollinator such as Hood.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS: The Pear (Pyrus spp.) in Florida Home Gardens (HS1393)
  • Tree Vitalize: Pear Trees in Florida

Gin Perry

Gin is an English perry pear (Pyrus communis) from west Gloucestershire, particularly the Newent area, first recorded as a vintage perry fruit in 1886. It bears heavy crops of small, almost round fruit, green with a slight orange or red flush at full ripeness.

It is grown for perry (pear cider) and is prized as a high-quality variety. Its name comes from the perry's aromatic, juniper-like character, which gives it a gin-like quality, with floral and herbal notes. The juice is classed as bittersharp, with medium acidity and medium tannin, and the variety is also valued for good resistance to scab and canker. It is sometimes blended with Blakeney Red.

2 sources
  • National Perry Pear Centre: Gin
  • Cider Review: The Gin Pear

Glou Morceau European

Glou Morceau was bred by the Abbe Hardenpont at Mons in Belgium around 1750, which is why it also goes by the name Beurre d'Hardenpont. It reached England in 1820 and became a prized Victorian dessert pear. The name translates roughly as 'delicious morsel'.

This is a late season pear with fine, melting, juicy flesh, very sweet and richly flavoured, though it needs warm conditions to ripen fully. The fruit is medium to large and oval to pear-shaped, with smooth greenish-yellow skin carrying very little russet. It hangs well on the tree and keeps into winter.

2 sources
  • Specialty Produce - Glou Morceau Pears
  • Keepers Nursery - Glou Morceau pear

Green Horse Perry

Green Horse is a very old English perry pear (Pyrus communis) long associated with the orchards of Gloucestershire and the Three Counties, recorded in the 1800s. The fruit is round and green to yellowish green, sometimes with a light orange flush, often carrying russet around the stem and eye spreading onto the cheeks.

It is grown chiefly for perry (pear cider), where it is rated a best-quality variety producing a high-acid, low-tannin juice. It ripens in mid to late autumn (around October in England) on a large, vigorous, reliable-cropping tree. Beyond perry, the hard fruit has historically also been stewed or pickled.

2 sources
  • National Perry Pear Centre: Green Horse
  • Slow Food Foundation Ark of Taste: Green Horse Perry Pear

Henry s Red Longdon Perry

Red Longdon is a traditional English perry pear (Pyrus communis) from the Three Counties region of England. The fruit is turbinate to pyriform, green or yellowish green with a strong red or red-orange flush spreading from the eye and russet around the stem, a colouring that gives the variety its name. In Australia the same variety is grown as Henry's Red Longdon, after Henry of Harcourt near Bendigo, where perry pears were introduced during the gold rush from 1850.

It is grown for perry (pear cider) rather than fresh eating, producing a good-quality perry of medium acidity and medium tannin. It is a vigorous, scab-resistant, midseason-harvesting tree. The Henry's Red Longdon name was adopted in Australia partly because no original index tree survived for direct comparison.

2 sources
  • National Perry Pear Centre: Red Longdon
  • Slow Food Foundation Ark of Taste: Red Longdon Perry Pear

Hood

Hood is a very low-chill pear released by the University of Florida for warm, subtropical growing where winters are too mild for traditional European pears. Needing only a low number of chill hours, it is one of the standard low-chill choices for Florida and similar climates, and it is interfruitful with Flordahome as a pollinating partner.

The fruit is large and early, ripening in mid-summer, with yellow-green skin and soft, sweet, mild flesh often compared to a Bartlett. It also carries moderate fire blight resistance, a key trait for humid, warm-climate orchards.

3 sources
  • UF/IFAS: The Pear (Pyrus spp.) in Florida Home Gardens (HS1393)
  • Tree Vitalize: Pear Trees in Florida
  • Growing Fruit forum: Hood Pear

Jargonelle European

Jargonelle is one of the oldest pear varieties known, thought to have originated in the former French region of Anjou and first recorded in England by John Parkinson in 1629. Its hardiness, longevity and reliable cropping saw it planted widely throughout northern Britain.

It is one of the earliest summer pears to ripen. The fruit is medium-sized and long conical, with greenish-yellow skin flushed brownish-red and dotted with small russet patches. The pale yellow flesh is tender and juicy with a very sweet, slightly musky flavour. It is a tip bearer, so it is not suited to training as cordons or espaliers.

2 sources
  • Forestry Journal - Jethart pears (Jargonelle)
  • Keepers Nursery - Jargonelle pear

Josephine de Malines

Josephine de Malines is an old Belgian winter pear (Pyrus communis) raised in the early 1800s by Major Pierre Joseph Esperen at Malines (Mechelen), who named it after his wife, Josephine. It is a late, long-keeping dessert pear, traditionally picked in autumn for eating through winter once it has mellowed in store.

The flesh is the draw: rich, buttery and melting, sweet and perfumed, often rated among the finest-flavoured of all pears. The small to medium fruit are conical and greenish-yellow, sometimes with a dull red flush. It is not self-fertile, so it needs a compatible pollinator nearby.

2 sources
  • Orange Pippin: Josephine de Malines
  • Specialty Produce: Josephine Pears

Ko Sui nashi

Kosui is a Japanese nashi (Asian pear) bred from a cross of Kikusui and Wasekozo, released in Japan in 1959 from the national pear breeding program. It produces medium-sized, rounded fruit largely covered in golden-brown russet, on a background ranging from light green to bronze. The name Kosui means good water, a nod to its juiciness.

The flesh is soft for a nashi yet crisp and very juicy, with a fine texture and a sweet, low-acid flavour. Kosui is an early-ripening variety, maturing in mid to late summer ahead of many other nashi, and for decades it was one of the most widely grown pears in Japan. Like other nashi it is eaten firm and crunchy rather than ripened soft.

3 sources
  • Advances in Japanese pear breeding in Japan (Saito, PMC/NIH)
  • Gardener's Path: 9 of the Best Asian Pear Cultivars
  • CooksInfo: Kosui Asian pear

Mirandino Rosso

Mirandino Rosso is an old Italian summer pear from the Mantua district of northern Italy, where it was recorded in the hunting reserves of the Guerrini Gonzaga marquises near Moglia from the 1700s. The same pear is known as Bella di Giugno and as Precoce di Moglia, the latter pointing to its place of origin, with Mirandino Rosso the name favoured in Emilia Romagna. It ranks among the earliest of all pears.

The fruit is small and typically piriform, with a yellow skin that flushes red on the parts most exposed to the sun. The flesh is white, melting and fairly juicy, with a sweet, aromatic character. Picking begins from the last third of June, so it is eaten fresh in the height of summer rather than stored. The Book of Pears lists it simply as an Italian summer pear.

3 sources
  • Angys, Pera Bella di Giugno
  • The Book of Pears (Fruit Forum directory)
  • Vivai Nannini, Mirandino Rosso pear

Moorcroft Perry

Moorcroft is a traditional English perry pear (Pyrus communis) believed to have originated at Moorcroft Farm in Colwall, in the Malvern Hills, in the early 1800s. East of the River Severn it is widely known as Malvern Hills, and it carries several other names including Malvern Pear, Choke Pear and Choker. It is famously linked to the cheese Stinking Bishop, which is washed in perry made from this pear and named after a former Moorcroft Farm occupant.

As its Choke Pear names suggest, the fruit is not for eating fresh but for perry (pear cider). It is regarded as a fine vintage perry variety, yielding a juice of medium acidity and medium tannin, with little or no citric acid, that ferments to a good-quality perry. It grows into a large, long-lived, oak-like tree.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Stinking Bishop (pear) / Moorcroft
  • Malvern Trail: Cider and Perry
  • National Perry Pear Centre: Moorcroft

Nashi Nijisseiki

Nijisseiki, whose name means 20th Century, is a Japanese nashi or Asian pear (Pyrus pyrifolia), not a European pear. It traces to a chance seedling discovered on a rubbish heap in Japan in the late 1890s, and went on to become one of the most popular and widely grown Asian pear cultivars in Japan and the United States. Unlike the soft, buttery European pears, nashi are eaten crisp and apple-like straight from the tree.

The fruit is round and apple-shaped with smooth, thin, yellow to greenish-yellow skin. Its white flesh is clean, crisp, very juicy and sweet, the qualities that made it famous. It ripens in late summer. As a smooth-skinned yellow nashi it is more prone to fire blight than russeted types, and at least two compatible cultivars are usually needed for reliable pollination.

2 sources
  • NC State Extension: Pyrus pyrifolia 'Nijisseiki'
  • Victoriana Nursery: Nijisseiki Asian Pear (Pyrus pyrifolia)

Packham's Triumph

Packham's Triumph is a famous Australian-bred pear (Pyrus communis), raised in 1896 by orchardist Charles Packham at Molong in the New South Wales central west, as a cross between the old English variety Uvedale's St Germain and Williams' Bon Chretien. Packham proposed the name Packham's Seedling, but the agriculture department suggested Packham's Triumph. It went on to become Australia's leading commercial and export pear.

The fruit has a classic pear shape with thin green skin (often lightly russeted) that fades to pale yellow as it ripens. The ivory flesh is fine-grained, juicy and creamy with a sweet, mildly musky, aromatic flavour, ripening softly rather than staying crisp.

4 sources
  • Australian Dictionary of Biography: Charles Henry Packham
  • Australian Food Timeline: Packham pear developed in NSW
  • Specialty Produce: Packham Pears
  • Australian Pears: About Australian Pears

Paradise

The Paradise pear traces back to Sicily, Italy, and has since been taken up in Australia. It is also sold under the names Faccia Bella (Italian for beautiful face), sugar pear and crystal pear, and is classed as a cocktail pear for its very small size, roughly that of a golf ball. The fruit is squat and round with smooth skin that shifts from green to gold with a red blush as it ripens.

Inside, the white flesh is crisp, moist and juicy, staying firm and crunchy even when fully ripe, with a sweet, fragrant taste. The small size suits cheese plates, salads and lunch boxes, and the fruit also takes well to poaching, baking, preserving and pickling. In Australia it is an early-season pear, ripening around January to February.

2 sources
  • Specialty Produce - Paradise Pears
  • Heritage Fruit Trees - Paradise Pear

Red Sensation

Red Sensation (also sold as Sensation or Williams Red) is a red sport of the Williams pear that arose in Australia around 1940. It is one of several red mutant clones derived from Williams by spontaneous mutation, alongside Max Red Bartlett and Rosired. The fruit ripens from yellow green to an all over ruby red, giving it the showy skin that sets it apart from the parent.

Because it is a Williams sport, it shares the parent's eating qualities: melting, juicy, creamy white flesh with a fine texture. It is an early season pear, harvested in summer (around February in Australia). The tree also carries striking red tinted foliage, adding ornamental value to its dual role as a dessert pear.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Williams pear
  • Keepers Nursery: Red Williams
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Williams Red

San Giovanni European

San Giovanni is a very old Italian summer pear. Its name refers to the feast day of Saint John (San Giovanni) in late June, the time when the fruit ripens.

It is an early ripening variety bearing small, pear-shaped fruit with a pale, light yellow-green skin. The white flesh is firm and juicy with a pleasant aromatic flavour. Like most early summer pears it does not store well and is best eaten fresh.

2 sources
  • The Book of Pears (Fruit Forum) - San Giovanni
