  • Verde Commerce - Pero San Giovanni (Pyrus communis)

Shinseiki

Shinseiki, whose name means new century, is a Japanese nashi (Asian pear) released in 1945 from the Okayama prefecture breeding program, with Nijisseiki (Twentieth Century) in its parentage. Unlike the russet nashi such as Chojuro, Shinseiki is a smooth-skinned, bright pale-yellow pear, round to slightly flattened in shape.

The white flesh is crisp, very juicy and sweet with a slight tartness, and it is eaten firm like other nashi. It is an early to mid-season variety, ripening around late August, and is valued in warm-winter regions for its low chilling requirement and reliable, precocious cropping. Several sources describe it as self-fertile, though some recommend a second cultivar nearby for best fruit set.

4 sources
  • Advances in Japanese pear breeding in Japan (Saito, PMC/NIH)
  • NC State Extension Gardener Plant Toolbox: Pyrus pyrifolia 'Shinseiki'
  • Gardener's Path: 9 of the Best Asian Pear Cultivars
  • Fedco Seeds: Shinseiki (New Century) Asian pear

Williams

Williams (full name Williams' Bon Chretien) is the classic European pear (Pyrus communis), thought to have arisen around 1765 to 1770 in the garden of a schoolmaster named John Stair at Aldermaston, England, and later spread by a nurseryman named Williams. In the United States and Canada the same variety is known as Bartlett, after Enoch Bartlett, who sold it without knowing its origin. The two were not recognised as identical until 1828.

Its flavour is so characteristic it is often called the definitive pear taste: aromatic and musky, with white to pale-yellow flesh that ripens soft, juicy and sweet. The skin turns from green to yellow as it ripens. Williams is hugely versatile, eaten fresh, widely canned, and distilled into the pear brandy Poire Williams.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Williams pear
  • Orange Pippin: Bartlett (Williams)
  • Specialty Produce: Williams Pears

Winter Cole

Winter Cole is an Australian heritage pear, a seedling of the Belgian winter keeper Winter Nelis raised by J.C. Cole at Richmond, Victoria, and introduced by the nurseryman J. Brunning and Sons in the late 1880s. Like its parent it is a European pear (Pyrus communis) rather than an Asian nashi, and it inherits Winter Nelis's reputation for late ripening and long storage, holding well into winter.

It is a medium-sized, cool-climate dessert pear with green skin marked by brown russet, and flesh that is juicy and rich in flavour. As a late-season variety it is valued as a keeper, picked in late autumn and eaten through the colder months. It is not self-fertile and crops best alongside other European pears such as Beurre Bosc, Williams or Doyenne du Comice.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Winter Nelis pear
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Winter Cole Pear
  • Woodbridge Fruit Trees: Winter Cole

Winter Nelis

Winter Nelis is an old Belgian late pear (Pyrus communis) raised from seed in the early 1800s by the amateur horticulturist Jean-Charles de Nelis, near Brussels, and also known as Bonne de Malines. Picked in late autumn, it is one of the better-keeping pears, storing for a couple of months for use through winter.

Despite its modest, often russeted skin (which kept it from large commercial production), it is prized for eating quality: yellowish, fine-grained flesh that is buttery and melting, with a rich, sugary, vinous flavour and fine aroma. The small to medium fruit suits both fresh eating and cooking.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Winter Nelis pear
  • Specialty Produce: Winter Nellis Pears

Ya Li Nashi

Ya Li is a Chinese white pear, a cultivar of Pyrus x bretschneideri native to north China and grown there for centuries. Its name means duck pear, after a shape that, unusually for an Asian pear, is elongated and narrow toward the stem like a European pear rather than round like a typical nashi.

Like other Asian pears it eats crisp rather than soft and buttery: the white to pale-yellow flesh is very juicy and crunchy, often compared to a mild Bosc but with higher water content and lower sugar. It is eaten fresh while firm, prized for its refreshing, watery crunch.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Pyrus x bretschneideri
  • Wikipedia: Asian pear

Pecan

2 varieties (back to top)

Pawnee

Pawnee is a USDA pecan (Carya illinoinensis) cultivar from a controlled cross of Mohawk by Starking Hardy Giant, made by the USDA Pecan Breeding Program in 1963 and released in 1984. Like other USDA cultivars it carries a Native American tribe name.

Its standout feature is large nuts combined with very early ripening, several weeks ahead of older western cultivars, which lets growers in cooler, shorter season areas harvest before autumn frosts. Pawnee produces large, good quality kernels with a high percentage of kernel, and is protandrous (a type I pollinator).

2 sources
  • New Mexico State University Extension: Pecan Cultivars for New Mexico (H659)
  • North Carolina Extension Gardener Plant Toolbox: Carya illinoinensis Pawnee

Wichita

Wichita is a USDA pecan (Carya illinoinensis) cultivar from a controlled cross of Halbert by Mahan made by L.D. Romberg at the USDA Pecan Field Station, Brownwood, Texas, in 1940, and named and released in 1959. It became one of the most widely planted USDA cultivars, a mainstay in Arizona, California and Sonora, Mexico.

It produces medium sized nuts with a high percent kernel and good fill, and is valued for heavy yields in hot, arid climates. Wichita is protogynous (a type II pollinator), so it is paired with a protandrous cultivar for cross pollination. It is very susceptible to scab, which limits it in humid regions.

2 sources
  • New Mexico State University Extension: Pecan Cultivars for New Mexico (H659)
  • USDA-ARS Carya (Pecan) Cultivars: Wichita

Plum

42 varieties (back to top)

Amber Jewel

Amber Jewel is an Australian-bred Japanese-type plum (Prunus salicina) that began as a chance seedling found in 1978 in the garden of R. A. Yates at Capel River, Western Australia. It was originally named Teak Gold, patented under that name, then renamed Amber Jewel for market. The fruit is large and conical with red skin over a cream to yellow background.

Its standout feature is amber to golden-yellow flesh that is very sweet, meaty, and crisp with low acidity. Amber Jewel matures late, about a month after Santa Rosa, and is partially self-fertile, pairing well with pollinators such as Santa Rosa or Laroda. It also has notably good storage and shelf life.

3 sources
  • ANFIC: Amber Jewel Plum
  • Specialty Produce: Amber Jewel Plums
  • US Plant Patent PP10277: Plum tree Teak Gold

Angelina

Angelina is the late-season Japanese plum (Prunus salicina) widely marketed as Angeleno or Angelino. It was released in the United States in 1995 by Sunworld International and has become one of the dominant very late plums in commercial markets worldwide.

The skin colours from green to a dark, almost black purple when ripe, over firm pale-yellow to amber flesh. The flavour is sweet and well balanced, with dense, crunchy flesh rather than soft and juicy. It ripens very late and stores and ships exceptionally well, keeping firm in the bowl for a long time.

2 sources
  • Good Fruit Guide: Angelino plum
  • Tough Kraut: Prunus salicina 'Angeleno' care guide

Angelina Burdett

Angelina Burdett is a heritage English dessert plum (Prunus domestica), raised by Mr Dowling of Woolston, Southampton, in about 1850 and first described in 1853. It is a completely separate variety from the modern Angeleno (Angelina) plum.

The fruit is medium sized and round-oval with purple to blue-black skin carrying a heavy bloom, over firm, juicy, yellowish-green flesh with good flavour. It is grown mainly as a dessert plum, has a semi-freestone stone, hangs and keeps well after picking, and ripens from late August into early September.

2 sources
  • National Fruit Collection: Angelina Burdett
  • Garden.org: Dessert Plum 'Angelina Burdett'

Angelino Black

Angelino (Angeleno) is a late-season Japanese plum (Prunus salicina) released in the USA in 1995 by Sun World International. The skin is dark red, almost black, over pale yellow flesh with a small stone.

Its flesh is dense, firm and crunchy with a sweet, well-balanced flavour built up over a long hang time on the tree. Because it stores and ships unusually well, it dominates the late-season plum window, reaching shops as other varieties finish.

2 sources
  • Good Fruit Guide: Angelino
  • Wikipedia: Prunus salicina

Black Amber

Black Amber is a Japanese plum (Prunus salicina) raised in Fresno, California by USDA breeder J.H. Weinberger from a Friar by Queen Rosa cross, released in 1980. It carries large, round fruit with dark purple to near-black skin over firm amber flesh.

The flavour is sweet and well balanced, and the fruit holds its firmness well, which made it a long-standing commercial shipping plum. It ripens mid season and is partially self-fertile, cropping more heavily with a compatible Japanese plum nearby. It resembles its parent Friar in look.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Black Amber (plum)
  • Wikipedia: Prunus salicina
  • Dave Wilson Nursery: Blackamber

Black Diamond

Black Diamond is a Sun World trademarked series of black-skinned, red-fleshed Japanese plums (Prunus salicina), not a single seedling. The skin is deep purple to almost black and the flesh is red to scarlet.

The eating character is crisp and firm with a sweet, juicy flavour carrying a hint of tartness, and the dark skin is mild rather than bitter. In Australia it crops over summer (roughly November to January) and, like most Japanese plums, sets best with a cross-pollinator such as Santa Rosa.

3 sources
  • Fruitnet (Eurofruit): Sun World Black Diamond plum series
  • Montague Farms: Black Diamond plum
  • The Yummy Fruit Co: Black Diamond

Burdekin

The Burdekin plum is not a true plum but the fruit of Pleiogynium timoriense, a rainforest tree in the Anacardiaceae family (the same family as cashew and mango), not the genus Prunus. It is native to north-eastern Queensland and extends north of Australia through Malesia to the Pacific, and is also called sweet plum or tulip plum.

The fruit is round and squat, around 3 to 4 cm across, ripening to a deep purple or near black, with a notably large stone that leaves little flesh. Picked fruit is hard and tart, so it is traditionally left to soften and sweeten after harvest before being eaten raw or made into jams. It was an important bush food for Aboriginal peoples.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Pleiogynium timoriense
  • Australian Native Plants Society: Pleiogynium timoriense

Coe's Golden Drop

Coe's Golden Drop is a heritage English European plum (Prunus domestica), raised by Jervaise Coe in Suffolk at the end of the 18th century, said to be a cross between Green Gage and White Magnum Bonum. It is one of the historic European dessert plums and considered among the best of the late-season varieties.

The fruit has golden yellow skin, often flecked with reddish brown and carrying a cinnamon-like bloom, over tender, juicy golden flesh. The flavour is rich and sweet with a distinctive apricot character, balanced by some acidity from its Magnum parent. It is a large freestone plum that ripens late, around mid-September, and needs a pollinator such as Green Gage or D'Agen to set fruit.

2 sources
  • Orange Pippin: Coe's Golden Drop (gage)
  • Gardening Know How: Coe's Golden Drop Gage

D'Agen Prune

The D'Agen prune (Prune d'Agen, also called Petite d'Agen, Ente or the California French prune) is a European plum (Prunus domestica) originating in the Agen region of southwestern France, the classic plum grown for drying into prunes. In California it is known simply as the French prune, and what Europeans call d'Agen is the same variety.

It is a small to medium purple to blue-skinned plum with firm, sweet, yellow-green flesh, prized for its dense flesh and high sugar that concentrate beautifully when dried. The variety was introduced to California by Louis Pellier in the 1850s, founding the Californian prune industry. It is best known for drying and prune making rather than fresh dessert use.

2 sources
  • California Prunes: History
  • Taste of France: What exactly is an Agen prune?

Damson

The Damson is a small European plum (Prunus domestica subsp. insititia) whose name derives from the Latin for plum of Damascus. It is a traditional cooking plum long grown in Britain, with skin ranging from dark blue through indigo to near black over yellow-green flesh, and a typically ovoid shape pointed at one end.

Damsons are distinctive for being both high in sugar and highly astringent, giving a rich flavour that is usually considered too tart for fresh eating. Most are clingstone, with the flesh adhering to the stone. They are grown mainly for cooking and preserving, going into jam, fruit preserves, and damson gin, and ripen in late summer to autumn.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Damson
  • Specialty Produce: Blue Damson Plums

Donsworth

Donsworth is a Japanese blood plum (Prunus salicina) long grown in Australia. It appears in the Heritage and Rare Fruits Network listing of Japanese plums, with grower records across several Australian sites, marking it as an established, recognised cultivar rather than a recent release.

It is an early season, large clingstone plum with dark pink to red skin and juicy, light red flesh. A vigorous, heavy-bearing tree, it suits fresh eating as well as jams and preserves.

3 sources
  • Heritage and Rare Fruits Network: Plums
  • Speciality Trees: Prunus salicina 'Donsworth'
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Donsworth Japanese Blood Plum

Elephant Heart

Elephant Heart is a large Japanese-type plum (Prunus salicina) raised by Luther Burbank in California in the early 1920s and released after his death. The fruit is big and heart-shaped with thick bronze-green skin that turns reddish-purple when fully ripe.

It is best known for its juicy, blood-red, freestone flesh and rich, sweet, distinctive flavour, which is why it is grouped among the blood plums. Elephant Heart ripens very late in the season, one of the last plums to mature, and crops better with a pollinator such as Santa Rosa. It is excellent fresh and also used for canning and freezing.

2 sources
  • Trees of Antiquity: Elephant Heart Plum
  • Specialty Produce: Elephant Heart Plums

Flavour Supreme

Flavour Supreme (sold as Flavor Supreme) is a pluot, an interspecific plum and apricot hybrid developed by Floyd Zaiger of Zaiger Genetics in California. Dave Wilson Nursery, the introducer of Zaiger's interspecific fruit, records it as the first pluot Zaiger released, in 1988.

The fruit has mottled green skin with red speckling, an unassuming look that hides intensely sweet, juicy, red (Bordeaux) flesh. It is one of the earlier pluots to ripen and is not self-fertile, so it needs a polleniser such as another pluot or a Japanese plum nearby to set a crop.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Pluot
  • Dave Wilson Nursery: Flavor Supreme Pluot
  • Grow Organic: Flavor Supreme Pluot

Governors

The Governor's plum is not a true plum but Flacourtia indica, a thorny shrub or small tree of the willow family (Salicaceae). It also goes by ramontchi, batoko plum, Madagascar plum and Indian plum. It is native to much of Africa, Madagascar and tropical to temperate parts of Asia, and bears spiny trunk and branches.

The small fruit is about an inch (2.5 cm) across and ripens from red to purple, with yellow or white flesh that is sweet with an acidic tang and several seeds in layered carpels. It is eaten raw or made into jam, jelly and wine, and various plant parts are used in traditional medicine.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Flacourtia indica
  • CABI Compendium: Flacourtia indica (governor's plum)

Green Gage

Green Gage is a European plum (Prunus domestica), the type known in France as Reine Claude after Queen Claude, wife of Francis I. The English name honours the Gage family, credited with introducing it to England in the early 18th century. The fruit is small and round-oval with green to yellowish-green skin and smooth pale green flesh.

Gages are prized as one of the finest dessert plums, with a rich, sweet, confectionery flavour, though they are also eaten unripe when they are crisp and pleasantly tart. Green Gage ripens in late summer and is not self-fertile, so it needs a compatible plum, gage, or damson nearby to pollinate it. It is eaten fresh and also stewed or made into jam.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Greengage
  • Orange Pippin: Old Green Gage

Gulf Beauty

Gulf Beauty (Gulfbeauty) is a low-chill Japanese plum (Prunus salicina) released by the University of Florida in 1998, part of the Gulf series bred by Wayne Sherman for mild-winter, subtropical regions with high disease pressure. All UF plum cultivars carry the Gulf prefix.

It is one of the earliest ripening plums in the series, with dark reddish purple skin over yellow flesh tinged green, sweet and sub-acid when ripe (the skin itself is sour). It is noted as very resistant to bacterial canker and moderately resistant to plum leaf scald, and benefits from a Gulf series pollinator.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS EDIS HS250: Growing Plums in Florida
  • UF/IFAS News: Four New Plum Varieties Developed By UF Researcher

Gulf Blaze

Gulf Blaze (Gulfblaze) is a low-chill Japanese plum (Prunus salicina) bred and patented by the University of Florida as part of the Gulf series, developed by Wayne Sherman for warm, subtropical climates with high disease pressure.

The fruit has dark red to purple skin over orange flesh that is sweet and sub-acid. It ripens in the middle of the Florida plum season, roughly 8 to 14 days after Gulf Beauty. The flesh is semi-freestone, weakly attached to the pit when ripe, and the fruit is firm and round.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS EDIS HS250: Growing Plums in Florida
  • UF/IFAS News: Four New Plum Varieties Developed By UF Researcher

Gulf Gold

Gulf Gold (Gulfgold) is a low-chill Japanese plum (Prunus salicina) from the University of Florida stone fruit breeding program, part of the Gulf series bred for mild-winter, subtropical climates. Unlike the patented members of the series, it is not patented.

It is a yellow-skinned plum, often with a red blush, over yellow flesh that turns semi-soft when ripe, and is regarded as the sweetest of the Gulf series. It ripens late in the series, from late May into mid-June. A drawback is its susceptibility to plum leaf scald, which generally limits tree life in Florida.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS EDIS HS250: Growing Plums in Florida
  • UF/IFAS News: Four New Plum Varieties Developed By UF Researcher

Gulf Ruby

Gulf Ruby (Gulfruby) is a low-chill Japanese plum (Prunus salicina) from the University of Florida stone fruit breeding program led by Wayne Sherman, part of the Gulf series bred for warm, subtropical climates with high disease pressure. It was selected and propagated in the early 1980s and, unlike most of the series, is not patented and may be freely propagated.

The fruit has red to purple skin over sweet yellow flesh with a greenish tinge. It ripens early in the Florida plum season, roughly 7 to 10 days before Gulf Blaze. A noted quirk is that Gulf Ruby has more trouble with bacterial canker than other low-chill cultivars, which can shorten tree life.

2 sources
  • UF/IFAS EDIS HS250: Growing Plums in Florida
  • UF/IFAS News: Four New Plum Varieties Developed By UF Researcher

Hog

The hog plum is not a true plum but most commonly refers to Spondias mombin (also called yellow mombin), a tree of the cashew and mango family (Anacardiaceae). It is native to the tropical Americas and the West Indies, and was carried by the Portuguese to Asia and Africa, where it has naturalised. True plums sit in a different family (Rosaceae).

The ovoid fruit is nearly 4 cm long and ripens yellow, with a leathery skin over a thin layer of sharp, somewhat acid pulp surrounding a single spiny kernel. It is eaten fresh or made into juice, jellies and sherbets, and the green fruit features in dishes such as Thai som tam and Nepali pickles.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Spondias mombin
  • Plants For A Future: Spondias mombin

Illawarra

The Illawarra plum is not a true plum but comes from Podocarpus elatus, a rainforest conifer in the Podocarpaceae family (not the genus Prunus). Also called plum pine or brown pine, it is native to the east coast of Australia in New South Wales and Queensland. Because it is a conifer, the seed is not enclosed in a fruit.

What people eat is a swollen fleshy stem (receptacle) that the single seed sits on, attached to the branch, ripening to a dark purple-black. It has a grape-like texture and a sweetish, mildly resinous pine flavour that is enhanced by cooking, and is used in jams, sauces, and bush-food dishes. The hard seed itself is discarded.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Podocarpus elatus
  • Australian National Botanic Gardens: Podocarpus elatus

Jambolan

The jambolan is not a true plum but the fruit of Syzygium cumini, an evergreen tree of the myrtle family (Myrtaceae), the same family as eucalyptus and guava. It is native to India, Burma (Myanmar), Sri Lanka and the Andaman Islands, and has naturalised across southern Asia and the tropics.

The clustered fruit ripens from green through light magenta to dark purple or nearly black, with juicy flesh that is often astringent and ranges from acid to fairly sweet. It is eaten fresh and turned into tarts, jam, jelly, syrup, wine and vinegar. The juice resembles grape juice and the fruit famously stains the mouth.

2 sources
  • Purdue NewCROP, Morton 'Fruits of Warm Climates': Jambolan (Internet Archive)
  • Wikipedia: Syzygium cumini

Java

The Java plum is not a true plum (genus Prunus) but Syzygium cumini, an evergreen tropical tree of the myrtle family (Myrtaceae), kin to eucalyptus and guava. It is native to the Indian subcontinent and Southeast Asia, where it is also known as jambolan, jamun and black plum, and it has naturalised widely in warm climates.

The oblong fruit colours from green to pink to deep purple-black as it matures. The flesh is juicy with a sweet, tart and astringent taste that tends to dye the tongue purple. It is eaten fresh and processed into juice, wine, vinegar and syrups. The species is considered invasive in parts of Florida, Hawaii and South Africa.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Syzygium cumini
  • Purdue NewCROP, Morton 'Fruits of Warm Climates': Jambolan (Internet Archive)

Jefferson

Jefferson is an old gage type European plum (Prunus domestica) raised from seed by Judge Buel of Albany, New York, around 1825, and named in honour of US President Thomas Jefferson. Its parentage is uncertain but it is thought to be a seedling of Coe's Golden Drop, which it resembles. It reached England in the 1840s.

The fruit is large and oval with golden yellow skin, often a pinkish red cheek and light bloom. The deep yellow flesh is juicy with a rich, sweet gage flavour balanced by some sharpness, making it a prized dessert plum. It ripens late in the season and the fruit does not all ripen at once.

2 sources
  • Orange Pippin: Jefferson gage
  • Trees of Antiquity: Jefferson Plum

Kaffir

The Kaffir plum is not a true plum but Harpephyllum caffrum, an evergreen tree of the cashew and mango family (Anacardiaceae). The older common name is now widely replaced by wild plum (or wildepruim in Afrikaans). The genus name refers to its sickle shaped leaflets. It is native to southern Africa, ranging from the Eastern Cape north through KwaZulu-Natal and into Zimbabwe.

The small fruit is a drupe that ripens from green to bright red in autumn and contains a single seed. It is distinctly tart and sour, which suits it better to preserves than to fresh eating. People use it for jams, jellies and even a rose wine, while the bark is used traditionally for skin washes.

2 sources
  • SANBI PlantZAfrica: Harpephyllum caffrum
  • Specialty Produce: Kaffir Plum

Kakadu

The Kakadu plum is not a true plum but the fruit of Terminalia ferdinandiana, a small tree in the Combretaceae family (not the genus Prunus). It grows wild across the tropical woodlands of northern Australia, from the Kimberley to Arnhem Land, and is also known as gubinge or billygoat plum. Aboriginal peoples have eaten it and used the bark and fruit medicinally for thousands of years.

The fruit is small, yellow-green, roughly almond sized, with a single stone. It is prized today as a native superfood because it is among the richest natural sources of vitamin C known, far exceeding common fruits. Almost all supply is still wild harvested by hand, and the fruit is processed into powders, jams, and skincare rather than eaten fresh in quantity.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Terminalia ferdinandiana
  • Nutritional and Functional Properties of Terminalia ferdinandiana Fruits (PMC11431513)
  • AgriFutures Australia: Australian Bush Plum (Terminalia ferdinandiana) collection

Mariposa

Mariposa is a Japanese-type blood plum that originated as a seedling at Pasadena, California, around 1923. It is sometimes sold as Improved Satsuma.

The fruit has deep red, juicy flesh and a rich, sweet, aromatic flavour with a tart skin, and the stone separates almost cleanly (nearly freestone). It ripens mid season (late summer in Australia). Mariposa crops best with a compatible pollinator such as Santa Rosa or Satsuma.

3 sources
  • Trees of Antiquity: Mariposa Plum
  • Daleys Fruit: Plum Mariposa
  • Vio's Kitchen: Mariposa Plums

Mirabelle

Mirabelle is a group of small European plums (Prunus domestica subsp. syriaca) most closely associated with the Lorraine region of France, where it has been grown for centuries. The two best known cultivars are Mirabelle de Nancy and Mirabelle de Metz.

The fruit is small and round to oval with golden yellow skin, often flecked or dappled with red, and sweet, aromatic yellow flesh around a freestone pit. Its high sugar content suits it to jam and to distillation into eau de vie, a clear fruit brandy, as well as eating fresh and baking.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Mirabelle plum
  • Specialty Produce: Mirabelle de Metz Plums

Narrabeen

Narrabeen is an Australian Japanese-type plum (Prunus salicina) that arose as a chance seedling in the Narrabeen area near Sydney. It produces large, round fruit with red skin over a yellow background.

The flesh is yellow to cream, sweet, firm, and juicy, and the stone separates cleanly, making it a freestone variety. Narrabeen ripens mid season (around January to February in Australia) and crops best with a pollinator such as Santa Rosa, Satsuma, or Mariposa. It is good eaten fresh and also stewed, bottled, or made into jam.

3 sources
  • Heritage & Rare Fruit Network: Plums
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Narrabeen Japanese Plum
  • Daleys Fruit: Plum Narrabeen

Natal

The Natal plum is not a true plum but the fruit of Carissa macrocarpa, a spiny evergreen shrub in the dogbane family (Apocynaceae), not the genus Prunus. It is native to the coastal regions of southern Africa, where it is also known as the large num-num, and is widely grown as an ornamental and security hedge thanks to its forked spines and fragrant white flowers.

The plant contains a milky latex, and only the ripe fruit is eaten; other parts are considered toxic. The plump, oval berries ripen to a deep crimson and have a tangy-sweet, raspberry-cranberry flavour. They are eaten fresh or cooked into jams, jellies, sauces, and pies.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Carissa macrocarpa
  • NC State Extension Gardener Plant Toolbox: Carissa macrocarpa (Natal Plum)

President

President is a European plum (Prunus domestica) raised by the Rivers Nursery at Sawbridgeworth, Hertfordshire, England, and introduced around 1901. It was valued as one of the latest plums to ripen, providing fresh fruit when most other plums had finished.

The fruit is very large and oval with dark blue to purple-black skin and greenish-yellow flesh, and it suits both eating fresh and culinary use such as jam and preserves. It ripens late, in mid September in the UK. President is not self-fertile, so it needs another plum variety nearby for pollination.

2 sources
  • RHS: Prunus domestica 'President' plum
  • Orange Pippin: Plum President

Primetime

Primetime is a Japanese plum (Prunus salicina) that originated in California. Its US plant patent records it as believed to be an F1 hybrid of 'Challenger' and 'Showtime', discovered by Eric Wuhl in a Fresno orchard and asexually propagated in 1987.

The fruit is very large and uniform, with skin that turns from reddish to a very dark, almost eggplant colour when fully ripe, over firm, crisp yellow flesh shot through with red and pink veining. Santa Rosa is a suggested pollinator, and in Australia it ripens early to mid season.

2 sources
  • US Plant Patent USPP9022P: Primetime plum tree
  • Flemings Nurseries: 'Primetime' [PBR] Plum

Purple Gage

Purple Gage is the English name for Reine Claude Violette, a traditional French gage type plum (Prunus domestica) first recorded in France around 1780. It belongs to the Reine Claude group of plums, which are prized as dessert fruit.

Unlike the green skinned gages, its medium, round fruit has purple to violet skin dusted with a bloom and dotted with yellow specks. The greenish amber flesh is firm, very juicy and sugary with a rich, sweet gage flavour. It ripens in late summer and is eaten fresh as a dessert plum.

2 sources
  • National Fruit Collection: Reine Claude Violette
  • Orange Pippin: Reine Claude Violette

Robe de Sergeant

Robe de Sergeant is an old French prune plum (Prunus domestica) that originated in France and was taken to California in the 1880s by the nurseryman Felix Gillet. Once planted in Californian prune districts, it fell out of commercial use because it yielded less than the Improved French prune.

The fruit is medium and roundish oval with deep purple to almost black skin under a thick grey bloom, and greenish yellow freestone flesh. It is richly flavoured, sweet and very juicy, and is used for drying into prunes, eating fresh, and for jam and preserves.

2 sources
  • Slow Food Foundation, Ark of Taste: Robe de Sergeant Plum
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Robe de Sergeant Prune

Ruby Blood

Ruby Blood is a Japanese-type blood plum (Prunus salicina) grown widely in Australia. The fruit is medium to large and round to heart-shaped, with dark red mottled skin over deep red, juicy flesh, which is what marks it out as a blood plum.

The flesh is sweet and the stone separates cleanly, making it a freestone variety. Ruby Blood ripens late in the season (late summer into autumn in Australia) and needs a cross-pollinator such as Narrabeen, Mariposa, or Satsuma. It is used fresh and also for preserves and jam.

3 sources
  • Heritage & Rare Fruit Network: Plums
  • Blerick Tree Farm: Plum Ruby Blood
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Ruby Japanese Blood Plum

Santa Rosa

Santa Rosa is a Japanese plum (Prunus salicina) bred by Luther Burbank and introduced in 1906 from his nursery at Santa Rosa, California. It became Burbank's most celebrated plum and was once the dominant commercial plum in California.

The fruit has purple-red skin over amber to yellow flesh, with a sweet, slightly tart flavour. It ripens mid season. Santa Rosa is self-fertile and is widely used as a pollinator for other Japanese plums, though crops improve when grown near a second variety.

3 sources
  • Wikipedia: Prunus salicina
  • Trees of Antiquity: Santa Rosa Plum
  • Specialty Produce: Santa Rosa Plums

Satsuma

Satsuma is a Japanese plum (Prunus salicina) introduced to the United States by Luther Burbank in the late 1800s and named after the Satsuma province of Japan. It was an early and influential blood plum in commercial cultivation.

It is known as a blood plum for its dark red skin and deep red flesh, with sweet, juicy fruit. It ripens mid season. Satsuma is at most partially self-fertile and crops far better with a pollinator such as Santa Rosa or Shiro.

3 sources
  • Trees of Antiquity: Satsuma Plum
  • Minneopa Orchards: The Satsuma Plum
  • Gardening Know How: How To Grow Satsuma Plums

Sloe

Sloe is the fruit of blackthorn (Prunus spinosa), a thorny wild shrub native to Europe and West Asia, not a cultivated dessert plum. Its dense, spiny growth has long been used to make stockproof hedges in Britain and northern Europe.

The small drupe, about 10 to 12 millimetres across, is black with a blue purple bloom and ripens in autumn. The thin flesh is intensely astringent when raw and is traditionally gathered after the first frosts, which softens it. Sloes are used to make sloe gin, where they are steeped with gin and sugar, and in jams, jellies and preserves rather than eaten fresh.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Prunus spinosa
  • Eatweeds: The Traditional and Modern Uses of Sloe (Blackthorn)

Sugar

The Sugar prune is a European plum (Prunus domestica) introduced by Luther Burbank in 1899, raised as a seedling of the French (Agen) prune. It quickly took on commercial importance in the Californian prune districts in the early 1900s.

The fruit is medium to large, larger than the French prune, oval and slightly flattened, with dark reddish purple skin under a thick white bloom. It is freestone, with golden yellow flesh that is juicy, tender and sweet with high sugar content. Despite its name, it tended to dry into a coarse, stringy product, so it was shipped extensively as a fresh plum and used for canning rather than as a top-quality dried prune.

2 sources
  • HortScience (ASHS): Luther Burbank's Plums, Vol. 50 Issue 2 (2015)
  • ResearchGate: Luther Burbank's Plums (Karp, HortScience 2015)

Sultan blood

Sultan Blood is a Japanese blood plum (Prunus salicina). The Heritage and Rare Fruits Network records it as a cross between Satsuma and Wickson, producing a large, oval, sweet fruit with dark crimson colouring.

As a blood plum it carries the deep red flesh typical of the group. It is a late ripening variety, with the same archive noting a February to March fruiting window. Like most Japanese plums it generally benefits from a compatible pollinator nearby.

2 sources
  • Heritage and Rare Fruits Network: Plums
  • Wikipedia: Prunus salicina

Victoria European

Victoria is the famous English plum (Prunus domestica), found as a seedling at Alderton, England, and introduced by the nurseryman Denyer in the 1840s as Denyer's Victoria. It went on to become the most popular plum variety in the UK.

The fruit has pale red, lightly speckled skin over golden-yellow flesh, and it is unusually versatile, good both fresh and cooked, where it makes excellent jam and pie fillings. Victoria is reliably self-fertile, so it crops without a pollinator, and ripens in late summer to early autumn.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Victoria plum
  • Brickfields Park: Victoria Plum (Prunus domestica Victoria)

Zwetschge

Zwetschge is the German name for the prune plum (Prunus domestica), a group of dark, oblong European plums grown widely across Central Europe. The same fruit is called Quetsche in Alsace and Lorraine. Many distinct varieties exist under this name.

The fruit is small and elongated with pointed ends, deep blue to purple skin under a whitish bloom, and firm flesh that parts cleanly from the long stone (freestone). Its low water and high sugar content make it the classic plum for drying into prunes and for baking, where it holds its shape, as in the German plum cake zwetschgenkuchen.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Prune plum
  • Orange Pippin: Hauszwetsche German Prune

Pomegranate

11 varieties (back to top)

Angel Red

Angel Red is a Californian cultivar (plant patent Smith, sold by Monrovia as Angel Red) prized as an early ripener, maturing from late summer into autumn ahead of many other pomegranates. It arose as a chance selection and was protected under the Plant Patent Act, so it cannot be propagated without permission.

The fruit is large and vivid red with red arils whose seeds are soft enough to eat fresh. Angel Red is noted for having less pulp and a higher juice content than many varieties, which makes it a strong choice for both fresh eating and juicing.

2 sources
  • Cultivar.guide: Pomegranate variety comparison
  • Monrovia: Angel Red Pomegranate (Punica granatum Smith)

Azerbaijani

Azerbaijani, sold in full as Gulosha Azerbaijani, takes its name from its origin in Azerbaijan and is one of the cultivars grown in Australian pomegranate evaluation trials. The skin carries a pinkish hue rather than the deep crimson of varieties like Wonderful.

Its appeal is the eating quality inside: the arils are deep red and very juicy. It is grown as a fresh-eating and juicing pomegranate.

3 sources
  • RFCA Archives, Pomegranate Variety Research Results
  • Sustainable Gardening Australia
  • Daleys Fruit Tree Nursery

Ben Hur

Ben Hur is an Australian-bred pomegranate developed for the home garden and grown above all for size: the fruit is unusually large, resembling cricket balls, with the heaviest specimens reported to reach around 1.2 kilograms.

Inside, Ben Hur carries plump deep red arils reported to be juicy, sweet and flavoursome. The vigorous, adaptable shrub crops heavily, making it a popular novelty choice for backyard growers who want oversized, eating-quality fruit.

2 sources
  • Sustainable Gardening Australia
  • Daleys Fruit Tree Nursery

Elche

Elche refers to the Spanish soft-seed cultivar properly called Mollar de Elche, named for the town of Elche in Alicante where it has long been grown. In 2016 the European Union granted it Protected Designation of Origin status, recognising it as a regional speciality.

It is best known for soft, edible seeds and a notably sweet flavour, which makes it an easy fresh-eating pomegranate. Fruit are large with dark red arils, while the skin ranges from cream to deep red depending on sun exposure. The main harvest falls in October.

3 sources
  • Fruitnet, Mollar de Elche pomegranates to get PDO
  • Fascinating Spain, Mollar Pomegranate of Elche
  • Sustainable Gardening Australia

Eversweet

Eversweet is a Californian cultivar best known for being sweet very early, with arils that taste sweet even before the fruit is fully ripe. The seeds are soft, and the variety is one of the sweetest pomegranates grown for fresh eating.

Its standout quirk is the juice, which is clear and non-staining rather than the deep red of most pomegranates, paired with pale pink to almost translucent arils under a light-coloured skin. That makes it a tidy choice for eating out of hand and for juicing without the usual red stains.

2 sources
  • Cultivar.guide: Pomegranate variety comparison
  • Dave Wilson Nursery: Eversweet pomegranate

Gayusha Rosavaya

Gayusha Rosavaya, more correctly Gulosha Rosavaya, is a Russian cultivar that rates among the top performers in Australian pomegranate variety trials. The fruit is large with attractive light pink skin and a slightly elongated, angular shape.

It is grown for flavour: the deep red arils are juicy and soft-seeded, with a sweetness lifted by gentle acidity that many growers rate the best of the balanced types. The fruit ripens through autumn, and the bush carries a distinctive slightly ruffled foliage.

3 sources
  • RFCA Archives, Pomegranate Variety Research Results
  • Sustainable Gardening Australia
  • Hanbury House, Pomegranate from Down Under: Gulosha Rosavaya

Jodhpur Red

Jodhpur Red is a favourite cultivar of the arid and semi-arid tracts of Rajasthan in northwest India, well adapted to hot, dry growing conditions. The tree is spreading and evergreen in habit. Fruit is round and smooth with pink skin carrying a reddish tinge, the kind of appearance suited to its desert home rather than the deep crimson of cooler-climate types.

Inside, the arils are pink and sweet in taste. Indian varietal reviews describe the seeds within those arils as firm rather than soft, so this is a fresh-eating and table fruit prized in its native region for sweetness rather than a melt-in-the-mouth soft-seeded type.

2 sources
  • The Pharma Innovation Journal: Varieties of pomegranate (Punica granatum) in India (2023)
  • Woodside Plants and Design: Punica granatum Jodhpur Red

Kazake

Kazake is a Central Asian selection, usually traced to Uzbekistan, that became known in the West as one of the most cold hardy pomegranates in trial. That hardiness makes it a choice for cooler gardens where many pomegranates struggle.

The fruit is lighter coloured, with yellow-green to pink skin over dark red arils that are very juicy. The flavour is a crisp sweet-tart, and unlike the soft-seed Spanish types its seeds are firm. A closed calyx helps it resist splitting, and it stores and keeps better than most, ripening around October.

2 sources
  • Cultivar Guide, Pomegranate Varieties
  • One Green World, Kazake Pomegranate

Mollar de Elche

Mollar de Elche is the famous Spanish soft-seed pomegranate from the Elche district of Alicante. The European Union recognised it with Protected Designation of Origin status in 2016, tying the name to fruit grown in that specific region of eastern Spain.

Its defining trait is very small, soft, edible seeds inside large dark red arils, paired with a sweet flavour that suits eating fresh out of hand. The skin colour varies from cream to deep red with sun exposure, and the crop is harvested through October.

3 sources
  • Fruitnet, Mollar de Elche pomegranates to get PDO
  • Fascinating Spain, Mollar Pomegranate of Elche
  • Sustainable Gardening Australia

Parfianka

Parfianka originated in Turkmenistan, collected by botanist Dr Gregory Levin and introduced to the United States through UC Davis. Like the other Levin selections it is a soft-seeded variety, with small, tender, edible seeds set in deep burgundy to cranberry-red arils.

Its reputation rests on flavour: a sweet-tart, somewhat wine-like taste that has seen it rated among the best in California Rare Fruit Growers tasting trials. It is widely grown for fresh eating and juicing, with juice as deeply coloured as the arils.

4 sources
  • Cultivar.guide: Pomegranate variety comparison
  • Ray Garden Day: Choosing varieties of pomegranate
  • Organic Garden Club of Ventura County: Pomegranate taste test
  • The Pharma Innovation Journal: Varieties of pomegranate (Punica granatum) in India (2023)

Wonderful

Wonderful is the leading commercial pomegranate cultivar in California, grown widely in the San Joaquin Valley and the variety behind the POM Wonderful brand. It is a prolific, ornamental and hardy tree producing large, red, thick-skinned fruit.

It is a main-season cultivar whose fruit ripens in late autumn (roughly October into winter). The arils are super-juicy with soft seeds and a sub-acid, sweet-tart, cherry-like, wine-like flavour, which makes it especially well suited to juicing.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: POM Wonderful
  • California Rare Fruit Growers: Pomegranate Fruit Facts

Pomelo

1 variety (back to top)

Nam Roi

Nam Roi is a Vietnamese pomelo cultivar grown mainly in the Mekong Delta provinces of Vinh Long and Hau Giang. It is prized for being nearly seedless, which made it one of Vietnam's most popular pomelos before newer export varieties rose to prominence.

The fruit is pear-shaped with a yellowish rough rind, and the flesh is pale (white to light yellow). Unlike the bitterness of grapefruit, Nam Roi has a sweet, juicy taste balanced by a mild acidity, with segments that separate easily. It is eaten fresh and also used in pomelo salads.

2 sources
  • Vietnam Online: Vietnamese Pomelo Fruit Types and Benefits
  • Great Farmer: Vietnamese Pomelo Origins, Characteristics and Varieties

Rambutan

1 variety (back to top)

Yellow

Most rambutan ripens to a bright red, but the fruit also occurs in yellow-skinned forms, and yellow rambutan refers to these golden selections. The familiar soft spines (hairs) and the white, sweet, juicy aril surrounding a single woody seed are the same; only the rind colour differs. The best documented yellow type is 'Amarillo', the first yellow rambutan registered with the Philippines' National Seed Industry Council, named for its golden colour resembling a marigold flower.

'Amarillo' produces oblong fruit with white, smooth flesh that separates easily from the seed, and it is recorded as very sweet. Its fruiting season runs roughly July to September. Yellow rambutans are uncommon next to the standard red types, so they are mainly grown as a novelty and collector's fruit rather than a commercial mainstay.

2 sources
  • NParks (Singapore) Flora & Fauna Web: Nephelium lappaceum
  • UPLB: 'Amarillo', the first Philippine yellow variety of rambutan

Raspberry

7 varieties (back to top)

Atherton

The Atherton raspberry is Rubus probus, a species native to Australia, found in the upland rainforests of Queensland (including the Atherton Tableland in far north Queensland). It is a scrambling, prickly shrub that can grow to around 2 metres and form dense thickets. This is a true native bushfood, not a cultivar of the introduced European raspberry (Rubus idaeus).

It bears bright red aggregate fruit roughly 10 to 20 mm across, ripening mainly in summer and early autumn. The edible berries are generally described as milder and sweeter than the common raspberry, used fresh or in jams and desserts. As a tropical and subtropical native, it suits warmer climates where standard raspberries can struggle.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Rubus probus
  • Aussie Green Thumb: Rubus probus (Atherton Raspberry) Growing Guide

Autumn Bliss

Autumn Bliss is a primocane-fruiting (autumn or fall-bearing) red raspberry bred at the East Malling Research Station in Kent, England, from a cross made in 1973 and released in 1984. It was the first primocane raspberry bred for UK conditions and a major breakthrough, fruiting on the tips of its first-year canes.

It ripens early for a fall-bearing type, roughly ten to fourteen days before Heritage, which concentrates much of the crop early (an advantage in cooler climates). Oregon State University and Cornell describe the fruit as large, oval to conical, attractive and medium to dark red, with a mild flavour, and note that it can be soft and darken with storage.

3 sources
  • US Plant Patent PP6597: Raspberry plant 'Autumn Bliss'
  • Cornell Cooperative Extension: Raspberry Variety Review (Weber)
  • OSU/WSU/U Idaho Extension PNW 655: Raspberry Cultivars for the Pacific Northwest

Chilcotin

Chilcotin is a summer-fruiting (floricane) red raspberry developed by the Agriculture Canada breeding program in British Columbia, named (in the program's tradition) after a British Columbian place. It is an older cultivar from this program that helped supply the fresh market.

Oregon State University lists it among floricane (summer-bearing) cultivars, describing medium-sized, medium-firmness fruit that is attractive and bright light red with good flavour and a non-darkening colour. It is grown by home gardeners across a range of climates, including in Australia.

2 sources
  • OSU/WSU/U Idaho Extension PNW 655: Raspberry Cultivars for the Pacific Northwest
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Chilcotin Raspberry

Chilliwack

Chilliwack is a summer-fruiting (floricane) red raspberry from the Pacific Agri-Food Research Centre program of Agriculture and Agri-Food Canada in British Columbia. It was selected from a cross between the selection BC 64-10-198 and Skeena, and released by H.A. Daubeny in 1987, taking its name (like other cultivars from the program) from a British Columbian place.

As a floricane variety it crops on second-year canes in summer, producing firm fruit. It was bred within the same British Columbian program that produced widely grown cultivars such as Tulameen, and it is grown in Australia by collectors and home gardeners.

2 sources
  • Kempler et al., HortScience (USDA-ARS): 'Saanich' Red Raspberry
  • Heritage Fruit Trees: Chilliwack Raspberry

Heritage

Heritage is a primocane-fruiting (autumn or fall-bearing) red raspberry from the Cornell University New York State Agricultural Experiment Station breeding program, released in 1969. It was a landmark variety, long considered the standard for fall-bearing raspberries, and it can be double-cropped (a summer crop on second-year canes plus an autumn crop on first-year primocanes).

Oregon State University describes its fruit as medium-sized, firm, attractive and bright red, with a mild flavour, ripening late so that it has a short fruiting season in cold climates. Its late primocane ripening means it does best where autumns are warm enough to ripen the crop before frost.

3 sources
  • Cornell Cooperative Extension: Raspberry Variety Review (Weber)
  • OSU/WSU/U Idaho Extension PNW 655: Raspberry Cultivars for the Pacific Northwest
  • Cornell CALS: New high-yield strawberry, raspberry varieties released

Molucca

The Molucca raspberry is Rubus moluccanus (also called the Molucca bramble), a wild scrambling shrub or climber native to eastern Australia, ranging from tropical north Queensland to Victoria in wet forests and rainforests. Its range also extends through Asia, Malesia and the Pacific Islands. It is a vigorous bramble that can form thickets, and it is not a bred cultivar.

It is recognised by its simple, lobed leaves (typically with three to five lobes) that are softly hairy beneath, and white or pink flowers in spring and summer. These are followed by bright red edible fruit. It is one of several Australian native Rubus species used as a wild bushfood rather than a commercial raspberry variety.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Rubus moluccanus
  • Australian Native Plants Society: Rubus moluccanus

Tulameen

Tulameen is a summer-fruiting (floricane) red raspberry from the Pacific Agri-Food Research Centre breeding program of Agriculture and Agri-Food Canada in British Columbia. It was selected from the cross of Nootka and Glen Prosen and released in 1991, following the program's tradition of naming cultivars after British Columbian places.

It is prized for its fruit quality, producing large to very large, firm, bright red berries with excellent flavour, and it has a notably long harvest season. Oregon State University lists it among floricane (summer-bearing) cultivars, where the fruit ripens later in the summer season. It has become widely planted internationally, including in Australia.

2 sources
  • OSU/WSU/U Idaho Extension PNW 655: Raspberry Cultivars for the Pacific Northwest
  • Kempler et al., HortScience (USDA-ARS): 'Saanich' Red Raspberry

Rollinia

2 varieties (back to top)

Biriba

Biriba (Rollinia deliciosa, also classified as Annona mucosa) is the best known edible member of its genus in the custard apple family, Annonaceae. That places it alongside soursop, cherimoya, custard apple and sugar apple as a close relative. It is native to tropical South America, ranging through the western Amazon basin from Peru and Brazil north to the Guianas, Venezuela and Colombia, and it is a favourite fruit of western Amazonia, much grown around Iquitos in Peru and in Brazil.

The tree is fast growing. The fruit is conical to heart shaped, with a yellow rind made up of segments, each tipped with a soft, wart like or spiky protrusion. A telltale quirk is that handling causes those conical projections to blacken quickly, so fruit is often picked green and hard for transport. Inside, the pulp is white, translucent, juicy and subacid to sweet, eaten fresh and sometimes fermented into wine in Brazil.

3 sources
  • Purdue NewCROP, Julia Morton, Biriba
  • Wikipedia, Rollinia deliciosa / Annona mucosa
  • Growables, Biriba (Rollinia mucosa)

Brazilian Custard Apple

Brazilian custard apple is a common name for Rollinia deliciosa (Annona mucosa), also called biriba, wild sugar apple and lemon meringue pie fruit. The fruit is large and conical to heart shaped, ripening from green to yellow, with a knobbly rind whose soft conical protuberances bruise and blacken with handling. This perishability is its biggest quirk: once fully ripe it keeps only a day or two before fermenting, with a total shelf life of less than a week, which is why it stays a regional fruit rather than a supermarket one.

What sets it apart is the eating quality. The pulp is white, translucent, juicy and creamy, with a mild subacid to sweet flavour widely likened to lemon meringue pie. It is usually eaten fresh out of hand, scooped from the rind, and the small dark seeds are not eaten. As a member of the custard apple family (Annonaceae), it sits beside soursop, cherimoya and the true custard apple, and the pulp has been blended into drinks and fermented into wine in Brazil.

4 sources
  • Purdue NewCROP, Julia Morton, Biriba
  • Wikipedia, Rollinia deliciosa / Annona mucosa
  • Growables, Biriba (Rollinia mucosa)
  • Daleys Fruit Tree Nursery, Brazilian Custard Apple (Biriba)

Sapodilla

3 varieties (back to top)

Krasuey

Krasuey (sometimes spelt Crasuey) is a small fruited Thai sapodilla, long admired by collectors who toured Thai markets and wondered why it was not yet grown in Australia. The fruit is modest in size, typically around 75 to 100 grams, with thin, light brown skin over dark brown flesh.

Its appeal is the eating quality rather than the size. The flesh is juicy and intensely sweet, almost syrupy, with the characteristic slightly grainy sapodilla texture. It is prized as one of the better flavoured Thai selections and carries a heavy crop of small fruit.

2 sources
  • Sapodilla, Rare Fruit Council of Australia archives
  • Fruit Tasting Tour of S.E. Asia, Thailand, RFCA archives

Ponderosa

Ponderosa is the heavyweight of the sapodilla world. It is a large fruited variety whose individual fruit can reach about 350 grams, well above the 120 to 200 grams typical of most other cultivars. The skin is light brown over light to mid brown flesh.

Despite its size the eating quality holds up. The flesh is described as sweet, juicy and smooth, with the usual rusty brown sapodilla skin and a flavour often likened to pear and brown sugar. Its scale makes it a standout among collector varieties.

3 sources
  • Sapodilla in Australia, RFCA archives
  • Sapodilla, Rare Fruit Council of Australia archives
  • Sapodilla, Encyclopaedia Britannica

Sawo Manilla

Sawo Manila is a long established sapodilla selection grown across South East Asia, where sawo is the local name for the fruit. It carries medium sized fruit of roughly 100 to 150 grams, with light brown skin over flesh that ranges from light to dark brown.

The flesh is juicy and sweet with only a slight graininess, true to the species character that pairs a malty, brown sugar sweetness with a soft, well ripened pear texture. A reliable, widely planted variety rather than a novelty.

2 sources
  • Sapodilla, Rare Fruit Council of Australia archives
  • Manilkara zapota, Wikipedia

Starfruit

3 varieties (back to top)

Arkin

Arkin is the benchmark commercial carambola, selected in Coral Gables, Florida by backyard horticulturalist Morris Arkin. It became the standard of the Florida industry and the dominant commercial variety grown in South Florida.

It is a sweet, low acid type that ripens to a yellow to deep yellow orange with crisp flesh. Much of its success comes from holding up to picking, packing and shipping better than other cultivars, making it the variety most people picture when they think of starfruit.

2 sources
  • Carambola Growing in the Florida Home Landscape, UF/IFAS
  • Carambola, Wikipedia

Fwang Tung

Fwang Tung is a sweet carambola from Thailand, valued for its very good flavour. Unlike the deep gold of many starfruit, its fruit ripen to a paler white to light yellow colour, a useful way to tell it apart on the tree.

The fruit are medium to large with the classic five angled, star in cross section shape. It is self pollinating and noted for cropping abundantly, even when planted in solid blocks rather than mixed with other types.

2 sources
  • Carambola Growing in the Florida Home Landscape, UF/IFAS
  • Growing Starfruit, Averrhoa carambola, Garden Oracle

Kary

Kary is a sweet carambola developed in Hawaii, where it was selected for local growing conditions. It is regarded as one of the better quality sweet types, with very good flavour and reliable cropping.

The fruit are medium to large and ripen to a yellow to deep yellow orange, with the familiar crisp, juicy, star shaped flesh. It is self pollinating, which makes it a practical choice for a single backyard tree.

2 sources
  • Carambola Growing in the Florida Home Landscape, UF/IFAS
  • Growing Starfruit, Averrhoa carambola, Garden Oracle

Tamarillo

3 varieties (back to top)

Orange

The orange (gold) tamarillo has golden-orange skin over pale orange to yellow flesh. Like the yellow form, it is milder, sweeter and less acidic than the tart red type, which makes it very good for eating fresh, simply halved and the soft pulp scooped out (often lightly sugared). The bitter skin and the tissue just beneath it are not eaten and are removed first, a trait shared by all tamarillos.

Tamarillo (Solanum betaceum) is a fast-growing subtropical small tree or shrub in the nightshade family, producing egg-shaped fruit within about one and a half to two years of planting. The orange form sits among the sweeter, gentler colour types alongside the yellow, in contrast to the sharper red.

3 sources
  • Tamarillo - Wikipedia
  • Tamarillo - California Rare Fruit Growers
  • Gold Tamarillos - Specialty Produce

Red

The red tamarillo is the most widely grown colour form and the one most people picture. It has glossy red to red-brown skin over orange to yellow-orange flesh studded with edible black seeds. The flavour leans sharper, tarter and more savoury than the gold and orange forms, with tangy, almost tomato-like acidity, which is why it shines in cooking as much as fresh. As with all tamarillos, the skin and the tissue just beneath it carry a bitter, tannin-rich taste and are scooped away rather than eaten.

The red form's acidity makes it the most versatile in the kitchen, working across sweet and savoury dishes: jams, jellies and chutneys, salsas and sauces for fish, chicken or curries. Tamarillo (Solanum betaceum) is a fast-growing subtropical small tree or shrub in the nightshade family, bearing egg-shaped fruit within about one and a half to two years of planting.

5 sources
  • Tamarillo - Wikipedia
  • Tamarillo - California Rare Fruit Growers
  • Tamarillos - New Zealand Tree Crops Association
  • Red Tamarillos - Specialty Produce
  • Brazenly Beautiful Tamarillo - RFCA Archives

Yellow

The yellow (gold) tamarillo carries the colour right through: yellow skin over yellow flesh, a noticeably milder, sweeter and less astringent profile than the tart red form. That softer acidity makes the yellow form a favourite for eating fresh, scooped straight from a halved fruit, and it is also valued for processing. As with every tamarillo, the bitter skin and the layer just beneath it are not eaten and are removed before serving.

Tamarillo (Solanum betaceum) is a fast-growing subtropical small tree or shrub in the nightshade family. It bears its egg-shaped fruit early, within about one and a half to two years of planting. The yellow form is one of the long-established colour types in cultivation and is prized where a gentler, sweeter tamarillo is wanted.

  • Tamarillos - New Zealand Tree Crops Association

Wax Jambu

4 varieties (back to top)

Pink

Pink is the familiar, representative colour form of the wax jambu (Syzygium samarangense), a glossy, bell shaped fruit also called wax apple, java apple, water apple and rose apple. The thin, waxy skin ripens to a rosy pink over white, spongy, juicy flesh, and the fruit carries a light, mildly sweet, faintly rose scented flavour. The high water content (often likened to watermelon) makes it a popular thirst quenching, fresh eating fruit.

Compared with the other colour forms, pink fruit is generally regarded as juicier and more flavourful. Several of the best known commercial cultivars are pink fruited, including Malaysian selections such as Masam Manis Pink, reflecting how the pink form has been selected and grown for quality rather than being a single wild type.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Syzygium samarangense
  • Growables: Wax Jambu (Syzygium samarangense)

Purple

The purple wax jambu (Syzygium samarangense) is the darkest end of the colour spectrum, ripening to deep purple, purplish red or nearly black. In Southeast Asia these dark fruited types are nicknamed Black Pearl and Black Diamond, and the purplish red Black Pearl is among the most highly prized wax apples in Taiwan.

Following the species rule that darker colour means greater sweetness, the deep purple forms are valued as sweet, premium fruit. They keep the same glossy waxy skin, bell shape and white spongy flesh as the lighter forms, but their intense colour sets them apart visually and commercially.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Syzygium samarangense
  • Growables: Wax Jambu (Syzygium samarangense)

Red

Red wax jambu (Syzygium samarangense) is the colour form most associated with sweetness. The skin ranges from rose red to crimson over the typical white, crisp to juicy flesh, and as a general rule the deeper the colour the sweeter the fruit, so the reddest fruits are considered the sweetest of the colour forms.

Because colour tracks quality so closely in this species, red selections are commercially prized, and named red fruited cultivars (such as Malaysian Jambu Madu Red types) are grown specifically for their sweet, deeply coloured fruit. The fruit keeps the same glossy, waxy, bell shape and refreshing, watery texture as the paler forms.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Syzygium samarangense
  • Growables: Wax Jambu (Syzygium samarangense)

White

The white wax jambu (Syzygium samarangense) is the palest colour form, with skin ripening to greenish white, ivory or cream rather than the usual pink to red. The very pale greenish white selections, sold under the name Pearl, are among the highest priced wax apples in fruit markets, so the white form is a premium type rather than an unripe looking one.

Like the coloured forms it has white, spongy, watery flesh, but the pale fruit is often noted for a crisp, almost crunchy texture and a clean, mild flavour. Under the species rule that the palest as well as the darkest fruits tend to be sweeter, the white Pearl form sits at the sweet, high quality end alongside the dark purple types.

2 sources
  • Wikipedia: Syzygium samarangense
  • Growables: Wax Jambu (Syzygium samarangense)

White Sapote

11 varieties (back to top)

Blumenthal

Blumenthal is a Californian white sapote variety. The fruit has pale yellow skin over creamy white flesh, with the sweet, custard like flavour the species is known for.

Unlike some self fruitful cultivars, Blumenthal is one of the white sapotes documented as needing cross pollination to set a good crop, so a compatible pollinator nearby helps. Cultivars such as Ortega and Vernon, which shed abundant pollen over a long flowering period, are noted as useful pollinators for varieties of this type.

2 sources
  • PROSEA (Plant Resources of South-East Asia), Casimiroa edulis
  • Daleys Fruit, White Sapote Blumenthal

Golden Globe

Golden Globe produces a small, round, symmetrical fruit noted for its excellent flavour. Ripeness is easy to judge, since the skin changes from green to pale yellow as the fruit matures.

It is one of the white sapote cultivars documented as benefiting from cross pollination, so a compatible pollinator nearby improves cropping. The flesh is the sweet, smooth, custard like pulp typical of a good white sapote.

3 sources
  • RFCA Archives, Casimiroa (white sapote varieties)
  • PROSEA (Plant Resources of South-East Asia), Casimiroa edulis
  • Daleys Fruit, White Sapote Golden Globe

Lemon Gold

Lemon Gold is a California selection of the common white sapote (Casimiroa edulis). The fruit is nearly round to slightly flattened, weighs roughly four to six ounces, and turns a light yellow as it matures. It is prized for excellent eating and keeping qualities, and unusually for the species it can be picked while still immature and will ripen well off the tree.

The tree is less vigorous than many cultivars and is a moderate cropper, typically maturing late in the season (often around November). It is very self-fertile.

3 sources
  • Rare Fruit Council of Australia archive: The White Sapote and Wooly-Leaf White Sapote
  • CRFG Fruit Facts: White Sapote (reproduced at wagrimes.net)
  • Sub-Tropical Fruit Club of Qld: White Sapote Tips

Mac's Golden

Mac's Golden is a cultivar of the woolly-leaf white sapote (Casimiroa tetrameria), the felted-leaf relative of the common white sapote. It carries good-sized fruit (around 6 to 7 ounces) with thick skin and firm flesh, and the flesh is a deep, dark yellow.

It is low-seeded, and the flavour is described as strong, aromatic and spicy. It is rated as one of the cultivars best suited to commercial production among the woolly-leaf white sapotes, and keeps well.

2 sources
  • Rare Fruit Council of Australia archive: The White Sapote and Wooly-Leaf White Sapote
  • CRFG Fruit Facts: White Sapote (reproduced at wagrimes.net)

Macs Golden

Mac's Golden is a cultivar of the woolly-leaf white sapote (Casimiroa tetrameria), the felted-leaf relative of the common white sapote. It carries good-sized fruit (around 6 to 7 ounces) with thick skin and firm flesh, and the flesh is a deep, dark yellow.

It is low-seeded, and the flavour is described as strong, aromatic and spicy. It is rated as one of the cultivars best suited to commercial production among the woolly-leaf white sapotes, and keeps well.

2 sources
  • Rare Fruit Council of Australia archive: The White Sapote and Wooly-Leaf White Sapote
  • CRFG Fruit Facts: White Sapote (reproduced at wagrimes.net)

Ortega

Ortega (also spelt Ortego) is a California selection of the common white sapote (Casimiroa edulis). The fruit is oval, averaging about four to five ounces, with good eating and keeping qualities, and is notable for usually carrying only a single seed. It ripens in the late mid-season.

Ortega is self-fertile and does not require cross-pollination, and growers value it as a reliable pollen source for other cultivars because it flowers over a long period. It is often grown as a more compact tree, which suits smaller spaces.

3 sources
  • Rare Fruit Council of Australia archive: The White Sapote and Wooly-Leaf White Sapote
  • Sub-Tropical Fruit Club of Qld: White Sapote Tips
  • Ross Creek Tropicals: White Sapote Ortega Grafted

Pike

Pike is a Californian selection (origin Santa Barbara, introduced by the USDA in 1928) and was one of the three most popular white sapote cultivars of the mid twentieth century, still found in commercial plantings. The tree is medium sized and a heavy cropper, carrying large fruit that stay green at maturity rather than turning yellow.

The flesh is sweet and custard like, typical of white sapote, with taste rated fairly good, though the skin is noticeably bitter. It is a mid season variety. Pike is also commonly grown as a vigorous seedling rootstock for grafting other cultivars.

2 sources
  • Growables (CRFG Fruit Facts reproduction), White Sapote
  • WA Grimes (CRFG Fruit Facts reproduction), White Sapote

Reinecke Commercial

Reinecke Commercial is a Californian variety (origin San Diego). The fruit is irregular in shape, weighing around 5 ounces (about 140 grams), with an attractive golden orange skin when ripe and a moderate number of seeds.

Flavour is good and the fruit has excellent keeping qualities. Even if picked prematurely it will soften and become fairly good eating, a useful trait for handling and transport. The trade off is that the tree is a relatively poor yielder.

2 sources
  • Growables (CRFG Fruit Facts reproduction), White Sapote
  • WA Grimes (CRFG Fruit Facts reproduction), White Sapote

Smathers

Smathers is a Florida white sapote variety, grouped with the woolly-leaf white sapotes (Casimiroa tetrameria), the felted-leaf relatives of the common white sapote. It is one of the more commonly available named selections in Florida.

The fruit is on the large side, around eight to nine ounces, with greenish-yellow skin and yellow pulp. University of Florida references rate its flavour as only fair, so it is grown more for reliability and fruit size than for top eating quality.

2 sources
  • Rare Fruit Council of Australia archive: The White Sapote and Wooly-Leaf White Sapote
  • UF/IFAS Extension HS304: White Sapote Growing in the Home Landscape

Suebelle

Suebelle (sometimes spelled with the synonym Hubbell) is the best known white sapote cultivar, originating in Encinitas, California, with Susan Hubbell and dating to about 1931. The fruit is on the small to medium side, yellow to yellow-orange when ripe, often slightly asymmetrical, with sweet, creamy, custard-like flesh of excellent flavour. It has long been a benchmark variety and remains common in the nursery trade.

Its standout trait is habit: Suebelle is a compact, semi-dwarf tree that stays smaller than many seedling white sapotes, making it a popular home-garden choice. It is also valued as an ever-bearing selection, producing fruit over a very long, almost year-round season with reliable, consistent yields rather than a single concentrated crop.

3 sources
  • University of California Cooperative Extension, Ventura County - White Sapote
  • California Rare Fruit Growers - White Sapote Fruit Facts
  • Rare Fruit Council Australia archive (via Growables) - White Sapote

Vernon

Vernon is a Californian white sapote selection associated with Vista, California, and recorded by the California Rare Fruit Growers as a seedling of the older Suebelle cultivar. It produces a round, green to greenish fruit of medium size with smooth thin skin and creamy white custard-like flesh. The flavour is sweet, rich and creamy in the typical white sapote style (often likened to banana, peach and vanilla), and a frequently noted quirk is that the flesh does not turn bitter when overripe, unlike some other selections.

The tree is vigorous with a rounded form and is regarded as productive. Vernon tends to ripen late, carrying its crop into the cooler months, though it is often described as an alternate (biennial) bearer rather than a steady year-round cropper. It is one of the more widely grown grafted white sapotes.
